Welcome to the BIF Act Guide

Select a topic from the guide on the left.

Requirements of a payment claim

A construction contract must exist

The BIF Act payment regime only switches on if there is a “construction contract” between you and the other side. Without one, no payment claim can be made and any adjudication that follows has no foundation.

Section 64 BIF Act — “construction contract”

construction contract means a contract, agreement or other arrangement under which 1 party undertakes to carry out construction work for, or to supply related goods and services to, another party.

What counts

The definition does three things at once. It captures any contract or agreement; it extends to an “other arrangement” beyond a contract; and it removes any requirement that the agreement be in writing. An oral agreement is enough, and so is a partly-oral / partly-written exchange — for example, a series of emails confirming scope and price, or a sequence of agreed meeting minutes.1

The work being undertaken must be construction work, or the supply of related goods and services in connection with construction work. Those concepts are defined separately at s 65 and s 66 and are dealt with on the next two pages.

The “other arrangement” limb

The “other arrangement” words exist to catch dealings that don’t quite fit the legal definition of a contract — for instance, a head contractor’s direction to a subcontractor to perform variation work where the parties are operating informally — without letting in every situation where someone has done work for someone else. Drawing the line has produced the most case law under this part of the Act.

The current position is that an “other arrangement” must give rise to either:

  • a legally binding obligation (not necessarily a contract — an estoppel or other source of legal compulsion can suffice, provided some legal consequence flows from it); or
  • a “concluded state of affairs” involving reciprocity — that is, both parties have accepted mutual rights and obligations relating to payment or price for the works.

The most recent appellate guidance is Bettar Holdings Pty Ltd trading as Hunt Collaborative v RWC Brookvale Investment Pty Ltd as trustee for Brookvale Development Trust [2025] NSWCA 242. The pleading in Bettar alleged only a common law contract or, in the alternative, an estoppel preventing denial of a contract. The NSW Court of Appeal held that wasn’t enough — what was missing was a separate allegation that the claimant had “undertaken” to perform work on some basis other than a common law contract. McHugh JA (Bell CJ and Kirk JA agreeing) put the test for an “other arrangement” in these terms:

[139] a legally binding obligation… or a concluded state of affairs… involving some element of reciprocity or acceptance of mutual rights and obligations relating to payment or price for the works.

His Honour expressly declined to resolve the earlier first-instance conflict between Lendlease Engineering Pty Ltd v Timecon Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 685 (Ball J — required a legally binding obligation) and Crown Green Square Pty Ltd v Transport for NSW [2018] NSWSC 1080 (McDougall J — adopted a less stringent reciprocity-based test),2 because the pleading in Bettar failed under either test. So which test applies in close cases remains open at appellate level.

The practical consequence is that a bare quantum-meruit claim — where work has been done but there is no agreement at all about price or payment terms — is unlikely to qualify. The “arrangement” requires reciprocal payment obligations, not merely the fact that work was performed.

What this means on a project

  • If you have a signed contract, a construction contract exists. Move on to the next element.
  • If your engagement is by exchange of emails, purchase orders or agreed meeting minutes, that’s still capable of being a construction contract — provided there is offer, acceptance and an intention to be bound. Document what was agreed and when; you may need to prove it later.
  • For variation or out-of-scope work without a written variation order, capture the instruction and your response in writing as soon as possible. Most “other arrangement” disputes turn on whether there is evidence of agreement on price or rate.
  • A bare quantum-meruit case — just a claim for the value of work done, with no agreement — will probably fall outside the Act. You’d need to show reciprocal payment obligations, not just the fact of work done.
  • If you anticipate a challenge to the existence of a contract, plead the arrangement carefully: the material facts of the agreement, the undertaking to carry out work, and the payment obligation. After Bettar the appellate courts are scrutinising those pleadings closely.
  1. That a written instrument is not required follows from the words “contract, agreement or other arrangement” in s 64: see Okaroo Pty Ltd v Vos Construction & Joinery Pty Ltd [2005] NSWSC 45 (Nicholas J); Machkevitch v Andrew Building Constructions Pty Ltd [2012] NSWSC 546 (McDougall J).
  2. Lendlease Engineering Pty Ltd v Timecon Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 685, [29]–[33] (Ball J); Crown Green Square Pty Ltd v Transport for NSW [2018] NSWSC 1080 (McDougall J). For Queensland, Capricorn Quarries Pty Ltd v Devcon Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 28 (Daubney J) is the leading reading of the contract limb of the BIF Act definition.

Requirements of a payment claim

The work must be construction work or related goods and services

A construction contract only attracts the BIF Act regime if what you are doing under it is “construction work” or the supply of “related goods and services”. The two terms are defined separately at s 65 and s 66, and together they set the outer boundary of who can claim under the Act.

Sections 65 and 66 BIF Act — construction work and related goods and services

65 Meaning of construction work

(1) Construction work means any of the following work—

(a) the construction, alteration, repair, restoration, maintenance, extension, demolition or dismantling of buildings or structures, whether permanent or not, forming, or to form, part of land;

(b) the construction, alteration, repair, restoration, maintenance, extension, demolition or dismantling of any works forming, or to form, part of land, including walls, roadworks, powerlines, telecommunication apparatus, aircraft runways, docks and harbours, railways, inland waterways, pipelines, reservoirs, water mains, wells, sewers, industrial plant and installations for land drainage or coast protection;

(c) the installation in any building, structure or works of fittings forming, or to form, part of land, including heating, lighting, air-conditioning, ventilation, power supply, drainage, sanitation, water supply, fire protection, security and communications systems;

(d) the external or internal cleaning of buildings, structures and works, so far as it is carried out in the course of their construction, alteration, repair, restoration, maintenance or extension;

(e) any operation that forms an integral part of, or is preparatory to or is for completing, work of the kind referred to in paragraph (a), (b) or (c), including—

(i) site clearance, earthmoving, excavation, tunnelling and boring; and (ii) the laying of foundations; and (iii) the erection, maintenance or dismantling of scaffolding; and (iv) the prefabrication of components to form part of any building, structure or works, whether carried out on-site or off-site; and (v) site restoration, landscaping and the provision of roadways and other access works;

(f) the painting or decorating of the internal or external surfaces of any building, structure or works;

(g) carrying out the testing of soils and road making materials during the construction and maintenance of roads;

(h) any other work of a kind prescribed by regulation.

(2) To remove doubt, it is declared that construction work includes building work within the meaning of the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991.

(3) However, construction work does not include any of the following work—

(a) the drilling for, or extraction of, oil or natural gas;

(b) the extraction, whether by underground or surface working, of minerals, including tunnelling or boring, or constructing underground works, for that purpose.

66 Meaning of related goods and services

(1) Related goods and services, in relation to construction work, means any of the following—

(a) goods of the following kind— (i) materials and components to form part of any building, structure or work arising from construction work; (ii) plant or materials (whether supplied by sale, hire or otherwise) for use in connection with the carrying out of construction work;

(b) services of the following kind— (i) the provision of labour to carry out construction work; (ii) architectural, design, surveying or quantity surveying services relating to construction work; (iii) building, engineering, interior or exterior decoration or landscape advisory services relating to construction work; (iv) soil testing services relating to construction work;

(c) goods and services, relating to construction work, of a kind prescribed by regulation.

(2) In this chapter, a reference to related goods and services includes a reference to related goods or services.

How the courts read these definitions

A single contract can mix “construction work” with work that isn’t. The Act applies to the part that qualifies. The starting point in Queensland is that the s 65 and s 66 definitions get a “natural” reading — not a strained pro-claimant or pro-respondent one. In Capricorn Quarries Pty Ltd v Inline Communication Construction Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 388; [2013] 2 Qd R 1, Jackson J rejected the argument that the predecessor Act should be given a “remedial” or liberal construction. His Honour held:

The language chosen by Parliament to define “construction contract”, “construction work” and “related goods and services” has no purpose other than to draw the line between who is in and who is out of those classes. There seems to be little logic in seeking to stretch that language either way.

The practical takeaway is that you can’t expand “construction work” just because the Act is protective of contractors, and a respondent can’t shrink it just because the Act imposes obligations. Read the words as written.

“Forming, or to form, part of land”

Most paragraphs of s 65(1) hinge on whether the building, structure or works form, or are to form, part of land. The leading Queensland authority is J & D Rigging Pty Ltd v Agripower Australia Ltd [2013] QCA 406, where the Court of Appeal rejected attempts to import common law fixtures concepts. Applegarth J (Holmes JA and Boddice J agreeing) held that the inquiry is a “practical inquiry into the physical relationship between an item and land” — what matters is the present physical state of things, not the parties’ subjective intention years earlier and not the law of fixtures. Degree of annexation is relevant; ownership rules are not.1 The phrase has been treated as one “of wide meaning”,2 and there is no requirement that the works be intended to be permanent.3

Preparatory and integral operations — s 65(1)(e)

Paragraph (e) sweeps in operations that are preparatory to, integral to, or for completing, work in paragraphs (a)–(c). It only catches an operation because of its close connection to that other construction work, so the connection to land in (a)–(c) carries through.4 Site clearing and grubbing fall within (e)(i),5 and prefabricated components — for example a shiploader and tripper assembled off-site for installation onto a wharf — fall within (e)(iv).6

The mining and resources carve-out — s 65(3)

Section 65(3) excludes drilling/extraction of oil or natural gas, and extraction of minerals (including tunnelling, boring or underground works “for that purpose”). Coal counts as a mineral.7 The exclusion is read narrowly: it is the actual process of extraction that is carved out, not work that is merely preparatory to opening or operating a mine. Work done to open up a mine, or to construct access to underground levels before extraction begins, has been held to remain within “construction work”.8

Related goods and services — the “for use in connection with” test

Section 66 picks up materials, plant, labour and a defined list of professional services. The phrase “for use in connection with the carrying out of construction work” in s 66(1)(a)(ii) is purposive: it has to be satisfied at the outset of the transaction, by reference to what the plant or materials were intended to be used for at the time of the contract or supply — not just by what happened to be done with them later.9 Architectural and project management services have been accepted as “related”,10 but a bare licence to use design drawings has not.11 Plant and materials a contractor orders simply to put itself in a position to perform — and the cost of demobilising equipment off site — have also been held to fall outside both s 65 and s 66.12

What this means on a project

  • If your scope is mainstream building, civil or fit-out work that ends up attached to land, you are squarely within s 65. Treat the question as settled and move to the next element.
  • For mixed contracts (e.g. construction plus pure equipment supply, or construction plus a software licence), separate the qualifying scope from the rest before you frame the payment claim. Only the qualifying scope can be claimed under the Act.
  • For off-site prefabrication, accept that fabrication of components destined for the works is captured by s 65(1)(e)(iv) — you don’t lose BIF Act coverage just because the work happens in a yard rather than on site.
  • On resources projects, draw the line carefully. Roads, dams, accommodation, processing plants and access tunnels built before extraction are likely to be construction work; the actual extraction of coal, minerals, oil or gas is not.
  • For plant hire, supply or design services, identify the construction project the supply is “for” at the time of the deal. If the connection to a specific construction project can’t be shown at the contract date, s 66 may not be available.
  • Don’t pad a payment claim with items that aren’t construction work or related goods and services (e.g. demobilisation costs, standalone IP licences). Including non-qualifying amounts won’t kill the whole claim, but it gives the respondent and adjudicator easy ground to cut value.
  1. J & D Rigging Pty Ltd v Agripower Australia Ltd [2013] QCA 406 (Applegarth J, Holmes JA and Boddice J agreeing) — “land” takes its ordinary meaning; the inquiry is into the physical relationship between the item and the land, not common law fixtures or the parties’ intention at the time of construction.
  2. Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345, [28] (Fryberg J) — “works to form part of land” is “a phrase of wide meaning” and should be given a practical interpretation that minimises potential disputes.
  3. Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345, [31] (Fryberg J) — works under s 65(1)(b) need not be intended to be permanent. See also BCS Infrastructure Support Pty Ltd v Jones Lang Lasalle (NSW) Pty Ltd [2020] VSC 739 (Stynes J) — maintenance of baggage conveyors, aerobridges, check-in kiosks and baggage carousels permanently attached to a terminal building qualified under the NSW equivalent of s 65(1)(b).
  4. Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307 (Philip McMurdo J) — an operation falls within paragraph (e) only because of its close connection with construction work in (a), (b) or (c), which carries the connection-with-land requirement through.
  5. Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345 (Fryberg J); affirmed Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2012] QCA 276; [2013] 2 Qd R 75 — clearing and grubbing fall within s 65(1)(e)(i).
  6. Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307 (Philip McMurdo J) — a prefabricated shiploader and tripper for installation onto a wharf were components to form part of the wharf within s 65(1)(e)(iv).
  7. Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345, [39] (Fryberg J) — coal is a “mineral” for the purposes of s 65(3).
  8. Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345, [42] (Fryberg J), approved Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2012] QCA 276; [2013] 2 Qd R 75, 91 [62]–[63] (Philippides J, Holmes and White JJA agreeing); followed in HM Mire Pty Ltd v National Plant and Equipment Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 4, [13] (Douglas J). See also Cadia Holdings Pty Ltd v Downer EDI Mining Pty Ltd [2020] NSWSC 1588 (Stevenson J) — tunnelling and boring to construct access to undercut and extraction levels was construction work; the mining exception applies only where the work is for the actual purpose of extracting minerals.
  9. Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 345 (Fryberg J), adopted in Capricorn Quarries Pty Ltd v Inline Communication Construction Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 388; [2013] 2 Qd R 1, 11 [49] (Jackson J) — “for use in connection with” carries a purposive meaning and must be satisfied at the time of the contract or supply, not by reference to subsequent use.
  10. Peter’s of Kensington v Seeksucker Pty Ltd [2008] NSWSC 897 — architectural services qualify; Biseja Pty Ltd v NSI Group Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 835 — project management services qualify.
  11. Sheppard Homes Pty Ltd v FADL Industrial Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 228 (Fryberg J) — an agreement granting a builder a licence to use design drawings was not the supply of “related goods and services”.
  12. Patrick Stevedores Operations (No 2) Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd [2014] NSWSC 1413 (Ball J) — plant and materials ordered to put the claimant in a position to perform, and the cost of removing equipment from site, were not construction work or related goods or services. See also Walter Construction Group Ltd v CPL (Surry Hills) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 266 (Nicholas J) — an extension of time claim was held to relate to construction work.

Requirements of a payment claim

Chapter 3 must apply (and not be excluded)

Even where there is a construction contract, Chapter 3 of the BIF Act — the part that gives you the right to make a payment claim and pursue adjudication — only operates if your contract is one to which the chapter applies. Section 61 sets out the gateway: a wide general application, then a list of carve-outs that take particular contracts (or particular parts of them) outside the regime. If your contract falls within an exception, the payment claim and any adjudication that follows are jurisdictionally exposed.

Section 61 BIF Act — Application of chapter

(1) Subject to subsections (2) to (4), this chapter applies to construction contracts—

(a) whether written or oral, or partly written and partly oral; and

(b) whether expressed to be governed by the law of Queensland or a jurisdiction other than Queensland; and

(c) whether entered into before or after the commencement of this section…

(2) This chapter does not apply to—

(a) a construction contract to the extent that it forms part of a loan agreement, a contract of guarantee or a contract of insurance under which a recognised financial institution undertakes [to lend, guarantee or indemnify in connection with the work]; or

(b) a construction contract for the carrying out of domestic building work if a resident owner is a party to the contract…; or

(c) a construction contract under which it is agreed that the consideration payable… is to be calculated other than by reference to the value of the work carried out or the value of the goods and services supplied.

(3) This chapter does not apply to a construction contract to the extent it includes [employee provisions, loan-conditional provisions, or lend/guarantee/indemnity provisions].

(4) This chapter does not apply to a construction contract to the extent it deals with construction work carried out outside Queensland or related goods and services supplied for construction work carried out outside Queensland.

The default position is broad application

Section 61(1) is deliberately wide. The chapter applies whether your contract is written, oral, or partly each; whether it is governed by Queensland law or another jurisdiction’s law; and whether it was entered into before or after commencement. The choice-of-law point is important on national projects: a contract expressed to be governed by NSW or Victorian law is still caught by the BIF Act if the work is carried out in Queensland. You cannot contract out by picking a foreign proper law.

The exceptions in s 61(2) to s 61(4) operate as carve-outs from this broad rule. The party who wants to keep a contract outside the Act bears the onus of bringing it within an exception.1 That onus matters in practice: if a respondent wants to argue that, say, the “domestic building work” carve-out applies, it is for them to prove the facts that engage it.

Strict compliance and the “rough and ready” regime

The Act sets up what McDougall J in Musico v Davenport [2003] NSWSC 977 famously described as “a somewhat rough and ready way of assessing a builder’s entitlement to progress claims” (at [107]). The trade-off for that speed is that the procedural requirements of the chapter must be strictly observed. As Lyons J put it in F.K. Gardner & Sons Pty Ltd v Dimin Pty Ltd [2007] 1 Qd R 10; [2006] QSC 243 at [24], the regime “depends on strict compliance with the provisions in the Act”.2 If you want the benefit of the statutory shortcut — quick payment, adjudication, judgment debt — you have to come within its four corners. The chapter coexists with your ordinary contractual rights: it creates a parallel statutory regime, not a replacement for the contract.3

The exclusions you actually meet on site

Most of the s 61 exclusions are obvious on their face — pure loan, guarantee or insurance arrangements, employment-style provisions, and the like. Three carve-outs come up often enough on commercial projects to need a closer look.

Domestic building work for a resident owner (s 61(2)(b)). The chapter does not apply to a contract for domestic building work where a “resident owner” (defined by reference to schedule 1B of the QBCC Act) is a party. The exemption is read against the policy of protecting end-consumers from a fast statutory regime designed for industry participants — and an owner can have more than one residence for this purpose.4 Importantly, the exception does not apply where the owner holds (or should hold) an owner-builder permit, or is a building contractor, under the QBCC Act.

Consideration not calculated by reference to value (s 61(2)(c)). If the price is a fixed lump unrelated to the value of the work or supplies — for example, a management fee structured purely as a percentage of head-contract amounts — the contract may sit outside the chapter.5

Work carried out outside Queensland (s 61(4)). The chapter does not apply “to the extent” the contract deals with work outside Queensland. The leading recent decision is Lendlease Building Pty Ltd v BCS Airport Systems Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 164, which arose from the Gold Coast Airport project — a site that physically straddles the Queensland–NSW border. Lendlease tried to quash an adjudication on the basis that some of BCS’s work was performed in NSW. Sullivan J rejected the argument. Section 61(4) only displaces the BIF Act where the construction work is “wholly” outside Queensland; what matters is the location of the building, structure or works, not the location of every individual activity. A payment claim is not invalidated merely because it includes a component referable to work outside the State, and the Act does not require a cross-border claim to identify the location of each work item with precision.

The same approach was taken earlier in Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 307, where Philip McMurdo J held that fabrication of a shiploader and tripper in Malaysia, while physically performed offshore, was “an integral part of construction work undertaken inside Queensland” at the Wiggins Island terminal — so s 3(4) of BCIPA (the predecessor to s 61(4)) did not displace the Act. The territorial limit is concerned with whether the contract has a “relevant association with Queensland”, defined by where the relevant land sits.

Indemnity-only claims fall outside the chapter

Section 61(3)(c)(iii) excludes provisions under which a party undertakes “to provide an indemnity relating to construction work carried out, or related goods and services supplied”. In Forte Sydney Carlingford Pty Ltd v Li [2022] FCA 1499, Stewart J held that where the entire payment claim was founded on an indemnity under the contract — rather than for the construction work itself — the equivalent NSW provision excluded the whole claim from adjudication, rendering the payment claim invalid. The lesson for Queensland is the same: if the substance of what you are claiming is an indemnity payment dressed up as a progress claim, s 61(3)(c)(iii) is likely to bite.

What this means on a project

  • Start by checking whether your contract falls within s 61(1). Written, oral, partly each, governed by foreign law, pre- or post-commencement — all are caught. The foreign-law point is important: choosing NSW law in your contract does not get you out of the BIF Act for Queensland work.
  • If you are a respondent wanting to rely on an exception, remember the onus is on you. Plead the facts that engage the carve-out and have evidence ready.
  • For cross-border projects (Gold Coast Airport, the Tweed, Wiggins-style fabrication offshore), do not assume s 61(4) gives the respondent a knockout. After Lendlease v BCS, the Act applies unless the work is “wholly” outside Queensland, and offshore fabrication that forms an integral part of a Queensland structure stays within the regime.
  • Watch s 61(3)(c)(iii) on claims that are really about indemnities — rectification cost recovery, third-party liability passes-through, insurance shortfalls. If the substance of the claim is an indemnity, it is excluded, and an adjudicator who decides it anyway is acting without jurisdiction.
  • Domestic-build claims involving a resident owner generally fall outside the Act — but only if the owner is genuinely a resident owner and is not themselves a building contractor or holder of an owner-builder permit. Check the QBCC Act schedule 1B definition before assuming the exemption applies.
  • The price-calculation exception in s 61(2)(c) is narrow but real. If your contract pays you on something other than the value of work or supplies, take advice before issuing a payment claim — an unsuccessful adjudication on a contract outside the chapter is wasted cost.
  • Treat the procedural requirements of the chapter as mandatory. The courts have consistently said that the price of the statutory shortcut is strict compliance.

Contrast the “reasonable compliance” line for payment-claim form: T&M Buckley Pty Ltd v 57 Moss Rd Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 381, [33] (Philippides J; Fraser and White JJA agreeing); Nepean Engineering Pty Ltd v Total Process Services Pty Ltd (in liq) [2005] NSWCA 409, [34] (Hodgson JA), [76] (Ipp JA); Hawkins Construction (Australia) Pty Ltd v Mac’s Industrial Pipework Pty Ltd [2002] NSWCA 136 (Davies AJA).

On the related Victorian test of whether a party is “in the business of building residences”, see BWAY Group Pty v Pasiopoulos [2019] VCC 691 (Marks J).
  5. Brambles Australia Limited v Philip Davenport & Ors [2004] NSWSC 120 (Einstein J), applying the approach of Nicholas J in Walter Construction Group Limited v CPL (Surry Hills) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 266; Sheppard Homes Pty Ltd v FADL Industrial Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 228 (Fryberg J) — a consultancy contract whose price was tied to amounts payable by the owner to the builder, rather than to the value of the consultant’s services, fell within the equivalent BCIPA exclusion. On “forms part of” in s 61(2)(a), see Consolidated Constructions Pty Ltd v Ettamogah Pub [2004] NSWSC 110, [21] (McDougall J) — inclusion or incorporation, not mere association. On “guarantee payment” in s 61(3)(c), see Walton Construction (Qld) Pty Ltd v Robert Salce & Ors [2008] QSC 235.

Requirements of a payment claim

The claimant must be entitled to a progress payment

Section 70 is the gateway to the BIF Act payment regime. Before you can serve a valid payment claim, you must be a person who is “entitled to a progress payment” — which means you have actually carried out construction work, or supplied related goods and services, under the contract, and a reference date has arisen.

Section 70 BIF Act — Right to progress payments

From each reference date under a construction contract, a person is entitled to a progress payment if the person has carried out construction work, or supplied related goods and services, under the contract.

The two limbs of entitlement

Section 70 sets up two cumulative requirements. First, you must have carried out construction work (s 65) or supplied related goods and services (s 66) under the contract — that is the work limb. Second, a reference date under the contract must have arisen — that is the timing limb. If either is missing, there is no statutory entitlement and any payment claim served is invalid. The leading authority is the High Court’s decision in Southern Han Breakfast Point Pty Ltd (in liq) v Lewence Construction Pty Ltd [2016] HCA 52, which confirmed that the existence of a reference date is a precondition to a valid payment claim, not just a timing rule.1

The 2017 Act simplified the BCIPA wording from “a person is entitled to a progress payment if the person has undertaken to carry out construction work” to “a person who has carried out construction work”. The explanatory notes record that s 70 “essentially replicates” s 12 of BCIPA, so earlier decisions on the BCIPA wording continue to apply.

“From” each reference date

The opening words “from each reference date” have generated a long-running argument in Queensland about whether a payment claim must be served on the reference date or whether it can be served on or after it. In Reed Constructions (Qld) Pty Ltd v Martinek Holdings Pty Ltd [2009] QSC 345 Daubney J read the words narrowly — one opportunity per reference date, and a late claim is postponed to the next one. Fryberg J expressly disagreed in Tenix Alliance Pty Ltd v Magaldi Power Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 7, taking the view that the words create an entitlement “on and after” the reference date.2 The better-aligned position, applied by Mullins J in McNab Developments (Qld) Pty Ltd v MAK Construction Services Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 293, is that the statutory right can be exercised at any time after the reference date arises, subject to the s 75 limits on how often you can claim and the six-month rule.

Preconditions in the contract: what is void and what isn’t

Contracts often try to bolt extra conditions onto the right to a progress payment — a statutory declaration about subcontractor payments, a superintendent’s opinion, a draft claim served first. The key Queensland authority is John Holland Pty Ltd v Coastal Dredging & Construction Pty Ltd [2012] QCA 150; [2012] 2 Qd R 435, where the Court of Appeal held that a contractual provision purporting to defer the statutory entitlement to a progress payment from a reference date that has otherwise arisen is void under what is now s 200 of the BIF Act. Fraser JA (White JA and Peter Lyons J agreeing) put the underlying principle this way:

None of those provisions bear upon the date upon which the statutory entitlement to progress payments accrues. Bearing in mind the statutory object and the role of s 12 and the definition of “reference date” in giving effect to that object, those provisions are incapable of justifying an implication that the date upon which the statutory entitlement to a progress payment accrues may be qualified by contractual provisions other than those captured by the unambiguous terms of the definition of “reference date”.

Applegarth J refined the analysis in Lean Field Developments Pty Ltd v E & I Global Solutions (Aust) Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 293, drawing a distinction that matters in practice. There are two categories: preconditions to making a payment claim where a reference date has already arisen (these are void, because they defer an entitlement that has accrued); and preconditions to the arising of the reference date itself (these can be valid, but only if they don’t make the reference date “more of a theoretical possibility than an actuality”).3 A statutory-declaration precondition has repeatedly been struck down on this approach — see BHW Solutions, BRB Modular and J Hutchinson v Glavcom.4

Termination, insolvency and other edge cases

Once a contract is terminated, no new reference date accrues under it — you can’t generate fresh entitlements out of a contract that no longer exists.5 A reference date that had already accrued before termination, but was unused, can still support a payment claim served afterwards. Insolvency is messier: Façade Treatment Engineering Pty Ltd (in liq) v Brookfield Multiplex [2016] VSCA 247 held the Victorian equivalent of s 70 does not give a company in liquidation an entitlement, while the NSW Court of Appeal in Seymour Whyte Constructions Pty Ltd v Ostwald Bros Pty Ltd (in liq) [2019] NSWCA 11 reached the opposite conclusion on the NSW wording.6 The Queensland District Court in Tantallon Constructions Pty Ltd (in liq) v Santos GLNG [2016] QDC 324 followed the Façade line under BCIPA. The position under the BIF Act has not been authoritatively resolved.

What this means on a project

  • Before serving any payment claim, check both limbs: have you actually performed the work, and has a reference date arisen under the contract (or under s 67 if the contract is silent)? Either gap is fatal.
  • If your contract makes payment conditional on a statutory declaration, a superintendent forming an opinion, or a draft claim being served first, those conditions are very likely void to the extent they defer an accrued statutory entitlement. Don’t treat them as a bar to claiming.
  • Don’t serve before the reference date. Even if the work is done, a claim served before the reference date arises is not supported by a valid reference date and will be invalid — Southern Han is clear on that.
  • If the contract has been terminated, identify the last reference date that accrued before termination. That’s the only one you can still use; new ones won’t come into existence.
  • If the claimant is in liquidation, expect a fight. The Queensland position is unsettled and you may need to structure the claim through a deed of company arrangement or pursue it outside the BIF Act regime.
  1. Southern Han Breakfast Point Pty Ltd (in liq) v Lewence Construction Pty Ltd [2016] HCA 52 — reference date is a precondition to a valid payment claim under the security of payment legislation; applied in Queensland in St Hilliers Property Pty Ltd v ACT Projects Pty Ltd [2017] ACTSC 177 (Walmsley AJ) and adopted as orthodoxy in subsequent Queensland decisions.
  2. For the contractual-entitlement (as opposed to statutory) reading, see CKP Constructions Pty Ltd v Gabba Holdings Pty Ltd [2016] QDC 356 (McGill SC DCJ) and the Queensland Court of Appeal in John Holland Pty Ltd v Coastal Dredging and Construction Pty Ltd [2012] 2 Qd R 435 on restrictive contractual language.
  3. Lean Field Developments Pty Ltd v E & I Global Solutions (Aust) Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 293, [66]–[74] (Applegarth J); reaffirmed and slightly recalibrated in BRB Modular Pty Ltd v AWX Constructions Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 218 (Applegarth J). For application to a “draft progress claim” precondition under the NSW Act, see East End Projects Pty Ltd v GJ Building and Contracting Pty Ltd [2020] NSWSC 819 (Ball J).
  4. BHW Solutions Pty Ltd v Altitude Constructions Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 214 (Mullins J); BRB Modular Pty Ltd v AWX Constructions Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 218 (Applegarth J); J Hutchinson Pty Ltd v Glavcom Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 126 (Ball J); Castle Constructions Pty Ltd v Ghossayn Group Pty Ltd [2017] NSWSC 1317 (Stevenson J — superintendent “forming an opinion” precondition void). For a Western Australian/SA application, see Wärtsilä Australia Pty Ltd v Primero Group Ltd [2020] SASC 162 (Stanley J).
  5. Walton Construction (Qld) Pty Ltd v Corrosion Control Technology Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 67, [42]–[45] (Peter Lyons J); followed in McNab NQ Pty Ltd v Walkrete Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 128. The unused-reference-date qualification comes from McNab Developments (Qld) Pty Ltd v MAK Construction Services Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 293, [15]–[18] (Mullins J), and was acknowledged in Kellett Street Partners Pty Ltd v Pacific Rim Trading Co Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 298 (Douglas J).
  6. Façade Treatment Engineering Pty Ltd (in liq) v Brookfield Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd [2016] VSCA 247; Seymour Whyte Constructions Pty Ltd v Ostwald Bros Pty Ltd (in liq) [2019] NSWCA 11; Tantallon Constructions Pty Ltd (in liq) v Santos GLNG [2016] QDC 324 (Reid DCJ); for the deed-of-company-arrangement workaround under the NSW Act, see Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd v Richard Crookes Construction Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 99 (Ball J). The BIF Act has no equivalent of NSW s 32B, so the Queensland position remains governed by Tantallon.

Requirements of a payment claim

A valid reference date must have arisen

Every payment claim under the BIF Act has to be tied to a “reference date”. If no reference date has arisen, or if you have already used the only one available, the document you serve is not a payment claim under the Act and any adjudication that follows is liable to be set aside for want of jurisdiction.

Section 67 BIF Act — meaning of reference date

(1) A reference date, for a construction contract, means—

(a) a date stated in, or worked out under, the contract as the date on which a claim for a progress payment may be made for construction work carried out, or related goods and services supplied, under the contract; or

(b) if the contract does not provide for the matter—

(i) the last day of the month in which the construction work was first carried out, or the related goods and services were first supplied, under the contract; and

(ii) the last day of each later month.

(2) However, if a construction contract is terminated and the contract does not provide for, or purports to prevent, a reference date surviving beyond termination, the final reference date for the contract is the date the contract is terminated.

The two routes to a reference date

Section 67 gives you a contractual route and a statutory default. Under s 67(1)(a), if the contract states a date or supplies a formula from which a date can be calculated, that is your reference date. Under s 67(1)(b), if the contract is silent, the default kicks in: the last day of the month in which work was first carried out, and the last day of every later month. Section 67(2) then provides a final reference date on termination, where the contract itself doesn’t deal with survival.

“Worked out under” the contract means worked out by applying facts to a formula in the contract — not construed from the words alone. A clause that ties the reference date to a milestone, the submission of a document, or another post-formation event is permissible, even if that event is not certain to occur. What you cannot do is hide a payment-claim entitlement behind a contractual condition that effectively prevents a reference date arising at all; s 200 of the BIF Act will strike that down.1

The leading Queensland reading

The clearest exposition is Applegarth J’s judgment in Lean Field Developments Pty Ltd v E & I Global Solutions (Aust) Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 293. His Honour confirmed that “worked out” carries its ordinary meaning of a process of calculation, and that parties are free to agree a formula that operates on post-contract conduct or events:

[30] The ordinary meaning of “worked out” in this context connotes a process of calculation… the statutory definition of “reference date” would seem to allow a reference date to be worked out by applying a formula to facts that are capable of being ascertained… The first and subsequent reference dates are worked out by reference to the conduct of the contractor after the formation of the contract in commencing work.

The corollary, also from Lean Field, is that a clause that makes the reference date illusory — for example, by deferring it indefinitely or making it conditional on something one party will never trigger — can be invalidated under s 200. The contract clause is read against that statutory backstop.

One reference date, one payment claim

You only get to claim once per reference date. A clause that “deems” an early payment claim to have been served on the next reference date does not save a premature claim — the date is the date.2 If you serve before a reference date has accrued, the document is not a payment claim under the Act, and an adjudicator who proceeds on it acts without jurisdiction.

The same single-shot rule applies after termination. Section 67(2) gives you a final reference date on the day the contract ends, but only one payment claim can be made off it. A subcontractor who has already served a post-termination claim cannot serve another one and run it to adjudication.3

The default in s 67(1)(b)

If the contract is silent, the default reference date is the last day of the month. For that date to arise, some construction work has to have been done in that month — you can’t generate a reference date out of an idle month.4 And if an adjudicator has to perform “surgical intervention” on the contract’s wording to spell out a reference date, the contract is treated as not providing for the matter, and the default applies.5

What this means on a project

  • Before serving a payment claim, identify the reference date you are claiming on. Write it on the front of the claim. If you can’t identify one, don’t serve.
  • If your contract states a reference date (e.g. “the 25th of each month”), serve on or after that date — never before. Deeming clauses don’t cure an early claim.
  • If the contract is silent, you’re on the s 67(1)(b) default: last day of the month, and only if you actually did work that month.
  • One reference date, one claim. Don’t double-up by serving two claims against the same reference date — the second is not a payment claim under the Act and any adjudication of it is void.
  • If the contract has been terminated, you have one final reference date under s 67(2) and one final payment claim. Make it count; you won’t get another.
  • Watch for contractual conditions that effectively block a reference date arising. A clause that makes the entitlement illusory is vulnerable to s 200, but it is far cleaner to negotiate it out than to fight about it later.
  1. Lean Field Developments Pty Ltd v E & I Global Solutions (Aust) Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 293, [30], [33]–[34] (Applegarth J) — s 99 BCIPA (the equivalent of s 200 of the BIF Act) may invalidate contractual conditions which effectively prevent or inordinately delay a reference date arising.
  2. Regal Consulting Services Pty Ltd v All Seasons Air Pty Ltd [2017] NSWSC 613 (McDougall J), upheld in All Seasons Air Pty Ltd v Regal Consulting Services Pty Ltd [2017] NSWCA 289 — a deeming clause cannot validate a payment claim made before the reference date; followed in Castle Constructions Pty Ltd v N & R Younis Plumbing Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 225 (Parker J).
  3. BCFK Holdings Pty Ltd v Rork Projects Pty Ltd [2022] NSWSC 1706 (Stevenson J); Taylor Construction Group Pty Ltd v Adcon Structural Group Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 723 (Rees J) — on the NSW equivalent of s 67(2), only one payment claim may be served after termination. See also Dickson Developments Precinct 5 Pty Ltd v Core Building Group Pty Ltd [2024] FCA 86 (Jackman J), applying Southern Han Breakfast Point Pty Ltd (in liq) v Lewence Construction Pty Ltd [2016] HCA 52 — a second claim against an already-used reference date is not a payment claim under the Act.
  4. Veer Build Pty Ltd v TCA Electrical and Communication Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 864, [45] (Darke J), citing Grid Projects NSW Pty Ltd v Proyalbi Organic Set Plaster Pty Ltd [2012] NSWSC 1571, [23]–[39]; approved in Broadview Windows Pty Ltd v Architectural Project Specialists Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 955, [34] (McDougall J).
  5. Argyle Building Services Pty Ltd v Dalanex Pty Ltd (No 2) [2022] VSC 452 (Delany J) — if “surgical intervention” is required to extract a reference date from the contract, there is no express provision and the statutory default applies. See also Waco Kwikform Ltd v Complete Access Scaffolding (NSW) Pty Ltd [2020] NSWSC 1702 (Stevenson J) — a clause merely conferring discretion to choose if and when to claim does not nominate a reference date for s 67(1)(a) purposes; State of Queensland v T & M Buckley Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 265, [17]–[19] (Margaret Wilson J) — on the distinction between a date “stated in” and one “worked out under” the contract.

Requirements of a payment claim

Identify the construction work

The first thing s 68 demands of a payment claim is that it identify what the progress payment is for. Get this wrong and the claim is a nullity — no payment schedule is required, no adjudication can follow, and any determination that does follow is open to be set aside for jurisdictional error. The threshold is not high, but it is real.

Section 68(1)(a) BIF Act — what a payment claim must do

68 Meaning of payment claim

(1) A payment claim, for a progress payment, is a written document that—

(a) identifies the construction work or related goods and services to which the progress payment relates…

The “reasonably comprehensible” test

In Queensland the standard is settled. A payment claim must identify the construction work or related goods and services sufficiently to enable the respondent to understand the basis of the claim — or, put another way, the claim must be “reasonably comprehensible” to the recipient. The leading appellate statement is T & M Buckley Pty Ltd v 57 Moss Rd Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 381, where Philippides J (Fraser and White JJA agreeing) reviewed the authorities and adopted the formulation Hodgson JA had used in Coordinated Construction Co Pty Ltd v Climatech (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2005] NSWCA 229:

What was required was that the payment claim purport in a reasonable way to identify the work the subject of the claim, and a payment claim was not a nullity for failure to identify the work unless the failure was patent on its face. The payment claim did not cease to satisfy the requirement concerning identification because it could be subsequently shown that the payment claim was not entirely successful in identifying all of the work.

Two things follow from that. First, the document is read in a common-sense, practical way and not from an unduly critical viewpoint — the regime emphasises speed and informality.1 Second, “sufficiency” is judged against what the parties already know. The Court of Appeal accepted in T & M Buckley that “the evaluation of the sufficiency of the identification takes into account the background knowledge of each of the parties derived from their past dealings and exchanges of documentation”. So a short line item that would be opaque to an outsider may still be enough between a head contractor and subcontractor who have been trading documents on the same job for months.

How much detail is enough — the rule in KDV Sport

The leading Queensland decision on the practical content of s 68(1)(a) is KDV Sport Pty Ltd v Muggeridge Constructions Pty Ltd [2019] QSC 178. Muggeridge served a payment claim for $2,365,432 supported by a “Trade Breakdown Schedule” listing some 51 categories of work, with a percentage-complete figure against each category and a number of unsubmitted variations tacked on. Brown J set the resulting adjudication aside. Her Honour held that merely citing the category of work and a percentage was not enough to identify the actual work being claimed for: it was not possible to tell what “20% completion” of a category represented as opposed to “30%”, the figures could not be reconciled to the dollars claimed, and the principal’s general familiarity with the project did not cure the deficiency.2

Percentage-only claims are not, however, automatically bad. In the same case Brown J accepted that where a percentage figure is anchored to an identifiable benchmark — for example, work claimed from a zero base — the description can be sufficient. Kelly J approved that distinction in Bridgeman Agencies Pty Ltd v S.E QLD Plumbing & Drainage Pty Ltd [2025] QSC 167, and added that the inquiry “cannot be answered by confining the inquiry to the actual document or documents comprising the payment claim” — supporting documents that have been provided do count, even if they aren’t physically attached.3

Documents in support, and information after the fact

A payment claim can incorporate other material — an earlier spreadsheet referred to in the invoice, a notice of dispute emailed at the same time, contractual line-item references — provided that material was already in the respondent’s hands when the claim was served.4 Information sent after service does not retrospectively rescue an inadequate claim: in Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd [2023] QSC 290, Williams J held that compliance with s 68(1)(a) is judged against information and material provided at or before delivery of the claim, and a previous practice of supplying further detail later was not enough to expand that timeframe.5

Errors and severance

Including an item that is plainly not construction work, or an item that turns out to be wrong, will not by itself invalidate the whole claim. The adjudicator is taken to have jurisdiction to value the work and to discount or sever items that don’t qualify or that are insufficiently identified.6 The exception is where the unidentified or non-qualifying portion is so substantial that the claim as a whole ceases to be reasonably comprehensible — KDV Sport is itself an example, and in Iris Broadbeach Williams J accepted the “big-ticket items” test: if the items that are not sufficiently described add up to a substantial amount of the total claimed, the whole claim falls.7

What this means on a project

  • Describe the work, not just the category. A trade name plus a percentage is the classic KDV Sport trap. Tie each line item to the actual scope item, drawing or work package and, where possible, to a dollar value the recipient can reconcile.
  • Where you do use percentages, anchor them. “50% of the driveway concreting” on a single-house contract is fine; “5% of concreting” across 56 townhouses is not.8
  • Use the parties’ shared history. Past payment claims, agreed schedules of rates, the contract’s priced bill of quantities and the project’s document register are all fair game and reduce the description burden.
  • If you rely on a supporting document — a costed-variations spreadsheet, a notice of dispute, a delay claim — serve it with the claim or make sure it has already been delivered. Don’t back-fill after service.
  • Don’t pad with non-construction items (bank guarantees called up, financing costs, pure damages). Including them won’t always kill the claim, but if they swamp the qualifying scope the whole thing can fail.
  • For variations, attach enough particulars at the time of the claim to identify the work, the instruction relied on and the calculation. Unsubmitted variations bolted onto the claim were a key factor in KDV Sport.
  1. Minimax Fire Fighting Systems Pty Ltd v Bremore Engineering (WA Pty Ltd) [2007] QSC 333, [20] (Chesterman J), approved Neumann Contractors Pty Ltd v Traspunt No 5 Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 119; [2011] 2 Qd R 114, 122 [24] (Muir JA, Holmes and Chesterman JJA agreeing) — the Act emphasises speed and informality; whether a document satisfies the statutory description is not to be approached from an unduly critical viewpoint.
  2. Approved as the test for s 68(1)(a) compliance in MWB Everton Park Pty Ltd v Devcon Building Co Pty Ltd [2024] QCA 94, [25] (Dalton JA, Brown J and Kelly J agreeing) and Denbrook Constructions Pty Ltd v CBO Developments Pty Ltd [2022] QDC 184 (Porter DCJ).
  3. Bridgeman Agencies Pty Ltd v S.E QLD Plumbing & Drainage Pty Ltd [2025] QSC 167 (Kelly J) — the question whether construction work is sufficiently identified cannot be answered by confining the inquiry to the four corners of the payment claim; percentage-based claims from a zero base, with an identifiable benchmark, can be sufficient.
  4.   7. Grocon (Belgrave St) Developer Pty Ltd v Construction Profile Pty Ltd [2020] NSWSC 409 (Ball J) — bank guarantees called up by the respondent were not amounts for construction work or related goods and services and could not validly be claimed.
  8. MWB Everton Park Pty Ltd as trustee for MWB Everton Park Unit Trust v Devcon Building Co Pty Ltd [2024] QCA 94, [25] and footnote 5 (Dalton JA, Brown J and Kelly J agreeing) — for a contract to build 56 townhouses it was meaningless to say that 5% of concreting or 12% of plumbing had been done; the example of 50% of the concreting on a single-dwelling driveway given as a hypothetical case where a trade summary could be sufficient.

Requirements of a payment claim

State the claimed amount

A payment claim has to put a number on the table. Section 68(1)(b) requires the written document to state the amount — called the “claimed amount” — of the progress payment that the claimant says is payable by the respondent. Without that figure the document is not a payment claim and the rest of the Chapter 3 machinery never engages.

Section 68(1)(b) BIF Act — states the amount

(1) A payment claim, for a progress payment, is a written document that—

…

(b) states the amount (the claimed amount) of the progress payment that the claimant claims is payable by the respondent…

What the requirement is asking for

This is the most mechanical of the s 68(1) requirements. The document must state, on its face, the dollar amount the claimant says it is owed. It does not need to state the “correct” amount — whether the figure is in fact payable is a matter for the payment schedule and, if it gets that far, for the adjudicator. It is enough that the document states the amount the claimant claims is payable.1

The Act does not prescribe any particular form. There is no requirement for a single grand-total line, no required label, and no rule against breaking the claim down across attachments. The courts have consistently said the document is not to be read from an unduly critical viewpoint — the only question is whether the content satisfies the statutory description.2

Multiple invoices or amounts spread across documents

A claim does not fail simply because the claimant did not bother to add up its own numbers. In Camporeale Holdings Pty Ltd v Mortimer Construction Pty Ltd & Anor [2015] QSC 211 the claimant served four invoices under a single covering email. The respondent argued the email had to cite a single total and that without it there was no “claimed amount”. Henry J disagreed:

The absence of a statement of the mathematical total of those amounts does not make the statements of the four amounts considered in combination any less a statement of the amount of the progress payment claimed… a payment claim which states the amounts of the progress payment claimed to be payable will comply with s 17(2)(b), as long as those amounts are stated as part of one payment claim.

His Honour reached that conclusion partly through s 32C of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) (singular includes plural) and partly through a common-sense reading of the bundle — when the documents were considered together it was “readily discernible” that the combined total was the amount claimed. The principle carries straight over to s 68(1)(b): you can state the claimed amount as a single figure, or as a series of amounts that, taken together, add up to the claim, provided the documents are served as one payment claim.

What does not satisfy s 68(1)(b)

The opposite case is MWB Everton Park Pty Ltd as trustee for MWB Everton Park Unit Trust v Devcon Building Co Pty Ltd [2024] QCA 94. The builder sent the principal an email with attached spreadsheets describing percentage progress against various trade items. The Court of Appeal (Dalton JA, Brown J and Kelly J agreeing) gave three independent reasons the documents were not a payment claim. The s 68(1)(b) reason was simply that the documents attached to the email did not state an amount which was the claimed amount of the progress payment. Percentages of trade categories, without a stated dollar figure that the claimant was asserting was payable, were not enough.

The point sounds obvious but it is worth stating: the figure stated has to be the figure claimed as payable. A spreadsheet of valuations, a running ledger of costs incurred, or a list of percentages against a schedule of rates is not, by itself, a statement of the claimed amount. There has to be a number, presented as the amount the claimant says the respondent owes for this progress payment.

Including amounts already paid into trust or held back

Section 68(2) expands what the claimed amount may include. It can pick up an amount the respondent is liable to pay the claimant under s 98(3), and an amount held under the construction contract by the respondent that the claimant says is due for release — typically retention or security being called on. Those amounts can be rolled into the figure stated under s 68(1)(b); they do not have to sit in a separate document.

What this means on a project

  • Put a dollar figure on the face of the claim. A single line stating the total claimed amount is the safest format. If your invoicing system produces multiple invoices for one claim period, serve them together under one cover so they are unmistakably one payment claim.
  • Don’t rely on the respondent to add up your numbers for you. Camporeale says they have to, but only if the documents make clear they form one claim. Make that obvious — same email, same date, same cover sheet.
  • A schedule of percentages or trade progress without a stated dollar amount is not a payment claim. After MWB Everton Park there is direct Court of Appeal authority on that point; do not assume the principal will infer the figure from the percentages.
  • The figure does not have to be correct. State what you in fact claim is payable; the contest over whether it is actually owing belongs in the payment schedule and the adjudication, not in the validity of the claim.
  • If you are claiming retention, security or amounts the respondent is liable to pay you under s 98(3), include them in the claimed amount — s 68(2) expressly allows this.
  1. That the claim need only state the amount the claimant claims to be payable, not the amount in fact payable, see De Neefe Signs Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd; Traffic Technologies Traffic Hire Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 279 (Fryberg J — sufficient to state the amount claimed to be payable; “payable” does not mean “due and payable”).
  2. The “not unduly critical” approach to the s 68(1) requirements: Camporeale Holdings Pty Ltd v Mortimer Construction Pty Ltd & Anor [2015] QSC 211 (Henry J), citing Minimax Fire Fighting Systems Pty Ltd v Bremore Engineering (WA Pty Ltd) & Ors [2007] QSC 333, [20] (Chesterman J); approved at appellate level in Neumann Contractors Pty Ltd v Traspunt No 5 Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 119; [2011] 2 Qd R 114, 122 [24] (Muir JA, Holmes and Chesterman JJA agreeing).

Requirements of a payment claim

The claim must request payment

Section 68(1)(c) requires that the written document actually “request payment of the claimed amount”. This is a requirement BCIPA never had, and it has become a live battleground — the Queensland Court of Appeal has now confirmed that a document which merely identifies and totals work done, without asking to be paid for it, is not a payment claim. The safe harbour is to use the word “invoice”: s 68(3) deems any written document bearing that word to satisfy s 68(1)(c).

Section 68 BIF Act — Meaning of payment claim (extract)

(1) A payment claim, for a progress payment, is a written document that—

…

(c) requests payment of the claimed amount; and

…

(3) A written document bearing the word ‘invoice’ is taken to satisfy subsection (1)(c).

Why s 68(1)(c) exists

Section 68(1)(c) is new to the BIF Act — the BCIPA equivalent (s 17) had no analogous requirement. The explanatory note tells us only that “[a] request for payment may be, for example, in the form of an invoice that contains a due date.” There is no extrinsic guidance on what other forms of words will do, and no other Australian security of payment statute imposes a comparable requirement.

Read with s 68(3), the structure of the section is straightforward. If the document bears the word “invoice”, the request element is taken to be satisfied and the inquiry stops. If it does not, the document still has to request payment in substance — and what that means is now the subject of binding Queensland authority.

The leading Queensland position: MWB Everton Park

The leading decision is MWB Everton Park Pty Ltd as trustee for MWB Everton Park Unit Trust v Devcon Building Co Pty Ltd [2024] QCA 94. The builder had emailed the principal a covering message with two spreadsheets attached, totalling the value of work done to date. The documents did not bear the word “invoice” and did not in terms ask the principal to pay. Dalton JA (Brown J and Kelly J agreeing) gave three independent reasons why the documents were not a payment claim, the third being that they did not request payment of the claimed amount as required by s 68(1)(c). Her Honour explicitly disagreed with Wilson J’s contrary analysis at first instance in Iris Broadbeach:

[36] … In [Iris Broadbeach] the judge was prepared to find that the documentation impliedly included a request for payment … While I cannot see any relevant distinction between the documents in this case and the documents in Iris Broadbeach Business, I am afraid that I am not persuaded by the analysis in that case. I do not think it can be said that the documents comprising the email and attachments of 30 June 2023 made a request for payment, independently of identifying the amount the subject of the claim.

The practical takeaway from MWB Everton Park is that you cannot infer a request for payment from the bare fact that a document identifies work and states a total. Something on the face of the document must ask for the money. Use the word “invoice” and you are home; rely on context and inference and you are exposed.

The earlier first-instance view in Iris Broadbeach

The position the Court of Appeal disapproved was Wilson J’s decision in Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 16. Her Honour found that a document headed “progress claim”, stating that it was submitted under the BIF Act and showing a “Total Progress Claim Value for the Month”, objectively requested payment even though the words “please pay” or “invoice” were absent. A supporting statutory declaration referring to “the payment of Progress Claim No. 7” was treated as part of the cumulative picture.1 After MWB Everton Park, that reasoning is unsafe. To the extent Iris Broadbeach survives, it does so on its facts and only because it was not appealed on this point.

Practical drafting

The cheapest and safest course is to put the word “invoice” on the face of the document. Section 68(3) is unconditional: a written document bearing that word is taken to satisfy s 68(1)(c), and there is no judicial gloss requiring the invoice to take any particular form. If “invoice” is for some reason not appropriate — for example, where you are issuing a single “progress claim” document under a head contract that uses the language of certificates rather than invoices — the document needs an express request for payment. “Please pay the claimed amount of $X by [date]” on the face of the claim is enough; a covering email saying the same will do, provided it is part of the same payment claim.

The courts continue to read s 68 as a whole “not from an unduly critical viewpoint” — the test articulated by Henry J in Camporeale Holdings Pty Ltd v Mortimer Construction Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 211 under BCIPA, approved by the Court of Appeal in Neumann Contractors Pty Ltd v Traspunt No 5 Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 119; [2011] 2 Qd R 114 — but that approach goes to construction of the document as a whole, not to filling in missing elements.2 Section 68(1)(c) is one of those elements; if it is missing, no amount of common-sense reading will rescue the claim.

What this means on a project

  • If you are the claimant, the easiest path to validity is to put the word “invoice” on the face of the document. Section 68(3) deems that to satisfy s 68(1)(c), full stop.
  • If your standard form is a “progress claim” rather than an invoice, add an express request for payment — for example, “Please pay the claimed amount of $X by [date]”. Don’t leave it to inference from a total.
  • Don’t rely on the title (“progress claim”), a reference to the BIF Act, or a covering statutory declaration to do the work of s 68(1)(c). After MWB Everton Park, those things are not enough on their own.
  • If you are the respondent and the document received does not bear the word “invoice” and does not contain any request for payment, that is now a clean jurisdictional point — raise it in your payment schedule and reserve it for any adjudication or court application.
  • For variation claims and one-off claims served outside the regular cycle, take particular care. These are the documents most likely to lack a clear request for payment because they are often drafted as letters, notices or spreadsheets rather than invoices.
  1. Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 16 (Wilson J) — treated a “progress claim” document, an endorsement that it was submitted under the BIF Act, a stated “Total Progress Claim Value for the Month” and a supporting statutory declaration as cumulatively amounting to a request for payment under s 68(1)(c). Disapproved on this point in MWB Everton Park Pty Ltd as trustee for MWB Everton Park Unit Trust v Devcon Building Co Pty Ltd [2024] QCA 94, [36] (Dalton JA, Brown J and Kelly J agreeing).
  2. Camporeale Holdings Pty Ltd v Mortimer Construction Pty Ltd & Anor [2015] QSC 211 (Henry J) — payment claims are not to be approached “from an unduly critical viewpoint”; Neumann Contractors Pty Ltd v Traspunt No 5 Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 119; [2011] 2 Qd R 114, 122 [24] (Muir JA, Holmes and Chesterman JJA agreeing) — same approach approved at appellate level. The explanatory note for s 68 records that a request for payment “may be, for example, in the form of an invoice that contains a due date”.

Requirements of a payment claim

Within six months

A payment claim has a shelf life. Section 75(2)–(3) tells you the longest period during which you can give a payment claim, measured from the last day work was carried out (or, for a final claim, from completion or the end of the defects liability period). Serve outside that window and the claim is out of time.

Section 75(2)–(3) BIF Act — Time limits for payment claims

(2) Unless the payment claim relates to a final payment, the claim must be given before the end of whichever of the following periods is the longest—

(a) the period, if any, worked out under the construction contract;

(b) the period of 6 months after the construction work to which the claim relates was last carried out or the related goods and services to which the claim relates were last supplied.

(3) If the payment claim relates to a final payment, the claim must be given before the end of whichever of the following periods is the longest—

(a) the period, if any, worked out under the relevant construction contract;

(b) 28 days after the end of the last defects liability period for the construction contract;

(c) 6 months after the completion of all construction work to be carried out under the construction contract;

(d) 6 months after the complete supply of related goods and services to be supplied under the construction contract.

How the rule works

Section 75(2) is the default rule for ordinary progress claims. You take the longer of the period in your contract (if it specifies one) and six months after the construction work to which the claim relates was last carried out, or the related goods and services were last supplied. The contract can extend the period — it can’t shorten it below the statutory six months.1

Section 75(3) is the final-payment variant. A “final payment” is defined in s 75(9) as the progress payment that is the final payment for construction work carried out, or for related goods and services supplied, under the contract. Here you measure from the longest of four candidates: the contract period; 28 days after the end of the last defects liability period; six months after completion of all construction work; or six months after the complete supply of related goods and services.

Section 75(2)–(3) replaced the previous 12-month limit under BCIPA. The shorter window matters — older Queensland authorities and interstate cases decided under 12-month equivalents are still good law on the principle, but the actual deadline you have to work to is half what it was.

“The construction work to which the claim relates”

The most-litigated question under this provision is what counts as “the construction work to which the claim relates”. Three readings have been argued: any work under the contract; any work that forms part of the payment claim; or every item of work in the claim. The leading appellate authority is the NSW Court of Appeal’s decision in Estate Property Holdings Pty Ltd v Barclay Mowlem Construction Ltd [2004] NSWCA 393, which adopted the middle reading. Hodgson JA, dealing with the equivalent NSW provision, held:

“construction work … to which the claim relates” … is also the construction work for which payment is claimed in the claim; and accordingly, the requirement … is that some of that construction work be carried out in the relevant twelve month period … ss.13–15 of the Act do not provide any basis for dividing up the construction work to which the claim relates into items which may be considered discrete, and asking in respect of each such item whether some work was carried out in the twelve month period.

The practical effect: if the payment claim relates to a body of work and at least some of it was carried out within the six-month window, the claim is in time, even if other items in the same claim go further back. You don’t have to dissect the claim line by line. But the work in the window must be work for which payment is being claimed — you can’t reach back twelve months simply because you’ve recently done unrelated work under the same contract. Estate Property Holdings has been followed in Queensland and the ACT, and was applied (as to the principle, before the limit was reduced to six months) in Canberra Drilling Rigs Pty Ltd v Haides Pty Ltd [2018] ACTSC 282.2

Final payment claims and the s 75(3)(c) trigger

For final payment claims, the timing question shifts: when does the six-month clock under s 75(3)(c) start running? The answer is “completion of all construction work to be carried out under the contract” — not the date the contract was terminated, and not the date you stopped going to site. In Ventralia Pty Ltd v Redline Water Infrastructure Pty Ltd [2023] QSC 291 Freeburn J held that s 75(3)(c) “should not be read as if the time limit was six months from the date the contract was terminated”. Termination may set the outer limit for any further work under the contract, but it doesn’t supply the start date for the six months — that has to be measured from the point work was actually complete. His Honour expressly cautioned against over-reading S.H.A. Premier Constructions Pty Ltd v Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 307, where Bond J had observed that termination brings about the “completion” of all work under a contract for s 75(3)(c) purposes.3 The reconciliation is that termination is useful evidence of when work ended, not a substitute for the inquiry.

Two related points come from the same line of authority. First, retention claims at the end of a job sit comfortably within s 75(3): in EHome Construction Pty Ltd v GCB Constructions Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 291 Bond J held that a payment claim seeking release of retention amounts that had been deducted from earlier progress claims is “plainly” a final payment claim within s 75(3), provided it is given within the longest of the four periods. Second, while a contract is on foot the possibility of further rectification work (which is itself construction work for the period of the defects liability period) means “completion” under s 75(3)(c) is unlikely to have occurred — unless you can show that on a particular earlier date the works were complete and free of defects.

Non-compliance and the adjudicator’s role

Compliance with s 75(2)–(3) is a jurisdictional question for the adjudicator. In South East Civil and Drainage Contractors Pty Ltd v AMGW Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 45 Jackson J held that a respondent’s failure to take the timing point in the payment schedule does not authorise an adjudicator to ignore it where the issue is apparent on the face of the material the adjudicator must consider. So even if the payment schedule is silent on timing, the adjudicator has to satisfy themselves the claim was given in time.4 The flip side is that the adjudicator’s opinion on whether the claim was given within the period is generally treated as a matter within jurisdiction — the courts will review whether the adjudicator formed an opinion at all, but will not readily revisit the correctness of that opinion.5

What this means on a project

  • For an ordinary progress claim, the deadline is the longer of any period in the contract and six months after the work to which the claim relates was last carried out. If your contract is silent, six months is your hard limit.
  • If you’re running close to the limit, the date that matters is the last day work to which the claim relates was actually carried out on site (or the related goods and services were last supplied) — not the date you raised the invoice, and not the reference date.
  • For a final claim, identify which of the four s 75(3) periods is longest in your circumstances. If the contract has a defects liability period, the “28 days after the end of the last defects liability period” trigger is often the most generous and is easy to overlook.
  • If the contract has been terminated, don’t assume the six months runs from termination. After Ventralia you measure it from when the work was actually complete — which may be earlier or later than the termination date.
  • If you want to recover retention at the end of the job, structure it as a final payment claim and serve it within the s 75(3) window. EHome confirms a retention-only claim qualifies.
  • For mixed claims spanning a long period, make sure at least some of the work for which payment is claimed was carried out within the last six months. After Estate Property Holdings that is enough to pull the older items along with it — provided they form part of the same claim.
  • Don’t rely on the respondent missing the timing point. Even if it isn’t in the payment schedule, an adjudicator who can see the issue on the face of the materials has to deal with it.
  1. The current s 75(2)–(3) replaced the 12-month limit under former s 17(4) BCIPA, and adopts the “longest of” structure introduced by the 2014 amendments to BCIPA: see the explanatory transition discussed in South East Civil and Drainage Contractors Pty Ltd v AMGW Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 45; [2013] 2 Qd R 189, [37]–[42] (Jackson J).
  2. Estate Property Holdings Pty Ltd v Barclay Mowlem Construction Ltd [2004] NSWCA 393, [50]–[55] (Hodgson JA), overturning Barclay Mowlem Construction Ltd v Estate Property Holdings Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 649 (Einstein J) and rejecting the broader reading adopted in Leighton Contractors Pty Ltd v Campbelltown Catholic Club Ltd [2003] NSWSC 1103 (Einstein J). Followed in Pacific General Securities Ltd v Soliman & Sons Pty Ltd (2006) 62 NSWLR 421; [2006] NSWSC 13, [37] (Brereton J); applied to the ACT 12-month equivalent in Canberra Drilling Rigs Pty Ltd v Haides Pty Ltd [2018] ACTSC 282 (McWilliam AsJ); see also Commercial Fitouts Australia Pty Ltd v Miracle Ceilings (Aust) Pty Ltd [2020] SASC 11 (Stanley J — adjudication quashed where the in-period work had already been paid).
  3. Ventralia Pty Ltd v Redline Water Infrastructure Pty Ltd [2023] QSC 291 (Freeburn J); S.H.A. Premier Constructions Pty Ltd v Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 307 (Bond J — while a contract is on foot, the possibility of rectification work means “completion” under s 75(3)(c) is not readily made out, but termination brings about completion unless an earlier date can be shown); EHome Construction Pty Ltd v GCB Constructions Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 291 (Bond J — retention-only payment claim qualifies as a final payment claim within s 75(3)).
  4. South East Civil and Drainage Contractors Pty Ltd v AMGW Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 45; [2013] 2 Qd R 189 (Jackson J), distinguishing Brookhollow Pty Ltd v R & R Consultants Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 1 (Palmer J). For an example where the timing point was held not apparent on the face of the record, see GW Enterprises Pty Ltd v Xentex Industries Pty Ltd [2006] QSC 399 (Lyons J). Contrast De Neefe Signs Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 279 (Fryberg J — pre-amendment view that compliance was not a condition precedent to a valid payment claim under former s 17(4) BCIPA).
  5. Iridium Developments Pty Ltd v A-Civil Aust Pty Ltd [2021] NSWSC 1601 (Williams J — adjudicator’s opinion on timing within jurisdiction); Eq Constructions Pty Ltd v A-Civil Aust Pty Ltd [2021] NSWSC 1604 (Williams J — determination void where the adjudicator failed to form an opinion on whether the work the claim related to was carried out in the period); Winyu Pty Ltd v King [2015] ACTSC 387 (Mossop AsJ — determination invalid where works to which the claim related were not carried out within the statutory period). See also Icon Co (NSW) Pty Ltd v Australia Avenue Developments Pty Ltd [2018] NSWCA 339 on the second category of jurisdictional fact relied on in Iridium.

Requirements of a payment claim

Only one claim per reference date

Each reference date under your construction contract gives you one shot at a payment claim. Serve a second claim for the same reference date and it is invalid — even if the second one is more carefully prepared, more comprehensive, or fixes errors in the first. The flip side, in s 75(5), is that nothing stops you re-claiming an amount in a later claim tied to a later reference date.

Section 75(4)–(5) BIF Act — Making payment claim

(4) The claimant can not make more than 1 payment claim for each reference date under the construction contract.

(5) A payment claim may include an amount that was included in a previous payment claim.

The one-claim rule
