The leading Queensland authority on s 75(4) is Tailored Projects Pty Ltd v Jedfire Pty Ltd [2009] QSC 32; [2009] 2 Qd R 171. Douglas J held that “where more than one payment claim issues for a reference date, the first must be considered to be the payment claim for the reference period so that any subsequent claims for that reference period are invalid”. The first claim out the door consumes the reference date. Anything that follows for the same reference date is a nullity, and an adjudication built on it has no foundation.

Kellett Street Partners Pty Ltd v Pacific Rim Trading Co Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 298 illustrates how unforgiving the rule can be on long-running projects. The claimant’s obligation to perform work had ended by 28 March 2012. Payment claim 10 was served on 31 August 2012 and was held to be the claim for the March 2012 reference date. Claims 11 to 14, served afterwards, were invalid — the claimant had used up its only opportunity for that reference date and no new reference date had arisen.1

What counts as “more than one” claim

Not every set of documents served on the same day is multiple claims. A covering email attaching a number of invoices — or a payment summary sheet with separate invoices behind it — will normally be read as one composite payment claim. In Tailored Projects, Douglas J treated 18 invoices behind a covering payment-claim document as a single claim, observing that to read it otherwise would be “the triumph of form over substance”. The same approach was applied in Camporeale Holdings Pty Ltd v Mortimer Construction Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 211 (covering email plus four invoices) and in J.R. & L.M. Trackson Pty Ltd v NCP Contracting Pty Ltd [2019] QSC 201 (one email, three invoices, one project).2

The most recent Queensland decision is Taringa Property Group Pty Ltd v Kenik Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 298. Hindman J held that of a series of emails sent by the claimant — eleven separate variation-claim emails followed by a twelfth attaching a formal invoice labelled “Payment Claim 31” with a supporting statement — only the final invoice was the payment claim. The earlier emails lacked the features of a payment claim (a clear demand for payment, a supporting statement) and were properly read as supporting documents. Her Honour held that a payment claim must be objectively recognisable as a single demand for payment.

Two qualifications matter on the ground. First, re-sending the same payment claim — even with additional supporting material — is not a fresh claim and does not breach s 75(4): Eco Steel Homes Pty Ltd v Hippo’s Concreting Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 135. Second, where two documents are served separately and only one is endorsed or framed as a payment claim under the Act, only the endorsed document is the payment claim: JAG Projects Qld Pty Ltd v Total Cool Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 229.

Section 75(5) — repeating an amount

Section 75(5) lets a later payment claim include an amount already claimed in an earlier one. The leading appellate reading is Spankie v James Trowse Constructions Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 355, where Fraser JA held that the equivalent of s 75(5) is “wholly inapt to impose any restriction” on the right to make a payment claim — its only function is to confirm that re-claiming an unpaid amount is permitted. So if a progress claim includes work or an amount that has previously been claimed and not paid, that does not by itself invalidate the later claim.

The limit on this is practical, not statutory. Re-agitating the same amount over and over invites resistance on issue-estoppel and abuse-of-process grounds — the Court of Appeal in Spankie expressly left those doors open. And once an adjudicator has determined an amount, the determination has the consequences set out in chapter 3 part 4; you cannot simply re-run it by lodging another payment claim.

Withdrawing and replacing a claim

A payment claim can be withdrawn and replaced, but only with the respondent’s consent — which can arise by necessary implication from conduct, but won’t be inferred lightly. Unilateral withdrawal is doubtful; in Kitchen Xchange v Formacon Building Services [2014] NSWSC 1602, McDougall J said that, given the consequences of failing to respond to a payment claim, “the circumstances must make it very clear to the respondent that a payment claim is to be withdrawn, if it is intended that withdrawal should occur”. The safer course is to get the other side’s agreement in writing before issuing a replacement.3

What this means on a project

  • Treat each reference date as a single chance. Decide what you are claiming, get the supporting material together, and serve it once. A second “cleaner” version for the same reference date will be invalid and you cannot adjudicate on it.
  • If you need to bundle invoices, send them together — ideally as attachments to one covering email or document that makes plain a single demand for payment is being made. Stagger them across separate emails and you risk being told you served multiple claims.
  • An amount left unpaid from an earlier claim can be rolled into a later claim under s 75(5). You don’t lose the right to claim it just because you didn’t adjudicate the first time.
  • If you discover a problem with a payment claim after service, do not simply send a “replacement”. Either get written agreement from the other side that the first claim is withdrawn, or wait until the next reference date and serve afresh.
  • If you are receiving a stream of documents from a claimant, look for the one that is objectively a single demand for payment with a supporting statement. After Taringa, that document — not the earlier emails — is the payment claim, and your s 76 schedule clock runs from when it is given.
  1. Tailored Projects Pty Ltd v Jedfire Pty Ltd [2009] QSC 32; [2009] 2 Qd R 171 (Douglas J); Kellett Street Partners Pty Ltd v Pacific Rim Trading Co Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 298. For the same conclusion on the equivalent NSW provision, see Doolan v Rubikcon (QLD) Pty Ltd [2007] QSC 168; [2008] 2 Qd R 117 (Fryberg J — identical payment claims not permitted).
  2. Camporeale Holdings Pty Ltd v Mortimer Construction Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 211, [29] (Henry J — covering email plus four invoices read collectively as one claim); J.R. & L.M. Trackson Pty Ltd v NCP Contracting Pty Ltd [2019] QSC 201 (Ryan J — emphasis not to be placed on form). Compare Spirito Development Pty Ltd v Sinjen Group Pty Ltd [2020] VCC 1368 (Woodward J — separate emails with discrete invoices, separated in time, treated as separate payment claims).
  3. Kitchen Xchange v Formacon Building Services [2014] NSWSC 1602, [17], [26]–[28] (McDougall J); NC Refractories Pty Ltd v Consultant Bricklaying Pty Ltd [2013] NSWSC 842, [39] (Hammerschlag J — revised tax invoice, earlier invoice withdrawn by necessary implication); Reitsma Constructions Pty Ltd v Davies Engineering Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 343, [21] (Ball J — agreed substitution); Citi-Con (Vic) Pty Ltd v Punton’s Shoes Pty Ltd [2020] VCC 804 (Marks J — unilateral withdrawal and re-service for the same reference date breaches the equivalent of s 75(4)).

Requirements of a payment claim

Not for unlicensed work

The BIF Act payment regime sits on top of the Queensland licensing scheme in the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991 (QBCC Act). Section 42 of the QBCC Act makes it unlawful to carry out, or undertake to carry out, building work unless you hold a licence of the appropriate class. If your scope is “building work” under the QBCC Act and you weren’t licensed to perform it, the QBCC Act bars you from any monetary recovery for that work — and a BIF Act payment claim is one of the things that bar reaches.

Section 42 QBCC Act — Unlawful carrying out of building work

42 Unlawful carrying out of building work

It is an offence under the QBCC Act for a person to carry out, or undertake to carry out, building work unless that person holds a contractor’s licence of the appropriate class. The Act extends the prohibition to specific trades through s 42C (fire protection work) and s 42CA (mechanical services work), and to operating without a nominee under s 42B. A short carve-out for newly created licence classes is preserved by s 42A. The financial-loss consequences of unlicensed work are dealt with separately at s 42E (avoidance of contractual obligations causing significant financial loss).

How s 42 bites on a BIF Act payment claim

Section 42 doesn’t just create an offence. It strips the unlicensed party of the right to monetary recovery for the unlawful work — including a contractual right to be paid, a quantum-meruit recovery, and (most importantly here) a statutory entitlement to a progress payment under s 70 of the BIF Act. The BIF Act regime is designed to enforce a statutory entitlement to be paid for construction work performed under a construction contract. If the QBCC Act says no enforceable entitlement to payment exists at all because the work was carried out without a licence, the BIF Act has nothing to operate on.

The practical consequence on a payment-claim challenge is two-fold:

  • If the entire scope of the contract is “building work” under the QBCC Act and the claimant is unlicensed for that scope, the payment claim is incapable of supporting a valid adjudication. There is no underlying entitlement to a progress payment for the adjudicator to determine.
  • If only part of the scope is unlicensed — for example, a head contractor licensed for civil works who picks up some plumbing or fire-protection work without holding the relevant trade licence — only the unlicensed part is barred. The licensed scope remains claimable. But the payment claim has to be capable of being read so that the unlicensed component can be cleanly separated; if it can’t, the claim is exposed in its entirety.

What the QBCC Act treats as “building work”

The QBCC Act’s definition of “building work” is broader than most people expect. It picks up not only the construction, alteration, repair and removal of buildings, but also site work directly attached to those activities, fire-protection work (s 42C), and mechanical services work (s 42CA). The licence categories that match these scopes are detailed in the QBCC Regulation, and the “appropriate class” question turns on what your contracted scope actually involved — not what you ended up doing or the title of the trade.

Three points come up repeatedly on projects:

  • The licence has to cover the work, not just the entity. A company that holds a builder’s licence is not automatically licensed for fire-protection or mechanical-services work. Sections 42C and 42CA stand on their own; you need the trade licence as well.
  • Unlicensed nominee work. Section 42B makes it an offence to carry out building work without a properly nominated nominee. A licensed company that loses or fails to nominate a nominee is, for the period of the gap, in the same position as an unlicensed contractor for s 42 purposes.
  • The s 42A grace period only applies to newly created licence classes within the 12-month transitional window. It is not a general defence to operating without a licence.

Section 42E and significant financial loss

Section 42E sits alongside s 42 and is the provision that most directly addresses the money side of unlicensed work. It restricts contractual recovery where unlicensed work has caused a party significant financial loss, and operates independently of s 42’s offence provisions. From a payment-claim perspective, the working assumption should be that any contract or part of a contract executed in breach of the licensing rules will be vulnerable on both fronts: an offence under s 42 and a recovery limit under s 42E. A BIF Act payment claim does not rehabilitate either.

What this means on a project

  • Before serving a payment claim, check that the entity issuing the claim holds the appropriate QBCC licence for every part of the scope being claimed. The licence has to be current and the right class — not just any licence.
  • If you have a mixed scope (e.g. building plus fire-protection plus mechanical services), make sure each trade is held by an entity that is licensed for it, or is sub-contracted to a properly licensed party. Sections 42C and 42CA are easy traps on integrated fit-out and services jobs.
  • If a nominee role has lapsed or hasn’t been filled, treat any payment claim served during the gap as exposed under s 42B. The fix is to regularise the nominee position before serving, not after.
  • If you are a respondent and the claimant is unlicensed for some or all of the scope, raise the s 42 / s 42E point in the payment schedule. Payment-schedule reasons drive what the adjudicator can consider, and licensing is a recurring respondent ground that has to be put in early.
  • For owner-builder work, confirm whether the s 43E permit was required and held. An owner-builder operating outside the permit conditions falls back into the s 42 prohibition and is in the same position as any other unlicensed contractor.
  • Don’t assume the BIF Act’s “construction work” definition in s 65 maps perfectly onto the QBCC Act’s “building work”. They overlap heavily but they are not the same; work that is “construction work” for BIF Act purposes can still be unlicensed “building work” under the QBCC Act and therefore unrecoverable.
  1. QBCC Act s 42 (unlawful carrying out of building work); s 42A (temporary exemption from s 42 for up to 12 months for new classes of licence); s 42B (carrying out building work without a nominee); s 42C (unlawful carrying out of fire protection work); s 42CA (unlawful carrying out of mechanical services work); s 42E (avoidance of contractual obligations causing significant financial loss).
  2. For owner-builder work, see QBCC Act s 43E (when owner-builder permit is required) and ss 44–44J (application, criteria, conditions and cancellation of owner-builder permits). Work outside the permit conditions falls back into the s 42 prohibition.

Requirements of a payment claim

Served on the right person, the right way

A payment claim only triggers the BIF Act regime if it is given by the right entity, to the right entity, in a way that the Act recognises as valid service. Pick the wrong respondent and the claim falls outside s 75(1); pick a method of service the Act doesn’t accept and the clock for a payment schedule may never start running. Both points are jurisdictional — an adjudicator who proceeds on a misdirected or misserved claim is acting without authority.

Section 75(1) and section 102 BIF Act — who, and how

75 Making payment claim

(1) A person (the claimant) who is, or who claims to be, entitled to a progress payment may give a payment claim to the person (the respondent) who, under the relevant construction contract, is or may be liable to make the payment.

102 Service of notices

(1) A notice or other document that, under this chapter, is authorised or required to be given to a person may be given to the person in the way, if any, provided under the relevant construction contract.

(2) Subsection (1) is in addition to, and does not limit or exclude, the Acts Interpretation Act 1954, section 39 or the provisions of any other law about the giving of notices.

(3) To remove any doubt, it is declared that nothing in this Act—

(a) excludes the proper service of notices or documents by a person’s agent; or

(b) requires a person’s acknowledgement of a notice or document properly given to the person.

Who you can serve: the “is or may be liable” question

Section 75(1) lets a claimant give a payment claim to the person who, under the relevant construction contract, “is or may be liable” to pay. The Queensland Court of Appeal nailed down what those words mean in Mansouri & Anor v Aquamist Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 209. The expression “may be liable” does not loosen the requirement that the respondent be a party to the construction contract for whom the claimant has actually undertaken to perform work or supply related goods and services. It picks up the fact that progress-payment liability is provisional — it may or may not ultimately be established — not the identity of who can be served. The Court put it this way:

The expression “may be liable” does not qualify that requirement. It serves a different purpose. Progress payments under BCIPA are made on account of a liability which may or may not ultimately be established… Neither expression suggests any liberalisation of the requirement that “the respondent” must be a person for whom the claimant undertook to carry out construction work or to whom the claimant undertook to supply related goods and services.

The respondent must therefore be a party to the construction contract, not merely someone who “may” be a party. NSW authority on the equivalent provision is to the same effect: in Grave v Blazevic Holdings Pty Ltd [2010] NSWCA 324, McDougall J (Allsop P and Macfarlan JA agreeing) held that the proposed recipient must not be a stranger to the contract, although primary or secondary liability (e.g. as guarantor) and disputes about quantification or whether anything is owed at all are all caught by “is or may be liable”.1

Service on agents, superintendents and project personnel

Section 102(1) is “facultative”: it lets you serve in any manner the contract provides, but it does not displace s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) or the general law on service.2 Section 102(3)(a) preserves service by a person’s agent. Whether a superintendent, quantity surveyor, project manager or other intermediary is the principal’s agent for receiving payment claims turns on the contract and on the parties’ conduct — not on the title.3 Where the contract expressly states that the superintendent has no authority to receive claims under the Act, service on the superintendent will fail.4

The most useful Queensland decision for project teams is Canadian Solar Construction Pty Ltd v Re Oakey Pty Ltd [2023] QSC 288. The claimant emailed its payment claim to the respondent’s sole director and to five individuals working on the project. The director never received it; five of the six recipients did, four of whom were superintendent/project-management representatives with authority to assess payment claims. Freeburn J held the claim had been validly given to the respondent, and described the function of contractual service clauses as being “to facilitate service, and not to restrict the means by which service might be effected”. His Honour endorsed the approach of the NSW Supreme Court in BCFK Holdings Pty Ltd v Rork Projects Pty Ltd [2022] NSWSC 1706 — a party who actually receives a payment claim should not be heard to say service didn’t happen because of some technical shortcoming in how it was effected.5

Service by email and through cloud platforms

Email is now the practical default. Section 102(1) lets the contract specify email; even where it doesn’t, s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act reaches email as a “similar facility” in many circumstances, although the position is not free from doubt.6 Under s 24 of the Electronic Transactions (Queensland) Act 2001, the time of receipt of an electronic communication is when it becomes “capable of being retrieved” at a designated address — the email need not be opened or read, and a payment claim caught by a spam filter is still capable of being retrieved.7

Cloud-based delivery is more fragile. Service by Dropbox link has been held to fall outside both s 39 and s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) because the email delivers only the means of access, not the document itself.8 The same logic has been applied to dedicated platforms such as Corrigo: the document is “received” only when the recipient becomes aware of it.9 In contrast, where the contract designates a platform like Aconex or Payapps as a service channel, service occurs when the document becomes capable of retrieval at that designated address, regardless of whether the recipient has actually opened it.10

Service on a corporation, and the post-office-box trap

For corporate respondents, s 109X of the Corporations Act permits service by leaving the document at, or posting it to, the company’s registered office, or by delivering it personally to a director resident in Australia. Service on a post-office box is not valid service under either s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act or s 109X — unless the contract itself authorises that mode.11 Where service is by post to the registered office, the document is “received” when it arrives at the office during ordinary business hours; it does not have to come to the attention of any particular individual.12

What this means on a project

  • Identify the respondent before drafting the claim. The respondent must be a party to the construction contract for whom you have actually undertaken to perform the work or supply the goods and services. Mansouri shuts the door on serving someone who “may” be a party.
  • Read the contract’s notice clause and follow it. If the contract nominates an email address, an electronic platform (Aconex, Payapps, Corrigo) or a postal address for service, use that channel. Section 102(1) makes contractual service routes valid by default.
  • If you are serving on a superintendent, project manager or quantity surveyor as agent, check the contract first. If the contract says the agent has authority to receive claims under the Act — or there is a clear pattern of dealing — service is effective. If the contract excludes that authority, serve the principal directly.
  • For email service, send to addresses the recipient has nominated for that purpose, copy multiple project personnel where possible, and keep the delivery and read receipts. After Canadian Solar, multi-addressee delivery to people who can act on the claim is a strong protection against a technical service challenge.
  • Avoid Dropbox or bare cloud links. The document must actually arrive in the recipient’s system, not sit behind a hyperlink. If you must use a cloud platform, attach the document to the email as well.
  • Don’t serve on a post-office box unless the contract authorises it — serve at the registered office or principal place of business. The clock for a payment schedule does not start running on a misserved claim.
  • If you are the respondent and you actually receive the claim, don’t assume technical service failures will save you. The courts have consistently held that actual receipt by someone with authority to deal with the claim cures most service irregularities.
  1. Grave v Blazevic Holdings Pty Ltd (2010) 79 NSWLR 132; [2010] NSWCA 324, [26]–[28] (McDougall J, Allsop P and Macfarlan JA agreeing); applied in Collective Crane Hire Pty Ltd v ICR Steel Pty Ltd [2021] VCC 132 (Woodward J — Grave requires only that the person served not be a “stranger” to the contract). Conduct after formation may be relevant to identifying the parties: Nefiko Pty Ltd v Statewide Form Pty Ltd (No 2) [2014] NSWSC 840 (Ball J). On scope of an adjudicator’s jurisdiction over post-termination and variation claims, see Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No 2) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 49 (Payne JA).
  2. Neumann Contractors Pty Ltd v Traspunt No 5 Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 119 (Muir JA, Holmes and Chesterman JJA agreeing) — the predecessor to s 102 is “facultative rather than mandatory in nature”. On strict compliance with time limits triggered by service, see Emag Constructions Pty Ltd v Highrise Concrete Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 903, [40]–[42] (Einstein J — the Act exhibits “zero tolerance” for delay).
  3. For service on a quantity surveyor as agent, see Abigroup Contractors Pty Ltd v River Street Developments Pty Ltd [2006] VSC 425 (Habersberger J); on a superintendent with a prior course of dealing, The Owners Strata Plan 56597 v Consolidated Quality Projects [2009] NSWSC 1476; on a superintendent expressly defined as agent, Metacorp Australia Pty Ltd v Andeco Construction Group Pty Ltd (2010) 30 VR 141; [2010] VSC 199 (Vickery J). Contrast service on a project manager without express authority: Penfold Projects Pty Ltd v Securcorp Limited [2011] QDC 77 (Irwin DCJ — not valid service).
  4. SJ Higgins Pty Ltd v The Bays Healthcare Group Inc [2018] VCC 805 (Woodward J) — where the contract explicitly provided that the superintendent was not authorised to receive claims under the Act, service on the superintendent was invalid.
  5. Canadian Solar Construction Pty Ltd v Re Oakey Pty Ltd [2023] QSC 288 (Freeburn J), endorsing BCFK Holdings Pty Ltd v Rork Projects Pty Ltd [2022] NSWSC 1706. See also Shells Venture Management v Agresta [2019] VSC 863 (Digby J — service by an agent of a party is valid where the contract does not preclude the use of an agent).
  6. Penfold Projects Pty Ltd v Securcorp Limited [2011] QDC 77 (Irwin DCJ) treated email as a “similar facility” for s 39; that view was doubted in Conveyor & General Engineering Pty Ltd v Basetec Services Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 30 (Philip McMurdo J). The safer path is to comply with the contract’s express service regime.
  7. Bauen Constructions Pty Ltd v Sky General Services Pty Ltd [2012] NSWSC 1123, [77] (Sackar J) — “capable of being retrieved” is broad and does not require the email to be opened or read; a document in a spam filter is still capable of being retrieved. On contractual deeming clauses purporting to push back the time of electronic service, see Piety Constructions Pty Ltd v Hville FCP Pty Ltd [2022] NSWSC 1318 (Stevenson J) — such clauses are invalid to the extent they alter when a document is “provided” under the Act.
  8. Conveyor & General Engineering Pty Ltd v Basetec Services Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 30, [29]–[32] (Philip McMurdo J) — service by Dropbox link did not deliver the document itself, only the means to access it.
  9. BCS Infrastructure Support Pty Ltd v Jones Lang Lasalle (NSW) Pty Ltd [2020] VSC 739 (Stynes J) — service via the Corrigo platform was effective only when the respondent became aware of the upload. Compare Fredon Infrastructure Pty Ltd v Hitachi Rail GTS Australia Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 1244 (Stevenson J — Aconex delivery; awareness on day of delivery established by surrounding facts).
  10. Roberts Co (NSW) Pty Ltd v Sharvain Facades Pty Ltd (Administrators Appointed) [2025] NSWCA 161 (McHugh JA) — service occurs when a document is emailed to an address specified for service and becomes capable of being retrieved; no requirement that the recipient access or be aware of the communication. Followed Claire Rewais and Osama Rewais t/as McVitty Grove v BPB Earthmoving Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 103. Where no email address has been specified for service, the claimant must prove actual receipt: Kumar v Frankies Cranes [2025] NSWSC 1264 (Peden J — handing over a business card, “without more”, is not specification).
  11. CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209, [16], [23] (Daubney J) — service to a post-office box is not valid service under s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) or s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) unless the contract authorises that mode.
  12. Falgat Constructions Pty Ltd v Equity Australia Corporation Pty Ltd [2006] NSWCA 259, [58] (Hodgson JA) — mail delivered to a registered office or place of business is received at that place when it arrives, without anyone needing to see it; if a document actually comes to the attention of a person with authority to deal with it, it does not matter whether any facultative service regime has been complied with. Adopted in CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209 (Daubney J) and QC Communications NSW Pty Ltd v CivComm Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1095 (Ball J).

Requirements of a payment schedule

Identify the payment claim

A payment schedule must make clear which payment claim it is responding to. If the document you send back doesn’t tie itself to the payment claim, you risk the schedule being treated as no schedule at all — and that exposes you to summary judgment for the full claimed amount and bars you from raising reasons later in adjudication.

Section 69 BIF Act — meaning of payment schedule (chapeau and (a))

69 Meaning of payment schedule

A payment schedule, responding to a payment claim, is a written document that—

(a) identifies the payment claim to which it responds; and

…

What “identify” actually requires

The Act doesn’t prescribe a form. The leading Queensland statement of the test comes from Minimax Fire Fighting Systems Pty Ltd v Bremore Engineering (WA) Pty Ltd [2007] QSC 333, where Chesterman J said the Act “emphasises speed and informality” and the document is to be assessed on content, not label:

One should not approach the question whether a document satisfies the description of a payment schedule… from an unduly critical viewpoint. No particular form is required. One is concerned only with whether the content of the document in question satisfies the statutory description.

That passage was approved by the Queensland Court of Appeal in Neumann Contractors Pty Ltd v Traspunt No 5 Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 119 at [24] (Muir JA, Holmes and Chesterman JJA agreeing), so it is the controlling Queensland authority on s 69 generally and on what s 69(a) requires in particular. A document does not need to be headed “payment schedule” or even use the phrase — what matters is whether, read in context, the recipient can tell that this is the response to a specific payment claim.1

How identification is achieved in practice

Identification can be express or by reasonable inference from context. Useful indicators include:

  • quoting the payment claim’s reference number, date or invoice number;
  • responding line-by-line to the items in the payment claim;
  • attaching or referring to the payment claim itself, or a letter of demand that contains it;2 or
  • responding the same day, where there is no other document with which the claim could be confused.3

You don’t need all of these. A short, “relatively informal” email referring to “paperwork, stating I owe you this outrageous amount of money” was enough in Gisley Investments Pty Ltd v Williams & Anor [2010] QSC 178, because it was sent the day the claim arrived and there was nothing else it could have meant. By contrast, an email aimed at one component of a multi-item claim — without making clear it is a response to the whole payment claim — will fail the s 69(a) test.4

Queensland is stricter than New South Wales

The point that often trips respondents up is that s 69(a) requires the schedule to identify the payment claim, full stop. In Melaleuca View Pty Ltd v Sutton Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2019] QSC 226, Brown J held that a respondent’s document failed as a payment schedule because it did not identify the initial payment claim it was supposedly responding to. Her Honour acknowledged the result might be seen as harsh, but emphasised that “the onus was on a respondent to ensure it had complied with the requirements of the Qld Act.” The contrast Brown J drew with the NSW regime is important: s 69 says “state” rather than the looser “indicate” used in New South Wales, and that signals a higher standard of clarity for Queensland respondents across all of s 69(a)–(c).

The take-away is that you can’t rely on the assessor or the court to reconstruct your schedule from external materials. If the document doesn’t identify the payment claim on its face (or with an obvious cross-reference to an attached or recently-served document), it is not a payment schedule.

What this means on a project

  • Open every payment schedule with an unambiguous reference: “This is our payment schedule under s 76 of the BIF Act in response to your payment claim dated [date], reference [number], for $[amount].” That single sentence neutralises any s 69(a) argument.
  • If you respond by email or short letter rather than a formal schedule, attach or quote the payment claim, or reply on the same email chain. Don’t leave the recipient guessing which document you’re engaging with.
  • If the payment claim has multiple components or invoices, make the schedule address the whole claim — an item-by-item response, or a clear global statement plus reasons. Responding to only one part risks the schedule being treated as not identifying the claim at all.
  • Don’t rely on a payment certificate issued by the superintendent doubling as your payment schedule unless your contract has a properly drafted deeming clause and the certificate, read with that clause, satisfies s 69 on its face.5
  • If two documents are sent in time, the later one can be the payment schedule. Make sure whichever document you intend to be the schedule clearly identifies the payment claim — don’t leave it to the adjudicator to pick.6
  1. Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2013] QCA 386, [11] (Muir JA, Gotterson and Morrison JJA agreeing): a document does not need to be described internally as a “payment schedule”.
  2. BCS Infrastructure Support Pty Ltd v Jones Lang Lasalle (NSW) Pty Ltd [2020] VSC 739 (Stynes J): the schedule satisfied s 15(2)(a) of the NSW SOP Act (equivalent to s 69(a) BIF Act) where it referred to a letter of demand that contained the payment claim, plus a figure plainly tied to the disputed amount.
  3. Springs Golf Club Pty Ltd v Profile Golf Club Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 344: a letter referring to “your letter dated Friday 29 July 2005” was held to be a valid payment schedule responding to the payment claim served on that date.
  4. Denham Constructions Pty Ltd v Islamic Republic of Pakistan (No 2) [2016] ACTSC 215 (Mossop AsJ): an email directed at a single variation component of a multi-item payment claim did not identify the payment claim itself and was not a payment schedule.
  5. RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd v Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd & Ors [2021] QCA 117 (deeming clause that treats the superintendent’s assessment as the payment schedule held valid, reversing the Supreme Court); cf. McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd v Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 223, [49] (Boddice J): a contractual label of “payment certificate” doesn’t automatically make every certificate a s 69 schedule.
  6. Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2013] QCA 386 (Muir JA, Gotterson and Morrison JJA agreeing): where two documents were served within time, the second was held to be the payment schedule; the first document satisfying the statutory criteria is not necessarily “the” schedule.

Requirements of a payment schedule

State the scheduled amount

A payment schedule has to do more than dispute the claim. Under s 69(b) it must state the amount — if any — that you propose to pay in response to the payment claim. That figure is what the courts and adjudicators call the “scheduled amount”, and it is the number that fixes your downside if you fail to pay it on the due date.

Section 69 BIF Act — meaning of payment schedule (chapeau and (b))

A payment schedule, responding to a payment claim, is a written document that—

…

(b) states the amount of the payment, if any, that the respondent proposes to make…

What “scheduled amount” means

Parliament chose not to define the phrase “the amount of the payment, if any, that the respondent proposes to make” as the “scheduled amount”, but the label survives from the earlier case law and is still used by adjudicators. The scheduled amount is the single overall figure the respondent proposes to pay across the whole payment claim — not a line-by-line tally against each item.1 If the payment claim is one invoice or many bundled invoices, you state one number for the whole thing.

The figure can be nil. An amount of “nothing”, “nil” or “zero” satisfies s 69(b) just as readily as a positive number, including where the respondent says the payment claim itself is invalid and proposes to pay nothing on that basis.2

Stating the amount — expressly or by inference

You don’t have to put the figure in a box marked “scheduled amount”. The amount can be stated expressly, or it can be clearly inferred from the document read as a whole. The leading Queensland authority is Minimax Fire Fighting Systems Pty Ltd v Bremore Engineering (WA) Pty Ltd [2007] QSC 333, where Chesterman J held that an email which did not put a number on the page nonetheless implied a nil scheduled amount because, read fairly, it rejected the invoice in its entirety. The catch in Minimax was that the email only addressed one of three items in the payment claim:

The machinery for prompt payment and enforcement of payment would break down if a document, said to be a payment schedule, took issue with part only of a claim but was silent as to what it proposed to pay in respect of the balance.

So the inference of nil works, but only if the inference covers the whole of the payment claim. Silence on part of the claim is fatal — the document either has to address every item, or has to state a global figure that on its face responds to the lot.

Two practical consequences follow. First, “without prejudice” settlement-style letters that propose a deal rather than state what will be paid do not qualify: they aren’t stating a scheduled amount, they’re inviting a negotiation.3 Second, a response that merely asks for clarification or a reissued claim — “please adjust your claim accordingly and resubmit”, or “could you expand on the difference between these two quotes” — doesn’t state any amount at all and so falls outside s 69(b).4

Conditional and deemed amounts

Conditional figures are a trap. A document that says an amount will be paid once two conditions are met has been held not to state any amount at all for the purposes of the equivalent of s 69(b): it is neither a statement that nothing will be paid nor a statement that the stated figure will be paid.5 By contrast, a superintendent’s assessment combined with a contractual deeming clause — common in standard-form contracts — can satisfy s 69(b), because read with the deeming clause the assessed figure becomes the amount the respondent proposes to pay.6 The lesson is that the document, taken with anything it incorporates, has to identify a definite figure.

What this means on a project

  • Put the scheduled amount on the front page of the schedule as a single dollar figure for the whole claim. If you propose to pay nothing, write “Scheduled amount: $0”. Don’t make the claimant infer it.
  • Make sure the amount you state covers every item in the payment claim. If you only address some of the items, you risk the whole document being held not to be a payment schedule.
  • Don’t dress the response up as a settlement offer or a “without prejudice” letter if you intend it to function as a payment schedule. State the figure you propose to pay, full stop.
  • Avoid conditional formulations (“we will pay $X once you do Y and Z”). State an amount now — nil if necessary — and deal with the conditions as reasons under s 69(c).
  • If the contract has a deeming clause that turns the superintendent’s assessment into the payment schedule, check that the assessment actually states a number, and that it’s issued and delivered within the s 76 timeframe.
  • Remember the downside. The scheduled amount is the figure the claimant can sue for as a debt under the Act if you fail to pay it on the due date. Get the number right.
  1. Cornerstone Danks Street v Parkview Constructions [2014] NSWSC 866, [25] (McDougall J) — “scheduled amount” is the overall amount proposed to be paid, not a per-item figure; Brimar Electrical Services Pty Ltd v Zi-Argus Australia Pty Ltd [2014] QDC 78 to similar effect for a payment claim made up of multiple invoices.
  2. Barclay Mowlem Construction Ltd v Tesrol Walsh Bay Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 1232, [15] (McDougall J); Façade Treatment Engineering Ltd v Brookfield Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd [2015] VSC 41, [37] (Vickery J); see also BCS Infrastructure Support Pty Ltd v Jones Lang Lasalle (NSW) Pty Ltd [2020] VSC 739 (Stynes J) on inferring nil where the respondent expresses an intention not to pay any amount.
  3. National Vegetation Management Solutions Pty Ltd v Shekar Plant Hire Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 3 — a “without prejudice” letter proposing settlement does not state a scheduled amount.
  4. Roberts Construction Group Pty Ltd v Drummond Carpentry Services Pty Ltd [2024] VSC 246, [98] (Niall JA); Actif Concrete v Design Builders [2025] VCC 275 (Lauritsen J) — an email seeking clarification of a payment claim is not a payment schedule.
  5. Tenix Alliance Pty Ltd v Magaldi Power Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 7 — conditional payment formulation held not to state any amount under the predecessor of s 69(b).
  6. RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd v Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd & Ors [2021] QCA 117 — a deeming clause coupled with the superintendent’s assessment can satisfy s 69(b); ACP Properties (Townsville) Pty Ltd v Rodrigues Construction Group Pty Ltd & Anor [2021] QSC 45 (Bradley J) — emails proposing $nil by reference to a contractual cap satisfied s 69(b) and (c).

Requirements of a payment schedule

Reasons for withholding payment

If your scheduled amount is less than the amount claimed — including where you propose to pay nil — s 69(c) requires the payment schedule to state why, and to set out your reasons for withholding any payment. This is the rule that locks in the dispute. Reasons you don’t state in the schedule cannot be raised later in adjudication, so the schedule has to do the work of defining the issues at the time you serve it.

Section 69 BIF Act — meaning of payment schedule (chapeau and (c))

A payment schedule, responding to a payment claim, is a written document that—

…

(c) if the amount proposed to be paid is less than the amount stated in the payment claim—states why the amount proposed to be paid is less, including the respondent’s reasons for withholding any payment…

What “state why” requires

The schedule has to define the dispute clearly enough that the claimant can decide whether to push the claim into adjudication, and what case it has to meet if it does. The classic formulation is from Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Luikens & Anor [2003] NSWSC 1140, where Palmer J said that for a respondent merely to state that a claim is rejected is no more informative than to say that payment is “withheld”:

Section 14(3) requires that reasons for withholding payment of a claim be indicated in the payment schedule with sufficient particularity to enable the claimant to understand, at least in broad outline, what is the issue between it and the respondent. This understanding is necessary so that the claimant may decide whether to pursue the claim and may know what is the nature of the respondent’s case which it will have to meet if it decides to pursue the claim by referring it to adjudication.

That standard governs s 69(c). The reasons don’t have to read like a pleading — an experienced builder and subbie working off the same contract and the same prior correspondence can use abbreviated language — but they have to apprise the parties of the real issues in dispute.1 Brown J in Melaleuca View Pty Ltd v Sutton Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2019] QSC 226 stressed that s 69 deliberately uses “state” rather than the looser “indicate” in the NSW equivalent, signalling that Queensland respondents are held to a higher standard of clarity than their NSW counterparts.

Reasons must cover every part of the claim

The schedule has to address the whole payment claim, not just the parts the respondent wants to argue about. Silence on the rest is fatal. Chesterman J’s warning in Minimax Fire Fighting Systems Pty Ltd v Bremore Engineering (WA) Pty Ltd [2007] QSC 333 still controls: “The machinery for prompt payment and enforcement of payment would break down if a document, said to be a payment schedule, took issue with part only of a claim but was silent as to what it proposed to pay in respect of the balance.” If the payment claim has multiple components, every component needs to be addressed — either with reasons of its own, or by a global response that on its face covers the lot.2

Bare denials, deferrals and clarification requests

The recent NSWCA decision in Joye Group Pty Ltd v Cemco Projects Pty Ltd [2021] NSWCA 211 illustrates how easy it is to fall short. The respondent had told the claimant it would not pay until the work was complete, and the District Court treated that — read with the parties’ background correspondence — as a sufficient reason. The Court of Appeal disagreed: a statement that “we will not pay your claim until…” says when, not whether, payment will be made; it doesn’t identify which items have been done, which haven’t, or what the respondent disputes. The Court of Appeal also held that a payment schedule is not to be reconstructed by reference to external materials in order to give it a particularity it doesn’t have on its face.

The same theme runs through other recent decisions. An email asking the claimant to “please adjust your claim accordingly and resubmit”, or to “expand on the difference between these two quotes”, is a request for clarification, not a reason for withholding payment.3 An email amounting to “little more than a bare assertion that further substantiation is required” doesn’t state a reason at all.4 And a vague reference to “many emails, photos, videos, back charges from builders and other trades, complains [sic] from my clients” isn’t enough — if you incorporate documents by reference, they have to be identified with enough particularity that the claimant knows what is being incorporated.5

What sufficient reasons look like

By contrast, in CPB Contractors Pty Ltd & Ors v MSS Projects (NSW) Pty Ltd t/as MSS Steel & Ors [2025] QSC 239, Johnstone J held that a payment schedule satisfied s 69(c) where the respondent expressly proposed to pay $0 for descoped work, identified the descoping direction, and explained its variation calculations by reference to the figures in the “Certified this Month” column of Schedule 2 of the schedule. Both sides knew what the dispute was and why. As his Honour confirmed, agreeing with Chesterman J in Minimax, there is no particular form required and no obligation to produce a detailed spreadsheet — just enough to define the scope of the dispute.6

One further point: incorporation by reference is permitted, but the assembled document — schedule plus annexures — has to satisfy s 69 on its face. The respondent can’t leave the claimant or the adjudicator to assemble miscellaneous prior correspondence into a payment schedule.7

What this means on a project

  • For every dollar you propose to pay less than the claimed amount, write a reason. If the schedule discounts ten line items, say something specific about each one — even if the language is short.
  • Address the whole payment claim. If you say nothing about an item, you risk the document being treated as no schedule at all.
  • Don’t treat “we’ll pay when the work is finished”, “please resubmit a corrected claim” or “we need more information” as reasons. They aren’t. State your actual basis for paying less — defective work, descope, contractual cap, set-off, valuation dispute — on the face of the schedule.
  • Use the contract and the parties’ prior correspondence as shorthand if you like, but make sure the schedule itself is intelligible. Don’t rely on the adjudicator reconstructing your case from emails you didn’t identify.
  • If you incorporate documents (back-charge ledgers, defect lists, superintendent’s assessments, set-off calculations), identify them precisely — date, title, version — and attach them. Vague references to “our records” will not do.
  • Remember the consequence. Reasons not stated in the payment schedule cannot be raised in your adjudication response. Get them all in now — bare denials and conditional language will leave you defenceless when the claim goes to adjudication.
  1. Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Luikens & Anor [2003] NSWSC 1140 (Palmer J) — payment claim and schedule are read by parties experienced in the industry, so abbreviated language is permitted, but precision and particularity must be sufficient to apprise the parties of the real issues; Perform (NSW) Pty Ltd v MEV-AUS Pty Ltd [2009] NSWCA 157, [49] (Giles JA, McColl and Young JJA agreeing) — “indicate” under the NSW Act applied in a common-sense practical manner, allowing reference to prior correspondence; Barclay Mowlem Construction Ltd v Tesrol Walsh Bay Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 1232, [26] (McDougall J) — the BCIPA equivalent of s 69(c) has no bearing on the adequacy or sufficiency of reasons provided they are part of the schedule.
  2. Turnkey Innovative Engineering Pty Ltd v Witron Australia Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 981 (Stevenson J) — payment schedule invalid where it gave reasons for one part of the claim but failed to address the variation components at all; T & T Building Pty Ltd v GMW Group Pty Ltd & Ors [2010] QSC 211 — an email saying “we do not agree with the above claim as the variations schedule is incorrect”, coupled with a part-payment, did not satisfy the requirement to state why the scheduled amount was less.
  3. Turnkey Innovative Engineering Pty Ltd v Witron Australia Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 981 (Stevenson J) treated “please adjust your claim accordingly and resubmit for approval” as sufficient on the repricing point but invalidating the schedule on the variation components; Actif Concrete v Design Builders [2025] VCC 275 (Lauritsen J) — an email asking the claimant to expand on differences between two quotes did not indicate an intention to dispute or specify a proposed payment amount.
  4. Roberts Construction Group Pty Ltd v Drummond Carpentry Services Pty Ltd [2024] VSC 246, [98] (Niall JA) — emails amounted to little more than a bare assertion that further substantiation was required; inviting a further, lesser claim is not stating a reason for withholding payment.
  5. Style Timber Floor Pty Ltd v Krivosudsky [2019] NSWCA 171 — use of the Act “is not a licence for informality or an excuse for vague, generalised objections to payment”; documents incorporated by reference must be identified with sufficient particularity for the recipient to know what is being incorporated.
  6. CPB Contractors Pty Ltd & Ors v MSS Projects (NSW) Pty Ltd t/as MSS Steel & Ors [2025] QSC 239 (Johnstone J); see also BCS Infrastructure Support Pty Ltd v Jones Lang Lasalle (NSW) Pty Ltd [2020] VSC 739 (Stynes J) — a substantive pricing dispute, described with some particularity and clearly responsive to the payment claims, was a valid reason for withholding payment under the NSW equivalent of s 69(c).
  7. Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2013] QCA 386 (Muir JA, Gotterson and Morrison JJA agreeing) — an obviously incomplete purported schedule may be completed within time by provision of the omitted material, but a claimant cannot be expected to compose a payment schedule for the respondent by assembling miscellaneous documents until the assembled materials satisfy the Act.

Requirements of a payment schedule

Within time (15 BD or shorter contractual)

Once a payment claim lands, the clock starts. Under s 76 you have to give a payment schedule within whichever ends first — the period in the contract, if any, or 15 business days after the payment claim is given to you. Miss that window and the consequences are severe: you can’t serve a schedule at all, and the claimant becomes entitled to recover the full claimed amount as a debt.

Section 76 BIF Act — Responding to payment claim

(1) If given a payment claim, a respondent must respond to the payment claim by giving the claimant a payment schedule within whichever of the following periods ends first—

(a) the period, if any, within which the respondent must give the payment schedule under the relevant construction contract;

(b) 15 business days after the payment claim is given to the respondent.

Maximum penalty—100 penalty units.

(2) However, the respondent is not required to give the claimant the payment schedule if the amount claimed in the payment claim is paid in full on or before the due date for the progress payment to which the payment claim relates.

(3) If the respondent gives the claimant a payment schedule, the respondent must pay the claimant the amount proposed in the payment schedule no later than the due date for the progress payment to which the payment schedule relates.

Two clocks, whichever ends first

Section 76(1) sets up two competing periods and tells you to use whichever runs out first. The statutory backstop is 15 business days from when the payment claim is given. If the contract specifies a shorter period for the respondent to give a payment schedule, that contractual period takes over. There is no scope for a contract to extend the 15-business-day cap — the Act sets the longest available window, and parties can only shorten it.

Whether a contractual clause actually displaces the statutory period is not always obvious. The courts require “clear contextual support for a necessary implication” that the contract has supplanted the Act’s timeframe.1 A clause that talks about the head contractor’s representative issuing a “payment certificate” under the contract may not be a clause about giving a statutory payment schedule at all — the two documents perform different functions and a certificate clause won’t automatically shorten the s 76 clock.

The Queensland appellate position

The leading Queensland decision is Allencon Pty Ltd v Palmgrove Holdings Pty Ltd trading as Carruthers Contracting [2023] QCA 6. The subcontract there required the Main Contractor’s Representative to issue a payment certificate “within 21 days after receipt of a claim for payment”, setting out the calculations and, where the certified amount differed from the claim, the reasons. The respondent argued the contract said nothing about a payment schedule and that it could rely on the full 15 business days under s 76(1)(b). The claimant said the 21-day contractual period applied and ran out first. The Court of Appeal sided with the claimant: because the contract stipulated a period of 21 calendar days for the issue of the certificate, that was the date by which the payment schedule had to be given, and the respondent — having missed it — was liable for the full amount claimed.

The Court distinguished the New South Wales decision in Thiess Pty Ltd v Lane Cove Tunnel Nominee Co Pty Ltd [2009] NSWCA 53 on the basis that the Thiess contract required an Independent Verifier’s certificate to accompany progress payments, which showed the relevant clause was a “reply to the contractual progress claim and not a reply to a statutory payment claim”. The point that matters for project teams is this: if your contract has a certification clause that, in substance, performs the work of a payment schedule — setting out what will be paid, the calculations and reasons for any difference — expect a court to read that period as the s 76(1)(a) period, even if the clause never uses the words “payment schedule”.

Contractual deeming clauses can’t buy more time

The other recurring trap is the deeming clause — a contractual provision that pretends a document was served later than it actually was, often to give the recipient extra working time. These don’t work to extend the statutory clock. In Roberts Co (NSW) Pty Ltd v Sharvain Facades Pty Ltd (Administrators Appointed) [2025] NSWCA 161, Hammerschlag CJ held that the equivalent NSW 10-business-day period commences from the date the payment claim is actually served and that parties may only agree to shorten, not lengthen, that period. The payment claim there was served on 28 February 2025 and the schedule issued on 17 March was held to be out of time, with the contractual deeming clause that purported to postpone service to 3 March having no effect on the operation of the statutory timeframe. Roberts became liable for the full amount claimed.

Service mechanics that affect the deadline

How and when the payment claim was “given” to you fixes the start of the count, so the service rules matter. Email service outside business hours still counts. In Queensland, an email payment schedule sent at 11:45pm on the last day of the period was held to have been validly given at the moment it became capable of being retrieved, on the strength of the contract permitting email service and s 24(1)(a) of the Electronic Transactions Act 2001 (Qld).2 Service through solicitors can also be effective, but only where the solicitor’s authority to give or receive the document is established — don’t assume it.3

What this means on a project

  • Diary the s 76 deadline the moment a payment claim is received. Count 15 business days from the date the claim was given, then check the contract for any shorter period. Work to whichever date is earlier.
  • Read the contract’s certification or assessment clauses with Allencon in mind. A clause that requires the head contractor’s representative to assess and certify within (say) 10 or 14 days will likely be treated as the s 76(1)(a) period, even if it doesn’t use the words “payment schedule”.
  • Don’t rely on a deeming clause to push out the start date. After Roberts v Sharvain, if the claim was actually given on Day 0, the clock runs from Day 0 regardless of what the contract pretends.
  • If you serve by email, the moment the message becomes capable of being retrieved is the moment of service — including 11:45pm on the deadline day. That cuts both ways: it can save a late-day schedule and it can shorten your time to respond to a claim served at 4:55pm.
  • If the deadline is going to be missed, consider s 76(2): paying the claimed amount in full on or before the due date removes the obligation to give a payment schedule altogether. That is sometimes the cleaner commercial answer to a small or undisputed claim.
  • Late schedules are not “late but valid” — they are no schedule at all. The downstream consequences (judgment debt under s 78, no adjudication response without a s 82 second-chance notice) flow from the missed deadline, not from anything wrong with the schedule itself.
  1. Thiess Pty Ltd v Lane Cove Tunnel Nominee Company Pty Ltd [2008] NSWSC 729, [21] (Hammerschlag J), upheld on appeal in Thiess Pty Ltd v Lane Cove Tunnel Nominee Company Pty Ltd [2009] NSWCA 53; approach accepted in Queensland in Tailored Projects Pty Ltd v Jedfire Pty Ltd [2009] QSC 32; [2009] 2 Qd R 171, 179 [23] (Douglas J).
  2. CKP Constructions Pty Ltd v Gabba Holdings Pty Ltd [2016] QDC 356 (McGill DCJ) — an email payment schedule sent at 11:45pm on the last business day of the period under s 99 was validly given by reference to s 24(1)(a) of the Electronic Transactions Act 2001 (Qld).
  3. Emag Constructions Pty Ltd v Highrise Concrete Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 903, [59] (Einstein J), applied in Taylor Projects Group Pty Ltd v Brick Dept Pty Ltd [2005] NSWSC 439 — strict proof of service required, general agency principles for solicitors must yield to the Act; cf Baulderstone Hornibrook Pty Ltd v Queensland Investment Corporation [2007] NSWCA 9 — solicitor’s authority to serve a payment schedule established on the facts after review of the retainer and prior conduct.

Requirements of a payment schedule

Served on the claimant

A payment schedule only does its job — locking in the amount you propose to pay and confining the dispute — if it is actually given to the claimant. Section 76(1) requires the respondent to “give” the schedule to the claimant; s 102 tells you how that giving may happen. Get service wrong and you are in the same position as a respondent that filed nothing: liable for the full claimed amount as a debt, with no right to put a defence at adjudication.

Sections 76(1) and 102 BIF Act — how to give the schedule

76 Responding to payment claim

(1) If given a payment claim, a respondent must respond to the payment claim by giving the claimant a payment schedule within whichever of the following periods ends first—

(a) the period, if any, within which the respondent must give the payment schedule under the relevant construction contract;

(b) 15 business days after the payment claim is given to the respondent.

102 Service of notices

(1) A notice or other document that, under this chapter, is authorised or required to be given to a person may be given to the person in the way, if any, provided under the relevant construction contract.

(2) Subsection (1) is in addition to, and does not limit or exclude, the Acts Interpretation Act 1954, section 39 or the provisions of any other law about the giving of notices.

(3) To remove any doubt, it is declared that nothing in this Act—

(a) excludes the proper service of notices or documents by a person’s agent; or

(b) requires a person’s acknowledgement of a notice or document properly given to the person.

“Giving” the schedule — a facultative regime

The Act uses “give”, not “serve” or “provide”. Nothing turns on that change of language: the courts treat “give”, “serve” and “provide” as equivalent for these purposes.1 Section 102 is “facultative rather than mandatory” — it gives you valid routes for giving the schedule, but it does not displace s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) or the general law on service.2 In practice that means three independent paths are open: the contract’s notice clause (s 102(1)), s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act, and actual receipt by someone within the claimant’s organisation who is authorised to deal with payment claims.

Section 102(3)(a) puts beyond doubt that giving by the respondent’s agent is effective. The standard arrangement — superintendent or contract administrator issuing the schedule on the principal’s behalf — is therefore valid where the contract makes the agent’s role clear. Service by a respondent’s solicitors is also generally effective, provided the retainer extends to that step; the Court of Appeal in Baulderstone Hornibrook Pty Ltd v Queensland Investment Corporation [2007] NSWCA 9 upheld solicitor service after looking at the retainer, prior conduct and the commercial sense of the conclusion.3

Service by email — when the schedule is “given”

Email is now the default. Section 102(1) lets the contract specify email expressly, and most do. Even where it does not, s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act reaches email as a “similar facility” in many circumstances, although that view is not free from doubt.4 The timing rule comes from s 24 of the Electronic Transactions (Queensland) Act 2001: an electronic communication is received when it becomes “capable of being retrieved” at a designated address. The email need not be opened or read, and a document caught by a spam filter is still capable of being retrieved.5

The most useful Queensland decision for project teams is CKP Constructions Pty Ltd v Gabba Holdings Pty Ltd [2016] QDC 356. The respondent emailed its payment schedule at 11:45pm on the fifth business day after the s 99 warning notice. The contract permitted service by email. Applying s 24 of the Electronic Transactions Act, McGill DCJ held the schedule had been given when it became capable of being retrieved — 11:45pm — and was therefore in time. Late at night is still in time, so long as it is the right night. The case is the answer to the question every contract administrator asks at 5pm on day 15: can I still get this away tonight?

The most recent appellate guidance on emailed service comes from Roberts Co (NSW) Pty Ltd v Sharvain Facades Pty Ltd (Administrators Appointed) [2025] NSWCA 161. The case is about a payment claim, but the reasoning applies equally to a payment schedule given by email under s 76. The Court held that service occurs when the document is emailed to an address specified by the recipient for service and becomes capable of retrieval — with no requirement that the recipient access or become aware of the communication. McHugh JA put the underlying point about service mechanics this way:

section 31 governs the mechanics of valid service under the Act, irrespective of awareness.

His Honour also held that a contractual “deeming” clause that purported to defer the time of service (treating an email arriving on Friday as served on Monday) had no effect on the statutory timeframe.6 A respondent cannot push back the s 76 clock by drafting a deeming clause; equally, a respondent cannot use one to claim its schedule was given earlier than it actually was. What you sent, when you sent it, and to where, is what matters.

Cloud platforms, post-office boxes and corporations

Cloud platforms are more fragile. Service by Dropbox link has been held to fall outside both s 39 and s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) because the email delivers only the means of access, not the document itself.7 The same logic has been applied to dedicated platforms such as Corrigo where the recipient was unaware of the upload.8 Where the contract designates a platform like Aconex or Payapps as a service channel, however, service occurs when the schedule becomes capable of retrieval at that designated address.9 If you must use a portal, attach the schedule to the email as well.

For corporate claimants, s 109X of the Corporations Act permits service by leaving the document at, or posting it to, the company’s registered office, or by delivering it personally to a director resident in Australia. Service to a post-office box is not valid service under s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act or s 109X unless the contract authorises that mode.10 Where service is by post to the registered office, the document is “received” when it arrives at the office during ordinary business hours; it does not have to come to the attention of any particular individual.11

What this means on a project

  • Identify the right service channel before you start drafting. Read the contract’s notice clause: if it nominates an email address or a platform (Aconex, Payapps), use that exact channel and send to that exact address.
  • Don’t leave it to the last hour. The schedule is “given” when it is capable of being retrieved at the recipient’s end, but the time of receipt — not the time of sending — is what counts. CKP Constructions says 11:45pm on the last day works; 12:01am does not.
  • Send to multiple addresses where possible — the claimant’s nominated address, the project manager, the contracts team. Keep the sent-mail record and any read receipts.
  • If the schedule is to be given by your solicitors or by the superintendent on your behalf, make sure their authority to do so is clear from the contract or a documented retainer. Section 102(3)(a) protects giving by an agent, but only if the agent is in fact authorised.
  • Don’t rely on a contractual deeming clause to extend the s 76 clock. After Roberts Co, those clauses do not alter when a document is given for the purposes of the Act.
  • Avoid Dropbox and bare cloud links. Attach the schedule itself to an email, even if you also upload it to a portal.
  • If the claimant is a company, send to the address nominated in the contract; failing that, the registered office during business hours. A post-office box only works if the contract authorises it.
  1. Falgat Constructions Pty Ltd v Equity Australia Corporation Pty Ltd [2006] NSWCA 259, [58] (Hodgson JA — “provide” does not mean anything different from “serve”); the same equivalence is applied in Queensland to “give” under the BIF Act.
  2. Neumann Contractors Pty Ltd v Traspunt No 5 Pty Ltd [2010] QCA 119 (Muir JA, Holmes and Chesterman JJA agreeing) — the predecessor to s 102 is “facultative rather than mandatory in nature”. On strict compliance with the time limits triggered by service, see Emag Constructions Pty Ltd v Highrise Concrete Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 903 (Einstein J — the Act exhibits “zero tolerance” for delay).
  3. Baulderstone Hornibrook Pty Ltd v Queensland Investment Corporation [2007] NSWCA 9 — the Court of Appeal upheld giving of a payment schedule by the respondent’s solicitors, after a detailed review of the retainer, the prior conduct of the parties and the commercial sense of the conclusion. Compare Emag Constructions Pty Ltd v Highrise Concrete Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 903 (Einstein J) and Taylor Projects Group Pty Ltd v Brick Dept Pty Ltd [2005] NSWSC 439 (Einstein J — solicitor without express authority unable to give a payment schedule).
  4. Penfold Projects Pty Ltd v Securcorp Limited [2011] QDC 77 (Irwin DCJ) treated email as a “similar facility” for s 39; that view was doubted in Conveyor & General Engineering Pty Ltd v Basetec Services Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 30 (Philip McMurdo J). The safer path is to comply with the contract’s express notice regime.
  5. Bauen Constructions Pty Ltd v Sky General Services Pty Ltd [2012] NSWSC 1123, [77] (Sackar J) — “capable of being retrieved” is broad and does not require an email to be opened or read; a document caught by a spam filter is still capable of being retrieved.
  6. Piety Constructions Pty Ltd v Hville FCP Pty Ltd [2022] NSWSC 1318 (Stevenson J — a contractual clause deeming documents delivered after 4:30pm to be given at 9:30am the next business day is invalid to the extent it purports to alter when a document is “provided” under the Act); applied in Roberts Co (NSW) Pty Ltd v Sharvain Facades Pty Ltd (Administrators Appointed) [2025] NSWCA 161 (Hammerschlag CJ; McHugh JA agreeing) — parties may shorten, not lengthen, the statutory response period.
  7. Conveyor & General Engineering Pty Ltd v Basetec Services Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 30, [29]–[32] (Philip McMurdo J) — service by Dropbox link did not deliver the document itself, only the means to access it.
  8. BCS Infrastructure Support Pty Ltd v Jones Lang Lasalle (NSW) Pty Ltd [2020] VSC 739 (Stynes J) — service via the Corrigo platform was effective only when the respondent became aware of the upload.
  9. Fredon Infrastructure Pty Ltd v Hitachi Rail GTS Australia Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 1244 (Stevenson J — Aconex delivery; awareness on day of delivery established by surrounding facts and s 13A(1) of the Electronic Transactions Act 2000 (NSW)).
  10. CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209, [16], [23] (Daubney J) — service to a post-office box is not valid service under s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) or s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) unless the contract authorises that mode.
  11. Falgat Constructions Pty Ltd v Equity Australia Corporation Pty Ltd [2006] NSWCA 259, [58] (Hodgson JA — mail delivered to a registered office is received when it arrives, without anyone needing to see it; if a document actually comes to the attention of a person with authority to deal with it, it does not matter whether any facultative service regime has been complied with). Adopted in CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209 (Daubney J) and QC Communications NSW Pty Ltd v CivComm Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1095 (Ball J).

Requirements of an adjudication application

A trigger condition must exist

You can’t apply for adjudication just because you are unhappy with how your payment claim has been treated. Section 79(1) sets out a closed list of trigger events — one of them must have happened, otherwise the registrar has no application to receive and any adjudication that follows is built on sand.

Section 79(1) BIF Act — when a claimant may apply

(1) A claimant may apply to the registrar for adjudication of a payment claim (an adjudication application) if—

(a) the claimant is entitled to apply for adjudication under section 78(2)(b) because of a failure by the respondent to pay an amount owed to the claimant by the due date for the payment; or

(b) the amount stated in the payment schedule, given in response to the payment claim, is less than the amount stated in the payment claim.

The three triggers in practice

Although s 79(1) reads as two paragraphs, the cross-reference to s 78(2)(b) pulls in the s 78 definition of “amount owed” and produces three distinct factual triggers. Each one matches a different time-bar in s 79(2)(b), so identifying the correct trigger is the first thing to do before you start counting business days.

  • No payment schedule, no payment. The respondent didn’t respond with a payment schedule under s 76 and didn’t pay the full amount claimed by the due date. The “amount owed” defaults to the amount claimed (s 78(5)(a)). Time-bar: 30 business days under s 79(2)(b)(i).
  • Schedule given, but not paid in full. The respondent did issue a payment schedule under s 76, the scheduled amount was something less than nil-but-greater-than-the-claim (i.e. the schedule effectively accepted part of the claim), and then they didn’t pay the scheduled amount by the due date. The “amount owed” is the scheduled amount (s 78(5)(b)). Time-bar: 20 business days under s 79(2)(b)(ii).
  • Scheduled amount less than claimed. The respondent issued a payment schedule under s 76 and the amount in it is less than the amount in the payment claim — whether or not anything has actually been paid. This is the s 79(1)(b) trigger and it is independent of any failure to pay. Time-bar: 30 business days from receipt of the schedule under s 79(2)(b)(iii).

Triggers one and two share a feature that trigger three does not: they depend on the respondent failing to pay by the due date. Trigger three operates the moment the schedule is received, because the dispute is about the gap between the claim and the schedule, not about the cash.

The election point under s 78(2)

Section 78(2) gives a claimant who is owed an amount two options — sue for the unpaid portion as a debt, or apply for adjudication. They are framed as alternatives, and once an adjudication application has been validly “made” the claimant is locked out of the debt route for the same amount.1 The election only bites where there is a valid trigger in the first place: in Kyle Bay Removals Pty Ltd v Dynabuild Project Services Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 334, Meagher JA held that where no valid payment claim had been served, no liability to pay attached and there could be no binding election under the s 78 equivalent. So if the underlying claim was bad, suing in court doesn’t prevent a later adjudication when a valid claim is finally made.

Telling the triggers apart matters

Misidentifying the trigger is one of the easier ways to lose an application. The leading Queensland illustration is Gisley Investments Pty Ltd v Williams & Anor [2010] QSC 178, where the claimant treated a “relatively informal” email as not amounting to a payment schedule and applied on the no-schedule trigger long after the 30-day window from receipt of that email had closed. Douglas J approached the question as one of statutory construction — was it a purpose of the Act that an application made on the wrong trigger should be invalid? — and held that, on the facts, the real objects of the Act (notice of the claim, opportunity to respond, hearing before the adjudicator) had been met, so the adjudicator’s decision was not a nullity. Gisley is a saving decision rather than a licence: don’t rely on it. If there is any chance the respondent’s document is a payment schedule, treat it as one and apply on the s 79(1)(b) trigger within 30 business days of receipt.

One further point on s 79(1)(b). The trigger is that the scheduled amount is “less than the amount stated in the payment claim”. A nil schedule is less than the claim, so a nil response is enough — you don’t need any payment failure on top. The wording also doesn’t require the schedule to be a valid one in every respect; what matters is that something purporting to be a payment schedule has been given and the dollar figure in it falls short.

What this means on a project

  • Before you start counting time, identify which of the three triggers you are running on. The trigger fixes both your time-bar and the “amount owed” you can pursue.
  • If you receive anything from the respondent in the schedule window — even an informal email pushing back on the claim — assume it is a payment schedule. Apply within 30 business days of receiving it under s 79(1)(b) rather than waiting out the no-schedule clock.
  • A nil schedule is a trigger. You don’t need a separate failure to pay; the gap between “claimed” and “nil” is the dispute.
  • Don’t commence court proceedings and an adjudication for the same amount in parallel. Once you have validly made an adjudication application under s 78(2)(b), the s 78(2)(a) debt route is closed for that amount.
  • If the underlying payment claim is shaky — for example, served outside the s 75 reference-date window — fixing that is upstream work. No s 79(1) trigger will save an application based on an invalid claim.
  1. Seymour Whyte Constructions Pty Ltd v Ostwald Bros Pty Ltd (In liquidation) [2019] NSWCA 11 (NSW Court of Appeal) — election between the two limbs of s 78(2) only operates where an adjudication application has actually been “made”; an invalid application does not lock the claimant out of the debt route. Canterbury-Bankstown Council v Payce Communities Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 1419 (Henry J) — running parallel court proceedings while a fresh payment claim goes to adjudication is not, without more, an abuse of process; the threshold is “exceptional circumstances” and the onus is “a heavy one”. National Management Group Pty Ltd v Biriel Industries Pty Ltd trading as Master Steel & Ors [2019] QSC 219 (Wilson J) — minor errors in the application document don’t defeat the trigger so long as the project, reference date and amount due are identifiable.

Requirements of an adjudication application

Within time (windows depend on trigger)

Once the entitlement to apply for adjudication has crystallised, you don’t have long. Section 79(2)(b) sets three different time windows, and the one that applies depends on which trigger you’re running on — no payment schedule, short-paid schedule, or short schedule. Miss the window and you can’t apply at all.

Section 79(2) BIF Act — time for an adjudication application

(2) An adjudication application—

(a) must be in the approved form; and

(b) must be made within—

(i) for an application relating to a failure to give a payment schedule and pay the full amount stated in the payment claim—30 business days after the later of the following days—

(A) the day of the due date for the progress payment to which the claim relates;

(B) the last day the respondent could have given the payment schedule under section 76; or

(ii) for an application relating to a failure to pay the full amount stated in the payment schedule—20 business days after the due date for the progress payment to which the claim relates; or

(iii) for an application relating to the amount stated in the payment schedule being less than the amount stated in the payment claim—30 business days after the claimant receives the payment schedule; and

(c) must identify the payment claim and the payment schedule, if any, to which it relates; and

(d) must be accompanied by the fee prescribed by regulation for the application.

Pick the right window

Three triggers, three deadlines. The first thing to do is identify which subparagraph of s 79(2)(b) you’re inside, because the calculation method differs:

  • No payment schedule and no payment — s 79(2)(b)(i): 30 business days after the later of (A) the due date for the progress payment, or (B) the last day the respondent could have given a payment schedule under s 76.
