The “later of” mechanic matters: if your contract has a long payment period, the due date can fall after the s 76 schedule deadline, and you need to use whichever of the two lands later.
  • Payment schedule given but full scheduled amount not paid — s 79(2)(b)(ii): 20 business days after the due date for the progress payment. This is the shortest window and it’s easy to miss — the trigger here is non-payment of an amount the respondent has already accepted.
  • Payment schedule short-pays the claim — s 79(2)(b)(iii): 30 business days after the claimant receives the payment schedule. The clock runs from receipt, not from service — the Act distinguishes the two, and the receipt date is the one that counts.

All three are measured in business days under the BIF Act definition, not calendar days. Diary the deadline as soon as the trigger event occurs.

The deadline is mandatory — an out-of-time application is no application

The leading Queensland authority on the consequence of missing the s 79(2)(b) window is De Neefe Signs Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd; Traffic Technologies Traffic Hire Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 279, where Fryberg J held that the equivalent timing requirement in the predecessor Act was an essential precondition to the existence of an adjudication application at all. His Honour’s reasoning, which has been applied to s 79(2) of the BIF Act, was that the natural meaning of the words “an adjudication application … cannot be made” produces a jurisdictional result:

If by statute an application cannot be made, then anything purporting to be one cannot be an application. The existence of an application is a basic and essential requirement of an adjudicator’s determination.

The same conclusion was reached, on different reasoning, in Nebmas Pty Ltd v Sub Divide Pty Ltd & Ors [2009] QSC 92, where McMurdo J held that the time limit in the predecessor provision used “emphatic” language equivalent to the “cannot be made” formula and was therefore an essential requirement.1 The practical consequence is that an adjudicator who proceeds on an out-of-time application has no jurisdiction, and the determination will be quashed if challenged.

Picking the wrong trigger can save you

A more forgiving line of authority deals with claimants who, in good faith, treat an ambiguous communication as something other than a payment schedule and apply on a different trigger. In Gisley Investments Pty Ltd v Williams & Anor [2010] QSC 178, the claimant was originally required to apply by 3 December 2009 on the assumption that a “relatively informal” email from the respondent was a payment schedule. Treating it instead as no schedule at all, the claimant didn’t apply until 1 February 2010, well after the (b)(iii) window would have closed. Douglas J upheld the validity of the resulting determination, reasoning that the imprecision in the respondent’s own email had caused the difficulty, and the real objects of the Act — notice of the claim and an opportunity to respond — had been met.2 The lesson is narrow but useful: where the trigger itself is genuinely uncertain because of the respondent’s conduct, the courts will not necessarily kill the application on a strict timing point.

What this means on a project

  • The day the trigger event happens, identify which subparagraph of s 79(2)(b) you’re running on and diary the deadline immediately. Don’t wait until you’ve briefed lawyers — the 20 business day window under (b)(ii) can disappear quickly.
  • For the (b)(i) “no schedule, no payment” window, calculate both the due-date deadline and the s 76 schedule deadline, and use whichever is later. Don’t pick the earlier one and lose time you’re entitled to.
  • For the (b)(iii) short-paid schedule window, the clock runs from receipt, not from service. Keep evidence of when the schedule actually came into your hands — receipt is the date that controls.
  • Count in business days, and be careful around public holidays and the Christmas shutdown. The Act’s business-day definition is not the same as “working days” under your contract.
  • If the respondent has sent you something that looks ambiguous — an email that could be a schedule or could be informal correspondence — take advice early. Gisley is a forgiving authority but you don’t want to be relying on it.
  • If you miss the deadline, don’t lodge anyway in the hope it slides through. After De Neefe the application is a nullity and you’ll wear the registrar’s fee, the adjudicator’s fees and likely the costs of a successful challenge.
  1. Nebmas Pty Ltd v Sub Divide Pty Ltd & Ors [2009] QSC 92 (McMurdo J — time limit in equivalent provision is “emphatic” and an essential requirement); De Neefe Signs Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd; Traffic Technologies Traffic Hire Pty Ltd v Build1 (Qld) Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 279 (Fryberg J — existence of an application is a basic and essential requirement of an adjudicator’s determination).
  2. Gisley Investments Pty Ltd v Williams & Anor [2010] QSC 178 (Douglas J — ambiguity caused by respondent’s own informal email; statutory objects met). For the broader principle that compliance with s 79(2) is generally treated as jurisdictional, see also Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30 (Dalton J — “must” in s 79(2)(a) and (c) denotes a mandatory requirement).

Requirements of an adjudication application

On the approved form to the registrar

An adjudication application starts with a single, deceptively simple step: lodging the right document, in the right form, with the registrar at the QBCC. Get the form wrong and the adjudicator has nothing valid to determine. The Act spells the requirement out in one line.

Section 79(2)(a) BIF Act — form of the application

An adjudication application—

(a) must be in the approved form; and

What the “approved form” actually is

The “approved form” is the form approved by the registrar for a claimant to apply to the registrar for adjudication. In practice it is the document the claimant lodges with the QBCC — either through the QBCC online portal or in hardcopy — to commence the adjudication process. It is not the receipt or acknowledgement the QBCC generates after lodgement.

That distinction matters because it has tripped up at least one well-resourced claimant. In Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2023] QSC 290, Williams J held that the “QBCC PDF Form” — the PDF the QBCC system automatically generated as a record once the claimant submitted the online “Electronic Form” — was not the approved form for the purposes of s 79(2)(a). The approved form was the Electronic Form itself; the PDF was effectively a receipt. Because the document the claimant gave the respondent (and treated as the application) was the PDF rather than the Electronic Form, the application failed the s 79(2)(a) requirement and the adjudicator had no jurisdiction.1

Two things have happened since. First, the QBCC Registry has changed how it processes adjudication applications, so the workflow that produced the problem in Iris Broadbeach has been reworked. Second, and more importantly, the Residential Tenancies and Rooming Accommodation and Other Legislation Amendment Act 2024 (Qld) was passed on 6 June 2024 with amendments to the BIF Act expressly responding to Iris Broadbeach. The Explanatory Note to the Third Reading recorded that intent. The practical effect is that the legal principle in Iris Broadbeach on the “approved form” point is now nugatory — that particular trap has been closed.

The “must” in s 79(2)(a) is mandatory

The form requirement is not directory or technical. In Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30, Dalton J read the “must” in s 79(2)(a) and s 79(2)(c) as imposing mandatory requirements, contrasting it with the discretionary “may” in the submissions limb. Non-compliance with s 79(2)(a) is therefore the kind of defect that goes to the validity of the application, not a mere irregularity an adjudicator can overlook.

That said, content errors short of using the wrong form will not always be fatal. In National Management Group Pty Ltd v Biriel Industries Pty Ltd trading as Master Steel & Ors [2019] QSC 219, Wilson J held that an adjudication application satisfied s 79(2)(c) despite a number of errors, because the document still referred to the project name, the reference date and the amount due.2 The point for present purposes is narrower: get the right form — the one the registrar has approved — and complete it accurately.

What this means on a project

  • Lodge the current QBCC-approved adjudication application form. If you are using the online portal, the form you fill in and submit is the approved form. The PDF the system generates after submission is a record of lodgement, not the form itself.
  • Keep a complete copy of what you actually lodged with the registrar — ideally a screen-capture or saved version of every page of the online form before you submit. You will need to give the respondent a copy of that same document under s 79(4).
  • Do not assume an old version of the form is still “the approved form”. Check the QBCC website each time; the registrar can update the approved form, and using a superseded version is a needless jurisdictional risk.
  • Treat s 79(2)(a) as mandatory. A wrong-form application is not cured by good content elsewhere, so this is the first thing to confirm before lodgement, not after.
  • If you are advising a respondent, the post-June 2024 amendments mean an “approved form” challenge of the Iris Broadbeach kind will rarely succeed. Energy is better spent on timing (s 79(2)(b)), identification (s 79(2)(c)) and service on the respondent (s 79(4)).
  1. Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2023] QSC 290 (Williams J). Williams J also held the QBCC PDF Form was not a “copy” of the Electronic Form for the purposes of giving the application to the respondent — the differences were “not trivial”. The June 2024 amendments to the BIF Act were enacted in direct response to this decision and have rendered the approved-form principle in Iris Broadbeach nugatory.
  2. National Management Group Pty Ltd v Biriel Industries Pty Ltd trading as Master Steel & Ors [2019] QSC 219 (Wilson J) — minor errors in the application did not defeat s 79(2)(c) where the document still identified the project, reference date and amount; Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30 (Dalton J) — “must” in s 79(2)(a) and s 79(2)(c) is mandatory, in contrast to the discretionary “may” governing submissions.

Requirements of an adjudication application

Identify the payment claim and schedule

An adjudication application has to point unambiguously back to the payment claim it is built on, and to the payment schedule it is answering — if there is one. Get this wrong and the adjudicator has no jurisdiction, because the application has not engaged the statute.

Section 79(2)(c) BIF Act — identification

79 Application for adjudication

(2) An adjudication application—

…

(c) must identify the payment claim and the payment schedule, if any, to which it relates…

What “identify” actually requires

The word “must” in s 79(2)(c) is mandatory, not directory. It is in the same league as the requirement in s 79(2)(a) to use the approved form, and it sits in deliberate contrast to the discretionary “may” that governs submissions. An application that fails to identify the right payment claim and the right payment schedule is not just defective — it is incapable of supporting an adjudication decision.1

That doesn’t mean the application has to be perfectly drafted. The leading Queensland decision on s 79(2)(c) is National Management Group Pty Ltd v Biriel Industries Pty Ltd trading as Master Steel & Ors [2019] QSC 219, where Wilson J held that an adjudication application satisfied s 79(2)(c) despite a number of errors, because “the adjudication application document refers to the project name, the reference date and the amount due.” The point is functional: can the respondent and the adjudicator tell, from the four corners of the application, exactly which payment claim and which payment schedule are in play? If yes, s 79(2)(c) is met even if there are imperfections elsewhere.

Identifying the wrong document is fatal

The clearest way to fall foul of the section is to identify the wrong payment schedule. In Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30, Dalton J set aside an adjudication decision because the claimant had not identified the payment schedule the respondent had actually issued — the application instead pointed to a different document. Her Honour was direct about the consequence:

The word “must” in each of section 79(2)(a) and 79(2)(c) of the BIF Act denotes a mandatory requirement of the Act whereas, in contrast, the word “may” in section 79(2)(e) of the BIF Act is used when the applicant for adjudication in fact has a choice as to whether or not to provide submissions.

So the risk is real even when the underlying claim is good: if the application names a document that isn’t the payment schedule, or omits the payment schedule altogether when one exists, the foundation of the adjudication is gone.

If there is no payment schedule

The words “if any” in s 79(2)(c) recognise that one path into adjudication — the s 79(1)(a) / s 78(2)(b) route — involves the respondent giving no payment schedule at all. In that situation you only need to identify the payment claim. But if a payment schedule did exist, even a late or informal one, you need to grapple with it and identify it; you cannot improve your position by ignoring it.

What this means on a project

  • State the payment claim’s date, claim number, project and claim amount on the face of the application. Attach the claim itself. The Biriel threshold — project name, reference date, amount due — is a sensible minimum, not a target.
  • If a payment schedule was given, identify it the same way: date, scheduled amount, who issued it, and attach a copy. Don’t pick and choose — if there are competing documents that might be the payment schedule, name all of them and explain which one you say is the schedule and why.
  • Don’t identify a different document by mistake. Kangaroo Point is the warning shot: getting the wrong schedule on the application is the kind of error that survives no amount of merit on the underlying claim.
  • If you’re running the “no payment schedule” route under s 79(1)(a), say so explicitly in the application and explain why no schedule was given (e.g. the s 76 deadline passed). That removes any argument that you have failed to identify a schedule that in fact exists.
  • Cross-check the application against the approved form before lodging. The mandatory requirements in s 79(2)(a) and s 79(2)(c) are the two most common jurisdictional knock-out points at the front end of an adjudication.
  1. Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30 (Dalton J) — “must” in s 79(2)(a) and s 79(2)(c) denotes a mandatory requirement, in contrast to the discretionary “may” in s 79(2)(e) governing submissions.
  2. National Management Group Pty Ltd v Biriel Industries Pty Ltd trading as Master Steel & Ors [2019] QSC 219 (Wilson J) — s 79(2)(c) satisfied despite errors where the application referred to the project name, the reference date and the amount due.
  3. Kuatro Build Pty Ltd v Elite Formwork Group Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 372 (Hmelnitsky J) — adjudication application validly made under s 17 of the NSW SOP Act (equivalent to s 79 BIF Act); no jurisdictional issue with lodgement or identification raised.

Requirements of an adjudication application

Pay the prescribed fee

An adjudication application must be accompanied by the fee fixed by regulation. No fee, no valid application — the requirement sits alongside the approved form, the timing rules and the obligation to identify the payment claim and schedule. The fee tier is keyed to the size of the progress payment you are claiming, so the right number to look at is the amount on the payment claim, not the broader contract sum.

Section 79(2)(d) BIF Act — fee requirement

(2) An adjudication application—

…

(d) must be accompanied by the fee prescribed by regulation for the application.

The fee schedule

The prescribed fees sit in the Building Industry Fairness (Security of Payment) Regulation 2018, set by reference to the size of the progress payment claimed. The schedule below is current as at 1 July 2025:

  • Progress payment of no more than $11,345.70 — $60.70.
  • More than $11,345.70 but no more than $56,728.80 — $182.15.
  • More than $56,728.80 but no more than $113,457.60 — $303.60.
  • More than $113,457.60 but no more than $283,644.05 — $425.20.
  • More than $283,644.05 but no more than $567,288.05 — $546.60.
  • More than $567,288.05 but no more than $850,932.15 — $668.05.
  • More than $850,932.15 but no more than $1,236,206.95 — $789.55.
  • More than $1,236,206.95 — the number of fee units equal to 0.0794188% of the progress payment amount, capped at 6,073.80 fee units.

The Regulation gives a worked example for the top tier: an application made on 1 July 2025 for a progress payment of $1,300,000 attracts a fee of $1,032.44 (942.01 fee units).1

Pick the tier from the claim amount

The relevant figure is the amount of the progress payment to which the adjudication application relates — in practice the amount on the payment claim that is being adjudicated. If your claim is for $250,000, you fall in tier (d) and pay $425.20. If your claim is for $900,000, you fall in tier (g) and pay $789.55. For very large claims (above roughly $1.236M) the fee scales with the claim amount until it hits the cap.

Two practical points. First, the fee is paid to the QBCC on lodgement — the application is made to the registrar (s 79(1)) and the registrar will not refer the application to an adjudicator under s 79(5) without the prescribed fee accompanying it. Second, fee units are reset annually under the standard Queensland fee-units mechanism, so check the current value before relying on a dollar figure for a tier (h) calculation.

Where the fee fits among the other s 79(2) requirements

Section 79(2) sets out a stack of mandatory requirements: approved form (s 79(2)(a)); within time (s 79(2)(b)); identify the claim and any schedule (s 79(2)(c)); and accompanied by the prescribed fee (s 79(2)(d)). The Queensland courts have treated the s 79(2) requirements as mandatory in character. In Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30, Dalton J drew the contrast between the “must” language used in s 79(2)(a) and (c) and the “may” language used elsewhere in the section, holding that “must” denotes a mandatory requirement of the Act.2 Paragraph (d) is drafted in the same mandatory form, so the same logic applies: the prescribed fee is not a discretionary add-on.

That said, the fee is the most administratively straightforward of the four requirements. Lodgement through the QBCC’s electronic system prompts for payment as part of the lodgement step, so the practical risk is paying the wrong amount — usually by misidentifying the tier — rather than forgetting to pay altogether.

What this means on a project

  • Identify the amount of the progress payment on the payment claim being adjudicated. That figure — not the contract sum, not the running total of the project — drives the tier.
  • For claims up to $1,236,206.95, read the dollar fee straight off the schedule above. For claims above that, calculate 0.0794188% of the claim amount, then check it against the 6,073.80 fee unit cap and convert at the current fee-unit value.
  • Pay through the QBCC’s lodgement system at the time you lodge. Don’t plan to pay it later — s 79(2)(d) requires the fee to accompany the application.
  • Re-check the schedule each financial year. The thresholds and dollar figures are indexed and the regulation is republished — the figures above are current as at 1 July 2025.
  • If a claim straddles a threshold (e.g. $56,728.80 exactly), read the words carefully: each tier is “more than” the lower bound and “no more than” the upper bound, so a claim sitting on the threshold falls in the lower tier.
  1. Building Industry Fairness (Security of Payment) Regulation 2018 (Qld), schedule of application fees for adjudication (Act, s 79(2)(d)), current as at 1 July 2025. The worked example is reproduced from the Regulation: an application made on 1 July 2025 for a progress payment of $1,300,000 attracts a fee of $1,032.44, equivalent to 942.01 fee units.
  2. Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30 (Dalton J) — “must” in s 79(2)(a) and (c) denotes a mandatory requirement, contrasted with the discretionary “may” in s 79(2)(e); the same mandatory character attaches to s 79(2)(d). See also National Management Group Pty Ltd v Biriel Industries Pty Ltd trading as Master Steel & Ors [2019] QSC 219 (Wilson J) on substantial compliance with the s 79(2) identification requirements generally.

Requirements of an adjudication application

Submission limits and the no-new-reasons rule

An adjudication application can be supported by submissions, but the BIF Act regime places real limits on what those submissions are allowed to do. They can argue your case — they cannot rebuild your payment claim from scratch or open up issues you should have flagged earlier.

Section 79(3) BIF Act — submissions accompanying the application

79 Application for adjudication

(3) The adjudication application may be accompanied by submissions relevant to the application.

What “submissions” means

The word is read broadly. It is not confined to legal argument or theory: it covers anything you put forward for the adjudicator to consider, including supporting documentation, statutory declarations and evidence verifying the claim. The leading reading is McDougall J’s judgment in Austruc Constructions Ltd v ACA Developments Pty Ltd; ACA Developments Pty Ltd v Sarlos [2004] NSWSC 131, where his Honour rejected the argument that “submissions” should be limited to advocacy and held that the dictionary meaning of “that which is submitted for decision or consideration” was the better fit. The same approach has been adopted in Queensland.1

Including submissions is also optional. The word in s 79(3) is “may”, and Dalton J in Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30 contrasted that discretionary language with the mandatory “must” in s 79(2)(a) and s 79(2)(c).2 So you can run the application on the face of the payment claim and payment schedule alone, but in practice almost everyone files submissions because they are the only opportunity to argue the case in writing.

The real limit — no new claim, no new reasons

The ceiling on s 79(3) submissions is that they must be “relevant to the application”, and the adjudicator’s jurisdiction is anchored to the payment claim and payment schedule that came before. Submissions that go beyond that — that put the claim on a basis the payment claim never raised — are not protected by s 79(3) and risk taking the determination down with them.

The leading judgment is Einstein J in John Holland v Cardno MBK (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 258. The point his Honour drew out is structural: under the Act’s NSW equivalent of s 82(4), the respondent is locked in to the reasons given in its payment schedule, so if the claimant uses its adjudication submissions to spring a new basis for the claim, the respondent has no way to answer it. His Honour put it this way:

The deploying for the first time in the adjudication application, of supporting documentation will require careful attention and becomes a matter of degree and detail. However in the main I do not see that a respondent which, by reason of insufficient information supplied with the payment claim, is unable to verify that claim, and says as much in the payment schedule… will be otherwise than barred… from including in its adjudication response reasons for withholding payment arising by reference to the later supporting documentation.

Put plainly: a claimant who supplies thin detail in the payment claim, draws a “cannot verify” payment schedule, and then dumps the missing detail into the adjudication submissions will likely lose the determination. The adjudicator either has no jurisdiction to deal with the new material or is obliged to set it aside, because acting on it would be a denial of natural justice to the respondent.3

What submissions can do

The line is between adding to the claim and answering the schedule. Submissions can engage with whatever the respondent raised in its payment schedule, develop the legal and factual arguments supporting the existing claim, attach evidence verifying figures already in the claim, and respond to defences flagged by the respondent. McDougall J in Minister for Commerce v Contrax Plumbing (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 823 confirmed that nothing in John Holland stops a claimant from dealing with issues raised in the payment schedule — the contrary view would force claimants to anticipate and pre-answer every conceivable defence in the payment claim itself, which would defeat the speed and simplicity the Act is built on.

What submissions cannot do is invent a new claim, swap in a fresh contractual basis, or fill a gap that should have been closed in the payment claim. The detail can be expanded; the foundations cannot be re-poured.

What this means on a project

  • Treat the payment claim as the place where the case is set. Itemise the work, identify the contractual basis, and include enough supporting detail that the respondent can sensibly verify or reject each item. If the claim is thin, submissions cannot rescue it.
  • Use s 79(3) submissions to argue your case — not to rebuild it. Address the reasons given in the payment schedule, expand on the legal and factual basis already pleaded, and attach the verifying evidence.
  • If the respondent’s payment schedule raises new defences or new factual contentions, you can answer those head-on in submissions. That is squarely within what s 79(3) is for.
  • Be careful with documents you produce for the first time in submissions. Going from “here are summary calculations” in the claim to “here are the full timesheets and rate cards” in submissions is usually fine. Going from “variation under cl 14” to “quantum meruit because there was no contract” is not.
  • Submissions are optional but in practice essential. Treat them as your one written run at the adjudicator and prepare them with the same care as the payment claim itself.
  1. Austruc Constructions Ltd v ACA Developments Pty Ltd; ACA Developments Pty Ltd v Sarlos [2004] NSWSC 131 (McDougall J), followed in Queensland in Syntech Resources Pty Ltd v Peter Campbell Earthmoving (Aust) Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 293, [32] (Daubney J).
  2. Kangaroo Point Developments MP Property Pty Ltd v RHG Construction Fitout and Maintenance Pty Ltd [2021] QSC 30 (Dalton J), distinguishing the mandatory “must” in s 79(2)(a) and s 79(2)(c) from the discretionary “may” governing submissions. The inclusion of submissions is also described as directory rather than mandatory: Abacus Funds Management Ltd v Davenport [2003] NSWSC 1027, [19] (McDougall J).
  3. John Holland v Cardno MBK (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 258 (Einstein J); Minister for Commerce v Contrax Plumbing (NSW) Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 823 (McDougall J) — reading John Holland as preventing a claimant from putting its claim on a new basis in the adjudication application but not from dealing with issues raised in the payment schedule. See also Probuild Constructions (Aust) Pty Ltd v Shade Systems Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 770 (Emmett AJA) — no denial of procedural fairness where the adjudicated amount differed from the amount claimed in the adjudication application on a fair reading of the claim, schedule, application and response together.

Requirements of an adjudication application

Serve a copy on the respondent

Lodging the adjudication application with the registrar is only half the job. Within four business days of making the application, the claimant must give the respondent a copy of the application and a copy of any submissions that accompanied it. Skip this step, get the timing wrong, or hand over a document that isn’t a true copy, and the adjudicator can be left without jurisdiction — even if everything else has been done properly.

Section 79(4) BIF Act — service on the respondent

(4) The claimant must give the following documents to the respondent within 4 business days after making the adjudication application—

(a) a copy of the adjudication application;

(b) a copy of the submissions, if any, accompanying the application under subsection (3).

“Give” means actually deliver, and the claimant has to prove it

The obligation in s 79(4) is to “give” the documents to the respondent. That word does real work. The Queensland Court of Appeal in Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v SHA Premier Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2019] QCA 177 upheld Ryan J at first instance and confirmed that an adjudication application must be served on the respondent “as soon as possible” after lodgement with the registrar. The reason is structural: the BIF Act runs on what Ryan J called “brutally fast timeframes”, and the respondent’s response clock and the adjudicator’s decision clock both depend on knowing that the application is on foot and what it contains.1

Because s 79(4) is a precondition to a valid adjudication, the claimant carries the onus of proving service. In Equinox Construction Pty Ltd v Henning & Anor [2021] QSC 223, Ryan J declared an adjudication determination void where the claimant relied on having posted the application in a “postage paid” envelope under s 39 / s 39A of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) but could not explain how postage had been calculated or paid, and the respondent denied receipt. Her Honour held that strict proof is required — evidence of attending a post office on a particular date is not enough to establish how posting was effected.2 The practical lesson: if you are going to rely on post, keep the receipts and the tracking; if you are going to rely on email, make sure the address is one the respondent has nominated for service and that you have a record of delivery.

The same theme runs through the cloud-storage cases. In McCarthy v TKM Builders Pty Ltd & Anor [2020] QSC 301, Martin J held that emailing the respondent a Dropbox link to the submissions was not “giving” the submissions for s 79(4) purposes — the respondent does not become aware of the contents of a document merely by being referred to a hosted file. The court required a positive action on the part of the receiver before the document can be said to have been given. The earlier reasoning of McMurdo J in Conveyor & General Engineering Pty Ltd v Basetec Services Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 30 on Dropbox service points the same way.3

What counts as “a copy”

The other recurring fight is over what the claimant has actually handed over. Section 79(4)(a) requires “a copy of the adjudication application”. In Equa Building Services Pty Ltd v KLG Trading Pty Ltd [2021] NSWSC 1674, Stevenson J held that the equivalent NSW provision tolerates degrees of non-compliance, provided the differences between what went to the adjudicator and what went to the respondent do not prejudice the substantive operation of the Act. The claimant in that case served the adjudication application electronically through an online “lockbox” and provided a hard copy to the respondent. The respondent complained that the hard copy was missing a video on a USB, three documents, contained illegible photographs and had mislabelled documents. Stevenson J found those differences trivial:

it would not be consistent with that legislative object for s 17(5) of the NSW SOP Act… to be read as having the effect that the slightest difference between the adjudication application as submitted to the adjudicator and the copy served on the respondent rendered invalid the purported service of the copy adjudication application, let alone an adjudication determination based on it.

The Queensland courts have taken a stricter view where the differences are not trivial. In Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2023] QSC 290, Williams J held that the “QBCC PDF Form” auto-generated as a record after lodgement was not “a copy” of the Electronic Form actually lodged — the differences were “not trivial”, the claimant could have screen-captured each page of the platform when lodging, and there was no evidence that doing so was onerous or inconsistent with the statutory timeframes. The June 2024 amendments to the BIF Act have rendered the Iris Broadbeach approved-form principle nugatory, but the underlying point about giving the respondent a true copy of what was lodged still holds.4

The four-business-day window and how s 102 fits in

The window is four business days after the application is “made” — that is, after the application is lodged with the registrar under s 79(1). The mechanics of service sit in s 102: a notice required to be given under Chapter 3 may be given in any way provided in the construction contract (for example, service by email, where the contract allows it), and that is in addition to, not in substitution for, s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) and any other law about giving notices. Service on an agent is preserved (s 102(3)(a)) and the respondent’s acknowledgement is not required (s 102(3)(b)). For corporations, s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) is also available — leaving or posting to the registered office, or personal delivery to a director resident in Australia.

Two practical points fall out of the s 102 framework. First, service on a post office box is not, by itself, valid service under s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act or s 109X of the Corporations Act, so unless the contract expressly provides for PO box service, do not rely on it. Second, when serving by email, the claimant should be able to show that the address was specified by the respondent for service under the Act — a generic project email or a quote inquiry address may not be enough.

What this means on a project

  • Diarise the four-business-day deadline the moment the application is lodged. The clock runs from lodgement, not from the registrar’s referral to an adjudicator under s 79(5).
  • Give the respondent the same document you lodged with the registrar — not a system-generated receipt or a PDF cover sheet. If you lodged through the QBCC online portal, screen-capture each page of the form before submitting and serve those.
  • Include the submissions. Section 79(4)(b) requires a copy of any submissions that accompanied the application; serving the form alone is not enough where submissions were lodged.
  • Use a service method you can prove. Personal delivery, courier with proof of delivery, or email to an address the respondent has nominated for service under the contract or under the Act all work; bare “postage paid” envelopes without supporting evidence may not.
  • Avoid Dropbox or cloud-link service for the documents themselves. A link is not the document; the respondent has to actually receive the contents within the four-business-day window.
  • If the contract specifies an email address or a method of service, follow it. Section 102(1) lets you use the contractual mechanism, and doing so makes proof of service straightforward.
  • If something goes wrong with service, fix it inside the four-business-day window if you can. Outside it, the only safe path is usually to lodge again — the courts will not rescue an application that was never properly given to the respondent.
  1. Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v SHA Premier Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2019] QCA 177, upholding Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v SHA Premier Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2019] QSC 91 (Ryan J) — service on the respondent must occur “as soon as possible” after lodgement, given the Act’s “brutally fast timeframes”; the QBCC form lodged with the registrar must itself be served on the respondent as part of the application.
  2. Equinox Construction Pty Ltd v Henning & Anor [2021] QSC 223 (Ryan J) — adjudication determination declared void where the claimant could not strictly prove postal service under s 39 / s 39A of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld); evidence of using a “postage paid” envelope and attending a post office on a particular date was not sufficient. Strict proof of service is required because of the statutory objective of quick and efficient resolution.
  3. McCarthy v TKM Builders Pty Ltd & Anor [2020] QSC 301 (Martin J) — service of submissions by Dropbox link was not “giving” under s 79(4); applying Conveyor & General Engineering Pty Ltd v Basetec Services Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 30 (Philip McMurdo J), an email containing a link to a hosted document does not amount to sending the document itself, and a positive act on the part of the receiver is required before the document can be said to have been given.
  4. Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2023] QSC 290 (Williams J) — the QBCC PDF Form was not a “copy” of the Electronic Form for s 79(4) purposes because the differences were “not trivial”; the underlying approved-form principle in this case has been rendered nugatory by the June 2024 amendments to the BIF Act, but the “true copy” requirement on which the service holding turned still applies. See also CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209 (Daubney J) — service on a post office box is not valid service under s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) or s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) absent contractual authorisation.

Requirements of an adjudication response

Within time — standard claims (10 BD)

If the adjudication you’re responding to relates to a standard payment claim, s 83(1) gives you a tight window. You have to lodge the adjudication response within the later of two periods — 10 business days from when the application documents arrive, or 7 business days from notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance. Miss that window and the adjudicator can’t consider your response at all.

Section 83(1) BIF Act — time for making adjudication response (standard claims)

(1) If responding to a standard payment claim, the respondent must give the adjudicator the adjudication response within the later of the following periods to end—

(a) 10 business days after receiving the documents mentioned in section 79(4);

(b) 7 business days after receiving notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance of the adjudication application.

Two clocks, whichever ends later

Section 83(1) sets up two periods running in parallel and tells you to use whichever ends later — the opposite of the s 76 payment-schedule rule, which uses whichever ends first. The 10 business day clock starts when you receive the s 79(4) documents (the application itself, plus the supporting submissions and any annexures the claimant elects to serve on you). The 7 business day clock starts when the adjudicator’s notice of acceptance reaches you. In practice the 10-day clock will normally be the operative one, because applications are usually served on you the same day or close to the day they go to the registrar. But if the registrar takes time to refer the application, or the adjudicator delays accepting, the 7-day clock from notice of acceptance can run later and give you the longer runway. Diary both dates the moment they happen and work to whichever falls later.

For a complex payment claim the periods are different (15 / 12 business days under s 83(2), with a discretionary extension of up to 15 additional business days under s 83(3)). There is no equivalent extension mechanism for a standard claim — under s 83(1) the 10/7 windows are what you’ve got.

Receipt, not service, fixes the clock

The trigger words in s 83(1)(a) and (b) are “receiving” — not “service”. The Act treats those concepts as distinct, and for s 83 purposes it is the date on which the documents (or the notice of acceptance) actually come into your hands that controls. The point matters because a document deposited somewhere you can’t practically retrieve it isn’t “received” just because the sender has done what their service clause requires. In CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209, an adjudicator’s notice of acceptance was sent to a post office box rather than the respondent’s registered office. Daubney J held that the adjudicator had erred in equating deposit of the notice into the post office box with receipt of the notice — service and receipt are not the same thing under the Act, and the clock under the equivalent timing provision did not start running until the notice was actually received.

The corollary of this is that fixing the date of receipt accurately is the single most important thing you can do as a respondent. If the adjudicator gets it wrong — deciding that the notice of acceptance, or the s 79(4) documents, were received earlier than they actually were — your response can be ruled out of time and excluded from consideration, with limited scope to overturn the result. In Rise Constructions v El-Hajj [2019] VSC 818, Digby J dismissed an application to quash an adjudication determination on procedural-fairness grounds where the adjudicator had decided that a notice of acceptance was served on a particular date and, on that finding, treated the adjudication response as having been lodged outside the equivalent of s 83(1)(b). His Honour concluded the adjudicator’s decision on the date of service was made within jurisdiction and on sufficient material, so the consequential decision not to consider the late response stood. The case is a salutary one: once an adjudicator has made a finding on receipt and treated the response as out of time, undoing it on review is hard.

What this means on a project

  • The day you receive the application documents under s 79(4), diary 10 business days. The day you receive the notice of acceptance, diary 7 business days from that date. Work to whichever is the later date — that is your s 83(1) deadline.
  • Keep contemporaneous evidence of when each document was actually received — envelope, email metadata, courier docket, file note. After CMF Projects v Masic, what controls is receipt, not what the claimant’s service clause says was deemed to happen.
  • Don’t serve the response on a registered-office address or PO box that nobody monitors and assume the same indulgence will be extended to you on the inbound documents. If you nominate an address for service, make sure mail to it is actually being collected and dated.
  • Count in business days under the BIF Act definition, not calendar days and not the contract’s “working days”. Public holidays and the Christmas shutdown can compress an already tight 10-day window.
  • Remember s 83(6): once the response is given to the adjudicator, you have 2 business days to give a copy to the claimant. That deadline runs separately from s 83(1) and is easy to overlook in the rush to lodge.
  • If you think the adjudicator has miscalculated your start date, raise it immediately and in writing. After Rise Constructions, an adjudicator’s finding on the date of receipt — even one made on thin evidence — is hard to disturb on review.
  1. CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209 (Daubney J) — adjudicator’s notice of acceptance sent to a PO box rather than the registered office; deposit into the PO box was not equivalent to “receipt” under the equivalent timing provision. The case is the leading Queensland authority for the service / receipt distinction relevant to s 83(1) and is dealt with more fully in the commentary to s 102 (meaning of “receipt” under the Act).
  2. Rise Constructions v El-Hajj [2019] VSC 818 (Digby J) — application to quash an adjudication determination dismissed; adjudicator’s decision on the date a notice of acceptance was served was made within jurisdiction and on sufficient materials, and the consequential decision not to consider the adjudication response (treated as out of time under the Victorian equivalent of s 83(1)(b)) stood.

Requirements of an adjudication response

Within time — complex claims (15 + 15 BD)

If the payment claim being adjudicated is a complex payment claim, you have longer to put your response together — and you can apply for a further extension on top. But the windows still run from fixed events, they’re measured in business days, and getting the trigger date wrong is one of the most common reasons a response is rejected as out of time.

Section 83(2) BIF Act — time for an adjudication response to a complex payment claim

(2) If responding to a complex payment claim, the respondent must give the adjudicator the adjudication response within the later of the following to end—

(a) 15 business days after receiving the documents mentioned in section 79(4);

(b) 12 business days after receiving notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance of the adjudication application.

(3) However, if responding to a complex payment claim, the respondent may apply to the adjudicator for an extension of time, of up to 15 additional business days, to give the adjudication response to the adjudicator.

(4) The application must—

(a) be in writing; and

(b) be made within the later of the following periods to end—

(i) 5 business days after receiving the documents mentioned in section 79(4);

(ii) 2 business days after receiving notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance of the adjudication application; and

(c) include the reasons for requiring the extension of time.

The two starting periods

Section 83(2) gives a complex-claim respondent the later of two periods. The first runs 15 business days from when you receive the s 79(4) documents — that is, the copy of the adjudication application the claimant is required to give you. The second runs 12 business days from when you receive notice of the adjudicator’s acceptance of the application. Whichever date falls later is the deadline for the response itself.

Most of the time the acceptance notice arrives after the s 79(4) documents, which means the 12-business-day limb does the work. But not always — if the adjudicator accepts quickly, the 15-business-day limb running from the s 79(4) documents will be the controlling deadline. Calculate both, then use the later one.

“Receiving” is not the same as “serving”

Both limbs of s 83(2) are measured from the date you received something — not the date the claimant or registrar dispatched it. The Act draws a deliberate distinction between service and receipt, and the courts have insisted on it. In CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209 the adjudicator’s notice of acceptance was sent to a post office box rather than the respondent’s registered office. Daubney J held that the adjudicator had erred by treating deposit of the notice into the post office box as receipt; service of a document and receipt of it by the addressee are two different events, and only the latter starts the s 83 clock.1

there is a distinction between the “service” and the “receipt” of documents under the Act — the deposit of a notice into a post office box is not, without more, receipt of the notice by the addressee.

The practical point is unforgiving. If the adjudicator or claimant treats a dispatch date as the receipt date and your response is rejected as late on that footing, the determination is at risk — but so are you, because you’ll need to prove when the documents actually came into your hands. Diary the actual receipt date for both events the moment they happen, and keep the envelope, email header or courier record that proves it.

The 15 business day extension under s 83(3)

Complex claims attract one further safety valve: under s 83(3) you may apply to the adjudicator for up to 15 additional business days. The application must be in writing, must include the reasons you need the extension, and must itself be made in time — the later of 5 business days after receiving the s 79(4) documents, or 2 business days after receiving the acceptance notice (s 83(4)). Those inner windows are tight, so the extension request usually has to be drafted in parallel with the response itself, not after the response is well under way.

The extension is not automatic. The adjudicator decides whether to grant it and may grant less than 15 days. Reasons that tend to land — volume of claim material, genuine availability problems for key witnesses or experts, a claim that pleads a new theory not foreshadowed in the payment schedule — should be set out specifically. Generic “we need more time” requests are easy to refuse.

Out of time means not considered

If the response is given outside the s 83(2) window (as extended under s 83(5) where applicable), the adjudicator must not consider it: see s 88(2). That is what happened in Rise Constructions v El-Hajj [2019] VSC 818, on the Victorian equivalent of s 83(1)(b). Digby J upheld the adjudicator’s decision that the notice of acceptance had been served on a particular date and that, in consequence, the adjudication response had been delivered outside the statutory time. The adjudicator validly declined to consider the response, and the determination stood.2 The lesson is that arguments about when the clock started are best run before the response is due, by way of correspondence with the adjudicator — not after the determination, when the response has already been excluded.

What this means on a project

  • The day you receive the s 79(4) documents and the day you receive the acceptance notice are the two trigger dates that matter. Diary both immediately and calculate both deadlines — 15 BD from the first, 12 BD from the second — and work to the later one.
  • Capture proof of actual receipt, not dispatch. After CMF Projects, the date the document hit your inbox, post office box pickup or registered office is what counts, and you may need to prove it.
  • If the claim material is voluminous or the schedule was tight, decide within the first 2–5 business days whether to apply for the s 83(3) extension. The inner window for the extension request is shorter than people expect.
  • Make the extension request specific. Identify the volume, the experts you need, the reason the standard window won’t do. Don’t assume the adjudicator will grant the full 15 days — they often grant less.
  • Don’t lodge a late response and hope it gets in. Section 88(2) requires the adjudicator to disregard it, and you’ll be left arguing the determination on the application alone.
  • Once the response is given, remember s 83(6): you must give a copy to the claimant within 2 business days. Build that step into the same diary entry as the response itself.
  1. CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209 (Daubney J — distinction between service and receipt; deposit of notice into a post office box is not, without more, receipt of the notice). For the equivalent commentary on s 102 of the BIF Act dealing with service and receipt of documents, see the discussion accompanying that section.
  2. Rise Constructions v El-Hajj [2019] VSC 818 (Digby J — on the Victorian equivalent of s 83(1)(b); adjudicator’s finding on the date the notice of acceptance was served was made within jurisdiction, and the adjudicator validly declined to consider an adjudication response delivered outside the statutory time).

Requirements of an adjudication response

Confined to reasons in the payment schedule

The adjudication response is your chance to argue your case to the adjudicator — but only on the reasons you already put in the payment schedule. Section 82(4) draws a hard line: any reason for withholding payment that wasn’t in the schedule when you served it is a “new reason” and must be left out. Get this wrong and the adjudicator can require you to resubmit, can disregard the new material, or — if they consider new reasons anyway — the determination can be set aside for jurisdictional error.

Section 82 BIF Act — adjudication response (subsections (2)–(5))

(2) However, the respondent must not give an adjudication response if the respondent failed to give the claimant a payment schedule as required under s 76.

(3) The adjudication response—

(a) must be in writing; and

(b) must identify the adjudication application to which it relates; and

(c) may include the submissions relevant to the response the respondent chooses to include.

(4) However, the adjudication response must not include any reasons (new reasons) for withholding payment that were not included in the payment schedule when given to the claimant.

(5) The adjudicator may require the respondent to resubmit the adjudication response without the new reasons.

Why the rule exists

The point of s 82(4) is fairness on a tight timetable. Once the payment schedule is served, the claimant decides whether to apply for adjudication based on the reasons it discloses. If the respondent could spring fresh reasons in the response, the claimant would be ambushed — and the Act does not give the claimant a right of reply. The leading statement is from Sackar J in State Water Corporation v Civil Team Engineering Pty Ltd [2013] NSWSC 1879, who emphasised the “obvious and powerful policy considerations in ensuring that the respondent discloses all of its reasons for non-payment in its payment schedule”, given that the claimant has no right to reply to whatever the respondent files in adjudication.1

Submissions vs new reasons

The prohibition is on new reasons, not on new submissions. You can elaborate, amplify and add evidence in support of a reason already stated in the schedule — you just can’t introduce a fresh basis for non-payment. McDougall J’s formulation in TQM Design & Construct Pty Ltd v Dasein Constructions Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 1216 has been adopted in Queensland and remains the working test:

[30] a respondent cannot include in its adjudication response any reason for withholding payment that has not been included in its payment schedule. However, it would be open to a respondent to argue, either in greater detail or with perhaps more supporting evidence, reasons that had been advanced… the adjudication response may do more than parrot the terms of the payment schedule.

That distinction explains why expert reports, spreadsheets and other supporting documents can usually be included in the response even if they weren’t attached to the schedule, provided they go to a reason already raised. In Syntech Resources Pty Ltd v Peter Campbell Earthmoving (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2011] QSC 293, Daubney J held that “submissions” in the BCIPA equivalent of s 82(3)(c) was not to be construed narrowly and could include relevant documentation in support — the spreadsheets in that case were “explanatory” of reasons already in the schedule, not new reasons.2 The same principle let in the expert report in Trysams Pty Ltd v Club Constructions (NSW) Pty Ltd [2007] NSWSC 941, where the report was “foreshadowed” in the schedule and “did not represent any shift from that position”.3 Where new material does cross the line into raising a fresh reason, the position flips: in Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307, Philip McMurdo J was unable to find that an EOT expert report did not raise a reason not in the schedule, and treated it accordingly.4

“Reasons for withholding payment” is read broadly

Don’t look for a narrow reading of what counts as a “reason for withholding payment”. In John Holland Pty Ltd v Roads & Traffic Authority of New South Wales & Ors [2007] NSWCA 19, Hodgson JA held that any basis on which the respondent says it doesn’t have to pay an amount — non-performance of work, defective work, set-offs, cross-claims, contractual caps, statutory limits, “or indeed any other suggested justification” — is a reason for withholding payment and must be in the schedule. Anything else, his Honour said, would “do violence to the language ‘withholding payment for any reason’, and be contrary to the plain purpose” of the prohibition. Philip McMurdo J adopted that reasoning in Wiggins Island for the Queensland Act.5

Jurisdictional points are different

Section 82(4) bars new reasons for withholding payment. It does not bar arguments going to whether the adjudicator has jurisdiction at all — for instance, that there is no reference date, that the claim is out of time, or that no construction contract exists. Those points couldn’t sensibly have been put in a payment schedule and can be raised in the response.6 The line between a “jurisdictional” point and a substantive reason has to be watched: in Watkins Contracting Pty Ltd v Hyatt Ground Engineering Pty Ltd [2018] QSC 65, Brown J accepted that detailed termination submissions in the response were an elaboration of a “no reference date” reason already in the schedule, and noted in obiter that even if they had been new, they were directed at jurisdiction and the s 82(4) bar would not have applied.

The consequences of getting it wrong

Where an adjudicator considers new reasons that should have been excluded, the affected part of the determination is liable to be declared void. In Acciona Agua Australia Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 133, Bond J found jurisdictional error where the adjudicator accepted set-off bases tied to alleged breaches of an implied contractual term that had “no mention” in the payment schedule; part of the determination was declared void and the balance left to stand. Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2021] QSC 92 (Martin J) is to similar effect, where the adjudicator applied a contractual clause raised for the first time in the response.7 But characterisation is for the adjudicator in the first instance: in Baguley Build Pty Ltd v Olcon Concrete & Construction Pty Ltd [2025] QSC 126, Copley J confirmed that whether reasons are “new” is a matter for the adjudicator to determine, and an error in mischaracterising submissions is an error within jurisdiction, not a ground for setting the determination aside.8

What this means on a project

  • Treat the payment schedule as your final pleading on reasons. Anything you might want to argue at adjudication — defective work, descope, set-off, cross-claim, contractual cap, time bar, valuation dispute — needs to be on the face of the schedule. There is no second bite.
  • You can amplify and support those reasons in the response with extra detail, calculations, photos and expert reports. You cannot use the response to add a basis for non-payment that the schedule didn’t flag.
  • Before lodging the response, run a line-by-line comparison with the schedule. For each reason in the response, ask: is this reason — not just this evidence — in the schedule? If not, take it out, or be prepared to defend it as either an elaboration or a jurisdictional point.
  • Expert reports and spreadsheets are usually fine if they go to a reason already raised. Check that the schedule actually flagged the underlying reason — e.g., if you want to run an EOT-based set-off in the response, the schedule needs to have raised the EOT issue, not just a generic “we dispute the claim” line.
  • Jurisdictional objections (no reference date, no construction contract, claim out of time, multiple applications) can be run in the response even if they weren’t in the schedule. Plead them clearly as jurisdictional, not as substantive reasons for withholding payment.
  • If you are the claimant and the response includes new reasons, draw the adjudicator’s attention to s 82(4) and ask that the response be resubmitted under s 82(5) without them, or that the new reasons be disregarded.
  1. State Water Corporation v Civil Team Engineering Pty Ltd [2013] NSWSC 1879, [63] (Sackar J); John Holland Pty Ltd v Walz Marine Services Pty Ltd & Ors [2011] QSC 39 (Margaret Wilson J) — the issues are defined by the payment claim and payment schedule; the response cannot be used as a vehicle to advance reasons the schedule omitted; GW Enterprises Pty Ltd v Xentex Industries Pty Ltd [2006] QSC 399, [36] (Lyons J) — “it is imperative that any reasons for withholding payment must be raised in the payment schedule or they cannot be raised at all”; Brambles Australia Ltd v Davenport [2004] NSWSC 120 (Einstein J) — appropriate to take a “reasonably strict” approach; reliance on cl 24.1 in the response, where the clause was not identified in the schedule, was rightly disregarded.
  2. Syntech Resources Pty Ltd v Peter Campbell Earthmoving (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2011] QSC 293, [25] (Daubney J) — spreadsheets supporting wet-hire and overpayment reasons were “explanatory”, not new; failure to consider them was a breach of an essential requirement and jurisdictional error.
  3. Trysams Pty Ltd v Club Constructions (NSW) Pty Ltd [2007] NSWSC 941, [49], [56] (Hammerschlag J) — expert report “foreshadowed” in the schedule was a submission, or part of a submission, duly made; failure to consider it was a substantial denial of natural justice; see also Owners Strata Plan 61172 v Stratabuild Ltd [2011] NSWSC 1000 (Macready AJ) on the “reasons” / “submissions” distinction.
  4. Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307, [65] (Philip McMurdo J).
  5. John Holland Pty Ltd v Roads & Traffic Authority of New South Wales & Ors [2007] NSWCA 19 (Hodgson JA), agreeing with Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Luikens [2003] NSWSC 1140, [65]–[68] (Palmer J); applied in Queensland in Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307, [52] (Philip McMurdo J); Thiess Pty Ltd & John Holland Pty Ltd v Civil Works Australia Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 187; [2011] 2 Qd R 276 (Daubney J) — no jurisdictional error in declining to consider contractual clauses raised only in the response.
  6. Rail Corporation (NSW) v Nebax Constructions [2012] NSWSC 6 (McDougall J) — the equivalent of s 82(4) speaks of “reasons for withholding payment” and does not preclude a respondent raising in the response a challenge to the adjudicator’s jurisdiction; Olympia Group (NSW) Pty Ltd v Hansen Yuncken Pty Ltd [2011] NSWSC 165, [11] (Ball J) to the same effect; Bega Valley Shire Council v Kenpass Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 399, [49] (Nixon J) — whether reasons are “new” is for the adjudicator and an error in interpreting the payment schedule is not reviewable.
  7. Acciona Agua Australia Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 133 (Bond J) — partial declaration of voidness for considering new reasons under s 88(3)(b) tied to s 82(4); Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2021] QSC 92 (Martin J) — s 82(3)(c) must be read subject to s 82(4); s 88(3)(b) breached once an adjudicator considers a matter s 82 does not permit, regardless of the outcome; Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd (No. 2) [2021] QSC 231 (Freeburn J) — revised determination partly void on the same principle (engagement of replacement contractor not raised in schedule); Castle Constructions Pty Ltd v Napoli Excavations and Civil Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 348 — failure to consider a submission duly made on the date of termination amounted to jurisdictional error; BuiltCom Construction Pty Ltd v VSD Investments Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 250 (Peden J) — adjudicator entitled to refuse to consider site-inspection documents not referenced in the payment schedule, applying EnerMech Pty Ltd v Acciona Infrastructure Projects Australia Pty Ltd (2024) 115 NSWLR 56.
  8. Baguley Build Pty Ltd v Olcon Concrete & Construction Pty Ltd [2025] QSC 126 (Copley J), applying Civmec Electrical & Instrumental Pty Ltd v Southern Cross Electrical Engineering Ltd [2019] QSC 300 (Mullins J) — failure to consider properly made submissions may be jurisdictional error, but mischaracterising submissions is an error within jurisdiction; see also Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v Robotic Steel Fab Pty Ltd (2023) 16 QR 336 (Applegarth J).

Requirements of an adjudication response

Form and content

An adjudication response is the respondent’s one written run at the adjudicator. The Act keeps the form simple but locks the content tightly to what the respondent already said in its payment schedule. Get either part wrong and the response is either inadmissible or, if the adjudicator acts on it, the determination is exposed.

Section 82 BIF Act — adjudication response

82 Adjudication response

(1) After being given notice of an adjudicator’s acceptance of an adjudication application under section 81, the respondent may give the adjudicator a response to the adjudication application (the adjudication response).

(2) However, the respondent must not give an adjudication response if the respondent failed to give the claimant a payment schedule as required under section 76.

(3) The adjudication response—

(a) must be in writing; and

(b) must identify the adjudication application to which it relates; and

(c) may include the submissions relevant to the response the respondent chooses to include.

(4) However, the adjudication response must not include any reasons (new reasons) for withholding payment that were not included in the payment schedule when given to the claimant.

(5) The adjudicator may require the respondent to resubmit the adjudication response without the new reasons.

Form — the easy bit

The form requirements in s 82(3)(a)–(b) are minimal. The response must be in writing and it must identify the adjudication application it answers. There is no prescribed form: an emailed PDF identifying the application reference, the parties, and the payment claim it relates to is enough. The harder rule is the threshold one in s 82(2) — if the respondent failed to give a payment schedule under s 76, it is barred from filing any response at all. There is nothing the respondent can do at the adjudication stage to recover from a missed payment schedule.

Submissions can amplify, not parrot, the schedule

Section 82(3)(c) lets the response include “submissions relevant to the response”. Read together with s 82(4), that means submissions can develop, explain and evidence the reasons already in the payment schedule — but they cannot introduce reasons that were not. The leading reading is McDougall J in TQM v Dasein [2004] NSWSC 1216, considering the NSW equivalent:

[30] If it were intended that an adjudication response should do no more than mirror, word for word, a payment schedule, then there is little point to the ability… for a respondent to lodge an adjudication response… the adjudication response may do more than parrot the terms of the payment schedule.

The same point runs through the documents cases. An expert report or spreadsheet that supports a reason already given in the payment schedule is properly part of the response, even if the document itself was not attached to the schedule: Syntech Resources Pty Ltd v Peter Campbell Earthmoving (Aust) Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 293; Trysams Pty Ltd v Club Constructions (NSW) Pty Ltd [2007] NSWSC 941. In Syntech, Daubney J held that “submissions” in the equivalent provision was not to be construed narrowly and could include relevant supporting documentation. In Trysams, Hammerschlag J went further: where the report was foreshadowed in the payment schedule and provided in support of the position already taken, the adjudicator’s refusal to consider it was a substantial denial of natural justice that voided the determination.1

The no-new-reasons rule in s 82(4)

The hard limit is s 82(4). Any reason for withholding payment that was not in the payment schedule is a “new reason” and cannot be raised in the response. “Reasons for withholding payment” is read broadly: it captures everything from non-performance and bad work to set-offs, cross-claims and contractual or statutory bars on payment.2 If the respondent does not propose to pay an amount in the claim, whatever justifies that decision is a reason for withholding payment and has to be in the schedule.

The Queensland authorities are unforgiving. In Acciona Agua Australia Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 133, Bond J declared part of an adjudication void where the adjudicator accepted set-off bases and an implied-term breach argument that had no “mention” in the payment schedule. In Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2021] QSC 92, Martin J held that s 69(c) requires the respondent to include all of its reasons in the schedule — not just the ones prompted by the claim — and that once the adjudicator considers a matter prohibited by s 82, s 88(3)(b) is breached “whether or not the consideration leads to a particular decision”. The drafting consequence is straightforward: every basis the respondent might want to run at adjudication has to be visible on the face of the schedule.

Quantum versus “other” reasons — no distinction

It is sometimes argued that reasons going only to quantum (why the scheduled amount is less) sit outside s 82(4) because the section talks about “reasons for withholding payment”. The appellate answer is no. Hodgson JA in John Holland Pty Ltd v Roads & Traffic Authority of New South Wales [2007] NSWCA 19 rejected any narrow reading: if the respondent is not paying an amount for any reason, those are reasons for withholding payment, and any other view would do violence to the section’s purpose of preventing late submissions in a tight statutory timetable. Philip McMurdo J followed the same approach for Queensland in Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 307.

Jurisdictional points are different

The s 82(4) bar applies to reasons for withholding payment, not to challenges to the adjudicator’s jurisdiction. A respondent can raise jurisdictional points in the response — for example, that there was no reference date, or that the application was out of time — even if those points were not in the schedule, because they could not have been taken there. In Watkins Contracting Pty Ltd v Hyatt Ground Engineering Pty Ltd [2018] QSC 65, Brown J also accepted that detailed submissions on the grounds of contract termination were a permissible elaboration of the schedule’s “nil — no reference date because the contract has been terminated” reasoning, and that even if they had been new, an adjudicator must consider jurisdictional points regardless of s 82(4).3

What this means on a project

  • If you missed the payment schedule, you cannot file a response. There is no fix at the adjudication stage. Make sure the schedule is in — even a thin one — before you worry about anything else.
  • Treat the payment schedule as the gateway. Every reason you might want to argue at adjudication has to be visible there: set-offs, cross-claims, defects, contractual bars, statutory bars, quantum points and termination grounds.
  • You can amplify in the response. Adding an expert report, a spreadsheet of calculations, or a detailed legal argument that supports a reason already given in the schedule is fine and often essential.
  • You cannot smuggle. Swapping in a fresh contractual clause, a new defect category, or a different termination basis will be struck down as a “new reason”, and if the adjudicator runs with it the determination is exposed under s 88(3)(b).
  • Quantum-only points are still reasons for withholding payment. A new rate, a new measurement, a new deduction calculation — if it was not in the schedule, it cannot come in via the response.
  • Jurisdictional objections are different. You can run them in the response even if the schedule did not foreshadow them, because they go to whether the adjudicator should be there at all.
  1. Syntech Resources Pty Ltd v Peter Campbell Earthmoving (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2011] QSC 293 (Daubney J) — “submissions” in the equivalent provision is not to be read narrowly and includes relevant supporting documentation; spreadsheets supporting reasons already given in the schedule had to be considered. Trysams Pty Ltd v Club Constructions (NSW) Pty Ltd [2007] NSWSC 941, [49], [56] (Hammerschlag J) — expert report foreshadowed in the schedule was part of submissions duly made; failure to consider it was jurisdictional error. See also Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307 (Philip McMurdo J); Owners Strata Plan 61172 v Stratabuild Ltd [2011] NSWSC 1000 (Macready AJ).
  2. John Holland Pty Ltd v Roads & Traffic Authority of New South Wales & Ors [2007] NSWCA 19 (Hodgson JA) — broad reading of “reasons for withholding payment”, covering non-performance, bad work, set-offs, cross-claims and contractual or statutory bars. Followed in Queensland in Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 307, [52] (Philip McMurdo J). See also John Holland Pty Ltd v Walz Marine Services Pty Ltd & Ors [2011] QSC 39 (Margaret Wilson J); Brambles Australia Ltd v Davenport [2004] NSWSC 120 (Einstein J); GW Enterprises Pty Ltd v Xentex Industries Pty Ltd [2006] QSC 399 (Lyons J); Thiess Pty Ltd & John Holland Pty Ltd v Civil Works Australia Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 187; [2011] 2 Qd R 276 (Daubney J); State Water Corporation v Civil Team Engineering Pty Ltd [2013] NSWSC 1879, [63] (Sackar J); Castle Constructions Pty Ltd v Napoli Excavations and Civil Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 348; BuiltCom Construction Pty Ltd v VSD Investments Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 250 (Peden J).
  3. Watkins Contracting Pty Ltd v Hyatt Ground Engineering Pty Ltd [2018] QSC 65 (Brown J); Rail Corporation (NSW) v Nebax Constructions [2012] NSWSC 6 (McDougall J); Olympia Group (NSW) Pty Ltd v Hansen Yuncken Pty Ltd [2011] NSWSC 165, [11] (Ball J) — s 20(2B) (the NSW equivalent of s 82(4)) does not prevent a respondent from raising jurisdictional grounds in the response. On whether reasons are “new” or merely an elaboration, that is a matter for the adjudicator: Baguley Build Pty Ltd v Olcon Concrete & Construction Pty Ltd [2025] QSC 126 (Copley J), applying Civmec Electrical & Instrumental Pty Ltd v Southern Cross Electrical Engineering Ltd [2019] QSC 300 (Mullins J) and Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v Robotic Steel Fab Pty Ltd (2023) 16 QR 336 (Applegarth J); Bega Valley Shire Council v Kenpass Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 399, [49] (Nixon J). For the “part-void” consequence where the adjudicator does consider a new reason, see Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd (No. 2) [2021] QSC 231 (Freeburn J).

Requirements of an adjudication response

Copy to the claimant within 2 BD

Lodging the adjudication response with the adjudicator isn’t the end of the job. Section 83(6) imposes a separate, free-standing obligation: you must give the claimant a copy of the response within 2 business days of giving it to the adjudicator. It’s a short, mechanical step at the back end of the response process — but missing it leaves you exposed.

Section 83(6) BIF Act — copy to the claimant

(6) If the respondent gives the adjudicator an adjudication response under this section, the respondent must give a copy of the response to the claimant not more than 2 business days after giving the response to the adjudicator.

What the obligation actually requires

Three things follow from the way s 83(6) is drafted. First, the trigger is the act of giving the adjudication response to the adjudicator — not the date the adjudicator acknowledges receipt, and not the date the application was received. Diary the 2 business day deadline from the moment your response goes out the door. Second, what you give the claimant is a copy of the response — the same document, with the same submissions and the same annexures. You can’t hand over a stripped-down version or hold back attachments you’d prefer the claimant didn’t see. Third, the window is measured in business days under the BIF Act definition, so weekends and public holidays don’t count.

The obligation only bites if you actually give an adjudication response. If you elect not to respond — which is sometimes a tactical call where the claim is hopeless on its face or where the schedule already covers the field — s 83(6) is never enlivened.

Service and receipt are different concepts under the Act

Section 83(6) speaks of “giving” a copy. The Act distinguishes between service of a document and the addressee’s receipt of it, and the distinction has been the subject of judicial comment in adjacent contexts. In CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209, an adjudicator’s notice of acceptance had been sent not to the respondent’s registered office but to a post office box. Daubney J drew a clear line between service and receipt, and held that the adjudicator had erred in equating deposit of the notice into the post office box with receipt of it.1 The takeaway for s 83(6) is straightforward: don’t cut the timing fine by relying on a method of delivery that puts the document somewhere in the postal system but not actually in the claimant’s hands. Email to a known address used in the claim, or hand delivery against signature, is the safer course.

What happens if you miss it

Section 83(6) is mandatory in form (“must give”), but the Act doesn’t spell out the consequence of breach. The closest analogue in the case law is Rise Constructions v El-Hajj [2019] VSC 818, where Digby J considered the equivalent Victorian timing provision. The challenge there was the other way around — the adjudicator had refused to consider an out-of-time response — but Digby J’s reasoning confirmed that the response timing rules in the Victorian Act (the equivalent of s 83(1)(b) here) are taken seriously, and that a response given outside the prescribed period can validly be disregarded.2 The point for s 83(6) is more limited: the claimant deprived of a timely copy may have natural justice arguments downstream, particularly if the adjudicator proceeds to determination without the claimant having had a fair opportunity to know what was put against it. Treat the 2 business day window as hard.

What this means on a project

  • The moment your response goes to the adjudicator, diary the 2 business day deadline for the claimant copy. Don’t wait for the adjudicator’s acknowledgement — the clock runs from when you give the response, not from any reply.
  • Send the claimant the same response and the same annexures you sent the adjudicator. A pared-back version is not a “copy”.
  • Use a method of service that puts the document in the claimant’s actual possession — email to the address used on the payment claim, or hand delivery against receipt. Don’t rely on a post office box or generic registered office address if you can avoid it.
  • Keep proof of what you sent and when. A sent-mail record with the response and annexures attached is usually enough; if you hand-deliver, get the package signed for.
  • If you decide not to lodge an adjudication response at all, s 83(6) doesn’t apply — but make sure that decision is recorded internally so no one second-guesses it later.
