  • Count in business days under the Act, not calendar days and not your contract’s definition of working days. Public holidays in the project’s jurisdiction matter.
  1. CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209 (Daubney J — distinction between service and receipt under the predecessor Act; deposit into a post office box is not receipt). See also s 102 of the BIF Act on the meaning of receipt.
  2. Rise Constructions v El-Hajj [2019] VSC 818 (Digby J — adjudicator validly declined to consider an adjudication response made outside the time required by s 21(1)(b) of the Victorian Act, the equivalent of s 83(1)(b) of the BIF Act).

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

The adjudicator must be eligible

An adjudicator’s decision is only valid if the person making it is eligible to adjudicate the application. Eligibility is dealt with at the front end of the adjudication process — before the adjudicator accepts the referral — but it stays live throughout, because a disqualifying conflict that emerges later still bites on the decision. Two disqualifying matters are spelt out in the Act: being a party to the contract, and having a conflict of interest prescribed by regulation.

Section 80 BIF Act — when adjudicator ineligible to adjudicate

An adjudicator is not eligible to adjudicate an adjudication application if the adjudicator—

(a) is a party to the construction contract to which the application relates; or

(b) has a conflict of interest as prescribed by regulation.

Party to the contract

The first limb is straightforward. An adjudicator who is a party to the construction contract under which the payment claim arises cannot adjudicate the dispute about that claim. In practice this is rarely an issue on its face — an adjudicator on the QBCC register is unlikely to be a contracting party to the project — but it can surface in less obvious ways. If the adjudicator’s firm or related entity is the contracting party (for example, a consultancy practice that signed the head contract), or if the adjudicator personally signed an associated agreement that forms part of the contractual matrix, the s 80(a) bar is engaged. The disqualification is automatic; it does not depend on actual bias or on the parties objecting.

Conflict of interest prescribed by regulation

The second limb depends on the regulation. The Building Industry Fairness (Security of Payment) Regulation prescribes the categories of conflict that disqualify. Typical prescribed conflicts include a current or recent professional or commercial relationship with a party, a financial interest in the outcome, or a personal relationship that would cause a fair-minded observer to apprehend bias. Because the conflict categories sit in the regulation, they can be amended without amending the Act — so check the current Regulation rather than relying on a remembered list.

Eligibility under s 80 sits alongside the broader common-law obligation on an adjudicator to act impartially and to afford the parties natural justice. The two overlap but are not identical: s 80 sets out the categorical bars to taking the appointment; the natural-justice obligations govern how the adjudicator must conduct the process once on foot. A breach of either will expose the decision to challenge.

Timing — when eligibility is tested

Eligibility is tested at the point the adjudicator accepts the application under s 81, but it is a continuing requirement. If a disqualifying matter emerges mid-adjudication — say, the adjudicator realises a party is a former client, or a prescribed conflict only becomes apparent once the submissions arrive — the adjudicator must withdraw. A decision delivered after a s 80 disqualification has crystallised is liable to be set aside on judicial review for jurisdictional error, because the Act conditions the adjudicator’s authority to decide on their being eligible.

What this means on a project

  • When the registrar refers the application and the adjudicator notifies acceptance, check who has been appointed. A quick conflict check on the adjudicator — firm name, recent engagements, any prior dealings with either party — can save a costly judicial review later.
  • If you spot a s 80 issue, raise it in writing with the adjudicator and the registrar promptly. The adjudicator should withdraw and the registrar will refer the application to another adjudicator. Sitting on the point and using it later as a backup ground of challenge is risky — courts may treat delay as waiver of the conduct ground, even if the jurisdictional bar itself cannot be waived.
  • Distinguish s 80 ineligibility from a natural-justice complaint about how the adjudicator has run the process. The former goes to whether the adjudicator could decide at all; the latter goes to whether they decided fairly. They have different remedies and different evidentiary requirements.
  • If you are challenging a decision after the fact on s 80 grounds, you are in jurisdictional-error territory — not an appeal on the merits. Frame the challenge as a Supreme Court application for a declaration that the decision is void, and act quickly.
  • Check the current Building Industry Fairness (Security of Payment) Regulation for the prescribed conflict categories before alleging a s 80(b) breach. The list is set by regulation and may have been updated since you last looked.
  1. Building Industry Fairness (Security of Payment) Act 2017 (Qld), s 80. The two limbs — party to the contract (s 80(a)) and prescribed conflict of interest (s 80(b)) — are cumulative grounds of ineligibility, either of which is sufficient to disqualify.
  2. Building Industry Fairness (Security of Payment) Regulation 2018 (Qld) prescribes the conflict-of-interest categories for the purposes of s 80(b). Consult the current Regulation for the operative list.

Requirements of an adjudicator's decision

The adjudicator must accept the referral

Once the registrar refers your adjudication application to a nominated adjudicator, that adjudicator has a short, statutory window to decide whether to take the job. Without acceptance under s 81, no-one is appointed and there is nothing for an adjudicator to decide. Acceptance is the formal hinge between “your application has been referred” and “an adjudicator has been appointed to decide it”.

Section 81 BIF Act — Appointment of adjudicator

(1) If the registrar refers an adjudication application to an adjudicator for a decision, the adjudicator must, unless the adjudicator has a reasonable excuse, accept or reject the referral within 4 business days after the referral is made.

(2) The adjudicator may accept the referral by serving written notice of the acceptance on the claimant, the respondent and the registrar.

(3) The adjudicator may reject the referral by notifying the registrar of the refusal.

(4) If the adjudicator rejects the referral or does not accept it within the time required under subsection(1)—

(a) the registrar must refer the adjudication application to another adjudicator within 4 business days after becoming aware of the refusal or failure; and

(b) no fee is payable for referring the adjudication application to another adjudicator.

(5) The other adjudicator may accept the referral by serving written notice of the acceptance on the claimant, the respondent and the registrar.

(6) The other adjudicator may reject the referral by notifying the registrar of the refusal.

(7) On accepting a referral of an adjudication application under subsection(1) or (5), an adjudicator is taken to have been appointed to decide the application.

How acceptance works

The mechanism is short and prescriptive. The registrar refers the application to an adjudicator. That adjudicator then has 4 business days to either accept by serving written notice on the claimant, the respondent and the registrar, or reject by notifying the registrar. Doing nothing within the 4 business days has the same effect as a rejection — the registrar must re-refer the application to another adjudicator within 4 business days of becoming aware of the failure, and no further fee is payable for that re-referral.

The point of acceptance is also the point of appointment. Section 81(7) provides that, on accepting the referral, the adjudicator “is taken to have been appointed to decide the application”. Until that moment the nominated adjudicator has no decision-making role; from that moment, the clock starts running on the decision deadlines in s 85.

Service of the notice of acceptance — agents and the post

Two practical issues recur around s 81: who can sign and send the notice of acceptance, and what happens if a party says they never received it. Both were addressed in Peekhrst Pty Ltd v Wallace & Anor [2007] QSC 159, which considered the equivalent provision under the predecessor Act. Douglas J held that an adjudicator may authorise an agent to accept on his or her behalf:

There is nothing in the section to suggest that an adjudicator may not authorise an agent to accept on his behalf. Given the speed with which these claims are to be dealt with, it may well be the case from time to time that a nominated adjudicator may not be available immediately to sign and serve a notice of acceptance personally. That does not mean that those steps may not be taken by an agent.

His Honour added that, even if the use of an agent had constituted a breach of the Act, “there is nothing to suggest that the use of an agent in circumstances like this should lead to invalidity”.1 The takeaway for project teams is that you should not waste time arguing that a notice of acceptance is invalid simply because it was signed or sent by an authorised nominee or staff member of the adjudicator’s office — that argument is unlikely to get you anywhere.

The second recurring issue is non-receipt. In Randhawa v Serrato [2009] NSWSC 170 the adjudicator’s notice of acceptance was served by post. The plaintiff led evidence that it had not received the notice. The Court held that “if delivery is not disproved the fact of non-receipt does not displace the result that the delivery is deemed to be effected at the time at which it would have taken place in the ordinary course of the post” (citing Fancourt v Mercantile Credits (1983) 154 CLR 87 at 97). The plaintiff’s evidence went only to non-receipt, not to non-delivery, and the challenge to the validity of the determination failed.2 Bare assertions of “we never got it” will not, on their own, unwind the appointment.

What this means on a project

  • Once your application is referred, watch for the notice of acceptance from the adjudicator within 4 business days. The clock for the decision under s 85 starts on acceptance, not on referral.
  • If you don’t see a notice of acceptance within the 4 business day window, contact the registrar. Section 81(4) requires re-referral to another adjudicator within 4 further business days, with no additional fee.
  • Don’t challenge a notice of acceptance just because it was signed by someone in the adjudicator’s office rather than the adjudicator personally — Peekhrst closes that argument off.
  • Keep registered post receipts and email delivery confirmations. If you ever need to dispute receipt of an acceptance notice, you’ll need evidence going to non-delivery, not just non-receipt — Randhawa is clear about that.
  • Diary the date of the notice of acceptance: it’s the date of appointment under s 81(7) and the trigger for the s 85 decision-time calculation that follows.
  1. Peekhrst Pty Ltd v Wallace & Anor [2007] QSC 159 (Douglas J — an adjudicator may authorise an agent to accept the referral on his or her behalf; use of an agent does not lead to invalidity), referring to Project Blue Sky v Australian Broadcasting Authority (1998) 194 CLR 355, 390–391 at [93] on the consequences of statutory non-compliance.
  2. Randhawa v Serrato [2009] NSWSC 170 — postal service of notice of acceptance; non-receipt does not displace deemed delivery in the ordinary course of post; following Fancourt v Mercantile Credits (1983) 154 CLR 87 at 97.

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

Decided within time

Once an adjudicator is appointed, the clock starts running. The Act fixes hard deadlines for the decision — 10 business days for a standard payment claim, 15 for a complex one — with a limited mechanism in s 86 to extend. In Queensland, unlike NSW or Victoria, missing those deadlines is fatal: the decision is void.

Section 85 BIF Act — time for deciding adjudication application

(1) Subject to section 86, an adjudicator must decide an adjudication application no later than—

(a) for a standard payment claim—10 business days after the response date; or

(b) for a complex payment claim—15 business days after the response date.

(2) The response date is—

(a) if the adjudicator is given an adjudication response under section 83—the day on which the adjudicator receives the response; or

(b) if the respondent is prevented from giving the adjudicator an adjudication response under section 82(2)—the last day on which the respondent could have given the adjudicator an adjudication response under section 83 had it not been prevented from doing so under section 82(2); or

(c) otherwise—the last day on which the respondent could give the adjudicator an adjudication response under section 83.

(3) An adjudicator must not decide an adjudication application before the end of the period that the respondent may give an adjudication response to the adjudicator under section 83, unless—

(a) the adjudicator decides he or she does not have jurisdiction to adjudicate the application; or

(b) the adjudicator decides the application is frivolous or vexatious.

Working out the response date

The deadline runs from the “response date”, not from the day the application was lodged. Section 85(2) gives three possibilities. If the respondent does file an adjudication response under s 83, the response date is the day the adjudicator receives it. If the respondent is locked out from responding (because no payment schedule was given on time, under s 82(2)), the response date is the last day the respondent could otherwise have responded. If no response is filed but the respondent could have, it’s the last day they could have done so. From whichever of those dates applies, count 10 business days for a standard claim or 15 for a complex one.

Extensions under s 86

Section 86 is the only lawful way to push past the s 85(1) deadline. The parties can agree in writing — either before or after the maximum period expires — that the adjudicator has additional time. For complex claims only, the adjudicator can take an extra 5 business days unilaterally if the parties have failed to agree. Any extension must be notified to the registrar within 4 business days. An adjudicator cannot manufacture an extension by, for example, imposing a tight deadline on the parties to object and treating silence as consent: in Allpro v Micos [2010] NSWSC 453, McDougall J held that s 86’s equivalent “requires agreement. That means real or actual agreement”, and an adjudicator is not entitled to purport to impose agreement of the parties.1

Queensland: out of time means void

The Queensland position diverges from the NSW and Victorian authorities. Interstate, courts have consistently held that the time limit is directory, not jurisdictional — an out-of-time decision remains valid.2 Queensland has gone the other way. In Galaxy Developments Pty Ltd v Civil Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd t/a CCA Winslow & Ors [2020] QSC 51, Dalton J held that an adjudication decision handed down outside the s 85 timeframe is void. Her Honour reasoned that the legislature’s “particularity and precision” in drafting the Queensland time limits, combined with the existence of s 86 as a deliberate extension mechanism, signalled that compliance with s 85 was essential. The Court of Appeal affirmed that view in Civil Contractors (Aust) Pty Ltd v Galaxy Developments Pty Ltd & Ors; Jones v Galaxy Developments Pty Ltd & Ors [2021] QCA 10, distinguishing the NSW and Victorian authorities on the basis that the BIF Act’s wording is materially different.

the changes to the current Queensland legislation were made after all the extant interstate decisions on similar provisions.

That distinction matters in practice: if you are dealing with a BIF Act adjudication, do not rely on the NSW or Victorian line of cases. The decision must be made — and, after Karam Group, communicated to the parties — within the s 85 window or it is a nullity.

The decision must also be communicated within time

It is not enough for the adjudicator to have written the decision before the deadline. In Karam Group Pty Ltd as Trustee for Karam (No. 1) Family Trust v HCA Queensland Pty Ltd & Ors [2022] QSC 290, Williams J held that an adjudicator who purportedly made their decision in time, but did not notify the parties until after the s 85(1) period had expired, had failed to make the decision within time. The decision was invalid. The s 88 obligations — including notice to the parties — form part of what it means to “decide” the application within the statutory period.3

What this means on a project

  • From the day the adjudicator is given the response (or the day the response period closes), diary 10 business days for a standard claim or 15 for a complex claim. That’s your decision deadline absent an extension.
  • If the adjudicator asks for more time, your agreement must be in writing under s 86(1) — an email is fine, silence is not. Do not let an adjudicator assume consent from non-response.
  • For a complex claim, the adjudicator can take an extra 5 business days without your agreement. Build that into your cashflow planning.
  • Watch the notification step. Under Karam Group, the decision is not “made” until it has been communicated to the parties. If the adjudicator’s reasons land on day 11 of a standard claim, the decision is void in Queensland even if the document is dated earlier.
  • If the decision lands out of time and you are the respondent facing enforcement, take advice immediately about a declaration that the decision is void on Galaxy Developments. The Queensland position is settled at appellate level and is materially more favourable to respondents than the position interstate.
  • If you are the claimant and the adjudicator has run out of time, your remedy is to withdraw and re-lodge under s 87 within the statutory window — you cannot rely on a late decision.
  1. Allpro Building Services Pty Ltd v Micos Architectural Division Pty Ltd [2010] NSWSC 453 (McDougall J — an adjudicator cannot impose deemed consent to an extension of time; s 86 requires real or actual agreement).
  2. MPM Constructions Pty Ltd v Trepcha Constructions Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 103 (McDougall J — time requirement not jurisdictional under NSW Act); Cranbrook School v JA Bradshaw Civil Contracting [2013] NSWSC 430 (McDougall J — following MPM); MT Lewis Estate Pty Ltd v Metricon Homes Pty Ltd [2017] NSWSC 1121 (Hammerschlag J); Ian Street Developer Pty Ltd v Arrow International Pty Ltd [2018] VSC 14 (Riordan J), affirmed [2018] VSCA 294 (Maxwell P, McLeish and Niall JJA — non-compliance with VIC Act time limit does not invalidate the determination); contrast Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2013] QCA 386 (Queensland Court of Appeal under predecessor BCIPA — obligation to decide within time not relieved unless extension mechanism engaged).
  3. Karam Group Pty Ltd as Trustee for Karam (No. 1) Family Trust v HCA Queensland Pty Ltd & Ors [2022] QSC 290 (Williams J — failure to notify parties of decision before expiry of s 85(1) period meant decision not made within time and was invalid). See also Demex Pty Ltd v Marine Civil Contractors Pty Ltd & Anor [2022] QSC 141 (Bowskill CJ — in obiter, noting the BIF Act position may differ from NSW where the decision is dated in time but communicated late).

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

Procedural fairness / natural justice

An adjudicator must give each side a fair hearing on the issues that drive the outcome. A “substantial” denial of natural justice is jurisdictional error and renders the determination void. This is one of the most commonly run challenges in the Supreme Court — usually framed as “the adjudicator decided the case on a basis we never got to address”.

Section 84(2)(b) and s 88(2) BIF Act — the statutory hooks

84 Adjudication procedures

(2) For a proceeding conducted to decide an adjudication application, an adjudicator…

(b) may ask for further written submissions from either party and must give the other party an opportunity to comment on the submissions…

88 Adjudicator’s decision

(2) In deciding an adjudication application, the adjudicator is to consider the following matters only—

… (c) the payment claim to which the application relates, together with all submissions, including relevant documentation, that have been properly made by the claimant in support of the claim;

(d) the payment schedule, if any, to which the application relates, together with all submissions, including relevant documentation, that have been properly made by the respondent in support of the schedule…

Where natural justice comes from in this Act

There is no free-standing “fair hearing” rule grafted onto the BIF Act from the general law. The content of procedural fairness is fixed by the statutory scheme itself. The leading statement is Hodgson JA’s in Brodyn Pty Ltd t/as Time Cost and Quality v Davenport [2004] NSWCA 394 — the requirement to give notice to the respondent and an opportunity to make submissions (now reflected in s 84 and s 88(2) of the BIF Act) carries with it a legislative intention that “no substantial denial of the measure of natural justice that the Act requires to be given” is essential to validity.1 Basten JA put the same point a different way in Coordinated Construction Co Pty Ltd v Climatech (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2005] NSWCA 229: the relevant content of procedural fairness is ascertained by reference to the specific statutory scheme; where the scheme is inconsistent with what general-law fairness might otherwise require, the general law gives way.

Two consequences follow. First, the timetable is short and the adjudicator is not obliged to chase further submissions on every loose end — that would defeat the speed-of-payment purpose of the Act. Second, the duty that does exist is real, and it bites hardest where the adjudicator strays beyond the issues actually joined between the parties.

The “substantial” threshold

Not every procedural slip vitiates a determination. The denial must be “substantial” — meaning material to the outcome. The canonical Queensland formulation is Applegarth J’s in John Holland Pty Ltd v TAC Pacific Pty Ltd [2009] QSC 205; [2010] 1 Qd R 302:

The adjective “substantial” has been used in the relevant authorities to capture the principle that the opportunity denied was material, namely that the matter about which the adjudicator did not provide an opportunity to be heard was a point upon which the adjudicator based his or her decision and was significant to the actual determination… even if the Court is satisfied that there has been a denial of natural justice, relief may be denied if it can be shown that compliance with the requirements of natural justice could have made no difference to the outcome. It is probably sufficient in this regard for the applicant for relief to show that there were substantial submissions that, as a matter of reality and not mere speculation, might have persuaded the adjudicator to change his or her mind.

The applicant therefore does not have to prove it would have won had it been heard. It only has to show “something to be put that might well persuade the adjudicator to change his or her mind”.2 The NSW Court of Appeal in Demex Pty Ltd v McNab Building Services Pty Ltd [2023] NSWCA 261 has more recently restated the same point as a “substantial practical injustice” test, and that has been picked up in Queensland.3

The classic trigger: a decision on a basis neither party contended

By far the most common scenario is the adjudicator deciding the dispute on a route neither side argued. In Hanson Construction Materials Pty Ltd v Brolton Group Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 1641 the adjudicator picked an alternative reference date that no one had contended for; the determination was set aside, and the Court of Appeal affirmed.4 The Queensland authorities are to the same effect — an adjudicator who is “minded” to decide a question on a basis not put by either party must put the proposed basis to the parties first.5

The most directly useful Queensland decision for project-level fact patterns is Ingeteam Australia Pty Ltd v Susan River Solar Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 30. There Applegarth J declared an adjudication determination void in part for jurisdictional error because the adjudicator had decided two limbs of a licensing point (whether the work was unlicensed building work, and whether the contractor had intended to comply with s 42 of the QBCC Act) without putting either limb to the contractor. His Honour found denial of procedural fairness in two respects: the adjudicator failed to give Ingeteam an opportunity to refute the evidence and submissions Susan River had put forward on unlicensed building work, and he reached a conclusion about Ingeteam’s intention to comply with s 42 without giving Ingeteam any opportunity to make submissions on it. The errors were also material — there was a real possibility the result would have been different if Ingeteam had been heard.

Other recurring trigger points

  • Material received from one party but not shown to the other. Direct correspondence with the adjudicator (a side email, a late attachment) that the other party never sees is a classic denial: J Hutchinson Pty Ltd v Cada Formwork Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 63 (the “10 June email”); TQM Design & Construct Pty Ltd v Dasein Constructions Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 1216.
  • Unilaterally disregarding a document and not flagging it. If the adjudicator decides that material is inadmissible or non-compliant, the parties should be told, otherwise they lose the chance to argue the point: Syntech Resources Pty Ltd v Peter Campbell Earthmoving (Aust) Pty Ltd [2011] QSC 293.
  • Failure to engage with a fundamental part of a party’s case. Reasons that simply do not grapple with a central submission can amount to a denial: Suprima Bakeries Pty Ltd v Australian Weighing Equipment Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 998.
  • Going outside the requested scope of further submissions. Where an adjudicator asks for further submissions under s 84(2)(b) but then receives material going beyond the request, and does not tell the parties whether that material will be used, the procedure may not result in justice being seen to be done: Minesco Pty Ltd v Anderson Sunvast Hong Kong Ltd [2017] VSC 299.

Where the challenge usually fails

Adjudicators are not required to invite further submissions on every issue, and the courts will not “sift finely” through reasons looking for slips. Failing to give lengthy or elaborate reasons, on its own, is not a denial of natural justice: see Future Power Developments Pty Ltd v TJ & RF [2017] NSWSC 232, picking up Clyde Bergemann Senior Thermal Pty Ltd v Varley Power Services Pty Ltd [2011] NSWSC 1039. A challenge will also fail where the “new” basis the adjudicator adopted was implicit in what one of the parties had already put,6 or where the disputed point was simply not material to the outcome.7 And there is no denial in failing to consider a submission that the adjudicator has, within jurisdiction, decided was not duly made.8

What this means on a project

  • If you are reading the determination and find the adjudicator decided a key point on a route neither side ran, that is your first natural-justice flag. Identify the exact passage and check whether the basis appears anywhere in the application, response or any further submissions.
  • Be ready to articulate, with specificity, what you would have said had you been given the chance. Vague assertions of unfairness fail. Draft the “missing” submission you would have made and show it would have at least had a real prospect of changing the result.
  • If the adjudicator asks for further written submissions under s 84(2)(b), make sure both sides see whatever the other side puts in. Side-channel emails to the adjudicator that the other party doesn’t see are an obvious risk — copy everything.
  • If you suspect material was received from your opponent that you weren’t shown, raise it with the adjudicator before the decision issues. Silence weakens the later argument that you were denied a hearing.
  • Don’t over-pitch the challenge. Reasoning errors, misunderstandings of evidence and short reasons are not, on their own, denials of natural justice. The argument that travels is “the adjudicator decided the case on a point we never got to address, and here is what we would have said”.
  1. Brodyn Pty Ltd t/as Time Cost and Quality v Davenport [2004] NSWCA 394; (2004) 61 NSWLR 421, [55]–[57] (Hodgson JA). Approved in Queensland in Northbuild Construction Pty Ltd v Central Interior Linings Pty Ltd and applied repeatedly under BCIPA / BIFSOPA.
  2. Trysams Pty Ltd v Club Constructions (NSW) Pty Ltd [2008] NSWSC 399, [45] (McDougall J), quoted in Northbuild Construction Sunshine Coast Pty Ltd v Beyfield Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 80, [38] (Philip McMurdo J).
  3. Demex Pty Ltd v McNab Building Services Pty Ltd [2023] NSWCA 261 — only a “significant departure” from procedural fairness leading to “substantial practical injustice” will invalidate; followed in Rogers Construction Group Pty Ltd v Mirage Interiors & Construction Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 1344 (Stevenson J).
  4. Hanson Construction Materials Pty Ltd v Brolton Group Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 1641 (Ball J); affirmed Brolton Group Pty Ltd v Hanson Construction Materials Pty Ltd [2020] NSWCA 63 (Gleeson JA, Meagher and Payne JJA agreeing).
  5. BM Alliance Coal Operations Pty Ltd v BGC Contracting Pty Ltd [2012] QSC 346, [52] (Applegarth J) — the “minded to” principle. Earlier Queensland applications: Vis Constructions Ltd v Cockburn [2006] QSC 416 (Jones J); David & Gai Spankie & Northern Investments Holdings Pty Ltd v James Trowse Constructions Pty Ltd (No 2) [2010] QSC 166 (P McMurdo J); Ostwald Bros Pty Ltd v Jaylon Pacific Pty Ltd [2016] QSC 240 (Burns J); Livingstone Shire Council v EarthTEC Pty Ltd [2018] QSC 271 (Lyons SJA).
  6. Watkins Contracting Pty Ltd v Hyatt Ground Engineering Pty Ltd [2018] QSC 65 (Brown J); Rogers Construction Group Pty Ltd v Mirage Interiors & Construction Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 1344 (Stevenson J).
  7. Ball Construction Pty Ltd v Conart Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 124 (Douglas J) — non-material departures do not vitiate; Rhomberg Rail Australia Pty Ltd v Concrete Evidence Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 755 (Ball J) — reasonableness standard applied to materiality; Acciona Infrastructure Australia Pty Ltd v Chess Engineering Pty Ltd [2020] NSWSC 1423 (Henry J).
  8. A-Civil Aust Pty Ltd v Ceerose Pty Ltd [2024] NSWCA 7 (Mitchelmore JA, Lemming and White JJA agreeing); Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No 2) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 49 (Payne JA) — the duty to consider a submission is not the same as a duty to deal with it correctly.

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

Decide only what was applied for

An adjudicator’s job under s 88(1) is narrow and specific: decide the amount of the progress payment (if any), the date it became or becomes payable, and the rate of interest on it. The adjudicator can’t go higher than the claim, can’t go lower than what the respondent already conceded in the payment schedule, and can’t award amounts the claimant never put in the payment claim. Step outside that envelope and the decision is liable to be set aside for jurisdictional error.

Section 88 BIF Act — adjudicator’s decision (extract)

(1) An adjudicator is to decide—

(a) the amount of the progress payment, if any, to be paid by the respondent to the claimant (the adjudicated amount); and

(b) the date on which any amount became or becomes payable; and

(c) the rate of interest payable on any amount.

(2) In deciding an adjudication application, the adjudicator is to consider the following matters only—

(a) the provisions of this chapter and, to the extent they are relevant, the provisions of the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991, part 4A;

(b) the provisions of the relevant construction contract;

(c) the payment claim to which the application relates, together with all submissions, including relevant documents, that have been properly made by the claimant in support of the claim;

(d) the payment schedule, if any, to which the application relates, together with all submissions, including relevant documents, that have been properly made by the respondent in support of the schedule;

(e) the results of any inspection carried out by the adjudicator of any matter to which the claim relates.

What s 88(1) obliges the adjudicator to decide

Three things, no more and no less. The adjudicated amount, the date it falls due, and the interest rate. The amount can be zero — a determination that no amount is to be paid is still a determination, and is no less valid than one that awards money.1 What the adjudicator can’t do is decline to make the call. Refusing to determine the application, or treating the question as “premature”, is a failure to perform the statutory task and produces a void determination.

That was the position in Parrwood Pty Ltd v Trinity Constructions (Aust) Pty Ltd [2020] NSWCA 172. The adjudicator there said in the reasons that he “decline[d] from determining” any entitlement under the payment claim because a finding would be premature. Instead of recording the adjudicated amount as nil or $0, he recorded “No amount”. The NSW Court of Appeal held that the adjudicator had failed to do the “very thing” the Act required, breached the equivalent of s 88(1)(a), and the determination was void for jurisdictional error. The Court also confirmed that the claimant did not have to wait for a court ruling before treating the determination as void and lodging a fresh adjudication — otherwise the claimant would be denied the chance to exercise its statutory rights.

The floor and ceiling: claimed amount and scheduled amount

The amount the adjudicator can award is bounded at both ends. At the top, by the amount actually claimed in the payment claim. At the bottom, by the amount the respondent admitted in the payment schedule. The leading articulation is Blue J’s in Maxcon Constructions Pty Ltd v Vadasz (No 2) [2017] SASCFC 2, [228]:

[228] the adjudicator had no jurisdiction in the circumstances to determine the amount of the progress payment at less than $141,163.55 or more than $204,864.55. Because of the manner in which the parties framed the issues in the payment claim, payment schedule, adjudication application and Maxcon’s submissions in response, the adjudicator was required to determine two discrete issues, namely the retention sum claim and the administration charges setoff.

The reasoning is that the BIF Act process is adversarial: the claimant defines the upper limit of the dispute through the payment claim, and the respondent defines the lower limit through what it concedes in the payment schedule. An adjudicator who awards more than was claimed has exceeded the jurisdiction conferred by the application; an adjudicator who awards less than was admitted has done the same in the other direction. Either way, it’s jurisdictional error.2

The same logic explains why an adjudicator can’t reach into amounts that weren’t in the payment claim at all. In Samsung C&T Corporation v Loots [2016] WASC 330, an adjudicator awarded $32.4 million on the basis that it had been previously certified but wrongly set off. Beech J held the amount had not been claimed in the payment claim before the adjudicator, so the adjudicator had directed his attention to the merits of competing claims and set-offs that lay outside the application — jurisdictional error, and the determination was set aside.3

There is a refinement worth flagging. In Binah Constructions Pty Ltd v PTMG Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 872, Ball J held that where the adjudicated amount exceeds the figure claimed in the payment claim itself but matches an amount the respondent had already certified by default in respect of a prior payment claim, that’s not a jurisdictional defect — the claimant was entitled to roll up previously certified amounts into the application, and it was open to the adjudicator to find those amounts were not in dispute and were payable.4 The principle in Maxcon is about awarding more than the claimant put forward across the application as a whole; it is not a mechanical comparison of the headline figure on the face of the payment claim.

What this means on a project

  • Whatever you put as the claimed amount in the payment claim is the absolute ceiling of any adjudicated amount. If you under-claim, the adjudicator can’t fix it for you — you live with the figure you served.
  • Whatever you certify in the payment schedule is the absolute floor. Don’t schedule a figure you’re not prepared to pay if the adjudication goes against you on every other issue; the adjudicator can’t go below it.
  • If you’re a respondent and the adjudicated amount is higher than the payment claim amount, look closely at how the figure was built up. Maxcon and Samsung suggest jurisdictional error; Binah says it depends on whether the extra is a previously certified amount that was implicit in the application. Get advice quickly — the s 101 enforcement clock won’t pause while you think about it.
  • If you’re a claimant and the adjudicator records “no amount” or refuses to decide because the question is premature, treat that as a Parrwood failure and consider lodging a fresh adjudication application under s 87. You don’t need to wait for a court declaration first.
  • The adjudicator must also fix the date the amount became payable and the interest rate. If those are missing from the decision, that’s a s 88(1) gap on its face — raise it before you pay.
  1. Alucity Architectural Produce Supply Pty Ltd v Australian Solutions Centre; Alucity Architectural Produce Supply Pty Ltd v Paul J Hick [2016] NSWSC 608 (Hammerschlag J — “A determination that no amount is to be paid because the claim is invalid is no less a determination than one which determines that no money is payable for some other reason.”).
  2. Creative Building Services Pty Ltd v TIO Air Conditioning Pty Ltd [2016] ACTSC 367 (Mossop AsJ — capacity to determine the amount of a progress payment is “confined, at the upper end, by the claimed amount”; awarding more than the claimed amount is jurisdictional error).
  3. Samsung C&T Corporation v Loots [2016] WASC 330 (Beech J — adjudicator who awards an amount not claimed in the payment claim before the adjudicator exceeds his statutory function and commits jurisdictional error).
  4. Binah Constructions Pty Ltd v PTMG Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 872 (Ball J — where an amount above the headline payment-claim figure represented a sum previously certified by default in respect of an earlier payment claim, the adjudicator did not exceed jurisdiction in awarding it; the application was dismissed).

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

Consider only what is permitted

Section 88(2) does two jobs at once. It tells the adjudicator what they are allowed to look at — and, by saying “the following matters only”, it locks the door on everything else. Just as importantly, the courts read the subsection as mandating consideration of every item on that list. A failure to engage with one of those items, where the point is material, is jurisdictional error and will void the decision.1

Section 88(2) BIF Act — matters the adjudicator must consider

88 Adjudicator’s decision

(2) In deciding an adjudication application, the adjudicator is to consider the following matters only—

(a) the provisions of this chapter and, to the extent they are relevant, the provisions of the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991, part 4A;

(b) the provisions of the relevant construction contract;

(c) the payment claim to which the application relates, together with all submissions, including relevant documents, that have been properly made by the claimant in support of the claim;

(d) the payment schedule, if any, to which the application relates, together with all submissions, including relevant documents, that have been properly made by the respondent in support of the schedule;

(e) the results of any inspection carried out by the adjudicator of any matter to which the claim relates.

What “consider” actually means

The obligation to “consider” each matter in s 88(2) is not a tick-box exercise. It is a substantive duty to engage. The canonical formulation is McDougall J’s in Laing O’Rourke Australia Construction v H & M Engineering & Construction [2010] NSWSC 818, repeatedly quoted in Queensland:

As a matter of plain English, the obligation to “consider” something requires that it be given attention, or looked at on its merits… the obligation to consider various matters imposed by s 22(2) of the Act should be read… as requiring an active process of intellectual engagement. It may be thought that this imposes a substantial burden on adjudicators. That may be so; but… the parties to an adjudication are entitled to have the adjudicator’s consideration, in the sense that I have explained, of the case that each of them brings.

That standard has been picked up in Queensland in QCLNG Pipeline Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd v Consolidated Contracting Company Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2011] QSC 292, where Peter Lyons J held that a failure to consider submissions of real significance is a failure to comply with a basic and essential requirement of the Act, and the resulting decision is invalid.2 The duty is real, but it is not unlimited — an adjudicator does not have to deal with every contention explicitly, and a failure to mention a particular submission in the reasons is not, by itself, proof that it was not considered.3

The five matters — and how they fit together

Paragraphs (a) and (b) put the legal framework in front of the adjudicator: the BIF Act itself (and the relevant licensing provisions of the QBCC Act), and the terms of the construction contract between the parties. Paragraphs (c) and (d) put the parties’ cases in front of the adjudicator: the payment claim and the claimant’s properly-made submissions, the payment schedule and the respondent’s properly-made submissions. Paragraph (e) covers anything the adjudicator has seen on a site inspection. Together, these are the universe of permissible inputs.

The shape of paragraphs (a) and (b) versus (c) and (d) matters. The legal framework (a)–(b) is not gated by what the parties have argued: an adjudicator is entitled (and in some cases obliged) to take account of provisions of the Act or the contract that they consider of real relevance, even if a party did not raise them in submissions properly made. Hodgson JA explained the point in The Minister for Commerce v Contrax Plumbing (NSW) Pty Ltd & Ors [2005] NSWCA 142, refining it in John Holland Pty Ltd v Roads & Traffic Authority of New South Wales & Ors [2007] NSWCA 19: the threshold is awareness of the matter plus a belief that it is of real relevance to issues arising under paragraphs (a) or (b).4 Paragraphs (c) and (d), by contrast, are tied to what the parties put forward in their submissions properly made; an adjudicator cannot range outside the dispute the parties have presented.

What “properly made” means — and who decides

Paragraphs (c) and (d) only oblige the adjudicator to consider submissions that have been “properly made”. Whether a submission has been properly made — on time, in the form required, and not introducing reasons that should not be there — is a matter the legislature entrusts to the adjudicator to decide. A wrong call on whether a submission was properly made is generally an error within jurisdiction, not a jurisdictional error: see Civmec Electrical & Instrumental Pty Ltd v Southern Cross Electrical Engineering Limited & Ors [2019] QSC 300 (Mullins J).5 But there is a flip side: if the adjudicator concludes within jurisdiction that a submission was not properly made, that is the end of any natural-justice complaint about not considering it.6

Failing to consider a submission that was properly made

The clearest Queensland illustration of how s 88(2) bites is Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 16. The adjudicator decided that Descon was entitled to release of the retention and dismissed Iris’s submission under clause 5.7(a) of the contract. Wilson J held that the adjudicator’s decision was affected by jurisdictional error on two grounds. The first was procedural — the adjudicator had decided the case partly on clause 5.2, which neither party had relied on. The second is the more directly relevant for present purposes:

Wilson J held that, even putting natural justice to one side, the adjudicator had not “considered” Iris’s submissions about the effect of clause 5.7(a), as mandated by s 88(2)(d) of the BIF Act. Engaging with the submission required actually grappling with what it said, not merely noting that the respondent had “a claim” without further engagement. The decision was declared void in part. The case is a useful reminder that s 88(2) is not just a list of permitted inputs — it is a list of things the adjudicator has to do, and a failure to do them on a material point will not survive judicial review.7

What this means on a project

  • Build your application or response so the adjudicator cannot miss the key points. Identify each contractual provision and each submission you rely on, with cross-references back to the payment claim or schedule, so that engagement (or non-engagement) is visible on the face of any later determination.
  • If you are the respondent, get your reasons into the payment schedule. Anything you raise for the first time in the adjudication response is at risk of being treated as not properly made under s 88(2)(d) and excluded from consideration — and the adjudicator’s view on that point is generally not reviewable.
  • If you are reading a determination and a key submission is simply absent from the reasons, that is a flag — but not, on its own, a winning ground. You need to be able to show that the omission reflects a failure of intellectual engagement, not a permissible decision to give the submission no weight or to treat it as not properly made.
  • Don’t over-stretch a “failure to consider” argument. After Niclin Constructions and Martinus Rail, the courts will not infer a failure to consider from silence in the reasons unless the omission is significant and unexplained.3
  • Where a contractual provision or BIF Act provision is plainly engaged but neither party has run it, expect the adjudicator to be entitled to raise it — and watch for a request for further submissions. If you don’t get one before the adjudicator decides on a basis neither side ran, you are into natural-justice territory rather than s 88(2) proper.
  1. Ware Building Pty Ltd v Centre Projects Pty Ltd & Anor (No 1) [2011] QSC 424, 6 (Fryberg J) — s 88(2) (formerly s 26(2) BCIPA) limits what an adjudicator may consider but mandates the consideration of those listed matters; Livingstone Shire Council v EarthTEC Pty Ltd and Ors [2018] QSC 271 (Lyons SJA) — adjudication decision declared void where the adjudicator refused to consider properly made submissions about amounts paid into court under the Subcontractors’ Charges Act 1974 (Qld).
  2. QCLNG Pipeline Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd v Consolidated Contracting Company Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2011] QSC 292, [40] (Peter Lyons J) — failure to consider submissions of significance is a failure to comply with an essential requirement of the Act.
  3. Niclin Constructions Pty Ltd v Robotic Steel Fab Pty Ltd & Anor [2023] QSC 218 (Applegarth J) — failure to refer in reasons to all submissions made on an issue does not, by itself, mean those submissions were not considered; Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No.2) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 49, [67]–[69] (Payne JA) — the duty to consider a submission is different from a duty to deal with it correctly; Taringa Property Group Pty Ltd v Kenik Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 298 (Hindman J) — an adjudicator must engage with submissions but is not required to address every contention explicitly.
  4. The Minister for Commerce v Contrax Plumbing (NSW) Pty Ltd & Ors [2005] NSWCA 142, [33]–[36] (Hodgson JA); John Holland Pty Ltd v Roads & Traffic Authority of New South Wales & Ors [2007] NSWCA 19, [48]–[49] (Hodgson JA, Beazley JA agreeing) — the “real relevance” threshold for considering matters under paragraphs (a) and (b) not raised in submissions duly made; followed in State Water Corporation v Civil Team Engineering Pty Ltd [2013] NSWSC 1879 (Sackar J). Considered in Queensland in Theiss Pty Ltd & John Holland Pty Ltd v Civil Works Australia Pty Ltd [2010] QSC 187; [2011] 2 Qd R 276 (Daubney J).
  5. Civmec Electrical & Instrumental Pty Ltd v Southern Cross Electrical Engineering Limited & Ors [2019] QSC 300, [38] (Mullins J) — an error in concluding that parts of an adjudication response should be rejected as not properly made is an error within jurisdiction and not reviewable; Ceerose Pty Ltd v A-Civil Aust Pty Ltd [2023] NSWCA 215 (Payne JA) — an error in identifying a submission as “duly made” is not jurisdictional; applied in Lipman Pty Ltd v A-Civil Aust Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 865 (Nixon J) and BuiltCom Construction Pty Ltd v VSD Investments Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 250 (Peden J).
  6. John Holland Pty Ltd v Roads & Traffic Authority of New South Wales & Ors [2007] NSWCA 19, [63] (Hodgson JA, Beazley JA agreeing) — no denial of natural justice in failing to consider a submission the adjudicator has, within jurisdiction, decided was not duly made; followed in Bega Valley Shire Council v Kenpass Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 399 (Nixon J).
  7. Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 16, [27], [63] (Wilson J). See also Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2021] QSC 92 (Martin J) — inference drawn that an adjudicator did not have regard to a properly submitted document, contradicting the respondent’s case, where the adjudicator failed expressly to refer to it; York Property Holdings Pty Ltd v Tomkins Commercial & Industrial Builders Pty Ltd (Treston J) — failure to consider material concessions and submissions on deductions for defective work was jurisdictional error under s 88(2) BIFSOPA.

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

Not consider what is prohibited

Section 88(2) tells the adjudicator what to consider. Section 88(3) is the matching prohibition — the closed list of things the adjudicator must not consider. In practice the live issue is almost always the second limb: a respondent putting up “new reasons” in the adjudication response that were not in the payment schedule. If the adjudicator runs with those new reasons, the determination is exposed to being set aside for jurisdictional error.

Section 88(3) BIF Act — matters the adjudicator must not consider

88 Adjudicator’s decision

(3) However, the adjudicator must not consider any of the following—

(a) an adjudication response, to which the adjudication application relates, that was not given to the adjudicator within the time required under section 83;

(b) a reason included in an adjudication response to the adjudication application, if the reason is prohibited from being included in the response under section 82.

Two prohibitions, one practical issue

Subsection (3)(a) is mechanical. If the response is late under s 83, the adjudicator cannot look at it. There is little case law because the timing question rarely produces a contestable issue — the response either landed in time or it didn’t.

Subsection (3)(b) is where the work happens. It locks together with s 82, which prohibits the respondent from including in the adjudication response any reason for withholding payment that was not included in the payment schedule. Read s 82 and s 88(3)(b) together and the rule is simple: the payment schedule fixes the universe of reasons. The adjudication response can develop, refine and support those reasons, but it cannot add new ones — and if it does, the adjudicator is forbidden from acting on them.

The Queensland authority on “new reasons”

The clearest Queensland statement of the rule is Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2021] QSC 92, where Martin J held that while an adjudicator may consider the provisions of the construction contract, the adjudicator must not consider a reason for withholding payment that relies on a particular contractual provision if that reason is a “new reason” not included in the payment schedule. The point came back in Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd (No. 2) [2021] QSC 231, where Freeburn J declared parts of an adjudication determination void in part because the adjudicator had appeared to acknowledge a respondent’s submission raising a new defence for withholding payment that was not in the payment schedule, and then proceeded as if it were properly before him.1

The most direct recent application of s 88(3) itself is Chevron Park Pty Ltd v Groupline Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2024] QSC 202. Chevron sought judicial review on the basis that the adjudicator had failed to consider payments made before and after the date of the payment claim. Treston J dismissed the application, holding that the adjudicator could not consider the subsequent payments precisely because of s 88(3): the adjudication response cannot include new reasons for withholding payment that were not raised in the payment schedule, and the adjudicator must not act on any such reason if it is included.

What counts as a “new reason”

The line between a new reason and further development of an existing reason is where the cases bite. In Miller v LMG Building Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 995, Ball J observed that the legislation “draws a distinction between the reasons for refusing a payment claim and the material that might be advanced in support of those reasons”, and said the dividing line turns on whether the claimant was sufficiently on notice of the reason that it could, if it wished, address it in the application. On the facts, the adjudicator had erred in excluding material produced after the payment schedule because the material did not introduce a new reason — it explained the overpayment that had already been identified in the schedule.2

The Queensland Supreme Court took the same approach in Paladin Projects Pty Ltd v Visie Three Pty Ltd & Ors [2024] QSC 230, where Williams J held that determining whether new reasons have been introduced requires assessing whether the narrative provided in the adjudication response aligns with the scope of the shorthand response in the payment schedule — there is no prohibition on articulating, in fuller form, reasons that were already there. And jurisdictional error only arises if the adjudicator actively considered the new reasons; brief reference without substantive engagement may not cross the line.3

Why getting this wrong is fatal

If the adjudicator does take a new reason into account, that is a textbook ground of jurisdictional error — the adjudicator has considered something the statute requires to be ignored. In Acciona Agua Australia Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 133, Bond J declared an adjudication determination void on that very basis: the adjudicator had acted in excess of jurisdiction by improperly considering new reasons in the adjudication response, and the reasons given were “nothing more than a vote one way”, revealing a failure of intellectual engagement with the issues.4 The same logic underpins the appellate gloss in NSW — the prohibition on a respondent travelling beyond the payment schedule is “a jurisdictional pre-condition” essential to the “pay now, argue later” scheme of the Act.5

What this means on a project

  • If you are the respondent, treat the payment schedule as the closing list of reasons. Anything you want the adjudicator to engage with later must be there in some form — even in shorthand — before the application is made.
  • If you are the claimant reading an adjudication response, mark up every reason and check it back against the payment schedule. Anything genuinely new is a candidate to be flagged to the adjudicator under s 82 / s 88(3)(b).
  • The line is reason vs material. Adding documents, calculations, expert input or further explanation that supports an existing reason in the payment schedule is fine. Adding a defence, a contractual clause or a setoff that was never mentioned is not.
  • For the respondent, the safest practice is to plead reasons in the payment schedule at a level of generality that captures the case you actually intend to run — without misleading the claimant about the basis for non-payment. A bare “Nil” with no reasons gives you nothing to develop later.
  • If you are challenging a determination on this ground, the question is twofold: was a new reason in the response, and did the adjudicator actively consider it? A passing reference is unlikely to be enough; engagement that affected the result is.
  1. Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd & Anor [2021] QSC 92 (Martin J); Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd v Aniko Constructions Pty Ltd (No. 2) [2021] QSC 231 (Freeburn J) — adjudicator obscured the “active process of intellectual engagement” by acknowledging a new defence not in the payment schedule and then focusing on the claimant’s evidence.
  2. Miller v LMG Building Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 995 (Ball J) — jurisdictional error to refuse material that explained, rather than introduced, a reason already in the payment schedule. See also Bega Valley Shire Council v Kenpass Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 399 (Nixon J) — where the payment schedule contained no “reasons for withholding payment” at all, anything in the response disputing quantum was treated as new.
  3. Paladin Projects Pty Ltd v Visie Three Pty Ltd & Ors [2024] QSC 230 (Williams J).
  4. Acciona Agua Australia Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 133 (Bond J).
  5. Lipman Pty Ltd v A-Civil Aust Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 865 (Black J), citing Shade Systems Pty Ltd v Probuild Constructions (Aust) Pty Ltd (No 2) (2016) 95 NSWLR 157; [2016] NSWCA 379 and Brodyn Pty Ltd (t/as Time Cost & Quality) v Davenport (2004) 61 NSWLR 421; [2004] NSWCA 394 — the equivalent of s 88(3) is a jurisdictional pre-condition essential to the “pay now, argue later” scheme.

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

Give written reasons

The adjudicator’s decision must be in writing and must include the reasons for the decision — unless both parties have agreed otherwise. This is not a formality. Reasons that don’t actually disclose how the adjudicator got to the result, or that side-step a critical issue altogether, can amount to jurisdictional error and bring the determination down. There is also a discretion in s 88(4) to disregard parts of an application or response that breach the page-limit and word-limit rules in the Regulation.

Section 88(4)–(5) BIF Act — the adjudicator’s decision: form and reasons

(4) Also, the adjudicator may disregard an adjudication application or adjudication response to the extent that the submissions or accompanying documents contravene any limitations relating to submissions or accompanying documents prescribed by regulation.

(5) The adjudicator’s decision must—

(a) be in writing; and

(b) include the reasons for the decision, unless the claimant and the respondent have both asked the adjudicator not to include the reasons in the decision.

Section 88(4) — the page-limit discretion

Section 88(4) lets the adjudicator disregard parts of the application or response that go beyond the limits prescribed by the Regulation on the length of submissions or accompanying documents. It is a discretion, not a duty, and it operates only to the extent of the contravention. The practical message is to keep your application or response within the prescribed envelope — if you don’t, the adjudicator may simply ignore the offending pages, which is rarely the result you want.

Section 88(5) — writing and reasons

The decision has to be written, and it has to include the adjudicator’s reasons for the decision unless both parties expressly opt out. The reasons must address the matters the adjudicator was actually required to decide under s 88(1) — the adjudicated amount, the date for payment and the rate of interest — and the issues the parties joined under s 88(2). They don’t need to be lengthy or elaborate. As Sackar J put it in Avopiling (NSW) Pty Ltd v Menard Bachy Pty Ltd [2012] NSWSC 1466, picked up with approval by Bond J in the Queensland decision JAG Projects Qld Pty Ltd v Total Cool Pty Ltd [2015] QSC 229:

There is clearly no requirement for the reasons to be lengthy elaborate or detailed. The reasons should be sufficient to show that the adjudicator has engaged actively with the dispute and dealt with it in a way that is reasoned, not perverse, arbitrary or capricious… A statement of reasons should be looked at as a whole.

The other side of that coin is the warning that a “mere indication” of having read the material, or a bare statement of satisfaction, “may not, subject to viewing the determination as a whole, survive as adequate reasons”.1 Reasons given under tight statutory timetables, often by non-lawyers, will be read fairly and as a whole — not parsed with a fine-tooth comb.2

What “include the reasons” really requires

The leading appellate authority on s 88(5)(b) is Cockram Construction Ltd v Fulton Hogan Construction Pty Ltd [2018] NSWCA 107. The NSW Court of Appeal held that asking whether an adjudicator’s reasons were “adequate” or “sufficient” raises a false issue: the statutory question is simply whether the adjudicator gave their reasons for their determination. In Cockram the adjudicator had treated a contractual clause as unworkable; the Court of Appeal held that was reason enough, and the adjudicator was not required to explain why the clause was unworkable. The Queensland courts read s 88(5) the same way.3

That doesn’t collapse the obligation into nothing. The point at which reasons become so thin that the determination falls is the point at which they are “opaque” in the sense that no reasons for decision are revealed at all. That formulation comes from Jackson J in Sierra Property Qld Pty Ltd v National Construction Management Pty Ltd [2016] QSC 108 and was clarified in Annie Street JV Pty Ltd v MCC Pty Ltd [2016] QSC 268, where Flanagan J explained that “opaque” means no reasons revealed — “a recognised basis for finding jurisdictional error”.4

When inadequate reasons become jurisdictional error

The Queensland authority closest to the line is Bond J’s decision in Acciona Agua Australia Pty Ltd v Monadelphous Engineering Pty Ltd [2020] QSC 133. The adjudicator there had considered new reasons in the response (a s 82(4) breach), and the reasoning that flowed from that improper consideration was held to be “nothing more than a vote one way” rather than the “active process of intellectual engagement with the issues and the submissions before him” that the BIF Act requires. The result was a finding of jurisdictional error under s 88(5) — partial declaration of voidness, with the balance of the determination left to stand.

The pattern across the cases is fairly settled. Reasons that show the adjudicator turning their mind to the disputed issues, weighing the competing positions and reaching a conclusion will satisfy s 88(5)(b), even if the reasoning is brief or in places clearly wrong. Reasons that simply parrot one side’s case, or that fail to engage with a critical item at all, will not.5 A useful check is whether the determination explains why each significant disputed item was allowed or rejected. A blanket statement that all submissions have been “considered”, without more, is the textbook example of what won’t do.6

What this means on a project

  • If you are reviewing an unfavourable determination, separate two questions. Did the adjudicator make an error in the reasoning? That is usually within jurisdiction and won’t get the determination set aside. Did the adjudicator fail to give any intelligible reasons on a significant disputed item? That can be jurisdictional error.
  • Look for items where the adjudicator says little more than “I am satisfied” or “I have considered the submissions” on a contested point. Bare conclusions on items that were genuinely in dispute are the strongest candidates for an “opaque reasons” challenge.
  • Don’t pitch the challenge as a complaint about brevity. Cockram closes that argument off — reasons can be very short and still comply with s 88(5)(b). The argument that travels is “there are no reasons revealed at all on this issue”, not “the reasons are too short”.
  • If you are running an adjudication application or response, keep within the page and word limits in the Regulation. Section 88(4) gives the adjudicator a discretion to disregard the excess, and you don’t want a critical exhibit cut for non-compliance.
  • Don’t agree to an adjudicator deciding without reasons. The opt-out in s 88(5)(b) is rarely worth taking — reasons are what give you a basis to test the determination later or, more commonly, to understand and absorb the result.
  1. Avopiling (NSW) Pty Ltd v Menard Bachy Pty Ltd [2012] NSWSC 1466 (Sackar J), drawing on Shell Refining (Australia) Pty Ltd v AJ Mayr Engineering Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 94, [25]–[26] (Bergin J) — a bald “I have read all the submissions” or “I am satisfied” without more may not survive as adequate reasons; SSC Plenty Road Pty Ltd v Construction Engineering (Aust) Pty Ltd [2015] VSC 631 (Vickery J) — the standard sits towards the lower end of the scale given the time constraints and the qualifications of adjudicators.
  2. Iskra v MMIR Pty Limited [2019] NSWCA 126 — no requirement to use legally correct language; a “fine-tooth comb” approach to an adjudicator’s reasons is to be avoided; Future Power Developments Pty Ltd v TJ & RF [2017] NSWSC 232, [58] (Ball J) — reasoning is read as a whole, in recognition that adjudicators are not legally trained, applying Clyde Bergemann Senior Thermal Pty Ltd v Varley Power Services Pty Ltd [2011] NSWSC 1039, [58], [66]–[67] (McDougall J).
  3. Cockram Construction Ltd v Fulton Hogan Construction Pty Ltd [2018] NSWCA 107 — the statutory question is whether reasons were given, not whether they were “adequate” or “sufficient”; applied in MTR Corporation (Sydney) NRT Pty Ltd v Thales Australia Ltd [2020] NSWSC 1147 (Stevenson J).
  4. Sierra Property Qld Pty Ltd v National Construction Management Pty Ltd [2016] QSC 108 (Jackson J) — no fixed list of matters that must be addressed; reasons required by the equivalent of s 88(5)(b) are an essential part of the decision and failure to provide them is jurisdictional error; Annie Street JV Pty Ltd v MCC Pty Ltd & Ors [2016] QSC 268 (Flanagan J) — clarifying that “opaque” in Sierra meant no reasons revealed; BGC Construction Pty Ltd v Citygate Properties Pty Ltd [2016] WASC 88, [25] (Tottle J) — an adjudicator cannot omit reasons entirely on significant items and leave the parties to work out the basis for themselves.
  5. Parrwood Pty Ltd v Trinity Constructions (Aust) Pty Ltd [2020] NSWCA 172 — declining to determine the amount, on the basis that a finding would be “premature”, was a failure to do the “very thing” the adjudicator was required to do under the equivalent of s 88(1)(a); MMIR Pty Limited v Iskra [2019] NSWSC 35 (Parker J) — awarding the amount claimed without “any rational analysis of whether the amount claimed in the payment claim was justified” was jurisdictional error (subsequent appeal: Iskra v MMIR [2019] NSWCA 126); CH2M Hill Australia Pty Limited v ABB Australia Pty Ltd [2016] NTSC 42, (Kelly J) — the adjudicator must make a bona fide attempt to provide reasons; Nova Builders Pty Ltd v Civil & Civic Corporation Pty Ltd [2022] ACTSC 209 — no “intelligible basis” for finding a construction contract; reasons inadequate.
  6. Shell Refining (Australia) Pty Ltd v AJ Mayr Engineering Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 94 (Bergin J) — catch-all statements that all material has been considered are unhelpful; compliance with the duty to consider is assessed from the content of the determination, not from the adjudicator’s self-certification; Ceerose Pty Ltd v A-Civil Aust Pty Ltd [2023] NSWCA 215 — an adjudicator is not obliged to investigate the “true merits” of a claim where the respondent has not put material in issue, overruling earlier authority to the contrary.

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

Bind valuation in later applications

If an earlier adjudicator has already decided the value of a particular item of construction work or related goods and services, a later adjudicator must adopt that same value — unless someone shows the value has actually changed since. The rule is in s 87 BIF Act, and it stops parties from re-running the same valuation argument again and again on rolling payment claims.

Section 87 BIF Act — Valuation of work etc. in later adjudication application

(1) This section applies if, in deciding an adjudication application, an adjudicator has decided the value of—

(a) any construction work carried out under a construction contract; or

(b) any related goods and services supplied under a construction contract.

Note—See section 72 for the valuation of construction work and related goods and services.

(2) Any adjudicator must, in any later adjudication application that involves the working out of the value of the construction work or of the related goods and services, give the work, or the goods and services, the same value as that previously decided by the adjudicator unless the claimant or respondent satisfies the adjudicator concerned that the value of the work, or the goods and services, has changed since the previous decision.

(3) However, if a decision or order of a court changes the value of the construction work or of the related goods and services, the adjudicator must give the work, or the goods and services, the same value as changed by the decision or order unless the claimant or respondent satisfies the adjudicator concerned that the value of the work, or the goods and services, has changed since the decision or order.

Valuation, not entitlement

The first thing to grasp is that s 87 only locks in valuation — it doesn’t lock in everything an earlier adjudicator decided. The leading distinction comes from McDougall J in Rothnere Pty Ltd v Quasar Constructions NSW Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 1151, refined in John Goss Projects Pty Ltd v Leighton Contractors Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 789; (2006) 66 NSWLR 707, between the calculation of the amount of a progress payment (what the adjudicator ultimately has to do) and the valuation of construction work (one input into that calculation). Only the second is captured by s 87. That distinction has been approved by the NSW Court of Appeal in Dualcorp Pty Ltd v Remo Constructions Pty Ltd [2009] NSWCA 69; (2009) 74 NSWLR 190 and by the Queensland Supreme Court in ACN 060 559 971 Pty Ltd v O’Brien [2007] QSC 91; [2008] 2 Qd R 396.1

The practical filter is this: if the dollar amount in dispute is not referable to a particular item of construction work or related goods and services, s 87 doesn’t bite. Liquidated damages are the classic example — in O’Brien, Mullins J held that liquidated damages under a construction contract were not referable to any particular item of construction work, so a later adjudicator who relied on s 87 (then s 27 BCIPA) to adopt the earlier adjudicator’s LD figure had erred in law.2

What “previously decided” actually covers

The bind only operates over items the earlier adjudicator actually valued. In Hitachi Ltd v O’Donnell Griffin Pty Ltd [2008] QSC 135, the first adjudicator had taken a selective approach to 113 variation claims, only assessing 12. The second adjudicator nonetheless treated the first decision as a valuation of the whole and assessed the later claim at $nil. Skoien AJ held that on the facts the first adjudicator had only valued six variation claims — none of which were in the later payment claim — so s 87 simply had nothing to grip on. Before you treat an earlier decision as binding, you need to identify which items it actually decided the value of.

The corresponding rule for the respondent is that you can’t hide an earlier adjudication and then complain the second adjudicator ignored it. In Bezzina Developers Pty Ltd v Deemah Stone (Qld) Pty Ltd [2008] QCA 213; [2008] 2 Qd R 495 the Court of Appeal held that where an adjudicator does not know there is, or probably is, a prior adjudication, no occasion arises to call for further submissions on it, and no obligation to seek them out should be implied. Tell the adjudicator about the earlier decision in your submissions, or live with the consequences.

Showing the value has changed

The exception in s 87(2) — that the value has changed since the previous decision — has to be actively engaged with by the adjudicator. Iris Broadbeach Business Pty Ltd v Descon Group Australia Pty Ltd [2024] QSC 16 is a recent Queensland illustration. An earlier adjudication had valued an architect’s work at $200,000. In the later adjudication Iris produced an invoice, dated after the previous decision, suggesting only $183,780 was payable. The adjudicator simply applied the earlier $200,000 figure without engaging with Iris’s submissions. Wilson J held that was jurisdictional error: s 87(2) required the adjudicator to consider whether the claimant or respondent had satisfied her that the value had changed since the previous decision, and on the evidence Iris’s material was capable of doing exactly that.

The cross-border position is consistent. In Goyder Wind Farm 1 Pty Ltd v GE Renewable Energy Australia Pty Ltd [2024] SASC 108, Stein J accepted that delay costs claimed across multiple payment claims were not a single “same construction work” for the purposes of the equivalent SA provision, so the second adjudicator wasn’t bound to apply the earlier valuation. And in Harlech Enterprises Pty Ltd v Beno Excavations Pty Ltd [2022] ACTCA 42, the ACT Court of Appeal emphasised that an adjudicator’s valuation can only be disturbed in the limited circumstances the section allows — the section is not a back-door issue estoppel protecting every finding the earlier adjudicator made.3

What this means on a project

  • If you’re the respondent and an earlier adjudication decided the value of items now back in play, put the earlier decision squarely in front of the second adjudicator. Identify the specific items valued and the values given. Under Bezzina, if you don’t raise it, the adjudicator isn’t obliged to chase it.
  • Don’t over-claim the bind. Section 87 only locks in the value of construction work or related goods and services. Liquidated damages, financing claims, set-offs not referable to a specific work item — none of those are caught.
  • If you’re the claimant trying to get out from under an earlier valuation, lead concrete evidence that the value has changed since — later invoices, defect rectification, scope changes, court orders. A bare assertion won’t do, but real evidence has to be engaged with: that’s the lesson of Iris Broadbeach.
  • Before you assume an item was “previously decided”, read the earlier decision carefully. As Hitachi shows, an adjudicator who took a selective approach may not have valued the items you think they did.
  • If a court has since changed the value of work (for instance, on a final-account dispute), s 87(3) makes that court figure the new baseline — subject again to the “changed since” exception.
  1. Rothnere Pty Ltd v Quasar Constructions NSW Pty Ltd [2004] NSWSC 1151 (McDougall J); John Goss Projects Pty Ltd v Leighton Contractors Pty Ltd [2006] NSWSC 789; (2006) 66 NSWLR 707 (McDougall J) — the valuation/entitlement distinction; approved in Dualcorp Pty Ltd v Remo Constructions Pty Ltd [2009] NSWCA 69; (2009) 74 NSWLR 190 and ACN 060 559 971 Pty Ltd v O’Brien [2007] QSC 91; [2008] 2 Qd R 396 (Mullins J).
  2. ACN 060 559 971 Pty Ltd v O’Brien [2007] QSC 91; [2008] 2 Qd R 396 (Mullins J) — liquidated damages not referable to a particular item of construction work, so s 87 (then s 27 BCIPA) cannot bind a later adjudicator to the earlier figure. See also obiter of Douglas J in Ball Construction Pty Ltd v Conart Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 124 that “the private decision of the parties not to enforce an earlier decision does not affect what the subsequent adjudicator must take into account for the purposes of s 27”.
  3. Goyder Wind Farm 1 Pty Ltd v GE Renewable Energy Australia Pty Ltd [2024] SASC 108 (Stein J) — delay costs across separate payment claims not a single item of “same construction work”; Harlech Enterprises Pty Ltd v Beno Excavations Pty Ltd [2022] ACTCA 42 — an adjudicator’s valuation may only be disturbed by a subsequent adjudicator in limited circumstances, and the SOP Act’s purpose is not to apply issue estoppel to broader findings.

Requirements of an adjudicator’s decision

Slip-rule corrections

Adjudicators occasionally make obvious mistakes — a typo, a transposed figure, the wrong party name, an arithmetic slip. Section 89 lets the adjudicator correct that kind of error after the determination has issued, either on their own initiative or on the application of either side. Used properly, it cleans up an honest slip without anyone having to run to the Supreme Court. Used as a back-door for a re-hearing, it doesn’t work.

Section 89 BIF Act — Adjudicator may correct clerical mistakes etc.

89 Adjudicator may correct clerical mistakes etc.

(1) This section applies if the adjudicator’s decision includes—

(a) a clerical mistake; or

(b) an error arising from an accidental slip or omission; or

(c) a material miscalculation of figures or a material mistake in the description of a person, thing or matter mentioned in the decision; or

(d) a defect of form.

(2) The adjudicator may, on the adjudicator’s own initiative or on the application of the claimant or respondent, correct the decision.

(3) The adjudicator may, if requested by the registrar, correct the decision.

What s 89 is for

Section 89 lists four discrete circumstances — a clerical mistake, an accidental slip or omission, a material miscalculation or material mis-description, or a defect of form — any one of which can found the adjudicator’s discretion to correct. The four limbs are disjunctive: the error only needs to fall within one. The unifying idea behind the section is that minor mistakes don’t need to bring down the whole determination. The legislature recognised that adjudication is fast and pressured, and that obvious slips should be cleaned up at the adjudicator level rather than treated as jurisdictional error.
