That logic is reflected in McNab Developments (Qld) Pty Ltd v MAK Construction Services Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QCA 232, where Jackson J held that the presence of s 28 of BCIPA (the predecessor to s 89) reflects the legislature’s view that an accidental or erroneous omission to consider part of a claim does not invalidate the adjudicator’s decision for jurisdictional error. Applying the Project Blue Sky approach, his Honour reasoned that because s 28(2) preserves the operation of an adjudicator’s decision and provides a power of correction, there is no reason to treat the decision as invalid until that power is exercised — and the parties may decide a particular error isn’t important enough to bother correcting at all.1

The inadvertence principle

The boundary of the section is inadvertence. Section 89 is not a vehicle for the adjudicator to revisit reasoning, change methodology or repair a deliberate decision that turned out to be wrong. The leading Queensland statement is Daubney J’s in Uniting Church in Australia Property Trust (Qld) v Davenport & Anor [2009] QSC 134, which picks up Macrossan CJ’s observation in Cawood v Infraworth Pty Ltd [1990] 2 Qd R 114 at 122:

Inadvertence, as distinguished from an error or mistake resulting from deliberate decision, is the basis of the jurisdiction to correct under the slip rule.

Daubney J then made the key point about how that maps onto s 89: of the four limbs in s 89(1), only paragraph (b) — “an error arising from an accidental slip or omission” — actually imports the notion of inadvertence. The other three limbs (clerical mistake, material miscalculation or mis-description, defect of form) operate independently. So a material miscalculation can be corrected even where it wasn’t strictly “inadvertent”, provided it is genuinely a miscalculation rather than a change of mind.2

What a miscalculation is not is a complete re-working of the reasoning. Both Uniting Church and Thiess Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd & Ors [2012] QSC 373 hold that a “correction” that would require the adjudicator to adopt a completely different method of calculation, or apply a different chain of reasoning to reach a different result, is not a correction of a miscalculation under s 89(1)(c). Lyons J in Thiess distinguished cases under rule 388 of the UCPR on the basis that something more than a different final figure has to be inserted before s 89 is engaged.3

The discretion to correct — and to decline

Even where an error falls within s 89(1), correction is permissive, not mandatory. As McDougall J said in Musico v Davenport [2003] NSWSC 977, a party has the right to request the adjudicator to consider exercising the power, not the right to have it exercised in a particular way. If the adjudicator declines — for example, because they don’t accept there is a mistake — that decision is an error within jurisdiction and is not reviewable for jurisdictional error.4 Section 89 is also not analogous to UCPR r 388: Daubney J in Uniting Church rejected that analogy as “inapt”, given r 388’s conjunctive structure compared to the four discrete disjunctive limbs in s 89(1).

What this means on a project

  • If the determination contains an obvious error in your favour or against you — a typo, the wrong party name, a transposed figure, an arithmetic slip — write to the adjudicator promptly and ask for a s 89 correction. Identify the limb you say is engaged and the correction you want made.
  • Don’t use s 89 as a back-door re-hearing. If your “correction” would require the adjudicator to change methodology or chain of reasoning to reach a different result, it isn’t a slip-rule case. That argument belongs in the Supreme Court, not in a s 89 request.
  • Material miscalculations and mis-descriptions don’t need to be “inadvertent” in the strict sense — only the s 89(1)(b) accidental slip limb does. So a genuine arithmetic mistake or wrong figure description is correctable even if the adjudicator was concentrating when they made it.
  • If the adjudicator refuses to correct, that’s the end of the road on the slip rule. The refusal is within jurisdiction and won’t be set aside on review. Your only remaining options are the substantive jurisdictional-error grounds covered elsewhere in this section.
  • If a typographical error in the determination doesn’t actually affect the result — for example, a wrong word in the first line that doesn’t change the operative finding — the courts will not intervene on judicial review either: see the approach in CPB Contractors Pty Limited v Heyday5 Pty Limited [2020] NSWSC 1625.
  1. McNab Developments (Qld) Pty Ltd v MAK Construction Services Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QCA 232 (Jackson J) — the existence of s 28 BCIPA (now s 89) shows the legislature intended that accidental or erroneous omissions to consider part of a claim do not invalidate the determination for jurisdictional error; applies the Project Blue Sky Inc v Australian Broadcasting Authority [1998] HCA 28 approach.
  2. Uniting Church in Australia Property Trust (Qld) v Davenport & Anor [2009] QSC 134 (Daubney J) — only s 89(1)(b) imports the notion of inadvertence; the other three limbs operate independently. Picks up Cawood v Infraworth Pty Ltd [1990] 2 Qd R 114 (Macrossan CJ, Kelly SPJ agreeing) on the inadvertence basis of the slip rule.
  3. Uniting Church in Australia Property Trust (Qld) v Davenport & Anor [2009] QSC 134, [37] (Daubney J); Theiss Pty Ltd v Warren Brothers Earthmoving Pty Ltd & Ors [2012] QSC 373, [69] (Ann Lyons J) — a “correction” that requires a completely different method of calculation or a different chain of reasoning is not a miscalculation. Thiess distinguished Queensland Pork Pty Ltd v Lott [2003] QCA 271 (UCPR r 388) on that basis.
  4. Musico v Davenport [2003] NSWSC 977 (McDougall J) — the s 89 power is permissive; a party has the right to request its exercise, not to have it exercised in a particular way. Followed in Hansen Yuncken Pty Ltd & Anor v Yuanda Australia Pty Ltd & Anor [2018] SASC 158 (Lovell J) — declining to exercise the discretion is within jurisdiction and not reviewable for jurisdictional error. See also CPB Contractors Pty Limited v Heyday5 Pty Limited [2020] NSWSC 1625 (Hammerschlag J) — a typographical error in the first line of a determination did not justify intervention.

Enforcing an adjudicator's decision

Due date for paying the adjudicated amount

An adjudicator’s decision in your favour is not self-executing — the respondent has to pay it. Section 90 fixes when payment must happen and creates a statutory offence if it doesn’t. The due date is also the practical pivot point for everything that follows: the right to suspend work, the right to file the decision as a judgment debt, and the disciplinary consequences for licensed contractors all hang off whether the adjudicated amount has been paid by the s 90 due date.

Section 90 BIF Act — Respondent required to pay adjudicated amount

(1) This section applies if an adjudicator decides that a respondent is required to pay an adjudicated amount.

(2) The respondent must pay the amount to the claimant on or before—

(a) the day that is 5 business days after the day on which the adjudicator gives a copy of the adjudicator’s decision to the respondent; or

(b) if the adjudicator decides a later date for payment under section 88(1)(b)—the later date.

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units.

(3) If the respondent pays the amount to the claimant, the respondent must—

(a) notify the registrar, using an approved way, within 5 business days after making the payment; and

(b) provide the registrar with evidence the payment was made.

Maximum penalty—20 penalty units.

Calculating the due date

The default due date under s 90(2)(a) is 5 business days after the day on which the adjudicator gives the respondent a copy of the decision. The trigger is delivery of the decision to the respondent, not delivery to the claimant and not the date the decision was signed. “Business day” takes its ordinary BIF Act meaning, so weekends and Queensland public holidays drop out of the count, as do the days in the statutory end-of-year shutdown period under the Act.

The day on which the adjudicator gives the respondent the decision is day zero; the next business day is day one. So a decision delivered to the respondent on a Monday (with no intervening public holidays) is due for payment by close of business the following Monday. If you are the claimant, ask the adjudicator or the registrar to confirm in writing the date the decision was given to the respondent — you will need that date to prove the due date in any subsequent enforcement step.

Where the adjudicator fixes a later date

Section 90(2)(b) lets the adjudicator override the 5-business-day default by fixing a later date for payment under s 88(1)(b). That power is sometimes used where the adjudicator considers a longer payment period is appropriate — for example, where the claim involves a large sum, or where the contract itself contemplated a longer payment cycle. If the adjudicator has decided a later date, that later date is the due date for s 90 purposes; the 5-business-day default does not apply and is not an alternative. Read the operative part of the decision carefully on first receipt and diary the date the adjudicator has fixed.

The penalty — and the QBCC disciplinary overlay

Failure to pay the adjudicated amount on or before the due date is an offence carrying a maximum penalty of 200 penalty units. That penalty sits on top of the civil consequences — the right to recover the amount as a judgment debt, the right to obtain an adjudication certificate, and the right to suspend work — rather than replacing them.

For licensed contractors and building companies, there is a further consequence that often matters more in commercial terms than the penalty. A failure to pay an adjudicated amount on or before the due date is a ground for taking disciplinary action under the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991. That can mean licence conditions, suspension or, in serious or repeated cases, cancellation. If you are the respondent and the cash isn’t there, the worst response is silence: deal with the payment, or with the QBCC, before the due date passes.

The respondent’s notification obligation under s 90(3)

Once the respondent pays, s 90(3) imposes a separate, free-standing obligation: notify the registrar in an approved way within 5 business days after making the payment, and provide evidence that the payment was made. The maximum penalty for non-compliance is 20 penalty units. This is an administrative step, but it is not optional — it allows the registrar to keep an accurate picture of compliance with adjudicators’ decisions across the industry. If you are the claimant and you have been paid, it is worth confirming with the respondent that the s 90(3) notification has been lodged; an unlodged notification can be a warning sign that the respondent is treating the payment, and the obligations around it, casually.

What this means on a project

  • The due date is 5 business days after the day the adjudicator gives a copy of the decision to the respondent — unless the adjudicator has fixed a later date under s 88(1)(b), in which case that date governs.
  • Confirm in writing the date the decision was given to the respondent. You will need that date to calculate the due date and to prove non-payment in any later enforcement step.
  • Read the operative part of the decision on first receipt for any later date the adjudicator has fixed under s 88(1)(b). Diary the actual due date, not just “5 business days”.
  • If you are the respondent and you cannot pay by the due date, don’t go silent. Non-payment is both an offence (200 penalty units) and a ground for QBCC disciplinary action against your licence. Engage early.
  • If you are the respondent and you have paid, lodge the s 90(3) notification with the registrar within 5 business days, with evidence of payment. Failure to do so is a separate offence (20 penalty units).
  • If you are the claimant, the due date is the gateway to the next steps in this chapter — the adjudication certificate, filing the decision as a judgment debt, and the right to suspend work all turn on whether the adjudicated amount has been paid by the s 90 due date.
  1. The penalty under s 90(2) is 200 penalty units; the penalty under s 90(3) is 20 penalty units. The current value of a penalty unit is fixed by the Penalties and Sentences Act 1992 (Qld) and is updated annually.
  2. The note to s 90 records that a failure to pay an adjudicated amount on or before the due date is also a ground for taking disciplinary action under the Queensland Building and Construction Commission Act 1991. That disciplinary overlay is, in practice, the most significant consequence for licensed respondents.
  3. The power to fix a later date for payment is found in s 88(1)(b) BIF Act, which is dealt with in the adjudicator’s decision chapter.

Enforcing an adjudicator’s decision

Adjudication certificate

Once an adjudicator has decided in your favour and the respondent hasn’t paid by the due date, the next step is to obtain an adjudication certificate from the registrar. The certificate is the document you take to court — it can be filed as a judgment for the adjudicated amount, which is what gives the BIF Act its teeth.

Section 91 BIF Act — Adjudication certificate

(1) As soon as practicable after being given a copy of a decision by an adjudicator, but no later than 5 business days after being given the decision, the registrar must give the claimant a certificate (an adjudication certificate) of the decision stating the following matters—

(a) the name of the claimant;

(b) the name of the respondent who is liable to pay the adjudicated amount;

(c) the adjudicated amount;

(d) the date on which payment of the adjudicated amount was required to be paid to the claimant;

(e) the rate of interest payable on the adjudicated amount;

(f) the fees, identified in the decision, that the respondent is to pay;

(g) that the certificate is made under this Act.

(2) However, the registrar does not have to give the claimant an adjudication certificate if—

(a) the respondent is not required to pay an amount under the decision; or

(b) the adjudicator decided he or she does not have jurisdiction to decide the application; or

(c) the adjudicator decided the adjudication application is frivolous or vexatious.

What the certificate is for

An adjudicator’s decision, by itself, is not a court order. You can’t walk into the sheriff’s office and enforce it directly. The adjudication certificate is the bridge between the adjudicator’s decision and the courts: once you have it, you can file it as a judgment debt and then deploy the usual enforcement mechanisms — statutory demands, garnishee orders, warrants of execution and the like.

The certificate is issued by the registrar of the Queensland Building and Construction Commission, not by the adjudicator. The adjudicator’s job ends when the decision is given; the registrar’s job is administrative — to take the decision and convert it into a certificate that can be filed in court.

Timing and the registrar’s obligation

Section 91(1) imposes a clear deadline on the registrar: the certificate must be given to the claimant “as soon as practicable” after the registrar receives a copy of the decision, and in any event no later than 5 business days afterwards. The 5-business-day cap is a hard outer limit, not a target. In practice, certificates are often issued within 1–2 business days of the registrar receiving the decision.

You don’t need to apply for the certificate or chase the registrar — the obligation runs the other way. The registrar must give it to you. That said, if 5 business days have passed and nothing has arrived, contact the registry: decisions occasionally go astray between the adjudicator and the registrar, and a polite follow-up is faster than waiting.

What the certificate must contain

The seven matters listed in s 91(1)(a)–(g) are mandatory. The certificate must identify the parties, state the adjudicated amount, give the date by which payment was required, set out the interest rate, identify the fees the respondent must pay, and recite that the certificate is made under the Act. Each of these matters then carries through into the judgment when the certificate is filed in court — so the certificate, on its face, is what fixes the amount of the judgment debt and the rate at which interest accrues.

The “date on which payment… was required to be paid” in (d) is the due date fixed by the adjudicator’s decision under s 88, not the date of the decision itself. That distinction matters for interest calculations and for working out when you became entitled to enforce.

When a certificate will not issue

Section 91(2) sets out three situations in which the registrar does not have to issue a certificate:

  • where the respondent is not required to pay any amount under the decision (for example, the adjudicator decided the adjudicated amount was nil or in the respondent’s favour);
  • where the adjudicator decided he or she did not have jurisdiction to decide the application; or
  • where the adjudicator decided the application was frivolous or vexatious.

In each of those cases there is nothing to enforce, so the certificate machinery doesn’t engage. If the adjudicator declined jurisdiction, your remedy is to address the jurisdictional defect (if you can) and re-apply, not to seek a certificate over a decision that found there was no jurisdiction in the first place.

What this means on a project

  • The day you receive the adjudicator’s decision in your favour, diary the due date for payment under s 88 and the 5-business-day window in s 91(1). If payment doesn’t land by the due date, the certificate will usually be in your hands within days.
  • Check the certificate carefully when it arrives. The seven matters in s 91(1)(a)–(g) flow straight through into the judgment, so any error — wrong party name, wrong amount, wrong due date — needs to be raised with the registrar before you file.
  • The certificate is not the judgment. To enforce, you need to file the certificate as a judgment in a court of competent jurisdiction. That’s a separate step under the enforcement provisions later in Chapter 3.
  • If the adjudicated amount is nil, or the adjudicator declined jurisdiction, or the application was held to be frivolous or vexatious, no certificate will issue. Don’t spend time chasing one — reassess your options instead.
  • Keep the original certificate. You will be asked for it when you file in court, and replacing one takes time you generally don’t have if the respondent is heading toward insolvency.
  1. The 5-business-day cap in s 91(1) runs from when the registrar is given a copy of the decision, not from when the decision is given to the parties. There is typically a short administrative gap between the two.
  2. The matters listed in s 91(1)(a)–(g) mirror what is needed to enter a money judgment: identification of debtor and creditor, principal sum, due date, interest rate and ancillary fees. The certificate is, in substance, a packaged judgment debt awaiting filing.

Enforcing an adjudicator’s decision

Filing the certificate as a judgment debt

If the respondent doesn’t pay the adjudicated amount, you don’t need to sue on the contract. You take the adjudication certificate from the registrar, file it in a court of competent jurisdiction, and from that moment you hold a judgment for the unpaid portion that you can enforce like any other money judgment.

Section 93 BIF Act — Filing of adjudication certificate as judgment debt

93 Filing of adjudication certificate as judgment debt

(1) An adjudication certificate may be filed as a judgment for a debt, and may be enforced, in a court of competent jurisdiction.

(2) An adjudication certificate can not be filed under this section unless it is accompanied by an affidavit by the claimant stating that the whole or a part of the adjudicated amount has not been paid to the claimant at the time the certificate is filed.

(3) If the affidavit states that part of the adjudicated amount has been paid, the judgment is for the unpaid part of the amount only.

Filing mechanics

The mechanics under s 93 are deliberately simple. Once you have the certificate (issued by the registrar after the adjudicated amount falls due and remains unpaid), you file it in a court that has jurisdiction over the dollar value — the Magistrates Court, District Court or Supreme Court depending on the amount. The certificate itself is what becomes the judgment; there is no fresh hearing on the merits.

Two procedural points matter. First, s 93(2) requires a supporting affidavit from the claimant stating that the whole or part of the adjudicated amount remains unpaid at the time of filing. Without that affidavit the certificate cannot be filed. Second, s 93(3) means the judgment tracks reality — if the respondent has already paid part of the adjudicated amount, the judgment is only for the unpaid balance.

Once filed, the judgment can be enforced by the usual means — garnishee orders, enforcement warrants over property, statutory demands. There is no obligation to give the respondent advance notice that the certificate has been filed or that enforcement is on foot.1

The Neller policy — cash flow trumps repayment risk

If the respondent applies to set aside or stay the judgment, the courts approach the application against the background of what the Act is for. The leading Queensland authority is R J Neller Building Pty Ltd v Ainsworth [2008] QCA 397; [2009] 1 Qd R 390. Keane JA (Fraser JA and Fryberg J agreeing) framed the policy this way:

It is evidently the intention of the BCIP Act, and, in particular, s 31 and s 100 to which reference has been made, that the process of adjudication established under that Act should provide a speedy and effective means of ensuring cash flow to builders from the parties with whom they contract… the risk that a builder might not be able to refund moneys ultimately found to be due to a non-residential owner after a successful action by the owner must, I think, be regarded as a risk which, as a matter of policy in the commercial context in which the BCIP Act applies, the legislature has, prima facie at least, assigned to the owner.

The practical effect is that a respondent who simply argues “we might not get our money back if we lose at final hearing” is unlikely to obtain a stay. The Act has already weighed that risk and assigned it to the respondent. Neller was treated as turning on a valid adjudication; where the respondent has a prima facie case that the determination is void for jurisdictional error, the analysis shifts — courts then weigh the policy as a factor going to the nature of the right in question, rather than as a near-conclusive answer.2 A common compromise is to grant interlocutory relief on condition that the disputed amount is paid into court, or secured by bank guarantee, pending final determination.3

What the respondent cannot do under s 93(4)

If the respondent commences proceedings to set the judgment aside, s 93(4) closes off the obvious lines of attack. The respondent cannot bring a counterclaim, raise a defence about matters arising under the construction contract, or challenge the adjudicator’s decision; and the respondent must pay the unpaid portion of the adjudicated amount into court as security pending the outcome.

The recent appellate decision in Hynash Constructions Pty Ltd v BRP Industries Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 14 illustrates how strictly that bar is applied. The respondent had paid an up-front 10% establishment and mobilisation fee under the contract and tried to argue, in proceedings to recover the “unpaid portion” of a payment claim, that the up-front payment ought to be credited against the adjudicated amount. The Court of Appeal rejected that — the “unpaid portion” refers to amounts unpaid in response to the payment claim, not to unrelated prior payments. If the respondent wanted credit for an earlier payment, it had to raise it in a payment schedule. By contrast, where a payment was made in direct part-satisfaction of the payment claim itself (for example, a direct payment to a third-party supplier whose invoices formed part of the claim), that is a factual question about how much remains unpaid, not an impermissible cross-claim.4

The s 93(4)(a)(ii) bar on raising defences “in relation to matters arising under the construction contract” has also been read to cover equitable set-off and contractual set-off arguments,5 but not arguments that sit outside the contract — for example, a misleading and deceptive conduct claim under the Australian Consumer Law.6 And where the underlying determination has been quashed for jurisdictional error, the resulting judgment can still be set aside — on the footing that there is in truth no adjudicator’s determination at all, rather than a challenge to the determination’s correctness.7

What this means on a project

  • Once the adjudicated amount is overdue, get the certificate from the registrar and file it. You do not need to issue fresh proceedings or prove the underlying claim again.
  • Prepare the s 93(2) affidavit before you file. It must state that the whole or part of the adjudicated amount remains unpaid at the time of filing. If the respondent has paid part, file for the unpaid balance only.
  • Choose the right court for the dollar value. The certificate is filed as a judgment in a court of competent jurisdiction — once filed, the usual enforcement tools (garnishee, enforcement warrant) are available.
  • If you are the respondent and want to challenge enforcement, recognise the steep climb. You will need to pay the unpaid portion into court as security, and you cannot rely on counterclaims, contract-based defences or a frontal attack on the adjudicator’s reasoning. A jurisdictional-error case is the realistic route, often coupled with payment in or a bank guarantee.
  • If you are the respondent and you have already made a payment that should reduce the claim, make sure the basis is clear — a direct payment against the payment claim is a factual reduction of the unpaid portion; a payment under some other contractual obligation is not, and after Hynash you cannot smuggle it in at the enforcement stage if it wasn’t in your payment schedule.
  1. Fitz Jersey Pty Ltd v Atlas Construction Group Pty Ltd [2017] NSWCA 53 (Beazley ACJ, Basten and Leeming JJA) — a judgment filed under the equivalent of s 93 may be enforced (including by garnishee order) without notice to the respondent; the respondent is in no better position than any other unsuccessful party against whom a judgment debt has been entered.
  2. BRB Modular Pty Ltd v AWX Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 222 (Bond J) — drawing the distinction between valid adjudication decisions (where the Neller policy applies in full force) and decisions affected by jurisdictional error (where the policy is one factor in the balance of convenience), and respectfully disagreeing with Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Sun Engineering (Qld) Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 170 (Daubney J). See also Sunshine Coast Regional Council v Earthpro Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 271, [15], [23] (Peter Lyons J); Low v MCC Pty Ltd & Ors; MCC Pty Ltd v Low [2018] QSC 6 (Jackson J) — injunction granted on serious-question / balance-of-convenience grounds where the reference date was in doubt and security by bank guarantee was offered.
  3. For the “payment into court as a condition of relief” line of authority, see Filadelfia Projects Pty Limited v EntirlTy Business Services [2010] NSWSC 473; Nazero Group Pty Limited v Top Quality Construction Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 232; Milburn Lake Pty Ltd v Andritz Pty Ltd [2016] VSC 3; Parkview Constructions Pty Limited v Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1911 (Slattery J); Hakea Holdings Pty Limited v Denham Constructions Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1120 (Ball J); Richard Crookes Constructions Pty Ltd v CES Projects (Aust) Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1119 (McDougall J). On the limits, see Bellerive Homes Pty Ltd v FW Projects Pty Ltd [2018] NSWSC 1435 (N Adams J) — stay refused where the criteria were not met; Paul Kennedy McCullough trading as The Green Side Up v New Start Australia Pty Limited [2023] NSWDC 82 (Dicker SC DCJ); Kuatro Build Pty Ltd v Elite Formwork Group Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 372 (Hmelnitsky J); Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No 2) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 49 (Payne JA) — entry of judgment under the equivalent of s 93(1) is not precluded by a pending arbitration or by mere repayment risk; a stay until the end of arbitration would be contrary to the statutory purpose.
  4. Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd (subject to Deed of Company Arrangement) v Linx Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 366 (Wright J), upheld in Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd v Linx Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWCA 243 — a direct payment to a third-party supplier whose invoices formed part of the payment claim was a factual reduction of the “unpaid portion” under the equivalent of s 93(3), not an impermissible cross-claim under s 93(4)(a)(i). Contrast BRP Industries Pty Ltd v Hynash Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWDC 392 (Russell SC DCJ), affirmed in Hynash [2025] NSWCA 14.
  5. Infinity Formwork Group Pty Ltd v Adobe Construction Group Pty Ltd [2023] NSWDC 220 (Andronos SC DCJ) — equitable set-off falls within the bar on raising defences in relation to matters arising under the construction contract; it had to be raised in a payment schedule.
  6. Marques Group Pty Ltd v Parkview Constructions Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 625 (Rees J), applying Bitannia Pty Ltd v Parkline Constructions Pty Ltd (2006) 67 NSWLR 9 — a misleading and deceptive conduct argument under the Australian Consumer Law is not a defence “in relation to matters arising under the construction contract”.
  7. Harlech Enterprises Pty Ltd v Beno Excavations Pty Ltd t/as Benex Pipeline [2024] NSWDC 151 (Montgomery DCJ), applying Brodyn Pty Ltd t/as Time Cost and Quality v Davenport & Anor [2004] NSWCA 394, [61] — a judgment entered on a determination later quashed for jurisdictional error can be set aside on the basis that there is in truth no adjudicator’s determination; this is not contrary to the s 93(4)(a)(iii) bar on challenging the decision. See also Amasya Enterprises Pty Ltd & Anor v Asta Developments (Aust) Pty Ltd & Anor [2015] VSC 233 (Vickery J).

Enforcing an adjudicator’s decision

What the respondent cannot raise as a defence

Once an adjudication certificate has been filed as a judgment, the respondent’s options for resisting payment are deliberately narrow. If the respondent commences proceedings to set the judgment aside, s 93(4) shuts the door on the usual contractual defences and counterclaims, and forces the disputed money into court as security. The point of the regime is to keep cash flowing to the claimant while the underlying merits are sorted out elsewhere.

Section 93(4) BIF Act — bar on defences and counterclaims, security for the unpaid amount

93 Filing of adjudication certificate as judgment debt

(4) If the respondent commences proceedings to have the judgment set aside—

(a) the respondent is not, in those proceedings, entitled—

(i) to bring any counterclaim against the claimant; or

(ii) to raise any defence in relation to matters arising under the construction contract to which the adjudication certificate relates; or

(iii) to challenge the adjudicator’s decision; and

(b) the respondent is required to pay into the court, as security, the unpaid portion of the adjudicated amount pending the final decision in those proceedings.

The three things you cannot do

Section 93(4)(a) lists three prohibitions, and they need to be read together. You cannot bring a counterclaim against the claimant in the set-aside proceeding. You cannot raise a defence to “matters arising under the construction contract” — in practice, the contractual arguments you would normally run about scope, value, defects or back-charges. And you cannot “challenge the adjudicator’s decision” on the merits as part of resisting the judgment. The matching obligation in s 93(4)(b) is that the unpaid portion of the adjudicated amount has to be paid into court as security while the proceeding runs.

The bar is not absolute, and two narrow categories of argument do still get through. The first is jurisdictional error — if the adjudicator’s decision is void, there is, in truth, no “decision” for s 93(4)(a)(iii) to protect, and the judgment built on it can be set aside. The second is a class of factual contention that goes to what is actually owing under the certificate (rather than to the underlying contract).

What counts as a “defence in relation to matters arising under the contract”

The clearest recent appellate guidance on the scope of s 93(4)(a)(ii) is the New South Wales Court of Appeal’s decision in Hynash Constructions Pty Ltd v BRP Industries Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 14, dealing with the equivalent NSW provision. The respondent there had failed to provide a payment schedule. Faced with a claim for the unpaid portion, it argued that an earlier 10% “establishment and mobilisation” payment it had made under the contract should be credited against the payment claim, so that the “unpaid portion” was reduced. The Court of Appeal rejected the argument: the “unpaid portion” refers to amounts unpaid in response to the payment claim itself, not unrelated prior payments under the contract. If the respondent wanted credit for a prior contractual payment, it had to put that case in a payment schedule. Trying to run it as a defence at enforcement was, in substance, a defence in relation to a matter arising under the construction contract, and so caught by the bar.

The same line is taken with equitable set-off. In Infinity Formwork Group Pty Ltd v Adobe Construction Group Pty Ltd [2023] NSWDC 220 the District Court held that an equitable set-off was within the scope of the bar — if the respondent wanted to rely on it, the place to do so was in a payment schedule, not at the summary-judgment stage. By contrast, a misleading-or-deceptive-conduct claim under the Australian Consumer Law is not a “matter arising under the construction contract” and is not caught: Marques Group Pty Ltd v Parkview Constructions Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 625.1 A factual contention that part of the claimed amount has already been paid — for example, where the claimant accepts that a direct payment to a third-party supplier should reduce the debt — is also not a counterclaim or defence within s 93(4); it goes to the unpaid portion as a matter of fact.2

Jurisdictional error and the “no decision” route

Section 93(4)(a)(iii) bars a challenge to “the adjudicator’s decision”, but it does not protect a purported decision that is void for jurisdictional error. The orthodox analysis comes from Brodyn Pty Ltd t/as Time Cost and Quality v Davenport [2004] NSWCA 394 — once the certificate is filed, the resulting judgment is not itself void, but an application can be made to set it aside on the basis that there is, in truth, no adjudicator’s determination. That route was applied in Harlech Enterprises Pty Ltd v Beno Excavations Pty Ltd t/as Benex Pipeline [2024] NSWDC 151, where Montgomery DCJ set aside a judgment and a garnishee order after the underlying determination had been quashed for jurisdictional error in a separate Supreme Court proceeding. The statutory scheme does not contemplate the enforcement of a judgment debt where the determination is infected with jurisdictional error.

The practical consequence is that if you want to resist enforcement, you generally need to be running a parallel attack on the determination itself — void for jurisdictional error — rather than trying to re-litigate the contract under the cover of the set-aside proceeding.

Pay-in as security

Section 93(4)(b) is a hard requirement, not a discretion. If you commence proceedings to set aside the judgment, the unpaid portion of the adjudicated amount has to go into court as security. That obligation reflects the same legislative policy that runs through the rest of Chapter 3: the claimant’s cash flow is preserved while the merits are determined elsewhere, and the risk of the claimant’s insolvency is, prima facie, allocated to the respondent. Stays and injunctions to hold up enforcement are available, but only where there is a serious question to be tried (typically jurisdictional error) and the balance of convenience favours intervention — and even then the courts have been emphatic that “considerable caution” should attend any such order.3

What this means on a project

  • If a payment claim has been served and you want to dispute any part of it — including by way of set-off, back-charge, prior payment or quality argument — the place to do that is the payment schedule, on time. Once you are at the s 93 enforcement stage, those arguments are barred.
  • Don’t expect to resist enforcement by re-running the contract dispute. A defence framed as “we don’t actually owe this under the contract” is exactly what s 93(4)(a)(ii) is aimed at.
  • The realistic challenges at the enforcement stage are (a) jurisdictional error in the underlying determination, and (b) factual contentions about what is actually unpaid on the certificate (e.g. payments since made, accepted third-party payments).
  • Non-contractual causes of action — misleading or deceptive conduct under the Australian Consumer Law is the obvious one — can sometimes be run, because they are not “matters arising under the construction contract”. They are still hard to win on a summary-judgment timetable.
  • If you are commencing set-aside proceedings, budget for paying the unpaid portion into court. That is a precondition of running the proceeding, not an optional step. If you cannot fund it, an undertaking to do so or a bank guarantee may sometimes be acceptable, but you will need to negotiate it.
  • If you are the claimant, the discipline is the mirror image: keep your enforcement clean, file the certificate with a proper s 93(2) affidavit, and be prepared to resist any stay application by pointing to the legislative policy and the s 93(4)(b) security requirement.
  1. Marques Group Pty Ltd v Parkview Constructions Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 625 (Rees J), applying Bitannia Pty Ltd v Parkline Constructions Pty Ltd (2006) 67 NSWLR 9 — an Australian Consumer Law misleading-or-deceptive-conduct argument is not a defence in relation to a matter under the construction contract and so is not caught by the equivalent NSW bar.
  2. Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd (subject to Deed of Company Arrangement) v Linx Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWSC 366 (Wright J), affirmed Kennedy Civil Contracting Pty Ltd v Linx Constructions Pty Ltd [2024] NSWCA 243 — an accepted direct payment to a third-party supplier reducing the “unpaid portion of the claimed amount” is a factual determination, not an impermissible cross-claim or defence under the equivalent of s 93(4)(a).
  3. Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No 2) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 49 (Payne JA) — entry of judgment under the equivalent of s 93(1) is not precluded by a pending arbitration, and a stay until the end of arbitration would be contrary to the statutory purpose; Kuatro Build Pty Ltd v Elite Formwork Group Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 372 (Hmelnitsky J) — “considerable caution” required before staying enforcement; on the underlying balance-of-convenience principles see R J Neller Building Pty Ltd v Ainsworth [2008] QCA 397; [2009] 1 Qd R 390 (Keane JA, Fraser JA and Fryberg J agreeing) and the qualification for jurisdictional-error cases in BRB Modular Pty Ltd v AWX Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 222 (Bond J).

Enforcing an adjudicator’s decision

Statutory demand (insolvency pathway)

The court-judgment route under s 93 is not the only way to put pressure on an unpaid respondent that is a company. An adjudicated amount is a debt due and payable, and once it is unpaid you can use it as the foundation for a creditor’s statutory demand under s 459E of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth). If the company doesn’t pay or apply to set the demand aside within 21 days, it is presumed insolvent — which opens the door to a winding-up application. Used carefully, that pressure often produces payment faster than enforcement of a filed judgment.

Section 93 BIF Act — underlying enforcement provision

93 Filing of adjudication certificate as judgment debt

(1) An adjudication certificate may be filed as a judgment for a debt, and may be enforced, in a court of competent jurisdiction.

(2) An adjudication certificate can not be filed under this section unless it is accompanied by an affidavit by the claimant stating that the whole or a part of the adjudicated amount has not been paid to the claimant at the time the certificate is filed.

(3) If the affidavit states that part of the adjudicated amount has been paid, the judgment is for the unpaid part of the amount only.

How the security-of-payment debt fits the Corporations Act

Section 459H of the Corporations Act lets a company apply to set aside a statutory demand on the basis of a “genuine dispute” about the existence or amount of the debt, or because the company has an “offsetting claim”. The threshold for a genuine dispute is famously low — the company only needs to show that even one issue has “a sufficient degree of cogency to be arguable”.1 The question is how much of that machinery survives where the debt is one created by the security-of-payment regime.

The leading approach is the analysis of Brereton J in Re Douglas Aerospace Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 167. His Honour drew a sharp line between the two limbs of s 459H. A statutory debt under the security-of-payment legislation — for example, a debt that has crystallised because the respondent failed to give a payment schedule — is generally not capable of being the subject of a “genuine dispute”, because the underlying contractual arguments have been shut out by the Act itself. But the company can still raise an “offsetting claim”: a counterclaim, set-off or cross-demand that does not deny the debt but asserts a countervailing liability the claimant owes back. The position was put as follows:

some debts are not capable of genuine dispute — because they are judgment debts or conclusive debts — but may still be amenable to an offsetting claim. It is difficult to see how a debt arising under s 14(4) (“SOPA”), which creates a statutory liability upon the failure to provide a payment schedule, could be the subject of a genuine dispute, if the conditions in s 15(1) are satisfied, regardless of any underlying dispute. As it seems to me, the only way in which a “genuine dispute” could be raised in respect of such a debt would be by disputing whether the circumstances referred to in s 15(1) existed.

The practical effect for a Queensland project is that a claimant holding an unpaid adjudicated amount — or an unpaid scheduled amount — has a very strong base on which to issue a s 459E demand. The respondent cannot reopen the underlying construction contract dispute to manufacture a “genuine dispute”; it must either point to an offsetting claim, or argue that the threshold facts that make the debt a statutory debt are themselves not made out (for example, that the document relied on was not a valid payment claim, or that no contract existed in the first place).2

Drafting the demand — describe the debt accurately

A statutory demand can be defeated on its own face if it doesn’t make clear what the debt is and where it comes from. In Re Roberts Construction Group Pty Ltd [2024] VSC 679 a demand based on an unpaid scheduled amount under the Victorian SOP Act was set aside because it didn’t identify the “scheduled amount”, the document under which the amount was due, or the SOP Act itself. Steffensen AsJ treated that as a substantive defect: the recipient of a statutory demand is entitled to know unambiguously what kind of debt is being asserted and on what legal footing, otherwise it cannot meaningfully decide whether to pay, dispute or move to set aside within the 21-day window.

For an adjudicated amount under s 93 BIF Act, that means the demand should identify the payment claim, the adjudication application, the adjudicator’s decision (with reference number and date), the adjudicated amount and the due date for payment, and should expressly state that the debt arises under the BIF Act. If you have already filed the certificate as a judgment debt, the demand can rest on the judgment — which is conclusive and even harder to disturb.

What this means on a project

  • If the company that owes you an adjudicated amount looks marginal financially, a s 459E statutory demand is often a faster lever than an enforcement warrant. The 21-day clock and the insolvency presumption focus the mind.
  • Build the demand on the cleanest possible debt. A filed judgment under s 93 is strongest; an adjudicated amount that is overdue but not yet filed is next; an unpaid scheduled amount or unscheduled payment claim is workable but more vulnerable to argument about whether the threshold conditions were met.
  • Describe the debt in the demand with precision — identify the payment claim, the adjudication, the adjudicated amount, the due date, and that the debt is owed under the BIF Act. After Roberts Construction Group, vague or mis-described demands get set aside.
  • Don’t expect to brush away every set-aside application. The respondent cannot rely on the underlying contract dispute as a “genuine dispute”, but it can still raise a credible offsetting claim — for liquidated damages, defective work, or backcharges that exist independently of the payment-claim dispute. Be ready to meet that on its merits.
  • If you are the respondent on the receiving end of a demand based on an adjudicated amount, focus your set-aside application on either (a) a quantified offsetting claim supported by evidence, or (b) the threshold facts — was there a construction contract, was the document a valid payment claim, was the debt-creating condition under the Act actually satisfied. A general protest that “we dispute the work” will not get you across the line.
  1. CGI Information Systems & Management Consultants Pty Ltd v APRA Consulting Pty Ltd [2003] NSWSC 728 (Barrett J) — the “genuine dispute” threshold is not demanding; once the company shows even one issue with sufficient cogency to be arguable, a finding of genuine dispute must follow. Cited in In the matter of Rockwall Homes Pty Limited [2017] NSWSC 223 (Black J).
  2. For the line of authority applying Re Douglas Aerospace Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 167 (Brereton J) in the security-of-payment context, see Diploma Construction (WA) Pty Ltd v KPA Architects Pty Ltd [2014] WASCA 91; Re J Group Constructions Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 1607 (Robb J) — following Douglas Aerospace while flagging “reasons for doubting” the wholesale application of Deputy Commissioner of Taxation v Broadbeach Properties Pty Ltd [2008] HCA 41; (2008) 237 CLR 473 to security-of-payment debts; In the matter of Rockwall Homes Pty Limited [2017] NSWSC 223 (Black J) — threshold facts (such as whether the recipient was a party to a construction contract) remain open to dispute.

Enforcing an adjudicator’s decision

Stays and injunctions

A respondent who wants to stop the claimant filing or enforcing an adjudication certificate has to ask the court for an interlocutory injunction or a stay. The Act doesn’t hand that out for the asking. The application is decided on ordinary equitable principles — prima facie case and balance of convenience — but read against a statutory policy that has already weighed the risk and assigned most of it to the respondent.

Section 93 BIF Act — the enforcement provision the stay would interrupt

93 Filing of adjudication certificate as judgment debt

(1) An adjudication certificate may be filed as a judgment for a debt, and may be enforced, in a court of competent jurisdiction.

(4) If the respondent commences proceedings to have the judgment set aside—

(b) the respondent is required to pay into the court, as security, the unpaid portion of the adjudicated amount pending the final decision in those proceedings.

The test — prima facie case and balance of convenience

The starting point is Australian Broadcasting Corporation v O’Neill [2006] HCA 46; (2006) 227 CLR 57. To obtain an interlocutory injunction restraining the filing or enforcement of an adjudication certificate, the applicant has to show a prima facie case and that the balance of convenience favours the grant of relief. “Prima facie case” here doesn’t mean it is more probable than not that the applicant will win at trial — it is enough to show a sufficient likelihood of success to justify preserving the status quo pending the trial.1

That part of the test is conventional. What makes stays and injunctions in the BIF Act context distinctive is the way the second limb — balance of convenience — gets read in light of the legislation’s purpose.

The Neller policy — cash flow, and where the risk sits

The leading Queensland authority is R J Neller Building Pty Ltd v Ainsworth [2008] QCA 397; [2009] 1 Qd R 390. The Court of Appeal decided that the legislative policy — an assured cash flow to the contractor, with the risk of later repayment failure assigned to the respondent — is itself a relevant factor going into the balance of convenience. Keane JA (Fraser JA and Fryberg J agreeing) put it this way:

The BCIP Act proceeds on the assumption that the interruption of a builder’s cash flow may cause the financial failure of the builder before the rights and wrongs of claim and counterclaim between builder and owner can be finally determined by the courts… the risk that a builder might not be able to refund moneys ultimately found to be due to a non-residential owner after a successful action by the owner must, I think, be regarded as a risk which, as a matter of policy in the commercial context in which the BCIP Act applies, the legislature has, prima facie at least, assigned to the owner.

The practical effect on a stay application is significant. A respondent who simply argues “we will not get our money back if we lose at final hearing” is unlikely to succeed — that is the very risk Parliament has already allocated to the respondent. The respondent has to point to something more, and the obvious candidate is a prima facie case that the underlying determination is void for jurisdictional error.2

The jurisdictional-error qualification

Where the applicant’s case is that the determination is affected by jurisdictional error, the Neller policy doesn’t apply with the same force. In BRB Modular Pty Ltd v AWX Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 222 Bond J drew the distinction expressly: the policy of the Act, in its full force, applies to valid adjudication decisions made within jurisdiction; it could not have been intended to allocate the same risk in relation to decisions which are void and liable to be quashed. Where the applicant has established a prima facie case of jurisdictional error, the Act’s policy still characterises the nature of the right an injunction would interfere with — it just doesn’t answer the balance-of-convenience question on its own.3

The common landing point in those cases is conditional relief: the court will restrain enforcement, but only on terms that the disputed amount is paid into court (or secured by bank guarantee, or covered by an undertaking to pay in) pending final determination. That mirrors the s 93(4)(b) regime that would otherwise apply if the judgment were already on foot.4

Recent appellate caution

The recent New South Wales authorities have hardened against soft stay applications. In Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No 2) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 49 the Court of Appeal confirmed that entry of judgment is not precluded by a pending arbitration or by mere repayment risk, and that a stay running to the end of an arbitration would be contrary to the statutory purpose. Applying that line in Kuatro Build Pty Ltd v Elite Formwork Group Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 372 Hmelnitsky J reiterated that “considerable caution should attend the grant of such an injunction or a stay”, and refused to continue an interim stay where the claimant’s financial evidence was unchallenged and the respondent’s insolvency concerns were not made out.5 The same general framework has been applied to refuse stays in NSW where a stay would convert what the Act intends as an interim payment into a final one without the statutory criteria being met.6

What this means on a project

  • If you are the respondent and you want to stop the certificate being filed or enforced, plan the application around the two limbs in O’Neill — a prima facie case (realistically, a jurisdictional-error case) and a balance of convenience that overcomes the Neller policy.
  • Don’t rely on “we might not get the money back”. After Neller, that risk has been pre-assigned to you by the legislature. You need a sharper case — usually a serious question that the determination is void.
  • Be ready to put the disputed amount into court, or to offer a bank guarantee or undertaking to do so. That is the standard condition of conditional relief, and it is also the substance of s 93(4)(b) once a judgment is in place.
  • Move quickly. Stays are interlocutory and discretionary — the more the underlying issue can be readied for an early final hearing, the more likely the balance of convenience tips your way.
  • If you are the claimant, resist on the policy and the recent appellate guidance. Martinus and Kuatro Build make clear that “considerable caution” attends the grant of a stay and that arbitration timetables and bare repayment risk are not enough to displace the Act’s cash-flow purpose.
  1. Australian Broadcasting Corporation v O’Neill [2006] HCA 46; (2006) 227 CLR 57 — the two-limb interlocutory injunction test (prima facie case; balance of convenience) and the gloss that “prima facie case” means a sufficient likelihood of success to justify preserving the status quo. Applied in the security-of-payment context in, among others, Parkview Constructions Pty Limited v Total Lifestyle Windows Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1911 (Slattery J) — injunction granted where there was a serious factual issue about service of the adjudication application and the matter could be readied quickly for hearing.
  2. Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Sun Engineering (Qld) Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 170 (Daubney J) — the Neller policy survives Kirk v Industrial Court of New South Wales [2010] HCA 1; (2010) 239 CLR 531; Sunshine Coast Regional Council v Earthpro Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 271, [15], [23] (Peter Lyons J) — describing the relevant repayment risk in terms of “substantial risk”.
  3. BRB Modular Pty Ltd v AWX Constructions Pty Ltd & Ors [2015] QSC 222 (Bond J) — respectfully disagreeing with Wiggins Island Coal Export Terminal Pty Ltd v Sun Engineering (Qld) Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 170 to the extent it treated the Neller policy as applying with full force to determinations affected by jurisdictional error; Low v MCC Pty Ltd & Ors; MCC Pty Ltd v Low [2018] QSC 6 (Jackson J) — injunction granted where there was a serious question about whether a valid reference date had accrued, on an undertaking to lodge a bank guarantee.
  4. Filadelfia Projects Pty Limited v EntirlTy Business Services [2010] NSWSC 473; Nazero Group Pty Limited v Top Quality Construction Pty Ltd [2015] NSWSC 232; Milburn Lake Pty Ltd v Andritz Pty Ltd [2016] VSC 3 — conditional injunctive relief on payment into court (or an undertaking, where present payment is not possible: Filadelfia); Hakea Holdings Pty Limited v Denham Constructions Pty Ltd; BaptistCare NSW & ACT v Denham Constructions Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1120 (Ball J) — injunction continued where the applicants showed prima facie cases and the respondent’s solvency was a real concern; Richard Crookes Constructions Pty Ltd v CES Projects (Aust) Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 1119 (McDougall J) — same approach where the adjudicator had arguably failed to perform the statutory function of valuing the work; Superb Build Pty Ltd v Petrosyan [2023] NSWDC 2 (Andronos J) — interim stay extended on the Hakea factors.
  5. Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No 2) Pty Ltd [2025] NSWCA 49 (Payne JA) — entry of judgment under the equivalent of s 93(1) is not precluded by a pending arbitration or mere repayment risk; Kuatro Build Pty Ltd v Elite Formwork Group Pty Ltd [2025] NSWSC 372 (Hmelnitsky J) — applying Martinus Rail Pty Ltd v Qube RE Services (No 2) [2024] NSWSC 1223 and Martinus [2025] NSWCA 49 in refusing to continue a stay.
  6. Bellerive Homes Pty Ltd v FW Projects Pty Ltd [2018] NSWSC 1435 (N Adams J) — stay refused; the discretion will be exercised in favour of a stay only in limited categories (failure to stay would convert an interim payment into a final one, substantial risk of irrecoverable money paired with proceedings on foot for final resolution, builder tactics calculated to delay, or post-contract restructuring increasing the risk to the owner). Paul Kennedy McCullough trading as The Green Side Up v New Start Australia Pty Limited [2023] NSWDC 82 (Dicker SC DCJ) — a stay should not be granted unless failure to do so would make permanent what the Act intended to be only interim and there is a likelihood of irreparable prejudice, citing Veolia Water Solutions & Technologies (Australia) Pty Ltd v Kruger Engineering Australia Pty Ltd (No 3) [2007] NSWSC 459.

Statutory suspension of works

Trigger — failure to pay scheduled amount

The right to suspend work under the BIF Act is not free-standing. It hangs off s 78 — the section that tells you what your options are when a respondent doesn’t pay an “amount owed” on a payment claim. Before you can serve a notice of intention to suspend under s 98, you need to be in the position s 78 contemplates: a payment claim has been made, the due date has passed, and the respondent hasn’t paid the amount owed in full. This page deals with the trigger where the respondent did issue a payment schedule but then failed to pay the scheduled amount by the due date.

Section 78(2)(a) BIF Act — recovery of unpaid scheduled amount

(1) This section applies if a respondent given a payment claim for a progress payment does not pay the amount owed to the claimant in full on or before the due date for the progress payment.

(2) The claimant may either—

(a) recover the unpaid portion of the amount owed from the respondent, as a debt owing to the claimant, in a court of competent jurisdiction; or

(b) apply for adjudication of the payment claim under part 4.

(3) In addition to the action mentioned in subsection (2), the claimant may give the respondent written notice of the claimant’s intention to suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under the relevant construction contract under section 98.

(5) In this section—

amount owed, to a claimant for a payment claim, means—

(a) if the respondent did not respond to the payment claim with a payment schedule as required under section 76—the amount claimed under the payment claim; or

(b) if the respondent did respond to the payment claim with a payment schedule as required to do so under section 76—the amount proposed to be paid under the payment schedule.

What the trigger looks like

The scheduled-amount trigger has three moving parts. First, you served a valid payment claim under s 75. Second, the respondent responded with a payment schedule under s 76, and that schedule proposed to pay some figure — often less than what you claimed, but a positive figure all the same. Third, the due date for the progress payment came and went and the respondent didn’t pay the scheduled amount in full. At that point s 78(1) is engaged, and s 78(5)(b) tells you the “amount owed” for the purposes of the section is the amount the respondent itself proposed to pay. That is the unpaid figure you can recover as a debt under s 78(2)(a), pursue in adjudication under s 78(2)(b), and use as the foundation for a notice of intention to suspend under s 78(3) and s 98.

The point worth holding onto is that the respondent has, by issuing the payment schedule, conceded the scheduled amount as payable. There is no live dispute about that figure — the dispute, if any, is about the gap between the claim and the schedule. So the s 78(2)(a) debt remedy on a scheduled amount is, in practice, a strong claim: you are recovering money the respondent has admitted it owes.

The election in s 78(2) and the suspension overlay

Section 78(2) presents the two recovery routes — debt action or adjudication — as alternatives. The leading appellate analysis is Seymour Whyte Constructions Pty Ltd v Ostwald Bros Pty Ltd (In liquidation) [2019] NSWCA 11, decided on the materially identical NSW provision. Ostwald had lodged an adjudication application that turned out to be invalid, and then sought to recover the scheduled amount as a debt due. Seymour Whyte argued the two limbs of s 78(2) were mutually exclusive and that, by going to adjudication first, Ostwald had elected and was locked out of the debt route. The Court of Appeal accepted the election principle in general terms — once an adjudication application has actually been “made”, the claimant cannot also pursue the unpaid scheduled amount as a debt — but held that an invalid application is not a “made” application for these purposes. So Ostwald was still entitled to debt recovery under the equivalent of s 78(2)(a).

where a claimant makes an adjudication application they are subsequently precluded from attempting, in addition, to recover the unpaid portion of the scheduled amount from the respondent as a debt due to the claimant.

The suspension right in s 78(3) sits outside that election. It is expressed as “in addition to” the action mentioned in s 78(2), so choosing the debt route or the adjudication route does not consume the right to give a notice of intention to suspend. You can sue for the scheduled amount and serve a s 98 notice; you can lodge an adjudication application and serve a s 98 notice. The trigger for the notice is the same underlying failure to pay the amount owed by the due date.

Defending the debt claim — what doesn’t work

Because the scheduled amount has been admitted by the respondent, defences to a s 78(2)(a) debt claim are narrow. The point is illustrated by Marques Group Pty Ltd v Parkview Constructions Pty Ltd [2023] NSWSC 625, where Rees J considered a respondent’s defence on a summary judgment application brought under the NSW equivalent of s 78(2)(a). Parkview argued the claimant had engaged in misleading and deceptive conduct by stating in the statutory declaration accompanying the payment claim that subcontractors had been paid (which was untrue), and that but for that conduct it would have scheduled nil. Rees J described the defence as “inherently unattractive” and as appearing to “strike at the heart” of the security of payment scheme — the very point of the scheme is that having proposed a figure to pay, you pay it — but found that on the strict summary-judgment threshold she could not say it was “so clearly untenable that it cannot possibly succeed”. The application for summary judgment was struck out at [20]. The takeaway for a claimant is that misleading-conduct style defences will rarely succeed at trial, but they are difficult to dispose of summarily; budget for the time and cost.

Court of competent jurisdiction

If you choose the debt route, s 78(2)(a) directs you to a “court of competent jurisdiction”. In Queensland, that means the Magistrates Court, District Court or Supreme Court depending on the size of the unpaid scheduled amount.1 The dollar figure in the payment schedule sets your jurisdiction; you don’t need to add interest or costs to clear a threshold.

What this means on a project

  • Check the basics first. There must be a valid payment claim under s 75, a payment schedule under s 76, and a due date that has passed without the scheduled amount being paid in full. If any one of those is missing, s 78 is not engaged and the suspension trigger is not available on this footing.
  • Identify the “amount owed” precisely. Under s 78(5)(b) it is the amount the respondent proposed to pay in its schedule, not the amount you originally claimed. That figure drives the debt action, the adjudication application, and the notice of intention to suspend.
  • The suspension right under s 78(3) is “in addition to” the recovery routes in s 78(2). You don’t lose the ability to serve a s 98 notice by choosing to sue, or by going to adjudication.
  • Don’t run a debt action and an adjudication for the same scheduled amount in parallel. Once an adjudication application has been validly made, the s 78(2)(a) debt route is closed for that amount; only an invalid application leaves the debt route open.
  • If you are the respondent and the cash isn’t there to pay the scheduled amount by the due date, expect the worst combination — a debt claim or an adjudication for the admitted figure, plus a notice of intention to suspend on top. The defences are narrow because you have already conceded the figure in your own schedule.
  1. Northside Roofing Pty Ltd v Pires Constructions Pty Ltd [2007] QDC 172 (Durward SC DCJ) — on the BCIPA predecessor to s 78, the only courts of competent jurisdiction to enforce the statutory debt are the Magistrates Court, District Court and Supreme Court.
  2. Kyle Bay Removals Pty Ltd v Dynabuild Project Services Pty Ltd [2016] NSWSC 334 (Meagher JA) — where no valid payment claim has been served, liability to pay does not attach and there can be no binding election between the two limbs of s 78(2). A premature court action on an invalid claim does not preclude a later adjudication when a valid claim is made.
  3. Canterbury-Bankstown Council v Payce Communities Pty Ltd [2019] NSWSC 1419 (Henry J) — running court proceedings under the NSW equivalent of s 78 while the dispute is also before the Supreme Court is not, without more, an abuse of process; the threshold is “exceptional circumstances” and the onus on the respondent is “a heavy one”.

Statutory suspension of works

Trigger — failure to pay adjudicated amount

Once an adjudicator has decided what the respondent owes, the claimant has a self-help remedy under the BIF Act if the cash doesn’t turn up. Section 92 lets you suspend work or the supply of related goods and services after a respondent fails to pay an adjudicated amount, provided you give the right notice first. It is one of two statutory triggers that unlock the suspension regime in s 98 — the other (failure to pay a scheduled amount) is dealt with on the next page.

Section 92 BIF Act — consequence of not paying adjudicated amount

92 Consequence of not paying adjudicated amount

(1) If the respondent fails to pay the whole or any part of the adjudicated amount to the claimant as required under section 90, the claimant may give the respondent written notice of the claimant’s intention to suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under the construction contract under section 98.

(2) The notice about suspending work must state that it is made under this Act.

What has to have happened

The trigger in s 92(1) has three moving parts and all of them have to be in place before the notice can validly go out.

  • An adjudicated amount. The adjudicator must have decided an amount is payable to the claimant under Part 4. A bare s 88 decision in your favour is enough — you don’t need to have registered an adjudication certificate as a judgment first.
  • A failure to pay under s 90. Section 90 fixes when the adjudicated amount is due. The respondent must have failed to pay “the whole or any part” of it by that date. Partial payment is enough to keep the trigger live for whatever balance remains.
  • Written notice of intention to suspend. The claimant must give the respondent a written notice that it intends to suspend under s 98, and the notice must state on its face that it is made under the Act.

Each of those is a precondition. If the adjudicator hasn’t decided an amount, or the s 90 due date hasn’t passed, or the notice doesn’t identify the Act, the s 98 right to down tools hasn’t crystallised and any walk-off is a contractual breach rather than a statutory suspension.

The link to s 90 and s 98

Section 92 doesn’t suspend anything by itself. It is an enabling provision: it tells you that, if the respondent has missed the s 90 due date for the adjudicated amount, you may give a notice of intention to suspend and that — once the timing in s 98 is satisfied — you may then actually stop work. The architecture is deliberately staged so the respondent gets a written warning and a window to pay before the site goes quiet.

Two practical consequences flow from that staging. First, the s 92 notice is the gateway to s 98; without it, you have no right to suspend on this trigger no matter how overdue the payment is. Second, because s 92(1) speaks of “the whole or any part” of the adjudicated amount, you can issue the notice even where the respondent has paid some of the determination but is short on the rest. You don’t need to wait for a complete default.

Form and content of the notice

The Act prescribes very little about the form of the s 92 notice itself. It must be in writing, it must communicate an intention to suspend carrying out construction work (or supplying related goods and services) under the contract, and — under s 92(2) — it must state that it is made under the Act. The detail of what the notice should say, and the mechanics of how the suspension itself works, sit on the dedicated “Form of suspension notice” and “When suspension may begin” pages.

One drafting point worth emphasising at the trigger stage: don’t blend the s 92 notice with a generic contractual default notice or a demand letter. The statutory notice has a specific function — arming you with the s 98 right to suspend — and a respondent challenging a later suspension will go straight to whether the document on its face does what s 92 requires. Keeping the s 92 notice as a discrete, clearly-labelled document is the simplest way to put that issue beyond argument.

What this means on a project

  • Diary the s 90 due date for the adjudicated amount the moment the determination is published. The s 92 trigger only opens once that date has passed without full payment.
  • Don’t suspend on the back of an unpaid determination without first issuing the s 92 notice. The notice is a precondition to the s 98 right; suspending without it converts a statutory remedy into a repudiation risk.
  • Make sure the notice is in writing, identifies the contract and the adjudicated amount that is unpaid, and states expressly that it is given under the BIF Act. The s 92(2) requirement that the notice state it is made under the Act is short, specific and easy to overlook.
  • Partial payment doesn’t close the door. If the respondent has paid some but not all of the adjudicated amount by the s 90 due date, the trigger is still available for the unpaid balance.
  • Treat the s 92 notice as a standalone document, not a paragraph buried in a default letter. The cleaner the notice, the harder it is to attack at the s 98 stage.
  1. Section 90 BIF Act fixes the date by which the respondent must pay the adjudicated amount; the s 92 trigger turns on a failure to pay by that date. Section 98 then sets out the rights that flow from a notice given under s 92, including when suspension may begin.
  2. Section 92(2) BIF Act — the notice must state that it is made under the Act; this is a content requirement of the notice itself, not merely good practice.

Statutory suspension of works

Notice of intention to suspend (2 BD)

The right to walk off site under the BIF Act is not self-executing. Before you can lawfully suspend, you must first give the respondent a written notice of your intention to suspend, and then wait at least 2 business days. The notice has to do two things at once: it must come from a triggering non-payment event under s 78 or s 92, and it must say on its face that it is given under this Act. Get either of those wrong and the suspension that follows is just a breach of contract.

Section 78(3)–(4) BIF Act — notice after non-payment of a payment claim

(3) In addition to the action mentioned in subsection (2), the claimant may give the respondent written notice of the claimant’s intention to suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under the relevant construction contract under section 98.

(4) The notice to suspend work must state that it is made under this Act.

Section 92 BIF Act — notice after non-payment of an adjudicated amount

(1) If the respondent fails to pay the whole or any part of the adjudicated amount to the claimant as required under section 90, the claimant may give the respondent written notice of the claimant’s intention to suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under the construction contract under section 98.

(2) The notice about suspending work must state that it is made under this Act.

What the notice has to do

The notice is the gateway to the suspension right in s 98. Section 98(1) only allows you to down tools once at least 2 business days have passed since you gave the respondent notice of intention to do so “under section 78 or 92”. Two consequences follow. First, you cannot give a valid notice in the abstract — it has to be anchored to either a s 78 trigger (non-payment of an amount owed under a payment claim, on or before the due date) or a s 92 trigger (non-payment of the whole or part of an adjudicated amount as required under s 90). Second, the notice itself has to state that it is made under this Act. That sentence is not optional dressing; it is what converts an ordinary commercial letter into a statutory notice that engages the s 98 right.

The clearest way to comply is to spell out the trigger and the statutory basis on the face of the document — for example, “This is a notice given under s 78(3) of the Building Industry Fairness (Security of Payment) Act 2017 (Qld) of the claimant’s intention to suspend carrying out construction work under the construction contract under s 98 of that Act.” A notice that is silent on the Act, or that is framed only as a contractual default notice, runs the obvious risk of being treated as something other than the statutory notice the Act requires.

The 2 business day clock

Section 98(1) requires “at least 2 business days” to have passed since the notice was given. The day the notice is given is day zero; the next business day is day one. Suspension cannot lawfully begin until at least the day after day two — in practical terms, you should not suspend earlier than the third business day after the day of the notice. Weekends and Queensland public holidays do not count as business days, and neither do the days in the statutory end-of-year shutdown period under the Act. If your notice goes out late on a Friday afternoon before a public holiday Monday, the earliest you can stop work is materially later than “in two days’ time”.

What “payment” means for the trigger

Whether the s 78 or s 92 trigger has actually been met turns on whether the respondent has “paid” the relevant amount. The leading reading of that word in this context is Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council (No 2) [2004] NSWSC 1160, where Master Macready treated “to pay” as the act of a debtor transferring by way of satisfaction whatever is owed to a creditor:

To give (a person) what is due in discharge of a debt, or as a return for services done, or goods received, or in compensation for injury done; to remunerate, recompense.” In turn, ‘payment’ is defined as “[t]he action, or an act, of paying.” From this it can readily be concluded that the touchstone of the ordinary and natural meaning of the expressions ‘to pay’ and ‘receives payment’ in the present context is the act of a debtor transferring by way of satisfaction whatever is owed by him or her to a creditor.

The point matters for the notice because money tendered on conditions — for example, against a secured bank guarantee, or with strings that prevent the claimant obtaining the benefit of the funds — is unlikely to amount to “payment” for s 78 or s 92 purposes. If the respondent has wired the amount but locked the claimant out of using it, the trigger for the notice is still alive.1

Once the notice is given, when does the right end?

The notice is the entry point, but its effect runs through to the duration of the suspension itself. Section 98(2) ties the right to suspend to the day the claimant receives payment of the amount mentioned in s 78(1) or s 92(1), with a further 3 business days’ tail. The appellate gloss on what counts as the end of the right is in On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163, where Crowe AJ held that, following an adjudication determination in favour of a claimant, the right to suspend continues until either the outstanding amount is set aside or there is an unequivocal communication acknowledging discharge of the payment claim, adjudicated amount or provisional judgment debt.2 The practical message for the claimant drafting the notice is to be precise about which trigger is engaged, because the trigger you nominate is the trigger you will need to show has been discharged before your suspension right falls away.

What this means on a project

  • You cannot lawfully suspend without first giving the s 78 or s 92 notice and waiting out the 2 business day period in s 98(1). A “suspension” without that notice is just a walk-off and a likely breach of contract.
  • State on the face of the notice that it is made under the BIF Act, and identify the trigger — either non-payment of the amount owed under s 78(1), or non-payment of the adjudicated amount under s 92(1). Don’t leave the statutory character of the notice to inference.
  • Diary the 2 business day period carefully. The day of the notice is day zero, weekends and Queensland public holidays do not count, and the end-of-year shutdown period drops out as well.
  • If the respondent has tendered money on conditions that stop you obtaining the benefit of it, you have probably not been “paid” for s 78 or s 92 purposes — the trigger for the notice is still live. Get advice before treating a conditional tender as discharging the trigger.
  • Keep proof of how and when the notice was given. The validity of any later suspension turns on whether the 2 business day clock actually started, which in turn requires you to show the date the notice was given to the respondent.
  1. Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council (No 2) [2004] NSWSC 1160 (Master Macready) — ordinary meaning of “pay” and “payment” in the security of payment context; conditional tender does not necessarily amount to payment where the claimant cannot obtain the benefit of the funds.
  2.
