On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163 (Crowe AJ) — on the equivalent of s 98(1), the right to suspend following non-payment of an adjudicated amount continues until the amount is set aside or there is an unequivocal communication acknowledging discharge, with the further 3 business day tail running from that point.
  3. The s 78 and s 92 triggers are dealt with separately on the “trigger — scheduled amount” and “trigger — adjudicated amount” pages; the form of the notice itself, the time at which suspension may begin, the protections during suspension, and resumption are dealt with on the related pages in this chapter.

Statutory suspension of works

Form and content of the notice

The right to down tools under the BIF Act doesn’t arise on its own. It is unlocked by a written notice of intention to suspend — given under s 78 (where a respondent has failed to pay a scheduled amount) or s 92 (where a respondent has failed to pay an adjudicated amount) — followed by a 2 business day waiting period. Get the notice wrong, and any subsequent suspension is a breach of contract rather than a protected statutory step.

Section 98 BIF Act — Claimant’s right to suspend work

(1) A claimant may suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under a construction contract if at least 2 business days have passed since the claimant gave notice of intention to do so to the respondent under section 78 or 92.

(2) The right conferred under subsection (1) exists until the day on which the claimant receives payment from the respondent of the amount mentioned in section 78(1) or 92(1), and continues for another 3 business days immediately following that day.

(3) If, in exercising the right to suspend carrying out construction work or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract, the claimant incurs a loss or expense because the respondent removes any part of the work or supply from the contract, the respondent is liable to pay the claimant the amount of the loss or expense.

(4) A claimant who suspends carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract under subsection (1) is not liable for any loss or damage suffered by the respondent, or by any person claiming through the respondent, because of the claimant not carrying out that work or not supplying those goods and services, during the suspension.

Which notice you are giving

Section 98 (1) requires the notice to be given “under section 78 or 92”. Those are two different gateways and they correspond to two different unpaid amounts:

  • s 78 notice — given where the respondent has not paid the scheduled amount (a payment schedule was issued but the scheduled amount wasn’t paid by the due date, or the respondent failed to schedule and didn’t pay the claimed amount).
  • s 92 notice — given where the respondent has not paid an adjudicated amount by the due date for payment of that amount.

The notice itself should state which provision it is given under. Drafting a single “notice of intention to suspend” without identifying whether it relies on the s 78 or the s 92 pathway invites a respondent to argue the notice is non-compliant. If both gateways are open on the facts — for example a scheduled amount unpaid and later adjudicated — identify the operative one based on the amount you are actually pursuing.

What the notice must convey

The Act doesn’t prescribe a form. But s 98 (1) demands that the document be a “notice of intention” to suspend, given to the respondent, under s 78 or s 92. To do its work the notice should:

  • identify the construction contract and the parties;
  • identify the unpaid amount — the scheduled amount (s 78) or the adjudicated amount (s 92) — and the date by which it should have been paid;
  • state expressly that, if the amount is not paid, the claimant intends to suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under the contract;
  • state the statutory basis (s 78 or s 92, and s 98); and
  • be dated and given to the respondent in a way you can later prove (email with delivery confirmation, or service in accordance with any contractual notice clause, recorded on the file).

The 2 business day clock in s 98 (1) runs from when the notice is “given”, so the date of giving has to be capable of proof. If the notice is delivered late in the day, count from the next business day to be safe.

What “payment” ends the suspension

The notice triggers a window that closes only on receipt of the unpaid amount, plus 3 further business days under s 98 (2). The leading reading of “payment” in this regime is Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council (No 2) [2004] NSWSC 1160, where Master Macready treated the touchstone as the actual transfer of value to the claimant:

the touchstone of the ordinary and natural meaning of the expressions ‘to pay’ and ‘receives payment’ in the present context is the act of a debtor transferring by way of satisfaction whatever is owed by him or her to a creditor.

His Honour added that a claimant will not have “received payment” where conditions placed on the funds — such as a secured bank guarantee — prevent the claimant from obtaining the benefit of them. So a notice is not spent simply because the respondent has put money somewhere; it is spent only when the claimant can actually use it.

The same point was reinforced in On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163, where Crowe AJ held that the right to suspend continues until either the underlying amount is set aside, or there is an unequivocal communication acknowledging discharge of the payment claim, adjudicated amount, or provisional judgment debt.1 The practical lesson for the notice drafter is that, once the notice has done its work, the claimant’s suspension right keeps running until something concrete — payment in fact, or a clear setting-aside — closes it out. You don’t need to issue a fresh notice every few days.

What this means on a project

  • Use a clean, dated, written notice. Identify the contract, the unpaid amount, and the statutory basis (s 78 or s 92, and s 98). Keep it short.
  • Send it in a way you can prove. Email with read receipt, or service per the contract’s notice clause. The 2 business day clock under s 98 (1) only starts when you can show the notice was given.
  • Don’t suspend before the 2 business days have actually passed. A premature suspension is unprotected by s 98 (4) and exposes you to a damages claim from the respondent.
  • Once you suspend, don’t stand the suspension down on a vague promise of payment. Under Beckhaus, “received payment” means money you can actually use; conditional or encumbered transfers don’t end the s 98 window.
  • If the respondent removes scope from the contract while you are lawfully suspended, log the loss or expense — s 98 (3) gives you a direct statutory entitlement to recover it.
  1. On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163 (Crowe AJ) was decided under s 26(2)(b) of the ACT security of payment Act, the equivalent of s 98 (1) of the BIF Act; Crowe AJ held that constructive receipt of the adjudicated amount being set aside occurred only after consent orders were made, and that the right to suspend then ended 3 business days after that date, mirroring s 98 (2).

Statutory suspension of works

When suspension may begin

If a respondent has not paid the scheduled or adjudicated amount on time, the BIF Act gives a claimant a statutory right to down tools. The right is powerful but it is gated — you cannot simply walk off site the day after payment is due. Section 98 sets out exactly when the right switches on, how long it runs, and what protection you carry with you while the suspension is in force.

Section 98 BIF Act — Claimant’s right to suspend work

(1) A claimant may suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under a construction contract if at least 2 business days have passed since the claimant gave notice of intention to do so to the respondent under section 78 or 92.

(2) The right conferred under subsection (1) exists until the day on which the claimant receives payment from the respondent of the amount mentioned in section 78(1) or 92(1), and continues for another 3 business days immediately following that day.

(3) If, in exercising the right to suspend carrying out construction work or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract, the claimant incurs a loss or expense because the respondent removes any part of the work or supply from the contract, the respondent is liable to pay the claimant the amount of the loss or expense.

(4) A claimant who suspends carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract under subsection (1) is not liable for any loss or damage suffered by the respondent, or by any person claiming through the respondent, because of the claimant not carrying out that work or not supplying those goods and services, during the suspension.

The two business day notice gate

The right to suspend does not arise the moment payment is missed. Section 98(1) requires the claimant to first give a notice of intention to suspend — under s 78 if the respondent has failed to pay a scheduled amount, or under s 92 if the respondent has failed to pay an adjudicated amount — and then to wait at least 2 business days from the giving of that notice. Only after those 2 business days have run can work lawfully stop. Walk off earlier and you lose the statutory protection in s 98(4); the suspension is then just a breach of contract by you.

The other prerequisite is hidden in the cross-reference. A s 78 or s 92 notice can only be given where the underlying conditions are satisfied — broadly, a payment claim that has crystallised into a debt (a scheduled amount that has gone unpaid by the due date, or an adjudicated amount the respondent has failed to pay). Suspension is a remedy that sits on top of an established non-payment; it is not a self-help tool for live valuation disputes.

How long the right lasts — and what counts as “payment”

Under s 98(2) the right runs until the day the claimant “receives payment” of the s 78(1) or s 92(1) amount, and then for a further 3 business days. The 3-day tail is deliberate — it gives the claimant time to remobilise rather than being expected to be back on the tools the same afternoon the funds clear.

What counts as “receives payment” was considered by Master Macready in Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council (No 2) [2004] NSWSC 1160. Drawing on the dictionary meaning of “pay” — “[t]o give (a person) what is due in discharge of a debt… to remunerate, recompense” — his Honour held that the touchstone is the act of a debtor transferring by way of satisfaction whatever is owed by him or her to a creditor, and that a claimant will not have “received payment” in circumstances where the claimant is unable to obtain the benefit of that payment due to conditions placed on its receipt (such as the provision of a secured bank guarantee). The practical point: a payment that lands in your account encumbered by conditions that prevent you from drawing on it does not start the 3-business-day clock.

The point was taken further in On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163. Crowe AJ held, in respect of the ACT equivalent of s 98(1), that a claimant’s right to suspend continues until either the outstanding amount is set aside or there is an unequivocal communication acknowledging discharge of the payment claim, adjudicated amount, or in that case the provisional judgment debt. A subsequent expert determination during litigation, which concluded the claimant in fact owed the respondent more than the adjudicated amount, did not end the suspension right; the right ran on until consent orders setting aside the adjudication amount were made, and then for 3 business days after.1

Protection while suspended

While the suspension is on foot, s 98(4) provides that the claimant is not liable for any loss or damage suffered by the respondent (or anyone claiming through the respondent) because the work is not being carried out. That immunity is the whole point of the section — without it, the principal could simply call the suspension a repudiation and terminate. Section 98(3) goes the other way: if the respondent uses the suspension as cover to descope or remove work from the contract, the respondent must pay the claimant for any loss or expense caused by that descoping.

What this means on a project

  • Do not stop work the day payment is missed. The right to suspend only arises once you have served a s 78 or s 92 notice of intention to suspend and at least 2 business days have passed since you gave it.
  • Diary the notice carefully. Record the date and time of service, the mode of service, and count business days from the day after service. If you down tools too early, s 98(4) does not protect you.
  • The right runs until you receive payment of the scheduled or adjudicated amount — plus 3 business days. Use that tail to remobilise; you are not expected to be back on site the moment funds clear.
  • “Receives payment” means money you can actually use. A transfer that lands subject to conditions you cannot satisfy (a bank guarantee or similar) does not start the 3-day clock.
  • If the respondent removes scope while you are off the tools, s 98(3) entitles you to your loss or expense from the descoping. Capture the costs (demobilisation, idle plant, restocking) contemporaneously.
  • If the respondent disputes that you ever received payment or argues the amount has been set aside, hold position until there is an unequivocal acknowledgement of discharge or a court order setting the amount aside — the right does not lapse on the strength of a contrary expert determination.
  1. On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163 (Crowe AJ) considered s 26(2)(b) of the ACT Act, the equivalent of s 98(1) of the BIF Act, and is persuasive on the duration of the suspension right under the BIF Act.

Statutory suspension of works

Protection from liability while suspended

If you have validly suspended work under s 98, the Act gives you two things you wouldn’t otherwise have at general law: a positive immunity from liability for loss the respondent suffers during the suspension, and a right to be paid back any loss or expense you incur if the respondent uses the suspension as cover to take work out of the contract. The trade-off is that the right is time-limited — it runs only until the respondent actually pays, plus three more business days — so getting the start and end dates right matters.

Section 98 BIF Act — claimant’s right to suspend work

98 Claimant’s right to suspend work

(1) A claimant may suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under a construction contract if at least 2 business days have passed since the claimant gave notice of intention to do so to the respondent under section 78 or 92.

(2) The right conferred under subsection (1) exists until the day on which the claimant receives payment from the respondent of the amount mentioned in section 78(1) or 92(1), and continues for another 3 business days immediately following that day.

(3) If, in exercising the right to suspend carrying out construction work or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract, the claimant incurs a loss or expense because the respondent removes any part of the work or supply from the contract, the respondent is liable to pay the claimant the amount of the loss or expense.

(4) A claimant who suspends carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract under subsection (1) is not liable for any loss or damage suffered by the respondent, or by any person claiming through the respondent, because of the claimant not carrying out that work or not supplying those goods and services, during the suspension.

The immunity in s 98(4)

Section 98(4) is the heart of the protection. While the suspension is on foot, you are “not liable for any loss or damage” the respondent (or anyone claiming through the respondent) suffers because the work isn’t being done or the goods aren’t being supplied. That covers the claims a respondent would otherwise be expected to bring — delay damages, liquidated damages for the period of the stoppage, the cost of bringing in another trade to keep the program moving, and any flow-on losses to head contractors or principals up the chain. The immunity attaches to the act of suspending, provided the s 98(1) preconditions are satisfied: a payment claim under s 78 or an adjudicated amount under s 92 is unpaid, you have given the respondent the required notice of intention to suspend, and at least two business days have passed since that notice.

Two practical points follow. First, the immunity only protects suspension — it doesn’t protect any other downing-of-tools dressed up as a s 98 suspension. If the s 78 or s 92 trigger isn’t there, or the notice hasn’t been given, the suspension is just a repudiatory breach and you wear the consequences. Second, the protection is time-bounded by s 98(2): you are immune during “the suspension”, and the suspension only legitimately runs while the right in s 98(1) subsists.

Removal of work during suspension — s 98(3)

Section 98(3) is the corollary. If, while you are validly suspended, the respondent reacts by removing part of the work or the supply from the contract — descoping, terminating for convenience over the suspended portion, re-letting the package to another contractor — and you incur a loss or expense as a result, the respondent has to pay you that loss or expense. That sits over the top of any contractual rights you may have on descoping or termination, and it doesn’t require you to elect between contractual and statutory remedies. The trigger is causal: the loss or expense has to be incurred “because” the respondent removed the work, while you were exercising the s 98(1) right.

When does the right end? Receipt of payment and three business days

Section 98(2) ties the duration of the right (and so the immunity) to the day the claimant “receives payment” of the s 78(1) or s 92(1) amount, plus another three business days. What counts as “receives payment” was considered in Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council (No 2) [2004] NSWSC 1160, where Master Macready treated the touchstone of “to pay” and “receives payment” as the act of the debtor transferring, by way of satisfaction, what is owed to the creditor — with the qualification that a claimant has not “received payment” if it cannot obtain the benefit of the money because of conditions placed on its receipt:

a claimant will not have ‘received payment’… in circumstances where the claimant is unable to obtain the benefit of that payment due to conditions, such as the provision of a secured bank guarantee, placed on its receipt.

The point matters because respondents sometimes try to end the suspension by tendering payment in a form the claimant cannot freely use — payment into a controlled account, payment conditional on the claimant signing a release, or payment by way of a bank guarantee held back against unrelated claims. On the Beckhaus approach, those don’t start the three-business-day tail in s 98(2) running, because the claimant has not yet received payment in the relevant sense.

The other end of the question — when the right ends if the legal status of the underlying debt later shifts — was considered in On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163. The claimant had suspended after the respondent failed to pay an adjudicated amount. While the proceedings were running, an expert determination under the contract concluded that, on net, the claimant in fact owed the respondent more than the adjudicated amount, and the respondent argued that the right to suspend ended at the date of the expert determination. Crowe AJ rejected that. The right to suspend continued until either the outstanding amount was set aside, or there was an unequivocal communication to the claimant acknowledging discharge of the payment claim, the adjudicated amount or, on those facts, the provisional judgment debt. Constructive receipt was held to occur only when consent orders were made setting aside the adjudication amount, and the right then ended three business days later.

What this means on a project

  • The s 98(4) immunity only attaches if the s 98(1) preconditions are met — a s 78 or s 92 trigger, the notice of intention to suspend, and the two business day wait. Skipping any of those throws away the protection and exposes you to delay, LD and step-in claims.
  • Diary the start of the suspension and keep a clean record of it. If the respondent later removes work from the contract during the suspension, s 98(3) gives you a statutory right to your loss or expense — but you will need to prove the timing and the causal link.
  • Don’t treat a conditional or controlled-account payment as the trigger for the three business day tail. If the funds aren’t freely available, on Beckhaus you have not yet “received payment” for the purposes of s 98(2).
  • If the respondent tries to argue that some later event (an expert determination, a contractual set-off, an interim accounting) has retrospectively ended the right to suspend, the answer in On Forbes is that the right runs until the outstanding amount is actually set aside, or until the respondent unequivocally communicates discharge of the relevant amount.
  • Lift the suspension promptly once payment is received in a usable form. The immunity tails out three business days after receipt; staying down past that point is no longer a s 98 suspension and no longer protected.
  1. Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council (No 2) [2004] NSWSC 1160 (Master Macready) — meaning of “pay” and “receives payment” under the equivalent NSW provisions; a claimant has not “received payment” where conditions on receipt prevent it from obtaining the benefit of the money.
  2. On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163 (Crowe AJ) — on s 26(2)(b) of the ACT Act (equivalent to s 98(1)): the right to suspend continues until the outstanding amount is set aside or the respondent unequivocally communicates discharge of the payment claim, adjudicated amount or provisional judgment debt; the s 98(2)-equivalent three business day tail then runs from constructive receipt.

Statutory suspension of works

Resumption when payment is made

Once you’ve lawfully suspended under s 98, the suspension doesn’t end the moment money lands. The Act gives you a buffer: the right to stay off site until you actually receive the outstanding amount, and then for another 3 business days. Knowing exactly when the right ends — and what counts as “receiving payment” — matters because demobilising and remobilising a site is expensive, and stepping back on too early can hand the respondent an argument that the suspension was abandoned.

Section 98 BIF Act — Claimant’s right to suspend work

(1) A claimant may suspend carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services, under a construction contract if at least 2 business days have passed since the claimant gave notice of intention to do so to the respondent under section 78 or 92.

(2) The right conferred under subsection (1) exists until the day on which the claimant receives payment from the respondent of the amount mentioned in section 78(1) or 92(1), and continues for another 3 business days immediately following that day.

(3) If, in exercising the right to suspend carrying out construction work or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract, the claimant incurs a loss or expense because the respondent removes any part of the work or supply from the contract, the respondent is liable to pay the claimant the amount of the loss or expense.

(4) A claimant who suspends carrying out construction work, or supplying related goods and services under a construction contract under subsection (1) is not liable for any loss or damage suffered by the respondent, or by any person claiming through the respondent, because of the claimant not carrying out that work or not supplying those goods and services, during the suspension.

When does the right end

Section 98(2) sets two endpoints. The first is the day you actually receive payment of the amount referred to in s 78(1) (an unpaid scheduled amount) or s 92(1) (an unpaid adjudicated amount). The second is 3 business days after that day. Until both have happened, the suspension is lawful and s 98(4) continues to immunise you against claims for loss or damage flowing from the stoppage.

The 3 business day tail is deliberate. Sites can’t restart instantly — labour, plant and subcontractors need to be recalled, materials reordered, and access reorganised. Parliament built that reality into the section so that a respondent can’t pay at 4:55pm on a Friday and demand crews back through the gate first thing Monday.

What counts as “receives payment”

The trigger is receipt of payment, not the issuing of a cheque, the lodgement of a transfer, or a promise to pay. In Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council (No 2) [2004] NSWSC 1160, Master Macready unpacked the ordinary meaning of “pay” and “receives payment” in the equivalent NSW provisions:

To give (a person) what is due in discharge of a debt, or as a return for services done, or goods received, or in compensation for injury done; to remunerate, recompense.” In turn, ‘payment’ is defined as “[t]he action, or an act, of paying.” From this it can readily be concluded that the touchstone of the ordinary and natural meaning of the expressions ‘to pay’ and ‘receives payment’ in the present context is the act of a debtor transferring by way of satisfaction whatever is owed by him or her to a creditor.

His Honour went on to hold that a claimant has not “received payment” where the money is encumbered — for instance, where it is paid in but conditioned on the provision of a secured bank guarantee — because the claimant cannot in fact obtain the benefit of it. The corollary is that the respondent has not “paid” in those circumstances either. So a payment hedged with conditions that prevent you from drawing on it does not start the 3 business day clock.1

Payment after a court challenge

A subtler problem arises where the adjudicated amount has been paid (or paid into court) but the parties remain in dispute about whether it was ever properly owing — for example because a later expert determination or final account reverses the balance. The ACT Supreme Court addressed exactly this scenario in On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163. The claimant suspended after a respondent failed to pay an adjudicated amount; while proceedings were on foot, an expert determination under the contract concluded the claimant in fact owed the respondent more than the adjudicated sum. The respondent argued the right to suspend had ended on the date of the expert determination.

Crowe AJ rejected that. The right to suspend continued until either the outstanding amount was set aside, or there was an unequivocal communication to the claimant acknowledging discharge of the payment claim, adjudicated amount or (in that case) provisional judgment debt. On the facts the right ended only after consent orders set the adjudication amount aside, with the 3 business day tail running from that date.2 The lesson for a claimant is that ambiguity about whether the debt has been discharged is resolved in favour of continued suspension — you don’t have to guess.

What this means on a project

  • The suspension lawfully continues until you actually receive the s 78(1) or s 92(1) amount, plus 3 business days. Don’t demobilise the demobilisation early.
  • “Receipt” means money you can use. If the payment is conditional — held in trust pending a guarantee, paid into court, or paid subject to set-off claims you haven’t agreed — take advice before treating the clock as having started.
  • Diary the resumption date the moment cleared funds hit the account. Count 3 business days from (and not including) the day of receipt; that’s the earliest day you have to be back on site.
  • If the respondent removes scope from the contract while you’re suspended, s 98(3) makes them liable for the loss or expense. Keep contemporaneous records of standby costs, demobilisation costs and any subcontractor charges.
  • If the underlying entitlement is being challenged in court or by expert determination, don’t resume on the basis of an interim ruling unless the order or communication unequivocally discharges the amount. Until then s 98(4) continues to protect you.
  • When you do resume, give the respondent written notice of the resumption date and the basis on which it’s been calculated. It’s a small piece of paper that prevents a later argument that you abandoned the suspension or returned late.
  1. Beckhaus Civil Pty Ltd v Brewarrina Shire Council (No 2) [2004] NSWSC 1160 (Master Macready) — meaning of “pay” and “receives payment” in the SOPA context; payment encumbered by conditions on receipt is not “payment” for the purposes of the equivalent of s 98(2).
  2. On Forbes Developments Pty Ltd v Chase Building Group (Canberra) Pty Ltd [2020] ACTSC 163 (Crowe AJ) — on s 26(2)(b) of the ACT Act (equivalent to s 98(1) BIF Act): the right to suspend continues until the outstanding amount is set aside or the claimant receives an unequivocal acknowledgement of discharge, with the 3 business day tail running from that point.

Project trust accounts

When a project trust is required

A project trust is the BIF Act’s mechanism for ring-fencing payments flowing down a head contract for the benefit of subcontractors. It is not optional, and it is not a contractual structure you opt into — once your contract crosses the eligibility line in s 14 and you enter a subcontract, the trust is required by force of statute. Three things have to line up: the contract must be eligible (s 14), it must not be exempted, and the contracted party must enter a subcontract for some or all of the work (s 12).

Section 12 BIF Act — When project trust required for a contract

(1) This section applies to a contract entered into on or after the commencement of this section.

(2) A project trust is required for a contract if—

(a) the contract is eligible for a project trust under subdivision 2; and

(b) the contract is not exempted under subdivision 3; and

(c) the contracted party enters into a subcontract for all or part of the contracted work.

(3) The requirement starts on the first day a project trust is required under subsection (2).

(4) The requirement continues until the project trust is dissolved under section 21, regardless of any of the following changes—

(a) a variation, or any other amendment, of the contract;

(b) a change in the contract price;

(c) a change in the contracted work.

(5) If a project trust is required for a contract and a project trust is also required for a subcontract of the contract, separate project trusts are required for the contract and the subcontract.

Section 14 BIF Act — Eligibility of contract for project trust when contract entered into

A contract is eligible for a project trust if, when it is entered into—

(a) more than 50% of the contract price is for project trust work; and

(b) the contract price is $1 million or more.

The two eligibility limbs

Section 14 sets the threshold test. Both limbs have to be satisfied at the time the contract is entered into. The first limb is qualitative: more than half of the contract price has to be for “project trust work” (the Chapter 2 expression for the categories of construction work captured by the regime — defined separately at s 8A and worked through on the next page). The second limb is purely numerical: the contract price has to be $1 million or more, calculated exclusive of GST under s 9(2). A $990,000 contract is not eligible. A $1,000,000 contract is.

Both limbs are tested at contract formation. That is the assessment date the section fixes — “when it is entered into”. If your contract starts at $900,000 and you sign it on that footing, s 14 is not satisfied at formation; later variations that push the price above $1 million do not retrofit eligibility under this section. The Act handles post-contract upward movement separately through the amendment provisions referenced in s 18(3) (s 14A on amendments affecting the trust requirement). The point for project teams is that you cannot ignore eligibility at execution and re-test later: if the contract is eligible at formation, the s 12 obligation is engaged the moment a subcontract is entered.

Eligibility is not enough on its own

Section 12 makes clear that eligibility under s 14 is a necessary but not sufficient condition. Two further things have to be true. First, the contract must not be exempted under subdivision 3 — the carve-outs sit outside this page and are dealt with separately, but they include the categories Parliament thought should sit outside the regime regardless of value. Second, and this is the trigger most often missed on a project, the contracted party must actually enter into a subcontract for all or part of the contracted work. A head contractor performing a $5 million project entirely with its own labour, plant and direct supply does not need a project trust under s 12, because s 12(2)(c) is not engaged. The trust is for the protection of subcontractors; if there are no subcontractors, the statutory mechanism does not switch on.

The flip side is timing. Once eligibility is satisfied and the first subcontract is entered, the requirement starts that day (s 12(3)) and the contracted party has 20 business days to open the project trust account (s 18(2)). That window is short. A head contractor that signs an eligible head contract today and lets its first subbie on site next week has effectively three to four weeks to have the account open at an approved financial institution.

Subcontracts are presumptively outside the regime

Section 15 cuts off a question that is otherwise easy to over-think. A project trust is not required for a subcontract unless the subcontract falls within one of the limited categories the Act picks out (s 14C and s 14E). The default position for a first-tier or second-tier subcontract is that no project trust is required for that subcontract, even if the head contract above it does have one. So a $2 million subcontract under a $20 million eligible head contract does not, of itself, need its own project trust. That is a deliberate policy choice — the regime puts the trust obligation at the top of the contractual chain, where the funds first arrive, and lets those funds cascade down through s 19 (deposit into the head trust) and s 20 (payments out to subcontractor beneficiaries from that trust).

Where s 14C or s 14E does engage a subcontract, s 12(5) confirms that you can end up with stacked trusts: a separate project trust for the head contract and a separate project trust for the subcontract. They do not consolidate. Each trust is administered, accounted for and dissolved separately under s 21.

The requirement, once engaged, is durable

Section 12(4) is worth reading carefully. Once the trust requirement is on, it stays on until the trust is dissolved under s 21. Variations, amendments, changes to the contract price, changes to the scope of the contracted work — none of those events switch the requirement off. A head contractor cannot escape the project trust regime by deleting scope or executing a deed of amendment that drops the price below $1 million. Conversely, the trust does not need to be re-established every time a variation is signed. The trust attaches to the contract and follows it through its life until either there are no subcontractor beneficiaries left, or the only outstanding work is maintenance work (s 21(1)).

What this means on a project

  • Test eligibility at contract execution against both limbs of s 14: more than 50% of the price for project trust work, and a contract price of $1 million or more (GST-exclusive). Both limbs, both at formation.
  • If both limbs are satisfied, identify whether any subdivision 3 exemption applies. If not, the trust is required as soon as the contracted party enters its first subcontract for any part of the contracted work.
  • If the head contractor will self-perform the entire scope with no subcontractors at all, s 12(2)(c) is not engaged and no project trust is required — but in practice this is rare on contracts above $1 million.
  • Diary the 20-business-day window in s 18(2) from the date of the first subcontract. Open the account at an approved financial institution before the clock expires; a contract clause purporting to push that window out is of no effect (s 18(6)).
  • Don’t assume your first-tier subcontract needs its own trust. Section 15 says it doesn’t, unless s 14C or s 14E applies. Check those sections expressly before establishing a stacked trust.
  • Once the trust is on, treat it as on for the life of the contract. Variations, descopes and price reductions don’t turn it off — only dissolution under s 21 does, and only on the limited grounds in s 21(1) (no remaining subcontractor beneficiaries, or maintenance-only work).
  • For projects sitting just below the $1 million line, watch the formation-date trap: the s 14 test is taken at execution. Structuring a contract to land at $999,000 and then varying it up after signature does not cleanly avoid the regime — the amendment provisions in s 14A are designed to catch exactly that kind of arrangement.
  1. The contract price for s 14(b) purposes is calculated exclusive of GST: BIF Act s 9(2). On reasonable estimates where the price cannot be accurately calculated at formation, see s 9(1) — the threshold is tested against the reasonable estimate of the amount the contracted party is entitled to be paid.
  2. On post-formation movements in price or scope, see BIF Act s 14A (referenced in the s 18(3) note) — amendments of contracts can engage the trust requirement after formation. Section 12(4) then locks the requirement in: once on, it does not switch off because of later variations, amendments, price changes or scope changes.
  3. On the trust’s purpose and beneficiary class, see s 7 (main purpose — protecting subcontractor interests in funds paid to the contracted party) and s 11(b) (the trust is “primarily for the benefit of subcontractors for the project trust contract”). Section 11A defines who counts as a subcontractor beneficiary.
  4. Stacked trusts under s 12(5) are not consolidated for administration: each trust has its own account (s 18(5) — not more than one project trust account per project trust), its own deposit obligations under s 19, and its own dissolution under s 21.

Project trust accounts

Opening the project trust account

Once a project trust is required for your contract under s 12 of the BIF Act, the contracted party (head contractor or principal contractor) has to actually open a dedicated bank account for that trust. The Act prescribes when the account must be opened, where it can be held, what it must look like, and who has to be told about it. Get any of these wrong and you are exposed to significant penalties — the obligations sit at 500 and 200 penalty units, with imprisonment available for some breaches.

Section 18 BIF Act — Contracted party must open project trust account

(1) If a project trust is required for a contract under section 12, the contracted party must open an account at a financial institution for the trust as required by this section.

Maximum penalty—500 penalty units.

(2) The project trust account must be opened within 20 business days after the contracted party enters into the first subcontract for the contract.

(3) However—

(a) if a project trust is not required for the contract until after an amendment of the contract; and

(b) the contracted party entered into a subcontract for the contract before the amendment of the contract;

the project trust account must be opened within 20 business days after the day the contract is amended.

(4) The project trust account must not be a virtual account or subordinate to any other account at a financial institution.

(5) There must not be more than 1 project trust account for the project trust.

(6) A provision of a contract that provides that the project trust account must be opened less than 20 business days after the contract is entered into is of no effect.

Timing — the 20-business-day clock

The trigger for opening the account is not the head contract or even the first payment. It is the first subcontract. Under s 18(2), the contracted party must open the project trust account within 20 business days after entering into the first subcontract for the project trust contract. That is the date you should be diarising the moment a project trust is required.

There is a separate trigger where the project trust requirement only arises later. If the head contract was not originally caught by chapter 2 but becomes caught after an amendment — for example, a variation pushes the contract price over the $1 million threshold in s 14, or pushes the project-trust-work share over 50% — and you had already entered into a subcontract before that amendment, the 20-business-day clock runs from the date of the amendment, not from the original subcontract date (s 18(3)). The cross-reference in the Act is to s 14A, which deals with how amendments affect the requirement to establish a project trust.

Section 18(6) is a quiet but important provision. A contract clause that purports to require the project trust account to be opened in less than 20 business days after the contract is entered into is “of no effect”. Principals sometimes try to write tighter timetables into their head contracts. They do not bind you. You still have the full 20 business days from the first subcontract.

Note also the relationship with s 17. The project trust itself is “established” by the first of three things happening — a payment from the contracting party to you, a payment from you to a subcontractor beneficiary, or a deposit into the project trust account. So the trust can be established (and your trustee duties switched on) before the account is even opened. Opening the account within the 20 business days does not give you a window to delay establishing the trust; it simply fixes the outer limit for getting the bank account in place.

Where the account can be held — approved financial institution

Section 18(1) requires the account to be opened “at a financial institution”. Read together with the chapter 2 definitions in s 8, an “approved financial institution” is one approved by the commissioner under s 55. The QBCC publishes the list of approved institutions on its website; only those institutions can hold a project trust account. Before you instruct your finance team to open the account, check the current list — it is updated from time to time, and a previously-approved institution coming off the list will affect any new accounts you open.

The account itself has to be a real, stand-alone bank account. Section 18(4) prohibits two structures that banks sometimes offer for cash-management efficiency: it cannot be a “virtual account” (a notional sub-ledger sitting under a parent account number), and it cannot be “subordinate to any other account at a financial institution” (for example, a sweep account that automatically empties into a master operating account overnight). Both structures are common in corporate cash-pooling arrangements. Neither is permitted here. The project trust money has to sit in its own discrete account in its own right.

Section 18(5) imposes a one-account-per-trust rule. There must not be more than one project trust account for a given project trust. You cannot split the funds across two accounts at the same institution, or hedge by holding part of the trust money at one bank and part at another. One trust, one account.

Account name and account purpose

The account must be opened “for the trust” (s 18(1)) — that is, in the contracted party’s name as trustee of the project trust for the relevant contract. In practice, banks approved by the commissioner will require you to open the account with a name that identifies it as a project trust account (commonly in a form such as “[Contracted Party] ATF [Project] Project Trust”). The account is a trust account in the legal sense: the funds in it are trust money held primarily for the benefit of subcontractor beneficiaries (s 11), and they are not available to your general creditors. Treat it accordingly from the moment it is opened — do not co-mingle with operating funds, do not use it as a working-capital float, and do not give the bank any set-off rights over other facilities.

Notifying the subcontractors

Opening the account is not the end of it. Section 23 requires the contracted party to give each subcontractor a written “notice of project trust” about the use of a project trust account. The notice must be in writing, must state that a project trust will be used for making payments to the subcontractor, and must include the further information prescribed by regulation.

The timing of the notice depends on whether the trust is already established when the subcontract is entered into:

  • if the project trust is not yet established when you and the subcontractor enter into the subcontract, the notice must be given within 10 business days after the trust is established (s 23(3)(a));
  • if the project trust is already established at the time of contracting, the notice must be given before you and the subcontractor enter into the subcontract (s 23(3)(b));
  • if the project trust requirement only arises after an amendment of the head contract and you had already entered into the subcontract pre-amendment, the notice must be given within 10 business days after the project trust account is opened (s 23(4)).

Failing to give the s 23 notice attracts 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment. It is one of the few obligations in chapter 2 that carries a custodial maximum, so it should be on the project mobilisation checklist alongside the bank-account paperwork.

What this means on a project

  • Diarise the 20-business-day clock from the date of the first subcontract, not from contract award and not from first payment. That is the date that triggers s 18(2).
  • If a variation later pushes the contract over the $1 million / 50% threshold and you already have a subcontract in place, the 20-business-day clock runs from the amendment date under s 18(3). Treat the variation paperwork as the trigger event.
  • Ignore any head-contract drafting that purports to require the trust account to be opened in less than 20 business days from contract execution — s 18(6) renders that clause of no effect.
  • Before opening the account, check the QBCC’s current list of approved financial institutions. The approval can change, and an account at a non-approved institution will not satisfy s 18(1).
  • Make sure the bank product is a real, segregated trust account in the contracted party’s name as trustee. No virtual accounts, no cash-pool / sweep arrangements, no shadow ledgers under a master operating account — s 18(4) prohibits both.
  • One project trust, one account (s 18(5)). Do not split funds across two accounts or two institutions for the same trust.
  • Build the s 23 notice into your subcontract execution pack. For subcontracts signed before the trust is established, send the notice within 10 business days of establishment; for subcontracts signed after the trust is up and running, send the notice before the subcontract is signed. Missing this carries 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.
  • Keep the bank-opening documents, the QBCC approval reference, the first-subcontract date and the s 23 notices together in one project-trust file. If the commissioner ever audits the trust, those are the documents that prove timely compliance with s 18 and s 23.
  1. Definitions of “approved financial institution” and “project trust account” appear in s 8 and s 10 of the BIF Act respectively; the commissioner’s power to approve financial institutions is at s 55.
  2. On when the trust is “established” (as distinct from when the account must be opened), see s 17 BIF Act — the trust comes into existence on the first of a payment from the contracting party, a payment to a subcontractor beneficiary, or a deposit into the project trust account, occurring after the trust is required under s 12.
  3. The information that must be included in a s 23 notice of project trust is prescribed by the BIF Regulation 2018; check the current regulation before finalising your notice template, as the prescribed content has been amended since the chapter commenced.

Project trust accounts

Subcontractor beneficiaries

The project trust exists for the benefit of a defined class of subcontractors — the “subcontractor beneficiaries” of the trust. Knowing who is in that class, and from when, matters because it controls who must be paid out of the project trust account under s 20, when the trust is established under s 17, when it can be dissolved under s 21, and what reporting obligations are triggered if a beneficiary is a related entity of the contracted party under s 24 and s 25.

Section 10 BIF Act — Definitions for part

In this part—

head contract means a contract for project trust work that is not also a subcontract of another contract.

project trust account means the account for a project trust at a financial institution.

subcontractor beneficiary, for a project trust, means a subcontractor who is a beneficiary of the trust under section 11A.

Section 11 BIF Act — What is a project trust

A project trust is a trust—

(a) over amounts—

(i) payable in connection with a project trust contract or project trust subcontract; and

(ii) required to be deposited in the project trust account under this chapter; and

(b) primarily for the benefit of subcontractors for the project trust contract.

Who counts as a subcontractor beneficiary

The defined-term route runs through two provisions. Section 10 tells you that a “subcontractor beneficiary” for a project trust is a subcontractor who is a beneficiary of the trust under s 11A. Section 11A then identifies which of the contracted party’s subcontractors actually fall within the beneficiary class for the particular project trust. The two work together: s 10 supplies the label, s 11A supplies the substantive test.

The starting point is s 11(b). The trust is “primarily for the benefit of subcontractors for the project trust contract”. That ties the beneficiary class to subcontractors of the head contract that has triggered the trust — not subcontractors of some other contract the contracted party happens to be performing, and not the contracting party (i.e. the principal) above. The trust runs downstream from the contracted party to the people the contracted party has engaged to perform parts of the contracted work.

Practically, that means a person becomes a subcontractor beneficiary of a particular project trust by being engaged by the contracted party under a subcontract for some or all of the work under the project trust contract. A subbie engaged on a different job, or by a different head contractor, is not a beneficiary of this trust. A second-tier subcontractor — engaged by a first-tier subbie, not by the contracted party — is also not a beneficiary of this project trust; the protection at second-tier sits inside any separate project trust required for the first-tier subcontract under s 14C or s 14E (see s 12(5) and the “stacked trusts” structure).

When subcontractor status engages

The Act treats the existence of a subcontractor beneficiary as the operative event for several other obligations. Section 12(2)(c) requires the contracted party to enter into a subcontract before a project trust is required at all — so the first subcontractor brought on board is the event that flips the trust requirement on (assuming eligibility under s 14 and no exemption). Section 17 then identifies how the trust is established, and one of the trigger events is “payment of an amount from the contracted party to a subcontractor beneficiary for subcontracted work under the contract” (s 17(b)). Section 23 requires the contracted party to give each subcontractor a written notice of project trust before entering into the subcontract (or within 10 business days of the trust being established, where the trust comes later).

The point is that beneficiary status is not declared by the contracted party or negotiated in a subcontract — it follows by force of statute from the fact of being a subcontractor of the contracted party for the project trust contract. You don’t opt in. Equally, you can’t opt out: a contract term that purports to disclaim beneficiary status, or to require the subcontractor to be paid from a different account, is incompatible with s 20(1), which says the contracted party may only pay an amount to a subcontractor beneficiary from the project trust account and into an account controlled by the beneficiary.

The class is dynamic

Subcontractor beneficiaries are not a fixed list locked in at the start of the job. The class moves with the project. New subcontractors engaged by the contracted party during the works become beneficiaries when they are engaged; subcontractors whose work is complete and who have been paid out drop away. Section 21(1)(a) reflects this directly: a project trust may be dissolved by the trustee if “there are no longer any subcontractor beneficiaries for the trust”. Until that point — or until the only remaining work is maintenance work (s 21(1)(b)) — the trust must continue, the account must remain open, and s 20 payment discipline applies to every payment out.

Related-entity subcontractors

The Act anticipates that a contracted party may engage a related entity (typically a subsidiary, sister company or other commonly-owned vehicle) as a subcontractor on a project trust job. There is no prohibition on doing so — but the regime imposes a transparency overlay so that the commissioner can see when trust funds are flowing within a corporate group rather than out to genuinely independent subcontractors.

Section 25 puts the obligation on the contracted party. Where a project trust is established for the contract and the contracted party enters into a subcontract with a related entity, the contracted party must inform the commissioner about the subcontract, using an approved way, within 5 business days after entering into it. Maximum penalty 200 penalty units. Section 24 mirrors that obligation onto the contracting party (the principal): if the contracting party knows, or ought reasonably to know, that a subcontractor beneficiary is a related entity of the contracted party, the contracting party must inform the commissioner within 5 business days. Maximum penalty 50 penalty units.

The two sections sit on either side of the contracted party. Section 25 catches the inside knowledge the contracted party has at the point of awarding a subcontract; s 24 catches what an attentive principal will work out from the contractor’s payment claims and supporting documentation. Together they let the regulator scrutinise intra-group payment flows without prohibiting them outright.

What this means on a project

  • Treat every subcontractor engaged by the contracted party for any part of the project trust contract’s scope as a subcontractor beneficiary of the project trust by force of s 10 and s 11A — status flows from the engagement, not from a contractual label.
  • Pay every subcontractor beneficiary strictly in accordance with s 20: out of the project trust account, into an account the beneficiary controls, and (if the beneficiary has its own project trust for its subcontract) into that trust account.
  • Don’t treat second-tier subbies as beneficiaries of the head trust — they aren’t. Their protection sits in any separate project trust required at the first-tier subcontract level under s 14C or s 14E, or in retention trust arrangements under s 31–s 32.
  • Maintain a live register of current subcontractor beneficiaries. You need it to track s 23 notice obligations, to monitor when the class has emptied for s 21(1)(a) dissolution, and to identify any related-entity subcontractors for s 25 reporting.
  • If any subcontractor beneficiary is a related entity of the contracted party, diary the 5-business-day s 25 reporting clock from the date the subcontract is entered into. Use the approved way prescribed by the commissioner; an internal note or email to the principal is not a substitute.
  • Principals (contracting parties) on project trust jobs should build a check into their payment-claim review: if you know, or it’s reasonably obvious, that a paid subcontractor is in the contractor’s corporate group, your own s 24 reporting obligation engages within 5 business days of you becoming aware.
  1. The defined term “subcontractor beneficiary” is set up by s 10 and filled out by s 11A, which identifies the subcontractors who are beneficiaries of a particular project trust. The trust’s purpose under s 7 and s 11(b) is to protect that defined class of subcontractors in respect of the project trust contract.
  2. On the “flip on” events, see s 12(2)(c) (the trust requirement engages on entry into the first subcontract for the contracted work) and s 17 (the trust is established by the first of: a payment from the contracting party to the contracted party, a payment from the contracted party to a subcontractor beneficiary, or a deposit in the project trust account).
  3. On payment discipline once beneficiary status is engaged, see s 20(1) — the contracted party may only pay a subcontractor beneficiary from the project trust account, into an account controlled by the beneficiary, and (where the beneficiary itself must run a project trust for its subcontract) into that beneficiary’s project trust account: s 20(3)(b). Retention amounts are dealt with separately under s 20(4).
  4. On exit from the class, see s 21(1)(a) — the trust may be dissolved if there are no longer any subcontractor beneficiaries for the trust. Until then, s 20 continues to govern every payment out.
  5. Related-entity reporting: s 25 (contracted party, 5 business days after entering a subcontract with a related entity, maximum penalty 200 penalty units); s 24 (contracting party, 5 business days after becoming aware (or ought reasonably to have become aware) that a subcontractor beneficiary is a related entity of the contracted party, maximum penalty 50 penalty units). Both sections require notification “using an approved way”.

Project trust accounts

Deposits into the project trust

Once a project trust has been established, s 19 controls the flow of money in. Every payment the contracting party makes to the contracted party in connection with the project trust contract has to land in the project trust account — not the contracted party’s general operating account, not a director’s loan account, not a related-entity sweep account. The deposit obligation is the mechanism that gives Chapter 2 its protective force: if the funds never reach the trust, the subcontractor beneficiaries in s 11A have nothing to claim against.

Section 19 BIF Act — All payments from contracting party to be deposited in project trust account

(1) This section applies to any of the following amounts paid by the contracting party to the contracted party in connection with a project trust contract—

(a) an amount paid in accordance with the terms of the contract;

(b) an amount paid because the contracting party is liable under section 77 to pay the amount to the contracted party in connection with the contract;

(c) an amount paid under chapter 3, part 4 because of an adjudication of a disputed progress payment relating to the contract;

(d) an amount paid because of a final and binding dispute resolution process relating to the contract;

(e) an amount paid because of a court order relating to the contract;

(f) an amount, paid for any other reason, that reduces the unpaid amount of the contract price for the contract.

(2) The contracting party must deposit the amount into the project trust account for the contract (the deposit obligation) unless—

(a) the amount was due to be paid before the trust was established; or

(b) the amount is paid into court; or

(c) the amount is to be withheld because of a payment withholding request given to the contracting party under section 97B; or

(d) the amount is paid directly to a person under chapter 4 in connection with a subcontractor’s charge; or

(e) the contracting party has a reasonable excuse for failing to deposit the amount into the account.

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units.

(3) Once the amount is deposited into the project trust account, the deposit is taken to be a payment made by the contracting party to the contracted party and discharges the contracting party’s liability to pay that amount to the contracted party.

(4) If an amount is paid to the contracted party or its agent in contravention of the deposit obligation, the contracted party must deposit the amount into the project trust account as soon as practicable after receiving the amount.

Maximum penalty for subsection (4)—200 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment.

What “all payments” actually means

Section 19(1) is drafted to be exhaustive. Read the six paragraphs together: any amount paid “in connection with” the project trust contract has to be deposited, whether the payment is made under the contract’s own progress claim machinery (paragraph (a)), as a default amount triggered by s 77 of the BIF Act (paragraph (b)), as the cash payable on a successful adjudication of a disputed progress payment (paragraph (c)), as a payment made to settle or finalise a dispute through ADR (paragraph (d)), as compliance with a court judgment or order on the contract (paragraph (e)), or for any other reason that has the effect of reducing the unpaid balance of the contract price (paragraph (f)).

Paragraph (f) is the catch-all, and it is deliberately broad. If a payment’s effect is to reduce what is still owed under the contract price, it is caught — regardless of the label put on the payment in the head contractor’s ledger. A “goodwill” payment from the principal towards an overdue claim, a settlement of a disputed variation, or a release of contested retention all reduce the unpaid amount of the contract price and so all attract the deposit obligation. The form the payment takes does not matter; the function does.

Timing of the deposit

Section 19(2) does not, on its face, prescribe a specific deadline for the contracting party to make the deposit. The obligation is expressed as “must deposit the amount into the project trust account”, and the natural reading is that the deposit must be made by way of the payment itself — in other words, the contracting party pays the contracted party by depositing into the trust account, rather than first paying into a general account and later sweeping. Section 19(3) confirms that mechanism: the deposit into the project trust account is the act that discharges the contracting party’s payment liability to the contracted party. So the timing of the deposit is the timing of the payment itself: whenever the contract or s 77 or the adjudicated decision or the court order calls for payment, that payment must be made into the trust account on that date.

The position is different where money has already been paid the wrong way. Section 19(4) deals with the case where, in breach of s 19(2), the contracting party has paid the amount directly to the contracted party (or its agent) into a non-trust account. In that scenario, the contracted party itself becomes responsible for moving the money into the trust account “as soon as practicable” after receipt. The penalty in s 19(4) — 200 penalty units or two years’ imprisonment — is materially heavier than the s 19(2) penalty on the contracting party, reflecting the fact that the contracted party is the trustee and is in immediate control of the funds the moment they hit its account.

Carve-outs from the deposit obligation

Section 19(2) lists five exceptions. Four are mechanical and tightly drawn:

  • amounts that fell due before the project trust was established (paragraph (a)) — an early progress claim that was due and payable before the trust came into existence under s 17 is not retrofitted into the trust;
  • amounts paid into court (paragraph (b)) — for example, payments made under an order to pay the disputed sum into court pending resolution;
  • amounts withheld in response to a payment withholding request from a subcontractor under s 97B (paragraph (c));
  • amounts paid directly to a third party under Chapter 4 in connection with a subcontractor’s charge (paragraph (d)).

The fifth exception is the soft one: paragraph (e) excuses non-deposit where the contracting party has a “reasonable excuse”. It is not defined in the Act and should not be relied on as a planning tool. Administrative inconvenience, a broken banking integration, or the head contractor having asked for payment to a different account will not, on any sensible reading, qualify. The exception is there to deal with the genuinely abnormal case — not to give the contracting party a discretion to bypass the trust.

Advance and upfront payments

Section 19 does not contain a special rule for advance, mobilisation or upfront payments — and that is the point. The section captures “any of the following amounts paid by the contracting party to the contracted party in connection with a project trust contract”, including in paragraph (a) any amount paid in accordance with the terms of the contract and in paragraph (f) any amount that reduces the unpaid amount of the contract price for any other reason. An advance payment, mobilisation payment, deposit on award, or any other upfront sum paid before work has been physically performed all sit within those words: each is paid in connection with the project trust contract and each, on its own terms, reduces the unpaid amount of the contract price (or, depending on the drafting, sits as a credit against future claims, which has the same arithmetic effect).

The practical consequence is that mobilisation and advance payments must be deposited into the project trust account in the same way as a progress payment. The only escape is the s 19(2)(a) timing carve-out: if the advance was contractually due and was paid before the trust was established under s 17, it sits outside the regime. The first payment from the contracting party to the contracted party is itself one of the events that establishes the trust under s 17(a), so in practice an advance payment paid after the s 12 requirement has been engaged but before the account is opened needs careful handling. The contracted party still has to open the account within 20 business days of the first subcontract (s 18(2)), and any payment that was due to be made after the trust was established has to be deposited; structuring a head contract so the entire mobilisation is paid into a non-trust account before the first subcontract is entered, in order to keep that money out of the trust, will not work where the s 12 requirement is already engaged at the time of payment.

What this means on a project

  • Once the project trust account is open, all payments from the principal under the head contract should be made directly into the trust account — not into the head contractor’s general operating account with a later transfer. The deposit is the payment for s 19(3) discharge purposes.
  • Paragraph (f) of s 19(1) is a catch-all. Settlement payments, payments resolving disputed variations, releases of contested retention and any other amounts that reduce the unpaid contract price all attract the deposit obligation, regardless of how they are labelled in correspondence.
  • Adjudicated amounts (s 19(1)(c)) and court-ordered payments (s 19(1)(e)) go into the trust account, not directly to the head contractor’s nominated account. Update payment instructions for the principal’s legal and finance teams accordingly.
  • Mobilisation, advance and upfront payments fall within s 19. Treat them like any other payment under the contract: deposit into the trust account once the trust is established. The s 19(2)(a) carve-out only saves payments that were due before the trust came into existence.
  • If money is mistakenly paid into the contracted party’s general account in breach of s 19(2), s 19(4) puts the obligation back on the contracted party to move it to the trust account “as soon as practicable”. The penalty for getting that wrong is 200 penalty units or two years’ imprisonment — meaningfully heavier than the s 19(2) penalty on the principal.
  • Don’t plan around the “reasonable excuse” exception in s 19(2)(e). It is for the genuinely abnormal case. Banking inconvenience, a head contractor request to pay elsewhere, or a contractual side-letter directing payment to a different account are not reasonable excuses.
  • For payments responding to a subcontractor’s s 97B payment withholding request, or a Chapter 4 subcontractor’s charge, follow the carve-outs in s 19(2)(c) and (d) precisely — those amounts bypass the trust and go where the statute directs.
  1. On when the trust is established — and therefore when the s 19(2)(a) timing carve-out closes — see s 17: the trust is established by the first of (a) a payment from the contracting party to the contracted party under the contract, (b) a payment from the contracted party to a subcontractor beneficiary, or (c) a deposit in the project trust account as required under Chapter 2.
  2. The s 19(2) penalty is 200 penalty units. The s 19(4) penalty for the contracted party failing to redirect a wrongly-received payment into the trust account is 200 penalty units or 2 years’ imprisonment. The disparity reflects the contracted party’s position as trustee under s 11.
  3. On the s 19(2)(c) carve-out for payment withholding requests, see s 97B (the request mechanism a subcontractor uses to require the principal to withhold from the contracted party). On the s 19(2)(d) carve-out for subcontractor’s charge payments, see Chapter 4 of the BIF Act.
  4. The discharge rule in s 19(3) means that, once the deposit is made, the contracting party has paid the contracted party for contractual purposes — the contracted party cannot insist on a separate payment outside the trust on the footing that money in the trust is “not yet” paid to it.

Project trust accounts

Withdrawals to pay subcontractor beneficiaries

Once a project trust is established, the project trust account is the only lawful conduit for paying a subcontractor beneficiary for work done under the head contract. Section 20 fixes both the source of the payment (the project trust account) and the destination (a financial-institution account nominated by, and controlled by, the beneficiary). Get either limb wrong and the contracted party commits an offence carrying up to 200 penalty units or one year’s imprisonment.

Section 20 BIF Act — All payments to subcontractor beneficiaries to be paid from project trust account

(1) The contracted party for a project trust contract may only pay an amount to a subcontractor beneficiary of the project trust—

(a) from the project trust account; and

(b) by depositing the amount into the account of a financial institution nominated by the beneficiary.

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

(2) To remove any doubt, it is declared that the obligation to pay an amount from the project trust account applies whether or not the amount is held in the account when it is to be paid.

Note—

See section 51 about covering shortfalls.

(3) The account nominated by the subcontractor beneficiary under subsection (1)(b) must be—

(a) controlled by the beneficiary; and

(b) if the beneficiary is also required to establish a project trust for its subcontract—the account for the project trust for the subcontract.

(4) This section does not apply to—

(a) a retention amount withheld from payment to a subcontractor beneficiary if the amount is deposited into a retention trust account of which the subcontractor is, or will be, a beneficiary; and

(b) a retention amount to be released to a subcontractor beneficiary from a retention trust account.

When a withdrawal is permitted

A withdrawal from the project trust account to a subcontractor beneficiary is only permitted to discharge an amount the contracted party owes that beneficiary for subcontracted work under the head contract. The trust is not a general operating account: s 11 makes the trust an account “over amounts payable in connection with” the project trust contract or subcontract, and “primarily for the benefit of subcontractors”. The corollary is that the contracted party (the head contractor as trustee) cannot withdraw funds from the trust account to pay its own overheads, its other projects, its tax, or anyone who is not a subcontractor beneficiary of that particular trust.

The permission to pay a subcontractor beneficiary is also a compulsion: under s 20(1) a payment to a subcontractor beneficiary “may only” be made from the project trust account and into the beneficiary’s nominated account. There is no lawful alternative route — the contracted party cannot pay the subbie out of its general business account and reimburse the trust later, and it cannot ask the subbie to take cash or a cheque drawn elsewhere. The form of payment is prescribed.

The payment-instruction process

Two pieces of information have to be in place before the contracted party instructs the financial institution to release funds. First, the trust account itself must be properly opened in the contracted party’s name as trustee at an approved financial institution under s 18 — not a virtual or sub-account, and not subordinate to any other account. Second, the beneficiary must have nominated a destination account that satisfies s 20(3): it must be an account at a financial institution that the beneficiary controls, and if the beneficiary is itself required to run a project trust for its own subcontract, the nominated account must be that downstream project trust account. The trustee is responsible for confirming both points before pushing the payment through.

The instruction itself is then a straightforward electronic transfer from the project trust account to the nominated account. The deposit is what discharges the head contractor’s payment obligation to the subcontractor beneficiary — mirroring the rule at s 19(3), where a contracting party’s payment is taken to be made when the amount is deposited into the project trust account. Cash withdrawals, transfers to the trustee’s own account first, or transfers to a third party at the subbie’s direction all fall outside what s 20 authorises.

Paying even when the account is short

Section 20(2) is the provision project teams most often misread. The obligation to pay a subcontractor beneficiary “from the project trust account” applies “whether or not the amount is held in the account when it is to be paid”. Put plainly: if the subbie is owed $200,000 today and only $50,000 is sitting in the trust account today, the trustee is still required to pay the full $200,000 from the trust account on the due date. The shortfall is not the subcontractor’s problem — it is the trustee’s. The note to s 20(2) cross-refers to s 51, which obliges the trustee to top the trust account up out of its own funds to cover the shortfall before the payment date.

That mechanism produces the order of payment the regime is built around. Funds come into the trust account from the contracting party under s 19. Funds go out of the trust account to subcontractor beneficiaries under s 20. If incoming funds are insufficient to meet the next outgoing payment in full, the trustee makes up the gap from its own resources under s 51 so that the s 20 payment still goes out on time and in full. The trust never lawfully runs an unfunded payment to a subbie — the trustee funds it.

Retention amounts sit outside section 20

Section 20(4) carves out two specific kinds of retention movement from the s 20 process. The first is a retention amount withheld by the head contractor from a payment otherwise due to a subcontractor beneficiary, where that retention is deposited into a retention trust account of which the subcontractor is (or will be) a beneficiary. The second is the release of retention from a retention trust account back to the subcontractor when retention is due to be released. Both of those movements run through the retention trust regime in Part 3, not through the project trust account. The practical point is that a single progress claim from a subbie can produce two separate flows: the cash component is paid out under s 20 from the project trust account into the subbie’s nominated account, and the retention component (if any) is dealt with under the retention trust rules.

What this means on a project

  • Every dollar paid to a subcontractor beneficiary for subcontracted work under the head contract must leave the project trust account and land in the beneficiary’s nominated account. There is no alternative route — not the head contractor’s general account, not cash, not a third-party direction.
  • Before instructing payment, confirm two things: the account being debited is the project trust account opened under s 18, and the destination account is one the beneficiary controls (and is the beneficiary’s own project trust account if a downstream trust is required of them under s 20(3)(b)).
  • Pay the full amount due on the due date even if incoming funds from the contracting party haven’t arrived in time. Section 20(2) puts the timing risk on the trustee, and s 51 requires the trustee to cover any shortfall from its own funds before the payment goes out.
  • Do not withdraw trust funds for any non-beneficiary purpose. The trust is for the beneficiary class only; using trust funds for the trustee’s overheads, other projects, or other creditors is outside s 20 and triggers the offence in s 20(1).
  • Run the cash and retention components of a progress claim through different pipes: cash through s 20 from the project trust account; retention into or out of a retention trust account under Part 3. Section 20(4) keeps retention movements outside the project trust process.
  • Keep the bank-account paperwork tidy. A subbie’s nominated-account form should record that the account is controlled by the subbie and, where the subbie is itself a head contractor on its own eligible contract, that the account is its project trust account. That is the documentation that demonstrates s 20(3) compliance if a withdrawal is later questioned.
  1. The criminal exposure in s 20(1) (200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment) sits alongside the trustee’s general fiduciary obligations in respect of trust funds.
