The Chapter 2 purpose provision at s 7 frames the regime as one designed “to ensure that funds paid to the contracted party for particular contracts are held in a trust to protect the interests of subcontractors”.
  2. On the “subcontractor beneficiary” class, see s 10 (definitions for the part) and s 11A (which identifies who is a beneficiary of the trust). Only payments to a subcontractor beneficiary for subcontracted work under the head contract are governed by s 20; the trust does not capture payments to suppliers, consultants or other creditors who do not meet the s 11A description.
  3. On covering shortfalls, see BIF Act s 51 (referenced in the note to s 20(2)). The trustee is required to deposit its own funds into the project trust account to cover any difference between the amount in the account and the amount payable to a subcontractor beneficiary, before the payment is made.
  4. On the destination account, s 20(3)(a) requires the account to be controlled by the beneficiary — an account in a related party’s name, or a holding account the beneficiary cannot operate, will not satisfy the section. Where the beneficiary is itself running a project trust under s 12 for its own subcontract, s 20(3)(b) requires the nominated account to be that project trust account, so the funds remain inside the trust regime as they pass down the chain.
  5. On the retention carve-out, see s 20(4) read with s 31 (definition of retention trust) and s 32 (when a retention trust is required). Retention amounts go into and out of a retention trust account, not the project trust account, and are governed by Part 3 of Chapter 2.

Project trust accounts

Withdrawals to pay the contracting party

The project trust exists primarily for the benefit of subcontractors, but the head contractor — sitting in the dual role of contracted party and trustee — is also entitled to draw down its own progress claims out of the same account. The Act doesn’t leave that to commercial discretion: s 20A confines the purposes for which money can come out at all, and s 20B then forces the head contractor’s self-payment to sit behind any current liability to a subcontractor beneficiary.

Section 20A(1) BIF Act — limited purposes for withdrawals

(1) A trustee must not withdraw an amount from the project trust account for any purpose other than—

(a) paying a subcontractor beneficiary an amount the contracted party is liable to pay the beneficiary in connection with its subcontract; or

(b) paying the trustee, as the contracted party, an amount the contracting party is liable to pay the contracted party for contracted work but only to the extent a subcontractor beneficiary is not entitled to be paid for the same work; or

(c) returning an amount paid in error by the contracting party; or

(d) depositing a retention amount into a retention trust account; or

(e) making payment relating to the contract in accordance with an adjudication under chapter 3, part 4; or

(f) making payment relating to the contract as ordered by a court; or

(g) making another payment prescribed by regulation.

The head contractor wears two hats

Under the BIF Act, the “contracted party” for a head contract is the party performing the work — in practice, the head contractor — and that same party is required to open the project trust account and act as trustee (s 18). When the head contractor pays itself out of the trust, it is doing so “as the contracted party”: receiving from the trust the amount the principal (the “contracting party” in BIF terminology) is liable to pay it for its own contracted work. Section 20A(1)(b) is the gateway provision that authorises that withdrawal.

The two limits in s 20A(1)(b)

Section 20A(1)(b) puts two distinct limits on the head contractor’s self-payment. First, the amount drawn must be an amount the contracting party is “liable to pay the contracted party for contracted work” — that is, money owing under the head contract for the head contractor’s own work, certified or otherwise payable. The trustee can’t draw down on amounts that aren’t yet due, and it can’t use the trust as a working capital facility for amounts the principal hasn’t become liable for.

Second — and this is the structural protection for subcontractors — the head contractor can only take out of the trust an amount “to the extent a subcontractor beneficiary is not entitled to be paid for the same work”. Where a portion of the principal’s payment relates to work that a subcontractor performed and is owed for, that portion belongs to the subcontractor, not the head contractor. The head contractor’s slice is the margin and the head contractor’s own work content; the subcontractor’s slice flows through.

Section 20B priority — pay subcontractors first

Section 20B BIF Act — order of priority

A trustee must not withdraw an amount from the project trust account to pay itself, or make another payment prescribed by regulation, unless there would still be a sufficient amount available in the account after the withdrawal to pay all amounts the contracted party is liable to pay subcontractor beneficiaries at the time of the withdrawal.

Section 20B is the hard rule on order of priority. Even where the head contractor is genuinely entitled under s 20A(1)(b) to take an amount, it cannot do so if the withdrawal would leave the account short of what it needs to pay subcontractor beneficiaries who are presently owed money. The test is forward-looking: would a sufficient amount remain “after the withdrawal” to cover all subcontractor liabilities owing “at the time of the withdrawal”? If not, the self-payment is prohibited — and the prohibition carries 300 penalty units or two years’ imprisonment.

That priority is reinforced from the other direction by s 51. If the account is short when a payment to a subcontractor beneficiary falls due, the trustee must immediately deposit an amount equal to the shortfall from its own funds. The Act does not allow the trustee to manage cash by paying itself first and topping the account up later.

Interest and bank fees sit outside the s 20A regime

Two carve-outs sit outside the s 20A purpose-list. Interest earned on amounts held in the project trust account belongs to the trustee under s 51D, and may be withdrawn once every 12 months or on dissolution — subject to the same proviso that the withdrawal must not prevent full payment of another amount that must be paid from the account. Fees charged by the approved financial institution for the project trust account are also outside s 20A (s 20A(4)). Neither category requires a s 20A(1)(b) entitlement, but both effectively defer to the same priority idea: the trust’s capacity to meet subcontractor liabilities comes first.

Withdrawals on dissolution

When the project trust is dissolved under s 21 — either because there are no longer any subcontractor beneficiaries, or because the only remaining work is maintenance — the trustee may pay itself any accrued interest entitlement under s 51D and “any remaining amount that is not owing to a subcontractor beneficiary” (s 21(4)). That is the one moment at which the residual balance can move cleanly into the head contractor’s own hands, because by definition no subcontractor beneficiary is left to be paid out of it.

What this means on a project

  • The head contractor can be paid from the trust, but only under s 20A(1)(b) — for amounts the principal is liable to pay it for its own contracted work, and only to the extent no subcontractor beneficiary is entitled to be paid for the same work.
  • Before each self-payment, run the s 20B test: after the withdrawal, will the account still hold enough to pay every subcontractor amount currently owing? If not, don’t take it. The criminal exposure (up to two years’ imprisonment) sits on the trustee personally.
  • Don’t treat a principal’s progress payment as “all yours” just because it landed in the trust account. Identify the subcontractor portion of the certified work first and ring-fence it; the head contractor can only draw the residue.
  • If the account is short when a subcontractor payment falls due, top it up under s 51 — do not delay the subcontractor payment and do not pay yourself first.
  • Annual interest and bank fees are not s 20A withdrawals; they are dealt with under s 51D and s 20A(4). Diary the 12-month interest sweep and check that it doesn’t collide with a pending subcontractor payment.
  • The clean exit point for any residual balance is dissolution under s 21: once there are no subcontractor beneficiaries left, the trustee can pay itself the remaining amount that is not owing to a beneficiary, plus any s 51D interest entitlement.
  1. Section 20A(1)(b) BIF Act — gateway authorising the trustee, as contracted party, to be paid from the project trust account for amounts the contracting party is liable to pay for contracted work, “but only to the extent a subcontractor beneficiary is not entitled to be paid for the same work”. Contravention of s 20A(1) carries a maximum penalty of 300 penalty units or 2 years’ imprisonment.
  2. Section 20B BIF Act — order of priority. The trustee must not withdraw to pay itself unless a sufficient amount would remain in the account after the withdrawal to pay all amounts the contracted party is liable to pay subcontractor beneficiaries at the time of the withdrawal. Maximum penalty 300 penalty units or 2 years’ imprisonment.
  3. Section 51 BIF Act — trustee to cover shortfalls: if there is an insufficient amount in the trust account to pay an amount due to a beneficiary, the trustee must immediately deposit a shortfall amount equal to the difference. Maximum penalty 100 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.
  4. Section 51D BIF Act — the trustee is entitled to all interest earned on amounts held in the trust account and may withdraw it once every 12 months or on dissolution, unless the withdrawal would prevent full payment of another amount that must be paid from the account. Section 20A(4) carves interest withdrawals and approved-financial-institution fees out of the s 20A purpose-list.
  5. Section 21(4) BIF Act — on dissolving a project trust, the trustee may pay itself any s 51D interest entitlement and any remaining amount that is not owing to a subcontractor beneficiary. Dissolution is only available where there are no remaining subcontractor beneficiaries or where the only remaining work is maintenance work (s 21(1)).

Project trust accounts

Notifications to QBCC, principal and subcontractors

Once a project trust is required, the trustee’s job isn’t just to open the account and start moving money. Chapter 2 builds a notification scaffolding around the trust: the commissioner has to be told when the trust opens, when it closes and when key things change; subcontractors have to be told that a trust is being used to pay them; and the contracting party has to flag related-entity arrangements. Miss any of these steps and you have a compliance breach sitting on top of an otherwise functioning trust account.

Section 17 BIF Act — establishment of project trust

Once a project trust is required for a contract under section 12, the trust is established by the first of the following being made after the trust is required—

(a) payment of an amount from the contracting party to the contracted party under the contract;

(b) payment of an amount from the contracted party to a subcontractor beneficiary for subcontracted work under the contract;

(c) a deposit in the project trust account as required under this chapter.

Telling the commissioner the trust has opened

The trust is “established” under s 17 by operation of law — you don’t establish it by filing a form. What triggers establishment is the first payment in or out, or the first deposit into the project trust account, after the trust becomes required under s 12. The account itself must be opened within 20 business days after the contracted party enters into the first subcontract for the contract (s 18). Where the trust requirement only kicks in after a contract amendment, the 20 business day clock runs from the day the contract is amended instead.1

The notification to the commissioner that the trust has been established sits in the surrounding administrative provisions of the chapter and is to be given “using an approved way” — in practice, through the QBCC’s online portal. The same approved-way framing appears in the related-entity provisions at s 24 and s 25, which require notification within 5 business days of the contracting party becoming aware of a related-entity subcontractor beneficiary, or the contracted party entering into a subcontract with a related entity. Both are penalty-bearing obligations.

Changes to the trust during the project

The trust requirement, once it starts, “continues until the project trust is dissolved under section 21, regardless of any of the following changes” — a variation or other amendment, a change in the contract price, or a change in the contracted work (s 12(4)). That continuity rule is important: variations don’t reset the obligation, and a price reduction below the eligibility threshold doesn’t end the trust. Where the trust account itself needs to move — for example, the trustee wants to change financial institutions — closing the existing account is not treated as dissolving the trust, provided the closure is for the purpose of transferring to another financial institution under s 18C (s 21(3)).

The contracting party’s related-entity reporting obligation under s 24 is triggered as soon as the party knows, or ought reasonably to know, that a subcontractor beneficiary is a related entity for the contracted party. The 5 business day clock runs from when the party first becomes aware, or ought reasonably to have become aware. The contracted party’s mirror obligation under s 25 runs from entering into the subcontract with the related entity. These are ongoing notification duties, not one-off disclosures at the start of the project.

Closing the trust

Dissolution is tightly controlled. The trustee can only dissolve the trust if there are no longer any subcontractor beneficiaries, or the only remaining work is maintenance work (s 21(1)). Dissolution itself happens in two steps: closing the project trust account, and giving written notice to the commissioner that the trust has been dissolved (s 21(2)). On dissolution, the trustee may pay itself any interest it is entitled to under s 51D, and any amount remaining that isn’t owing to a subcontractor beneficiary (s 21(4)).

Notifying subcontractors that a trust is being used

The contracted party must give every subcontractor a written “notice of project trust” under s 23. The notice has to state that a project trust will be used for making payments to the subcontractor and include the information prescribed by regulation. Timing depends on the sequencing of trust establishment and subcontract entry:

  • If the project trust is not yet established when the subcontract is entered into — within 10 business days after the trust is established.
  • If the project trust is already established when the subcontract is entered into — before the subcontract is entered into.
  • If the trust requirement is triggered by a later contract amendment, and the subcontract was entered into before the amendment — within 10 business days after opening the project trust account.

The penalty for failing to give a compliant notice is 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment, which puts s 23 in the same penalty band as the obligation to pay subcontractors only from the project trust account under s 20. Treat it as a hard, non-negotiable step in the subcontract execution process — not a paperwork formality to catch up on later.

Principal-side information

The contracting party (the principal, or the head contractor where the trust sits at the subcontract layer) carries two distinct information duties. The first is the related-entity report under s 24, discussed above. The second flows from s 19: every payment the contracting party makes to the contracted party in connection with the project trust contract has to be deposited into the project trust account, unless one of the carve-outs applies (payment into court, a payment withholding request under s 97B, a subcontractor’s charge payment under chapter 4, or a reasonable excuse). The principal’s “information” obligation in practical terms is to know the project trust account details and direct payments accordingly — if it pays the contracted party direct in contravention of the deposit obligation, the contracted party then has to deposit the amount into the project trust account as soon as practicable, with the higher penalty (200 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment) attaching to that downstream step.

What this means on a project

  • Diarise the 20 business day clock for opening the project trust account from the date you enter into the first subcontract (s 18(2)). If a contract amendment is what triggered the trust, the clock runs from the day of amendment, not the day of the original first subcontract.
  • Notify the commissioner through the QBCC’s online portal when the trust opens. Use the same channel for related-entity reports under s 24 and s 25 — both have a 5 business day window and both carry penalties.
  • Build the s 23 notice of project trust into your subcontract execution pack. If the trust is already established, the notice has to be given before the subcontract is entered into — you can’t backfill it.
  • Don’t treat variations or price changes as a reason to revisit the trust requirement. Once it’s on, it stays on until s 21 dissolution.
  • When you close the trust, do both steps: close the account and give written notice to the commissioner under s 21(2)(b). Closing the account on its own is not dissolution, and an unnotified closure leaves the trust technically extant.
  • If you are the principal, make sure your accounts payable team has the project trust account details and pays into that account — not the contracted party’s general operating account — for every payment captured by s 19(1).
  1. Section 18(3) BIF Act. The 20 business day rule for opening the account is mandatory; s 18(6) renders of no effect any contract provision that purports to require the account to be opened less than 20 business days after the contract is entered into. The maximum penalty for failing to open the account as required is 500 penalty units (s 18(1)).
  2. Section 24 BIF Act (contracting party to report related entities — 50 penalty units); s 25 BIF Act (contracted party to report related-entity subcontracts — 200 penalty units). Both obligations are discharged “using an approved way”, which in practice is the QBCC online portal.
  3. Section 23(2)(c) BIF Act requires the notice of project trust to include “the information prescribed by regulation” in addition to the statutory statement that a project trust will be used. The penalty for failing to give a compliant notice is 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment (s 23(1)).

Project trust accounts

Records and reporting

Running a project trust is not just a banking exercise. Chapter 2, part 3 of the BIF Act layers a bookkeeping regime, an external review regime, and a long-tail retention obligation on top of the account itself. Get any of those wrong and you are exposed to penalty units, imprisonment risk for individuals, and follow-on regulatory action by the commissioner — quite apart from the civil consequences of a beneficiary being short-paid.

Sections 51, 52 and 57 BIF Act — shortfalls, records, audit

51 Trustee to cover shortfalls

(1) This section applies if there is an insufficient amount available in a trust account to pay an amount due to be paid to a beneficiary of the trust.

(2) The trustee must immediately deposit an amount equal to the shortfall in the trust account (a shortfall deposit). Maximum penalty—100 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

52 Trust records

The trustee for a project trust or retention trust must—

(a) keep records for the trust as prescribed by regulation; and

(b) retain the records for at least 7 years after the trust is dissolved. Maximum penalty—300 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

57 Engaging auditor for review of trust account

(1) The trustee for a project trust or retention trust must engage an auditor to carry out a review of the trust account as required by this section. Maximum penalty—200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

(5) The review must be complete within 40 business days after starting the review.

(6) The review must be carried out by an auditor that is independent of the trustee and has not been excluded by the commissioner under section 54E.

Bookkeeping requirements

Section 52 imposes the core bookkeeping duty: the trustee “must… keep records for the trust as prescribed by regulation”. The substantive content of those records sits in the BIF Regulation and the commissioner’s approved forms — deposits and withdrawals tied to each beneficiary, the underlying payment instruction, the source contract, and the running balance — but the obligation to maintain them at all is statutory, and breach carries a maximum penalty of 300 penalty units or one year’s imprisonment. That is the heaviest individual penalty in the records-and-reporting suite, which gives some indication of how seriously the legislature treats the bookkeeping foundation.1

The records do real work. They are the audit trail an auditor needs to complete a s 57 review; they are the evidence the commissioner relies on if a complaint is made; and they are how the trustee proves it has actually paid each beneficiary the amount the trust held for them. They also feed the s 51 shortfall obligation: if reconciliation against the records shows the account is short of the amount due to a beneficiary, the trustee must “immediately” deposit a shortfall amount into the account. You cannot identify a shortfall, or defend an allegation that one existed, without contemporaneous records.

Trust account reviews and audits

Section 57 requires the trustee to engage an auditor to carry out a periodic review of the trust account. The engagement must comply with regulation requirements; the review must be carried out at the times prescribed by regulation; and once started, it must be completed within 40 business days. The review period itself — the slice of time the auditor is reviewing — is the period prescribed by regulation, not a period of the trustee’s choosing.

Independence is non-negotiable. Under s 57(6) the auditor must be independent of the trustee, and s 57(7) spells out what that means: not an employee, not (for a corporate trustee) an executive officer, investor or shareholder, not (for a partnership) a partner, and not a related entity. The auditor must also not have been excluded by the commissioner under s 54E. An “auditor” for these purposes is a person registered as an auditor under part 9.2 of the Corporations Act, or a person prescribed by regulation (s 50).

There are three narrow situations in which a trustee does not have to engage an auditor for a given review period. Two of them assume an essentially dormant account: no retention amount was held during the review period, or there were no transactions or changes for the account during the review period. In each case the trustee still has to give the commissioner a written statement, in an approved way, within 10 business days after the end of the review period explaining or confirming that fact. The third is whatever circumstances are prescribed by regulation. Outside those exceptions, the audit obligation is mandatory and carries a maximum penalty of 200 penalty units or one year’s imprisonment.

The output of the audit is an “account review report” (s 50, picking up s 57A(1)). If the commissioner reasonably suspects the auditor’s conduct has breached a professional standard or condition, s 58 lets the commissioner pass redacted information to the relevant professional body for investigation — with all information identifying the trustee removed before disclosure. The audit regime is therefore policed at both ends: the trustee is policed through the audit, and the auditor is policed through the professional bodies.

Retention of records

The retention period is the trap most trustees underestimate. Section 52(b) requires records to be retained “for at least 7 years after the trust is dissolved”. The clock does not start when the project finishes, when practical completion is reached, when the last payment is made, or when the bank account is closed. It starts when the trust is dissolved — and a project trust is only dissolved once the dissolution conditions in chapter 2 have been satisfied. In practice this means trust records routinely have to be kept for well over a decade from the date the underlying contract was signed.

Treat the seven-year period as a floor, not a ceiling. Limitation periods for contract claims, security-of-payment recovery and statutory demand processes can run longer in particular fact patterns, and the records the trustee is keeping under s 52 are often the same records you will want if a beneficiary later alleges short-payment or improper withdrawal. Destroying them on the seven-year anniversary, then being asked to prove a payment in year eight, is a foreseeable problem.

What this means on a project

  • Set the trust bookkeeping up at the same time the trust account itself is opened. Section 52 starts to bite from day one of the trust, not from the first payment claim — and the 300 penalty unit / 1 year maximum applies to the duty to keep records, not just to record falsification.
  • Reconcile the trust account against the records on a regular cycle. That is what surfaces a s 51 shortfall in time to make the “immediate” deposit the section requires — and it is the same reconciliation the auditor will redo at review time.
  • Engage the auditor early in the prescribed review period, not at the end. The 40-business-day completion clock under s 57(5) starts when the review starts; an auditor brought in late has less room to chase missing records before the deadline.
  • Confirm the auditor’s independence in writing before engagement. Walk through the s 57(7) categories — employee, executive officer, investor, shareholder, partner, related entity — and check the commissioner’s exclusion list under s 54E.
  • If you are relying on the “no retention amount held” or “no transactions” exceptions in s 57(8), diarise the 10-business-day deadline for the written statement to the commissioner. Missing that statement is what converts an exempt review period into a breach.
  • Calendar record destruction off the date the trust is dissolved, not off the date the project finishes. Until you can identify dissolution, the seven-year clock has not started.
  1. Section 52 BIF Act. The content of the prescribed records is set by regulation; this page does not reproduce the regulation-level detail. The 300 penalty unit / 1 year maximum penalty in s 52 should be read alongside the 100 penalty unit / 1 year maximum for failing to make a shortfall deposit under s 51 and the 200 penalty unit / 1 year maximum for failing to engage an auditor under s 57 — together they form the records, reconciliation and review tier of the chapter 2 enforcement regime.
  2. On auditor independence and oversight: s 57(6)–(7) BIF Act (independence categories); s 54E (commissioner’s power to exclude an auditor); s 58 (referral of redacted information to professional bodies where the commissioner reasonably suspects a breach of professional standard or condition). “Auditor” is defined in s 50 by reference to part 9.2 of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cwlth) or a regulation-prescribed person.

Project trust accounts

Closing the project trust

A project trust does not run forever. Section 21 of the BIF Act sets the gates the trustee must walk through before dissolving the trust — when dissolution is permitted, what happens to any money still sitting in the project trust account, and the written notice that has to go to the commissioner once it is done.

Section 21 BIF Act — Ending project trust

(1) Once a project trust is established for a contract, the trustee may dissolve the trust only if—

(a) there are no longer any subcontractor beneficiaries for the trust; or

(b) the only remaining work to be carried out under the contract is maintenance work.

(2) A project trust is dissolved by the trustee—

(a) closing the project trust account; and

(b) giving written notice to the commissioner of the trust having been dissolved.

(3) A trustee is taken not to dissolve the project trust by closing the project trust account if the account was only closed for the purpose of transferring the account to another financial institution under section 18C.

(4) When dissolving the project trust, the trustee may pay itself the following amounts—

(a) any amount for interest that the trustee is entitled to under section 51D;

(b) any remaining amount that is not owing to a subcontractor beneficiary.

(5) In this section—

maintenance work see section 15D(2).

When the trust may be closed

Dissolution is not at the trustee’s option. Section 21(1) only permits the trust to be wound up in two situations: there are no subcontractor beneficiaries left, or the only work still to be done under the head contract is maintenance work. Until one of those gates is open, the trust must continue — the requirement for the project trust runs from the day it is first required and continues until the trust is dissolved under s 21, regardless of any later variation, change in contract price or change in scope.

“No longer any subcontractor beneficiaries” means each subcontractor beneficiary has been paid out everything owing for subcontracted work. A subcontractor stops being a beneficiary only when the contracted party has no further payment obligation to them under the subcontract. If you still owe a subcontractor a retention release, a final claim, an adjudicated amount or a court-ordered amount, that subcontractor remains a beneficiary and the trust cannot be dissolved on the s 21(1)(a) limb.

The maintenance-work limb in s 21(1)(b) reflects the fact that defects-liability and maintenance phases can run for a year or more after practical completion, well past the point where any further subcontract work is being done. Once the only remaining work is maintenance work as defined in s 15D(2), the trustee can move to dissolve even if the head contract itself has not yet ended.

Distribution of remaining funds

Section 21(4) tells you what happens to whatever is sitting in the project trust account at the point of dissolution. The trustee may pay itself two categories of money: interest the trustee is entitled to under s 51D, and any remaining amount that is not owing to a subcontractor beneficiary. The order of operations matters. Subcontractor beneficiary entitlements come first — you cannot dissolve the trust under s 21(1)(a) while a beneficiary is still owed money, and even on the maintenance-work limb the trustee’s sweep under s 21(4)(b) is confined to amounts “not owing to a subcontractor beneficiary”.

That structure is consistent with the purpose of the chapter, which is to ensure that funds paid to the contracted party for particular contracts are held in trust to protect the interests of subcontractors. The trustee is not permitted to clear the account in its own favour and leave a beneficiary unpaid; the trust funds remain impressed with the trust until the beneficiary side is satisfied. Only the residue — head-contract margin, retention released back from subcontract retention trusts that has flowed through the account, surplus on the contracting party’s payments — can be drawn down by the trustee on dissolution.

Interest sits in its own subsection because s 51D entitles the trustee to interest accrued on the project trust account in defined circumstances; that entitlement crystallises on dissolution and is paid out under s 21(4)(a).

Closing the account and notifying the commissioner

Section 21(2) prescribes the two mechanical steps that actually effect dissolution. The trustee must close the project trust account at the financial institution, and must give written notice to the commissioner that the trust has been dissolved. Both steps are required — closing the account without notifying the commissioner does not dissolve the trust for the purposes of the Act, and notifying the commissioner without closing the account is equally incomplete.

There is one carve-out. Under s 21(3), if the only reason the account is being closed is to transfer the project trust to another financial institution under s 18C, the trustee is taken not to have dissolved the trust. That preserves the integrity of the trust during a routine bank change — the trust continues, the new account simply takes over, and no notice of dissolution should be given.

What this means on a project

  • Do not close the project trust account at practical completion as a matter of habit. The s 21(1) gates have to be open first — either every subcontractor beneficiary has been paid out in full, or the only work still to be done is maintenance work.
  • Before dissolving, run a final reconciliation against every subcontract: claims paid, retention released, any adjudicated or court-ordered amount, any pending charge under chapter 4. If anything is still owing to a subcontractor beneficiary, the trust must continue.
  • On dissolution, you may pay yourself the s 51D interest and any residue not owing to a subcontractor beneficiary — in that order, and only after the beneficiary side is clear. Document the calculation; the trust records have to be kept under s 52.
  • Two formal steps close the trust: close the project trust account at the financial institution, and give written notice to the commissioner that the trust has been dissolved. Doing one without the other is not dissolution.
  • If you are simply moving banks, do not treat that as a dissolution. Use the s 18C transfer mechanism — s 21(3) deems the trust to continue and no notice of dissolution should be given to the commissioner.
  1. The duration of the project trust requirement, and its insulation from contract variations, follows from s 12(4) BIF Act — the requirement “continues until the project trust is dissolved under section 21” regardless of any variation, amendment, change in contract price or change in contracted work.
  2. The protective purpose informing s 21 is stated at s 7 BIF Act — “to ensure that funds paid to the contracted party for particular contracts are held in a trust to protect the interests of subcontractors”. Trust records relating to closure must be retained under s 52, the source of the “trust records” definition in s 8.

Project trust accounts

Penalties for non-compliance

Chapter 2 is enforced almost entirely through criminal offence provisions. Each substantive trust obligation — opening the account, depositing the right money in, paying subcontractors out of it, giving the right notices, reporting related entities — carries its own maximum penalty expressed in penalty units, and several of the core obligations also carry a term of imprisonment. The maxima are not symbolic. They reflect a deliberate policy choice that the trust regime is the protective backbone of the Act, and that breaching it is to be treated as an offence rather than a private contractual default.

Section 18(1) BIF Act — Contracted party must open project trust account

(1) If a project trust is required for a contract under section 12, the contracted party must open an account at a financial institution for the trust as required by this section.

Maximum penalty—500 penalty units.

Section 19 BIF Act — All payments from contracting party to be deposited in project trust account (penalties)

(2) The contracting party must deposit the amount into the project trust account for the contract (the deposit obligation) unless …

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units.

(4) If an amount is paid to the contracted party or its agent in contravention of the deposit obligation, the contracted party must deposit the amount into the project trust account as soon as practicable after receiving the amount.

Maximum penalty for subsection (4)—200 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment.

The headline offence: failing to open the account

The largest standalone maximum in the bundle of offences attached to setting up a project trust sits at s 18(1): if a project trust is required under s 12 and the contracted party does not open the account at a financial institution as required, the maximum penalty is 500 penalty units. There is no imprisonment limb on this provision, but the financial exposure dwarfs the next tier of trust offences. Section 18 also contains structural prohibitions that reinforce the offence — the account cannot be a virtual account or subordinate to another account (s 18(4)), there cannot be more than one account for the trust (s 18(5)), and any contractual term purporting to extend the 20-business-day opening window beyond what s 18(2) allows is “of no effect” (s 18(6)). A head contractor cannot drafting-out of the timing.

Deposit in, payment out: the imprisonment-tier offences

The next tier targets the flow of money through the trust, and this is where imprisonment first appears. Section 19(2) imposes a deposit obligation on the contracting party: any amount paid by the contracting party to the contracted party in connection with a project trust contract has to be paid into the project trust account, subject to the carve-outs in s 19(2)(a)–(e). Breach carries a maximum penalty of 200 penalty units. There is no imprisonment limb at the contracting-party level, reflecting that the contracting party is typically the principal at the top of the chain rather than the trustee.

Section 19(4) sits below it and is more serious. If money has reached the contracted party (or its agent) in contravention of the deposit obligation — for example, where the principal pays the head contractor directly into a general operating account — the contracted party must deposit that money into the project trust account as soon as practicable. Breach carries 200 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment. The same imprisonment-grade exposure attaches to the payment-out side. Section 20(1) requires the contracted party to pay subcontractor beneficiaries only from the project trust account, and only by depositing the amount into the financial-institution account nominated by the beneficiary. Maximum penalty: 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment. Together, s 19(4) and s 20(1) bracket the trust — money that is meant to be in must be in, and money that goes out has to leave through the right pipe.

Notices and reporting: lower-tier but easy to miss

The notice and reporting offences carry smaller maxima but track obligations that are easy to overlook on a busy project. Section 23(1) requires the contracted party to give each subcontractor a written notice of project trust before entering into the subcontract (or, where the trust is established later, within 10 business days of establishment): maximum 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment. Section 25 requires the contracted party to inform the commissioner within 5 business days when it enters into a subcontract with a related entity: maximum 200 penalty units. Section 24 imposes the mirror obligation on the contracting party where it knows, or ought reasonably to know, that a subcontractor beneficiary is a related entity for the contracted party: maximum 50 penalty units, the lowest in the suite.

Reading the maxima as a hierarchy

The maxima are not random. Read together, they sketch a hierarchy that maps onto the seriousness of the underlying breach:

  • 500 penalty units — failing to open the project trust account at all (s 18(1)). The largest standalone exposure in the establishment phase.
  • 200 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment — the contracted party failing to remediate a payment that has bypassed the trust (s 19(4)). The only two-year limb in this part of Chapter 2.
  • 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment — paying subcontractor beneficiaries other than from the trust account (s 20(1)) and failing to give the required notice of project trust (s 23(1)).
  • 200 penalty units (no imprisonment) — the contracting party’s deposit obligation (s 19(2)) and the contracted party’s related-entity reporting (s 25).
  • 50 penalty units — the contracting party’s related-entity reporting under s 24.

Two patterns emerge. First, imprisonment limbs cluster on the contracted party — the trustee — rather than the principal. The Act treats misuse of trust funds at the trustee level as more culpable than a principal’s administrative failure to route a payment correctly. Second, omissions to report and notify carry materially lower maxima than misdirecting the money itself, but they are not trivial: s 23(1) sits at imprisonment grade despite being “just” a notice obligation, because absence of notice frustrates the protective purpose of the regime in s 7.

Common compliance traps

Most prosecutions and audit findings under this part come from a small set of recurring failures rather than from sophisticated avoidance. The patterns worth flagging are:

  • Missing the 20-business-day window in s 18(2). The clock runs from the first subcontract, not from the head contract. Project teams that diary the head-contract execution date instead of the first subcontract date routinely under-shoot the window.
  • Drafting around s 18(6). A head-contract clause that purports to give the contracted party more than 20 business days to open the account is “of no effect”. The s 18(1) offence is engaged on the statutory timeline regardless of what the contract says.
  • Treating direct payments outside the trust as a contractual issue. Where the principal pays straight into an operating account, both s 19(2) (principal’s deposit obligation) and s 19(4) (head contractor’s remediation obligation) are engaged. The remediation limb is the imprisonment-grade offence.
  • Paying subbies out of the wrong account. Section 20(1)(a) permits payment to a subcontractor beneficiary “only” from the project trust account. A payment made from a general account to a subbie — even one that clears the same day — is the s 20(1) offence on its face.
  • Forgetting the notice of project trust under s 23. The notice has to be given before the subcontract is entered into (or within 10 business days of trust establishment if the trust is established later). It is a written notice with prescribed content; a generic project induction document will not satisfy it.
  • Under-reporting related-entity subcontracts. Section 25 catches the contracted party; s 24 catches the contracting party where it knows or ought reasonably to know. Both are short-fuse 5-business-day obligations, and the “ought reasonably to know” limb in s 24 means a principal cannot rely on willed ignorance.
  • Assuming amendments turn off exposure. Section 12(4) keeps the trust requirement on regardless of variations, amendments or price changes. The matching offence provisions stay live until dissolution under s 21.

What this means on a project

  • Build the s 18(2) 20-business-day clock into your subcontracting workflow, not into your head-contract execution checklist. The trigger is the first subcontract.
  • Route every incoming payment from the principal through the trust account. If a payment lands elsewhere, treat s 19(4) remediation as a same-day priority — this is the only 2-year imprisonment exposure in the part.
  • Pay subcontractor beneficiaries only from the project trust account into a beneficiary-controlled account (s 20(1), s 20(3)). Do not net off, set off informally, or pay from a parent-company account.
  • Issue the s 23 notice of project trust before each subcontract is signed. Build the prescribed content into your standard subcontract pack so the notice goes out with every award letter.
  • Identify related-entity subcontractors at award stage and run the 5-business-day reporting clock under s 25 (contracted party) and s 24 (contracting party) from the date of subcontract execution, not the date someone first asks the question.
  • Treat s 18(6) as a hard limit when negotiating head-contract clauses about trust set-up. Do not accept extended-timing clauses; they will not protect the contracted party from the s 18(1) offence.
  • Keep the trust live until s 21 dissolution criteria are actually met (no remaining subcontractor beneficiaries, or maintenance-only work). Do not close the account on the strength of a variation that drops scope or price — s 12(4) keeps the requirement on.
  1. Penalty maxima referenced in this page are taken directly from the offence-creating subsections: s 18(1) (500 penalty units); s 19(2) (200 penalty units); s 19(4) (200 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment); s 20(1) (200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment); s 23(1) (200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment); s 24(2) (50 penalty units); s 25(2) (200 penalty units). The dollar value of a penalty unit is set by Queensland legislation outside the BIF Act and is updated periodically.
  2. The s 18(1) offence is supplemented by structural prohibitions in s 18(4) (no virtual or subordinate account), s 18(5) (only one project trust account per project trust) and s 18(6) (a contract clause extending the 20-business-day opening window in s 18(2) is of no effect).
  3. The carve-outs from the s 19(2) deposit obligation are listed at s 19(2)(a)–(e), including amounts due before the trust was established, amounts paid into court, amounts withheld under a payment withholding request under s 97B, amounts paid under a subcontractor’s charge under chapter 4, and amounts where the contracting party has a reasonable excuse for failing to deposit. The reasonable-excuse limb is the only general defence on the face of s 19(2).
  4. Section 20(4) carves out retention amounts that move into or out of a retention trust account from the s 20(1) payment-out offence. The retention trust regime begins at s 30 and runs in parallel with the project trust offences in this part.
  5. The protective purpose underpinning the offence regime is stated in s 7 — ensuring funds paid to the contracted party for particular contracts are held in trust to protect the interests of subcontractors. Section 11(b) confirms the trust is “primarily for the benefit of subcontractors for the project trust contract”, which is the policy lens through which the maxima above are calibrated.

Retention trust accounts

When a retention trust is required

A retention trust is the BIF Act’s mechanism for ring-fencing cash retention held back from progress payments. The regime piggybacks on the project trust framework in Chapter 2: if you are already inside the project trust net, every dollar of cash retention you withhold has to sit in a dedicated trust account, not in your operating funds. The trigger sits in s 32 and pulls its threshold concepts from the eligibility rules for project trusts in s 14 and the carve-outs in subdivision 3.

Section 32 BIF Act — When retention trust required

(1) A retention trust is required for a retention amount withheld from payment under a contract if—

(a) the contract is a withholding contract at the time the retention amount is withheld; and

(b) the retention amount is withheld by the contracting party in the form of cash.

(2) Also, a retention trust is required for a retention amount withheld from payment under a contract if—

(a) the contract was not a withholding contract at the time the retention amount was withheld but the contract has since become a withholding contract; and

(b) the contract is a first tier subcontract; and

(c) the retention amount was withheld by the contracting party in the form of cash; and

(d) the retention amount had not been released to the parties entitled to it at the time the contract became a withholding contract.

(5) This section does not apply to a retention amount withheld from payment under a contract if—

(a) the contracting party is the State, the Commonwealth, a state authority, a local government or another entity prescribed by regulation; or

(b) the contract price for the contract is at least the minimum contract price.

(6) In this section—

withholding contract means—

(a) a project trust contract that is—

(i) a head contract; or

(ii) a subcontract that is eligible for a project trust under section 14C or 14D; or

(b) a project trust subcontract for a project trust contract mentioned in paragraph (a).

The trigger turns on three things

Reading s 32(1) on its own, a retention trust is required when three conditions all hold at the moment cash is held back. The contract has to be a “withholding contract”; the retention has to be in cash (a bank guarantee or insurance bond is outside the section); and the contracting party — the party doing the withholding — has to be the one taking the deduction. The trust is established the moment the cash is withheld (s 33), and the obligation to have an account open at an approved financial institution runs in parallel (s 34).

The work in s 32 is being done by the definition of “withholding contract” in s 32(6). That definition pulls the retention regime onto the same spine as the project trust regime: a withholding contract is either a project trust contract (a head contract, or a subcontract that is eligible under s 14C or s 14D), or a project trust subcontract for one of those head contracts. So a retention trust requirement only ever attaches where a project trust requirement is already in play above it — the two regimes are joined at the hip.

Project value thresholds — inherited from s 14

Because the retention trust trigger keys off project trust contracts, the threshold question collapses back into s 14. A contract is eligible for a project trust if, when it is entered into, more than 50% of the contract price is for project trust work and the contract price is $1 million or more. That same $1 million / 50% gateway is what brings cash retention under a contract into the s 32 net — if the head contract above you is not a project trust contract, no retention trust is required for retention you withhold under it.

Section 32(5) then layers a separate price ceiling on top. The retention trust requirement does not apply if the contract price is “at least the minimum contract price”, which is defined in s 30 as the contract price amount prescribed by regulation. That carve-out is the Act’s way of removing very large contracts from the retention trust regime — the policy view being that on contracts above the prescribed ceiling the parties have other tools (bonds, parent-company guarantees) for managing security.1

Catch-up trusts on contracts that become withholding contracts later

Section 32(2) handles the awkward case of a contract that wasn’t a withholding contract when the cash was first held back, but became one later — for example, because of an amendment that pushes the contract price or scope mix across the s 14 line, or a related-entity subcontract that engages s 14C. If all four limbs of s 32(2) are satisfied (the contract is now a withholding contract, it is a first tier subcontract, the retention was held in cash, and the cash had not yet been released back), a retention trust is required for the existing retention as well. In that situation s 34(2)(b) gives the contracting party 20 business days from the day the contract becomes a withholding contract to open the account, and s 35(2)(b)–(c) sets the deposit timing.

Once the requirement starts, it stays on

Section 32(3) fixes the start date as the first day the contracting party withholds the retention amount from payment. Section 32(4) then locks that requirement in: it continues until all of the retention amount has been released to the parties entitled to it under the withholding contract, regardless of any variation or other amendment of the contract or any change in the contracted work. Releasing scope, dropping the price, or restructuring the work part-way through does not switch the trust off. Only release of the retention to the contracted party does.

Government and prescribed-entity carve-outs

Section 32(5)(a) takes the State, the Commonwealth, state authorities, local governments and other entities prescribed by regulation out of the requirement entirely — but only when one of those entities is the contracting party (i.e. the party withholding). A private-sector head contractor that subcontracts to a government-owned entity is not exempted, because the entity is on the contracted-party side of the deduction. The exemption is for the withholder, not the recipient.

The other set of carve-outs sits one layer up, in the project trust subdivision 3 exemptions that feed into the “withholding contract” definition. If the head contract above you is exempted under s 15A (contracts with prescribed entities), s 15B (contracts only between the State and a state authority), s 15C (small-scale residential construction work for less than 3 living units), s 15D (maintenance-only work) or s 15E (architects, engineers, building designers and landscape architects on advisory, design or contract administration work), the contract is not a project trust contract and therefore not a withholding contract under s 32(6) — so the retention trust requirement never engages. Section 15F — the “less than 90 days to practical completion” carve-out — is in the same family.

What this means on a project

  • If your head contract isn’t a project trust contract, you don’t need a retention trust for cash retention you withhold under it. The retention trust regime sits entirely on top of the project trust regime — s 32(6) makes the link explicit.
  • If your head contract is a project trust contract under s 14 (more than 50% project trust work and contract price of $1 million or more, GST-exclusive), and you withhold cash retention from a subcontractor, the retention trust requirement engages from the day you make the deduction (s 32(3)).
  • Bank guarantees, insurance bonds and similar non-cash security sit outside s 32 — the section only bites on retention “withheld by the contracting party in the form of cash” (s 32(1)(b), s 32(2)(c)). If you switch a project from cash retention to bank guarantees, you can take that contract out of the retention trust regime even though the project trust above it remains.
  • Watch the s 32(2) catch-up. If a contract was below the project trust line at execution but tips over later because of an amendment that engages s 14A or a related-entity subcontract under s 14C, any cash retention already held has to be moved into a retention trust account — you have 20 business days under s 34(2)(b) and the deposit windows in s 35(2) to do it.
  • The s 32(5)(b) ceiling for contracts “at least the minimum contract price” is a regulation-prescribed number; check the current BIF Regulation before assuming a very large contract is exempt.1
  • The exemption for government and prescribed entities under s 32(5)(a) only operates when the government party is the one withholding. A private head contractor subcontracting to a government-owned trading entity gets no benefit from that limb.
  • Once the requirement is on, it stays on. Variations, descopes and price drops don’t end it — only release of the retention to the parties entitled to it under the contract does (s 32(4)).
  • One retention trust account can hold retention amounts withheld under any number of contracts the same contracting party is on (s 34(3)); you don’t need a separate account per project. But that operational economy doesn’t change when the duty to have the account open is triggered.
  1. The “minimum contract price” for s 32(5)(b) is the contract price amount prescribed by regulation: BIF Act s 30 (definition). The figure sits in the BIF Regulation 2018 and is updated from time to time — verify the current prescribed amount before relying on the s 32(5)(b) carve-out.
  2. On the project trust eligibility threshold that feeds the “withholding contract” definition: BIF Act s 14 ($1 million / more than 50% project trust work, tested at contract formation); s 14A (amendments that bring a contract into eligibility post-formation); s 14B (multiple contracts at the same site or adjacent sites taken to be a single contract); s 14C (first-tier subcontracts with related entities); s 14D (contracts of a type prescribed by regulation).
  3. The subdivision 3 carve-outs that take a contract out of the project trust regime — and therefore out of the “withholding contract” definition in s 32(6) — are: s 15A (contracting or contracted party is a prescribed entity); s 15B (contracts between the State and a state authority only); s 15C (small-scale residential construction work for less than 3 living units); s 15D (maintenance work only); s 15E (advisory, design or contract administration work by architects, engineers, building designers and landscape architects); s 15F (less than 90 days between the trigger date and expected practical completion).
  4. On the mechanics flowing from a s 32 trigger: s 33 establishes the trust the moment the cash is withheld; s 33A creates a statutory charge over the retention amount in favour of the contracted party; s 34 obliges the contracting party to open a retention trust account at an approved financial institution (before withholding under s 32(1), or within 20 business days under s 32(2)); s 35 sets the deposit obligation; s 40 requires written notice of the retention trust to the contracted party before withholding.

Retention trust accounts

What money is held in retention trust

The retention trust regime in Chapter 2 of the BIF Act is a cash-only regime. It captures money that the contracting party has actually withheld from a progress payment as security — not every form of security the contract might allow, and not money that simply sits in the contracting party’s general account waiting to be withheld.

Section 35 BIF Act — deposit obligation

35 All retention amounts withheld must be deposited in retention trust account

(1) This section applies if, under section 32, a retention trust is required for a retention amount withheld from payment under a contract.

(2) The contracting party must deposit the retention amount withheld under the contract in a retention trust account (the deposit obligation) as follows—

(a) if the retention trust is required under section 32(1)—when the retention amount is withheld;

(b) if the retention trust is required under section 32(2) and there is an existing retention trust account into which the retention amount may be deposited—within 5 business days after the contract becomes a withholding contract;

(c) if the retention trust is required under section 32(2) and there is no existing retention trust account into which the retention amount may be deposited—when the retention trust account is opened.

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment.

(3) A term of a contract is of no effect to the extent it is inconsistent with the deposit obligation.

(4) The retention amount to which the deposit obligation relates is inclusive of any GST related to the amount.

Cash retention — the only thing that goes in

The trust is established by the act of withholding. Section 33 says it bluntly: where a retention trust is required under s 32, “the retention trust is established by the contracting party withholding the retention amount from payment.” There is no separate declaration of trust, no instrument to sign — the moment money is held back from a progress payment under a withholding contract, the trust comes into existence over that amount.1

The deposit obligation in s 35 then forces that money into a dedicated bank account. For a contract that was a withholding contract from the start, the cash must hit the retention trust account at the moment of withholding — not at the end of the month, not when the next reconciliation happens. For a contract that becomes a withholding contract part-way through (the s 32(2) case), there is a 5-business-day window if a trust account is already open, or until the new account is opened if it isn’t. The penalty for breach is 200 penalty units or 2 years’ imprisonment, so this is not a back-office tidy-up obligation.

Two features of s 35 are worth pausing on. First, s 35(3) overrides any contrary contract term — you cannot drafting-around the deposit obligation by, for example, agreeing that retention will sit in the head contractor’s working-capital account until practical completion. Second, s 35(4) confirms the amount deposited is GST-inclusive: if you withhold 5% of an invoice, you deposit 5% of the GST-inclusive figure, not the GST-exclusive figure.

Bank guarantees and other non-cash security

The retention trust regime simply does not apply to bank guarantees, insurance bonds, parent-company guarantees or any other non-cash form of security. Sections 33, 34, 35, 35A and 36 all speak in terms of “retention amount” being “withheld from payment” and “deposited” into “an account” at “a financial institution.” The statutory machinery is built around money moving through a bank account. A bank guarantee is an instrument issued by a bank to the principal — nothing has been “withheld from payment” to the subcontractor, and there is no cash to deposit anywhere.

Section 35A reinforces the point from the other direction. It is a criminal offence for a trustee to deposit anything into the retention trust account other than (a) a retention amount withheld from payment under a contract for which the trustee is the contracting party, or (b) a repayment of an amount withdrawn in error. Putting the cash proceeds of a called bank guarantee into the retention trust account would not fall within either limb — the proceeds of a guarantee are not a “retention amount withheld from payment” to the contracted party. The trust account is for cash retention, full stop.

The practical consequence is that if your subcontract permits security in the form of either cash retention or a bank guarantee, only the cash retention engages Chapter 2. If the subcontractor delivers a bank guarantee for the full security amount, no retention trust is required for that contract. If the subcontractor delivers half cash and half guarantee, only the cash half goes into the retention trust account. The guarantee sits outside the regime entirely and is governed by its own terms and the general law of guarantees.

The contracted party’s charge

Money in the trust account is also subject to a statutory charge in favour of the contracted party. Section 33A creates a charge over the retention amount “for securing the release of the amount when the party becomes entitled to the amount.” The charge is enforceable as if it had been given under a written agreement, it is declared to be a statutory interest for the purposes of s 73(2) of the Personal Property Securities Act 2009 (Cwlth) (so it does not need to be registered on the PPSR to be effective), and it is GST-inclusive. The charge is released over a portion of the retention amount only when the contracting party becomes entitled to be paid that portion under the contract — for example, on a properly substantiated set-off for defective work.

What this means on a project

  • If you are the contracting party on a withholding contract, the cash you deduct from a progress payment must hit the retention trust account on the day you withhold it. There is no grace period on a fresh withholding contract.
  • The amount deposited is GST-inclusive. Don’t deposit the ex-GST figure and treat the GST as your own working capital — that’s a deposit-obligation breach.
  • Bank guarantees, parent-company guarantees and insurance bonds are outside the regime. You don’t open a trust account for a contract secured entirely by guarantee, and you don’t put the proceeds of a called guarantee into a trust account if you do happen to have one for other contracts.
  • If the security is mixed (part cash, part guarantee), only the cash component goes into trust. Track the two streams separately.
  • A contract clause that says retention will be held in your general account, or released on terms inconsistent with the deposit obligation, is of no effect to that extent under s 35(3). Don’t rely on bespoke retention drafting to displace the regime.
  • Treat the contracted party’s s 33A charge as a real encumbrance on the cash. An attempt to defeat it — for instance, by routing the money through a related entity — is “of no effect” against the contracted party under s 33A(5).
  1. See s 33 BIF Act (trust established by withholding) read with s 35 (deposit obligation) and s 35A (limited purposes for which money may be deposited). The combined effect is that the only money that can lawfully be in a retention trust account is cash retention withheld under a withholding contract for which the trustee is the contracting party, plus repayments of amounts withdrawn in error and interest credited by the financial institution.

Retention trust accounts

Opening the retention trust account

Once a retention trust is required under s 32, the contracting party has to open a dedicated bank account to hold the retention money. The Act controls three things about that account: when it has to be opened, the name it has to be held under, and the kind of financial institution that can hold it. Get any of those wrong and you are exposed to a penalty before a single dollar of retention has even been withheld.

Section 34 BIF Act — contracting party must open retention trust account

(2) The contracting party must open an account for the retention trust at a financial institution—

(a) if the retention trust is required under section 32(1)—before the retention amount is withheld; or

(b) if the retention trust is required under section 32(2)—within 20 business days after the contract becomes a withholding contract.

Maximum penalty—500 penalty units.

(3) However, the contracting party need only establish 1 retention trust account for all retention amounts withheld by the party under any number of contracts for which it is the contracting party.

When the account has to be opened

The timing rule splits according to which limb of s 32 brought the trust into existence. If the trust is required under s 32(1) — the ordinary case, where the contract is a withholding contract from the outset — the account must be open before the retention amount is withheld. You cannot withhold first and bank later. The first dollar of retention deducted from a progress payment has to land in an account that already exists.

If the trust is required under s 32(2) — the case where a contract becomes a withholding contract part-way through, typically because a later variation or further contract pushes the party over the threshold — you have 20 business days from the day the contract becomes a withholding contract to get the account open. That window only applies to the s 32(2) trigger; it does not extend the s 32(1) deadline.

The penalty for missing either deadline is 500 penalty units. That is the highest penalty attached to the opening obligations and reflects the structural importance of the account being in place before retention money starts flowing.

One account, many contracts

Section 34(3) makes clear that a contracting party only needs one retention trust account, not one per contract. Every retention amount the party withholds, across every contract for which it is the contracting party, can sit in the same account. That keeps the compliance burden manageable for principals and head contractors who run multiple jobs, and it fits with the way the rest of Part 4 is drafted — the trustee’s record-keeping and notification obligations are calibrated to a single pooled account.

The account name

Section 34A(2) requires the trustee to ensure the account is held under a name that includes both the trustee’s name and the word “trust”. Practically that means the account label your bank prints on statements has to identify the trustee entity (the contracting party) and contain the literal word “trust” — for example, “ABC Constructions Pty Ltd Retention Trust”. A generic project account, or an account in the trustee’s name without the word “trust”, will not satisfy the section. The maximum penalty is 200 penalty units.

The naming requirement matters for a reason that goes beyond compliance: the account name is the principal external signal that the funds are trust money rather than the contracting party’s general operating cash. If the contracting party becomes insolvent, the account name is part of how a liquidator, the contracted parties and the commissioner identify the trust pool.

The financial institution

Section 34A(1) requires the account to be held at an “approved financial institution”. The list of approved institutions is maintained administratively; it is not enough that a bank is willing to open the account, it has to be one that has been approved for the purpose of holding retention trust money. Holding the account anywhere else is an offence carrying up to 200 penalty units.

If you later want to move the account, s 34C controls the change. The receiving institution must also be an approved financial institution, every dollar in the existing account has to transfer with the account, and the trustee has to inform every contracted party from whom retention has been withheld about the transfer in the form prescribed by the regulation. Section 34B layers a separate notification obligation on top: the commissioner has to be notified, in an approved way, within 5 business days of opening, closing, renaming or transferring the account.

What this means on a project

  • If your contract is a withholding contract from day one, open the retention trust account before you make the first deduction. Do not wait for the first progress claim and then scramble — the s 34(2)(a) deadline is “before the retention amount is withheld”, and the maximum penalty is 500 penalty units.
  • If a contract becomes a withholding contract mid-project (for example through a variation that pushes you over the s 32(2) threshold), diarise the 20-business-day window from that date to get the account open.
  • You only need one retention trust account across your business, not one per contract. Pool the retention amounts from every contract you administer as contracting party in the same account.
  • When you open the account, make sure the account name on the bank’s system includes your full trustee entity name and the word “trust”. “Project retention” or your trading name alone will not satisfy s 34A(2).
  • Confirm with your bank that the account is being opened at an approved financial institution for the purposes of s 34A(1). Do not rely on a generic business transaction account — ask the bank to confirm the product is suitable for a BIF Act retention trust.
  • Within 5 business days of opening the account, lodge the s 34B notice with the commissioner using the approved way. The same notice obligation applies if you later rename, close or transfer the account.
  • If you ever need to switch banks, work through s 34C: confirm the destination institution is approved, transfer every dollar across with the account, and notify all affected contracted parties as prescribed by regulation.
  1. Sections 34, 34A, 34B and 34C of the BIF Act, read with s 32, set out the opening, naming, location and notification regime for a retention trust account. The list of approved financial institutions is maintained administratively by the commission.

Retention trust accounts

Beneficiaries (whose retention it is)

A retention trust is not the head contractor’s money parked in a special account. It is held on trust for the party from whom the retention was withheld — the contracted party. That is who the beneficiary is, and the whole of Part 2A is built around protecting that person’s interest in the cash until they earn the right to be paid it back.

Sections 33, 33A and 36 BIF Act — how the trust is established and who benefits

33 Establishment of retention trust

(2) The retention trust is established by the contracting party withholding the retention amount from payment.

33A Charge over retention amounts held in retention trust

(2) In addition to the retention trust, the retention amount is also subject to a charge in favour of the contracted party for securing the release of the amount when the party becomes entitled to the amount.

36 Limited purposes for which money may be withdrawn from retention trust account

(1) A trustee must not withdraw an amount from the retention trust account for any purpose other than—

(a) paying a beneficiary who is a contracted party from whom a retention amount was withheld from payment…

The contracted party is the beneficiary

The trust is created automatically the moment the contracting party (the head contractor, or anyone above the subcontractor in the chain who is doing the withholding) holds back a retention amount from payment. There is no need for a deed, a declaration, or any positive act of settlement — s 33(2) does the work. The beneficial interest sits with the contracted party: the subcontractor, supplier or trade whose money it is. They are referred to in the Act as the “contracted party from whom a retention amount was withheld from payment”, and in s 36(1)(a) they are named as “a beneficiary” of the trust.

So on a typical project the beneficiaries of the head contractor’s retention trust account are all of the subcontractors whose progress payments have been clipped for retention. Each of them has a separate beneficial interest, contract by contract, in the portion of the pooled account that traces back to their withheld cash. The head contractor is the trustee; it is not the beneficiary of its own account.

What the beneficial interest carries with it

Being the beneficiary is more than a label. Three protections attach to that status under Part 2A:

  • A statutory charge. Section 33A gives the contracted party a charge over the retention amount “for securing the release of the amount when the party becomes entitled to the amount”. The charge is enforceable by the contracted party as if it had been given under a written agreement,1 any act done to defeat it is of no effect against the contracted party, and it is declared to be a statutory interest under s 73(2) of the Personal Property Securities Act 2009 (Cwlth) — meaning it does not need to be registered on the PPSR to bite.
  • A locked withdrawal regime. The trustee can only take money out of the account to pay the beneficiary, to fund correction of defects or omissions in that beneficiary’s contracted work, or to secure performance of that beneficiary’s contract (s 36(1)). Money cannot be moved between subcontractors’ entitlements, and it cannot be applied to the trustee’s general business.
  • Transactional transparency. Every deposit into and withdrawal out of the retention trust account must be notified to the contracted party from whom that retention amount was withheld within 5 business days (s 40A). Beneficiaries are entitled to see the trust moving, not just the closing balance at release.

Release: when the beneficiary actually gets paid

Identifying the beneficiary matters most at release. Once the contracted party becomes entitled to the retention — typically at practical completion, or at end of the defects liability period for the second tranche — the trustee “must not release the retention amount other than by… withdrawing the amount from the retention trust account; and… depositing the amount into the contracted party’s account at a financial institution” (s 36A(2)). Release has to be a direct trust-to-beneficiary electronic transfer. Set-off by journal entry, payment to a related party, or netting against another debt are not release.

And when the trust over a particular retention amount is exhausted — everything has been released to the contracted party who was entitled to it — the trust is dissolved (s 37(1)). Only at that point can the trustee pay itself any residue, and only to the extent the residue is “not owing to another beneficiary” (s 37(2)). Until then the cash belongs, beneficially, to the subcontractor.

What this means on a project

  • If you are the subcontractor whose retention has been withheld, you are the beneficiary. The money in the head contractor’s retention trust account that traces to your withheld payments is held on trust for you, not as part of the head contractor’s working capital.
  • You have a statutory charge under s 33A in addition to the trust. You don’t need to register anything on the PPSR — the charge is a statutory interest under s 73(2) of the PPSA. If the head contractor goes under, that charge is your security for getting the retention back.
  • If you are the head contractor running the account, you are the trustee, not the beneficiary. You cannot dip into the account for general cash flow, you cannot use one subcontractor’s retention to fix another subcontractor’s defects, and you cannot release retention by book entry — release has to land in the subcontractor’s nominated bank account (s 36A).
  • Keep the deposit and withdrawal notices under s 40A flowing to each beneficiary. Failure to notify is a 100-penalty-unit offence and is also an early-warning signal to QBCC that the trust is being run loosely.
  • At release time, line up the entitlement (under the contract) with the statutory release mechanic (under s 36A). The contract tells you when the beneficiary becomes entitled; the Act tells you how the release has to be effected.
  1. Section 33A(4) BIF Act: “The contracted party may enforce the charge as if the charge had been given to it under a written agreement between it and the contracting party.” The s 33A charge sits alongside the s 33 trust — the beneficiary therefore has both a beneficial interest in the trust fund and a statutory charge over the retention amount.
  2. For the trustee’s residual interest after all beneficiaries have been paid out, see s 37(2) BIF Act: on dissolution “the trustee may pay itself all amounts held in the trust that are not owing to another beneficiary”. The trustee’s entitlement is residual only — subordinate to every contracted party’s claim.

Retention trust accounts

Releasing retention back to the subcontractor

Retention isn’t a fund the head contractor gets to sit on indefinitely. Once the subcontractor becomes entitled to the money under the contract — typically at practical completion for the first half, and at the end of the defects liability period for the balance — the trustee must release it, route the payment through the trust account, and tell the subbie what has happened. The same regime that protects the money on the way in controls how it comes out.

Section 36A BIF Act — release of retention amounts from the trust account

36A All retention amounts withheld to be released from retention trust account

(1) This section applies if a contracted party becomes entitled to the release of a retention amount held in a retention trust.

(2) The trustee must not release the retention amount other than by—

(a) withdrawing the amount from the retention trust account; and

(b) depositing the amount into the contracted party’s account at a financial institution.

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

When the entitlement to release arises
