Section 36A is a release-mechanism section, not a release-trigger section. The trigger sits in the underlying contract: the subcontractor “becomes entitled to the release of a retention amount” when the contract says the money is to come back — usually a milestone tied to practical completion (release of the first moiety) and to expiry of the defects liability period (release of the balance). Once that contractual entitlement crystallises, s 36A controls how the money is moved.

The mechanics are tightly drawn. The trustee may not release retention by any path other than withdrawing it from the retention trust account and depositing it into a financial-institution account in the subcontractor’s name. Set-off by journal entry, application against an unrelated debt, or payment in cash all sit outside s 36A and attract the 200 penalty unit / 1 year imprisonment maximum. The companion provision at s 36 reinforces the point from the other side: withdrawals from the trust account are confined to paying the contracted-party beneficiary, paying the trustee for correcting defects (and only after the defects liability period ends), or paying a third party engaged to correct defects.

Notice to the subcontractor on every withdrawal

The release is paired with a separate notification duty. Under s 40A, every time the trustee deposits a retention amount into the trust account, or withdraws all or part of one, the trustee must give the contracted party from whom the amount was withheld a notice of the deposit or withdrawal within 5 business days. The notice has to include the information prescribed by regulation, and the only carve-out is a “reasonable excuse”. The maximum penalty is 100 penalty units.

For the subcontractor, that means a release of retention — whether full or partial — should be matched by a written s 40A notice within a week. If the cash arrives but no notice follows, that is itself a contravention and a useful trigger to ask the head contractor what has been withdrawn and why. The duty also runs the other way: a withdrawal that goes to the trustee (under s 36(1)(b) for defect rectification) or to a third-party rectifier (s 36(1)(c)) still attracts a s 40A notice to the subcontractor, so the subbie should see every movement that affects their money.

Disputing a withdrawal or a refusal to release

The Act doesn’t set up a bespoke retention-dispute tribunal. Disputes about release tend to run on three tracks, and the right one depends on what is actually in issue.

  • Contractual entitlement disputes. If the argument is whether the subcontractor has reached the milestone that releases retention — practical completion, end of defects liability, no outstanding defects — that is a contract dispute. It is resolved through the contract’s dispute mechanism, by adjudication under chapter 3 of the BIF Act if the released retention is claimed in a payment claim, or by court proceedings. Adjudication is often the fastest route because the released retention is a “progress payment” once the entitlement has accrued.
  • Misuse of the trust account. If the trustee has withdrawn money for a purpose outside s 36(1) — for example, applying retention to an unrelated debt, or withdrawing for defect rectification before the defects liability period has ended (prohibited by s 36(2)) — that is a trust-account contravention. The statutory remedies sit with the QBCC: s 36(3) requires the trustee to repay any amount withdrawn in contravention of s 36(1) as soon as practicable, and s 36A and s 36 carry their own penalties. The contracted party also has the benefit of the statutory charge under s 33A securing release of the amount, enforceable as if granted under a written security agreement, and protected against acts purporting to defeat it.
  • Refusal to release once entitlement has crystallised. If the contract clearly says the money is due and the trustee will not pay, the subbie can include the released retention in a payment claim and pursue adjudication, sue for the debt, or report the conduct to the QBCC as a likely breach of s 36A. The statutory charge under s 33A means the retention is treated as secured property in the contracted party’s favour — the charge is a statutory interest under s 73(2) of the Personal Property Securities Act 2009 (Cwlth) — which materially improves the subbie’s position if the head contractor goes into external administration before release.

Closing out the trust

Once every retention amount under a contract has been released to the parties entitled to it, the retention trust for that contract is dissolved (s 37(1)). On dissolution the trustee may pay itself any residual amount that is not owing to another beneficiary (s 37(2)). The retention trust account itself is a different question: under s 34A(4) the trustee must not close the account unless all retention amounts have been released to the parties entitled, or the account is being transferred to another approved financial institution under s 34C.

What this means on a project

  • Map your release entitlements off the contract, not the Act. Diary the practical completion date and the defects liability end date for every subcontract, and treat each as the moment the corresponding moiety becomes payable.
  • Expect a s 40A notice within 5 business days of any deposit or withdrawal that touches your retention. If a release lands in your account without a notice, ask for one in writing — the notice is the trustee’s obligation, not a courtesy.
  • If a withdrawal looks like it has gone to the head contractor for “defects” before the defects liability period has ended, that is a s 36(2) issue. Raise it in writing, ask for repayment under s 36(3), and report to the QBCC if it isn’t corrected.
  • To force release of retention you say is overdue, the fastest route is usually to include the amount as a line in your next payment claim and run it through adjudication. The contractual entitlement is the live question; s 36A simply controls how the trustee must pay once it loses.
  • If the head contractor’s solvency is in doubt, remember that the s 33A charge sits over the retention amount in your favour and is protected against acts that would defeat it. That is your principal protection if administration or liquidation intervenes before release.
  • Keep your own ledger of retention withheld, deposit notices and withdrawal notices. When release time comes, the s 40A notices — and the absence of any unexplained withdrawals — are the evidence trail you will rely on.
  1. Section 36A BIF Act prescribes the only permitted method of release: withdrawal from the retention trust account and deposit into the contracted party’s account at a financial institution. Maximum penalty 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.
  2. Section 40A BIF Act — trustee must give the contracted party written notice of every deposit or withdrawal of a retention amount within 5 business days, including the information prescribed by regulation, unless there is a reasonable excuse. Maximum penalty 100 penalty units.
  3. Section 36 BIF Act confines withdrawals from the retention trust account to paying the contracted-party beneficiary, paying the trustee for correcting defects (only after the defects liability period ends — s 36(2)), or paying a third party engaged to correct defects. Amounts withdrawn in contravention of s 36(1) must be repaid as soon as practicable (s 36(3)).
  4. Section 33A BIF Act creates a statutory charge over the retention amount in favour of the contracted party, securing release of the amount. The charge is declared to be a statutory interest to which s 73(2) of the Personal Property Securities Act 2009 (Cwlth) applies, and is inclusive of any GST.
  5. Section 37 BIF Act — the retention trust is dissolved once all amounts have been released to the parties entitled to them under the contract; on dissolution the trustee may pay itself any residual amount not owing to another beneficiary. Section 34A(4) prevents closure of the retention trust account itself except where all amounts have been released or the account is transferred to another approved financial institution under s 34C.

Retention trust accounts

Set-off / contract claims against retention

Cash retention sitting in a retention trust account is the contracted party’s money, held on trust, secured by a statutory charge in its favour under s 33A. The contracting party can only reach into that account for the narrow purposes listed in s 36 — and the most important of those, in practice, is using retention to fund defect rectification or to back the head contractor’s contract claims for set-off. The Act does not give an independent right of recourse; it tells you when a recourse the contract already provides for can be exercised against trust money.

Section 36 BIF Act — Limited purposes for which money may be withdrawn from retention trust account

(1) A trustee must not withdraw an amount from the retention trust account for any purpose other than—

(a) paying a beneficiary who is a contracted party from whom a retention amount was withheld from payment; or

(b) paying the trustee, as contracting party, for the purpose of correcting defects or omissions in contracted work, or otherwise to secure, wholly or partly, the performance of a contract; or

(c) paying another person for the purpose of correcting defects or omissions in contracted work.

Note—As the contracting party for a contract, the trustee’s ability to make a payment mentioned in paragraph(a), (b) or (c) would be governed by the contract under which the retention amount was withheld.

(2) A trustee must not withdraw an amount from the retention trust account for a payment mentioned in subsection(1)(b) until after the defects liability period, applying to the amount, ends.

(3) The trustee must repay all amounts the trustee withdraws in contravention of subsection(1) as soon as practicable after withdrawing the amount.

(5) This section does not apply to that part of a retention amount withheld from payment under a contract that is beyond the amount that may be lawfully withheld under the contract.

The contract has to authorise the recourse first

The note to s 36(1) is doing real work. It says that the trustee’s ability to make a payment under paragraphs (a), (b) or (c) “would be governed by the contract under which the retention amount was withheld”. Section 36 is permissive, not enabling: it tells you the categories of withdrawal the BIF Act will tolerate from the trust account, but it does not itself give you a right to set off, to rectify, or to call on the retention. That right has to be in the contract — usually a defects-liability clause, a set-off clause, or a security/recourse clause. If the contract does not give you a right to apply the retention to a contract claim, s 36 will not manufacture one.

So before any withdrawal under s 36(1)(b) or (c), the contracting party needs to be able to point to: the contractual right being exercised (e.g. a clause permitting set-off of moneys due or to become due, or a clause permitting the principal to have defects rectified at the contractor’s cost and recover that amount from retention); the factual trigger that has engaged it (a notified defect not rectified within the allowed period, an established cross-claim, a debt now due); and the quantum, calculated honestly. A withdrawal that goes further than the contract allows is a contravention of s 36(1) and must be repaid as soon as practicable under s 36(3), with the trustee exposed to a maximum penalty of 300 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment.

Defect rectification — s 36(1)(b) and (c)

Paragraphs (b) and (c) are the two defect-rectification limbs. Under s 36(1)(b) the contracting party can pay itself out of the trust account where it has done the rectification work in-house, or wants to apply the retention to secure performance of the contract more generally — for example, to fund completion after termination, where the contract permits. Under s 36(1)(c) the contracting party can pay a third party who is doing the rectification work — the rectification contractor or trade engaged to fix the defect.

Section 36(2) imposes an important timing brake on s 36(1)(b) only: the trustee cannot withdraw under that paragraph until after the defects liability period applying to the amount has ended. The policy is straightforward — while the defects liability period is still running, the contracted party should be given the chance to come back and rectify itself. Paying yourself out of trust to do work the subcontractor is still entitled to do under the contract is not allowed. Note that s 36(2) only applies to s 36(1)(b) — payments to a third-party rectifier under s 36(1)(c) are not subject to the same defects-liability-period gate, reflecting that those payments are usually made when the contracted party has failed or refused to rectify.

Set-off for other contract claims

The words “or otherwise to secure, wholly or partly, the performance of a contract” in s 36(1)(b) are broader than defects. They pick up the standard set-off use case: the contracting party has a contract claim against the contracted party — liquidated damages, back-charges, an overpayment on a previous claim, damages for breach — and the contract permits that claim to be set off against money the contracting party would otherwise owe, including retention. Where the contract supports it, the retention can be applied to that claim under s 36(1)(b). The s 36(2) defects-liability-period restriction still applies to a withdrawal under that paragraph, so the timing point matters: the contracting party can hold the retention against the claim, but cannot actually withdraw it from the trust account until the defects liability period has ended.

Two practical limits sit on top of this. First, s 36(5) carves out from the whole of s 36 any part of a retention amount that exceeds “the amount that may be lawfully withheld under the contract” — a reference back to the QBCC Act part 4A retention caps. Money over-withheld in the first place was never properly trust money, and s 36 doesn’t prevent its return. Second, the QBCC Act minimum financial requirements and the unfair-contract-terms regime in the QBCC Act both bear on whether the contractual set-off clause being relied on is itself enforceable.

Dispute mechanism

The BIF Act does not set up a bespoke dispute procedure for retention set-off. If the contracting party gives notice that it intends to apply retention to a contract claim, and the contracted party disputes that — whether on the merits, on quantum, or on the timing under s 36(2) — the contracted party has the same suite of remedies it would have for any unpaid retention amount. The most direct route is usually a payment claim under Chapter 3 for the disputed retention as a progress payment, followed by adjudication if the payment schedule disputes it. Adjudicators routinely value retention sums and rule on whether a claimed set-off has been made out: the question is whether, on the contract and the facts, the principal had a present right to apply the retention against the claim it has identified.

Alongside the Chapter 3 route, the contracted party can sue on the statutory charge in s 33A — that charge sits in its favour over the retention amount, secures release when the party becomes entitled, and is enforceable as if granted by written agreement (s 33A(4)). The trustee’s obligation under s 36A — that release must be by withdrawal from the trust account and direct deposit to the contracted party’s account — reinforces that the money is the contracted party’s once entitlement crystallises. And s 36(3) gives the contracted party a self-help repayment claim if the contracting party has already withdrawn money in contravention of s 36(1): the trustee must repay it “as soon as practicable” after the contravention.

If the contracted party contends the contracting party is taking retention contrary to s 36 — for example, withdrawing under s 36(1)(b) before the defects liability period has ended, or paying itself for work the subcontractor was never given the chance to rectify — a complaint to the QBCC about the trustee’s conduct is also available. The QBCC commissioner’s audit and direction powers over retention trustees are the supervisory backstop; the criminal penalties in s 36(1) and (3) are there to deter, not to remedy, and on a live project the adjudication route is the practical one.

What this means on a project

  • Section 36 controls when retention can leave the trust account, but it does not create a right of set-off. Find the right in the contract first — a defects-rectification clause, a set-off clause, or a security/recourse clause — before drawing on the retention.
  • For defect rectification done in-house, withdrawals are under s 36(1)(b) and cannot occur until the defects liability period for that retention has ended (s 36(2)). Until then, the contracted party gets the first chance to rectify.
  • For defect rectification done by a third-party rectifier, withdrawals are under s 36(1)(c) and the s 36(2) timing brake does not apply — reflecting that you usually only engage a third party because the contracted party hasn’t rectified.
  • For other contract claims — liquidated damages, back-charges, overpayments — the “otherwise to secure, wholly or partly, the performance of a contract” words in s 36(1)(b) cover set-off where the contract permits it. The defects-liability-period brake in s 36(2) still applies; you can hold the retention against the claim but cannot actually withdraw it until that period ends.
  • Document the entitlement before you withdraw: identify the contract clause, the defect notice or back-charge, the cost, and the calculation. The trustee record-keeping obligations apply, and an unjustified withdrawal must be repaid as soon as practicable under s 36(3).
  • Section 36(5) is a useful reminder that any over-withheld retention — above the caps in part 4A of the QBCC Act — was never properly trust money and is not protected by s 36 against being returned.
  • If the contracted party disputes a proposed or completed set-off, the dispute is resolved on the contract: usually by a Chapter 3 payment claim for the disputed retention, with adjudication on whether the set-off was contractually open. Section 33A gives a parallel charge enforceable as if under a written agreement, and the QBCC has supervisory jurisdiction over the trustee’s conduct.
  • The criminal penalty for a wrongful withdrawal is serious — 300 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment for contraventions of s 36(1) and s 36(3). Treat the retention trust account as a regulated trust account, not a working capital line.
  1. The note to s 36(1) makes the contractual basis explicit: the trustee’s ability to make a payment under paragraphs (a), (b) or (c) “would be governed by the contract under which the retention amount was withheld”. The BIF Act does not create an independent right to apply retention to a claim; it polices the categories of withdrawal that the trust framework will tolerate.
  2. The s 36(2) timing restriction is on s 36(1)(b) only. Withdrawals to a third-party rectifier under s 36(1)(c) are not subject to the same defects-liability-period gate. Both paragraphs (b) and (c) require that the work being paid for fall within “correcting defects or omissions in contracted work”, and (b) extends to securing performance of the contract more generally.
  3. On retention caps: s 36(5) puts beyond doubt that the section does not apply to retention withheld in excess of the amount lawfully able to be withheld under the contract. The lawful retention caps are in the QBCC Act part 4A; over-withheld retention is recoverable independently of the s 36 framework.
  4. On the related provisions: s 33A creates the statutory charge in favour of the contracted party, enforceable as if granted by written agreement (s 33A(4)); s 36A requires release of retention by withdrawal from the trust account and direct deposit to the contracted party’s account; s 35A confines the deposit side of the account to retention withdrawn from the relevant contracts and corrections of withdrawals made in error.

Retention trust accounts

Notifications

Running a retention trust under chapter 2 of the BIF Act is not just a matter of opening the bank account and parking the money. The Act layers three separate notice obligations on top: a notice to the QBCC commissioner whenever you open, close, transfer or rename the account; a notice to the contracted party telling them their retention will be held in trust before you withhold a cent; and a transactional notice to that contracted party every time you deposit money into, or withdraw money from, the account on their behalf. Each carries its own penalty unit exposure and its own short timing window, and each is drafted in mandatory terms.

Sections 34B, 40 and 40A BIF Act — the three notification obligations

34B Notice of retention trust account’s opening, closing or name change

(1) This section applies if a trustee, or another person on behalf of the trustee, takes any of the following actions in relation to the retention trust account—

(a) opens the account;

(b) changes the name of the account;

(c) closes the account;

(d) transfers the account.

(2) Within 5 business days after taking the action, the trustee must, using an approved way, give the commissioner a notice—

(a) stating the action taken; and

(b) including the information prescribed by regulation.

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units.

40 Notice of retention trust before withholding retention amount

(1) If, under section 32, a retention trust is required for a retention amount withheld from payment under a contract, the contracting party must give the contracted party a notice about the use of a retention trust (notice of retention trust) as required by this section.

Maximum penalty—200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

(2) The notice of retention trust must—

(a) be in writing; and

(b) include a statement that a retention trust will be used for withholding retention amounts under the contract; and

(c) include the information prescribed by regulation.

(3) The notice of retention trust must be given to the contracted party before withholding the retention amount.

(4) However, if the retention trust account was not opened before the retention trust is required under section 32, the notice of retention trust must be given to the contracted party within 5 business days after opening the account.

40A Beneficiary to be informed of transactions affecting retention amount

(1) This section applies if a trustee—

(a) deposits a retention amount into the retention trust account; or

(b) withdraws all or part of a retention amount held in the retention trust account.

(2) Within 5 business days after making the deposit or withdrawal, the trustee must give to the contracted party from whom the retention amount was withheld a notice of the deposit or withdrawal that includes the information prescribed by regulation, unless the trustee has a reasonable excuse.

Maximum penalty—100 penalty units.

(3) This section does not apply to the deposit of a retention amount if the trustee has informed the contracted party of the deposit under section 23A.

Notifying the commissioner about the account itself

Section 34B is the regulator-facing notice. Whenever the trustee, or somebody acting on the trustee’s behalf, opens the retention trust account, renames it, closes it or transfers it to another financial institution, the QBCC commissioner must be told within 5 business days. The notice has to be given “using an approved way” — in practice the QBCC’s online portal — and it has to state the action taken and include whatever further information the regulation prescribes. The penalty for getting this wrong is 200 penalty units. The trigger is the action itself, not when you got around to documenting it; if you open the account on a Monday, the clock starts running that day.

The s 34B obligation pairs with s 34C (change of financial institution), which separately requires the trustee to inform every contracted party whose retention is held in the account about a transfer to an alternative financial institution. So a transfer event generates two notifications — one to the commissioner under s 34B(1)(d), and one to each affected contracted party under s 34C(1)(c) — and both have to be in hand within their respective windows.

Telling the subcontractor before you withhold

Section 40 is the front-end notice to the contracted party. Before you withhold a single dollar of retention under a contract that triggers a retention trust under s 32, you must give the contracted party a written “notice of retention trust” saying that retention amounts under the contract will be held in a trust, plus the prescribed information. This is not a courtesy; the maximum penalty is 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.1

The default rule is that the notice goes out before the first withholding. There is one carve-out in s 40(4): if the retention trust account had not been opened before the retention trust was required under s 32 — for example, where an existing contract becomes a withholding contract under s 32(2) and the trustee opens the account in response — the notice can be given within 5 business days after opening the account. That is the only relief from the “before withholding” sequencing.

Practically, the s 40 notice should be embedded in the back-office workflow so it goes out at the same moment the contract is executed (or, for a contract that becomes a withholding contract mid-stream, at the same moment the account is opened). Because a term of a contract that is inconsistent with the deposit obligation in s 35 is of no effect, you cannot contract around the notice either — even a sophisticated subcontractor cannot “waive” their entitlement to it.

Transactional notices for deposits and withdrawals

Section 40A is the running, transaction-level notice. Every time the trustee deposits a retention amount into the account, or withdraws all or part of one, the contracted party from whom that amount was withheld must be given a notice of the deposit or withdrawal within 5 business days, including the information prescribed by regulation. The penalty is 100 penalty units, but there is a “reasonable excuse” defence built into the section.

Two features of s 40A are worth flagging. First, the obligation runs per transaction and per contracted party, not in a periodic statement form — if you make ten withdrawals across a month, that is potentially ten separate notices. Second, s 40A(3) carves out duplicate notices: if the trustee has already informed the contracted party of a deposit under s 23A (the project trust deposit-notice provision), a fresh s 40A notice for the same deposit is not required. That avoids double-handling where the same trustee operates a project trust and a retention trust on the same project.

What this means on a project

  • Build a single notification register that tracks all three streams: s 34B (commissioner notices for the account), s 40 (one-off notice to each contracted party at the front of the contract), and s 40A (one notice per deposit and per withdrawal). Each stream has a 5-business-day clock except the s 40 default, which runs to “before withholding”.
  • For s 40, send the notice as part of contract execution — the first progress claim is too late if any of it includes retention. If you are catching an existing contract under s 32(2), open the account first, then issue s 40 notices within 5 business days under s 40(4).
  • For s 34B, treat “opens, names, closes, transfers” as four discrete trigger events and don’t bundle them. A rebrand of the trustee company that flows through to the account name is a name change under s 34B(1)(b) and triggers its own 5-business-day clock.
  • For s 40A, automate the deposit/withdrawal notices off the bank feed if you can. The 100 penalty unit exposure is per breach, and it scales quickly across a portfolio of subcontractors.
  • Use the “approved way” for the s 34B notice — the QBCC portal — not email or post. The s 40 and s 40A notices are not constrained to an “approved way”, but they must be in writing for s 40 and must include the regulation-prescribed information for both.
  • Keep evidence of dispatch and of the prescribed-information content for every notice. The penalties for the front-end s 40 notice include imprisonment, and proof of compliance will sit with the trustee.
  1. The s 40 notice is one of the few obligations in chapter 2 that carries an imprisonment exposure (1 year), reflecting its role as the gateway notice that puts the contracted party on notice that the retention regime is engaged. The deposit obligation in s 35 and the limited-purpose withdrawal restriction in s 36 carry comparable 2-year imprisonment maxima, underlining that the retention trust regime is treated by parliament as a quasi-fiduciary scheme rather than a purely administrative one.

Retention trust accounts

Records, reporting, training and audit

Running a retention trust account is not just a banking exercise. The BIF Act treats the trustee — usually the head contractor or principal holding cash retention from a subcontractor — as the steward of trust money, and backs that role with hard obligations to keep records, cover shortfalls, and submit the account to independent audit. The penalties for getting it wrong are not theoretical: failure to keep records carries up to 300 penalty units or a year’s imprisonment, and failure to engage an auditor up to 200 penalty units or a year’s imprisonment. This page pulls those obligations together.

Section 52 BIF Act — trust records

The trustee for a project trust or retention trust must—

(a) keep records for the trust as prescribed by regulation; and

(b) retain the records for at least 7 years after the trust is dissolved.

Maximum penalty—300 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

Keeping and retaining records

Section 52 is the bedrock obligation. Two duties sit inside it. The first is to keep the records — the form and content are pushed out to regulation, but the obligation to keep them is in the Act itself. The second is to retain the records for at least seven years after the trust is dissolved. The clock does not start when the project finishes or when the last retention is released; it starts when the trust is dissolved, and runs for seven years from that point. In practice that means a head contractor cannot purge retention trust records on completion of the works. The records have to be archived in a form that can be produced years later, well after the project team has demobilised.

The seven-year retention period is also why record-keeping cannot be treated as a project-level task. The trustee — the legal entity holding the trust — carries the obligation across all of its retention trusts, and the records have to survive personnel changes, system migrations and corporate restructures.

Shortfalls and the trustee’s personal exposure

Section 51 sits alongside the records obligation and bites the moment the account does not have enough money to pay a beneficiary. If there is an insufficient amount in the trust account to pay an amount due to a beneficiary, the trustee “must immediately deposit an amount equal to the shortfall in the trust account” — called a shortfall deposit. The maximum penalty is 100 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment. The point for a project director is that a retention trust shortfall is not a commercial dispute to be worked through over weeks; it is a statutory trigger that requires the trustee to top the account up immediately from its own funds, and then sort out the underlying cause separately.

Annual auditor review under s 57

The trustee for a retention trust must engage an auditor to carry out a review of the trust account. Section 57 is the operative section and several mechanical points fall out of it:

  • The engagement of the auditor must comply with the requirements prescribed by regulation (s 57(2)).
  • The review must be carried out at the times prescribed by regulation, and the “review period” itself is the period prescribed by regulation (s 57(3)–(4)).
  • Once the review starts, it must be complete within 40 business days (s 57(5)).
  • The auditor must be independent of the trustee and must not have been excluded by the commissioner under s 54E (s 57(6)).

Independence is defined exhaustively in s 57(7). The auditor cannot be an employee of the trustee; if the trustee is a company, the auditor cannot be an executive officer, investor or shareholder of that company; if the trustee is a partnership, the auditor cannot be a partner; and the auditor cannot be a related entity for the trustee. In a corporate group setting that captures the obvious cases — the in-house finance team, a sister company auditor, or the trustee’s usual statutory auditor where there is a shareholding or related-entity link — and rules them out.

When the review is not required

Section 57(8) carves out three situations where the trustee does not need to engage an auditor. The first is where no retention amount was held in the account during the review period and the trustee gives the commissioner, within 10 business days after the end of the review period, a written statement using an approved way explaining why no auditor was engaged. The second is where there are no transactions or changes for the account during the review period and the trustee gives the commissioner, within the same 10-business-day window, a written statement using an approved way confirming that fact. The third is any further circumstance prescribed by regulation. The exceptions are narrow and procedural — they do not get the trustee out of recordkeeping; they only switch off the audit step for a dormant account.

What the auditor produces and where it goes

The output of the s 57 review is the “account review report” defined in s 50 by reference to s 57A(1). Section 58 then deals with what happens if the auditor’s own conduct is problematic. If the commissioner reasonably suspects the auditor’s conduct breaches a professional standard or condition, the commissioner may inform the relevant professional body and give it any information necessary to investigate. Crucially, the commissioner must redact all information identifying the trustee of the project trust or retention trust before passing material on (s 58(3)). For a trustee that is concerned about commercial confidentiality if its auditor comes under scrutiny, that statutory redaction is the protection the Act provides.

Register and approved financial institutions

Two background controls support all of the above. The commissioner must maintain a register of project trusts and retention trusts of which the commissioner has been notified, and may publish information about them (s 53). And trust accounts can only be held at a financial institution the commissioner has approved — approval is conditional on the institution having an agreement with the commission about providing financial services for trust accounts, which can extend to telling the commissioner what is in the accounts and to auditing arrangements (s 55). The register and the approved-institution regime are the framework that makes the audit and records obligations enforceable in practice.

What this means on a project

  • Treat retention trust records as a corporate-level archive, not a project file. The seven-year clock under s 52(b) only starts when the trust is dissolved — design your records system so the file survives demobilisation, system changes and personnel turnover.
  • Build a shortfall protocol into your treasury function. Section 51 requires the shortfall to be deposited “immediately” — you cannot wait for an internal investigation. Make sure someone with payment authority can move funds into the trust account on the day the shortfall is identified.
  • Diary the s 57 review window. The review period and timing are set by regulation; once the review starts you have 40 business days to complete it. Engage the auditor early enough that the 40-day clock is not a problem.
  • Test the auditor’s independence against s 57(7) before engagement. Employees, executive officers, investors, shareholders, partners and related entities are out. If you are using your usual statutory auditor for the trust review, confirm there is no related-entity link.
  • If the account has been dormant or held no retention during the review period, you can use the s 57(8) exception — but only if you give the commissioner the written statement, in the approved way, within 10 business days after the end of the review period. Miss that window and the audit obligation revives.
  • Only deposit retention trust money in a financial institution the commissioner has approved under s 55. The list is published on the commission’s website; check it before opening the account.
  1. Section 52 BIF Act sets out the records obligation and the seven-year retention period from dissolution of the trust; the form and content of the records are prescribed by regulation under s 52(a).
  2. On shortfalls, see s 51 BIF Act; the trustee’s obligation to make a “shortfall deposit” is immediate and the maximum penalty is 100 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.
  3. On audit, see s 57 BIF Act (engagement, timing, 40-business-day completion, independence), s 57(8) (dormant-account exceptions and 10-business-day notification), and the “account review report” definition in s 50 by reference to s 57A(1). On commissioner reporting of auditor misconduct, see s 58, including the mandatory redaction of trustee-identifying information under s 58(3).
  4. For supporting infrastructure, see s 53 (commissioner’s register of project trusts and retention trusts) and s 55 (approval of financial institutions at which trust accounts may be kept, and publication of approved institutions on the commission’s website).

Retention trust accounts

Penalties for non-compliance

The retention trust regime in Chapter 2, Part 3 is enforced almost entirely through criminal-style penalties attached to specific obligations. Most of the operative duties — opening the account, depositing the retention, releasing it back to the subcontractor, notifying the commissioner, completing training — carry their own maximum penalty, and the heavier ones include a custodial alternative. Understanding which obligation carries which penalty matters because it tells you where the regulator and the courts are most likely to push back, and where the personal exposure of the trustee’s officers is greatest.

Penalty units across Chapter 2, Part 3 BIF Act

s 34(2) — failure by the contracting party to open a retention trust account at a financial institution within the time required: 500 penalty units.

s 34A(1) — account not held at an approved financial institution: 200 penalty units.

s 34A(2) — account not held under a name that includes the trustee’s name and the word ‘trust’: 200 penalty units.

s 34A(3) — deposits or withdrawals made by methods other than ones that create an electronic record: 500 penalty units.

s 34A(4) — closing the account other than as permitted: 200 penalty units.

s 34B(2) — failure to notify the commissioner of opening, closing, name change or transfer of the account within 5 business days: 200 penalty units.

s 34C(1) — transferring the account to an alternative financial institution otherwise than as permitted: 200 penalty units.

s 35(2) — failure by the contracting party to deposit the retention amount into the retention trust account: 200 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment.

s 35A(1) — depositing money into the account for any purpose other than withholding a retention amount or repaying a withdrawal made in error: 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

s 36(1) — withdrawing for an unauthorised purpose: 300 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment.

s 36(2) — withdrawing for a defects-correction payment to the trustee before the defects liability period ends: 300 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment.

s 36(3) — failure to repay an amount withdrawn in contravention of s 36(1) as soon as practicable: 300 penalty units or 2 years imprisonment.

s 36A(2) — releasing a retention amount otherwise than by withdrawing it from the trust account and depositing it into the contracted party’s account: 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

s 40(1) — failure to give the contracted party a notice of retention trust before withholding the amount: 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.

s 40A(2) — failure to notify the contracted party of a deposit or withdrawal within 5 business days, without reasonable excuse: 100 penalty units.

s 41(5) and s 41(6) — failure to ensure that the nominated administrator (or, if none, the trustee itself) completes the prescribed retention trust training within the prescribed time: 100 penalty units.

The structure of the penalty regime

Look at the penalties as a hierarchy and the legislative priorities are obvious. The heaviest exposure — 300 penalty units or two years’ imprisonment — sits on s 36, which polices what comes out of the retention trust account. Withdrawing for an unauthorised purpose, withdrawing too early for defects work, or failing to repay money withdrawn in breach of the section all attract the same maximum. The next tier — 200 penalty units or two years’ imprisonment — sits on s 35(2), the deposit obligation: the act that puts money into the trust in the first place. Together, these two sections carry the regime. If retention money never reaches the trust, or is taken out for the wrong reason, the protective machinery in Part 3 has nothing to operate on.

A second tier of imprisonable offences — one year, plus 200 penalty units — targets three integrity rules: s 35A(1) (no foreign deposits into the trust account), s 36A(2) (release must be by trust-to-beneficiary bank transfer, not cash, set-off or in-kind), and s 40(1) (the contracted party must be told, in writing, that a retention trust is being used before the money is withheld). These provisions exist to keep the trust account untainted, to ensure release happens in money rather than by accounting entries, and to put the subcontractor on notice that their retention is held on trust rather than as a general debt.

Penalty-only obligations

The remaining contraventions are punishable by a fine alone. The 500-penalty-unit obligations — s 34(2) (open the account) and s 34A(3) (use only electronic-record methods for deposits and withdrawals) — are the highest of the fine-only band, reflecting that without an account, and without an audit trail, none of the downstream protections function. The 200-penalty-unit band covers the account-naming, account-location, account-closure, commissioner-notification and institution-transfer rules in s 34A and ss 34B–34C. The lightest penalties — 100 penalty units — sit on the beneficiary-notification obligation in s 40A(2) and the training obligation in s 41(5)–(6).

The training and notification penalties being lower does not mean those obligations can be deprioritised: they are the obligations the commissioner is most likely to detect on a routine audit, because they leave the clearest paper trail. Missing the s 34B(2) notice of an account opening is the kind of thing that surfaces by comparison of bank records to commissioner records, with no need for a complaint from a subcontractor.

Imputed conduct under s 36(4)

Section 36(4) extends the withdrawal offence by deeming the trustee to have made a withdrawal where the trustee either authorises another person to make it, or knowingly contributes to it being made. That is significant in a corporate-trustee context: a director who signs off on a payment instruction, or who knowingly allows the bookkeeper to draw down retention to fund payroll, is within the deeming provision and is exposed to the s 36(1) maximum. The penalty is on the trustee, but the deeming language — “authorises any person”, “knowingly contributes” — is the hook that practically reaches the people inside the trustee.

What this means on a project

  • The two offences carrying imprisonment maxima of two years — s 35(2) (failure to deposit) and s 36(1)/(2)/(3) (unauthorised withdrawal or failure to repay) — are where the regulator’s and the courts’ attention will be sharpest. Build your retention controls around those two events: every retention amount goes in, and nothing comes out except for the s 36(1)(a)–(c) purposes (and only after the defects liability period for the s 36(1)(b) trustee-payment limb).
  • Treat s 36(4) as a personal-exposure provision. A director or officer who authorises or knowingly contributes to a non-compliant withdrawal is within the deeming language. Document who has authority over the trust account and confine that authority to people who understand the s 36(1) purposes.
  • Don’t use the retention trust account as a convenience deposit. Section 35A(1) prohibits any deposit other than a retention amount or a repayment of a withdrawal made in error — one year’s imprisonment available. If a deposit is made into the wrong account by mistake, repay it under s 35A(1)(b), don’t leave it in.
  • Release retention only by bank transfer from the trust account to the contracted party’s account (s 36A(2)). Do not release by set-off, by cheque drawn on the trustee’s general account, or by leaving the money on account. The penalty is 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.
  • Give the s 40 notice of retention trust to the contracted party in writing before retention is withheld. If the account had not been opened in time, the notice must follow within 5 business days of opening (s 40(4)). The penalty for missing s 40 entirely is 200 penalty units or 1 year’s imprisonment.
  • The lower-tier penalties — account naming and location (s 34A(1)–(2)), commissioner notifications (s 34B(2)), institution transfers (s 34C(1)), beneficiary notices (s 40A(2)) and training (s 41(5)–(6)) — are the items most likely to appear on a commissioner audit. Calendar them and treat them as compliance hygiene, not optional admin.
  • All deposits and withdrawals must be made by methods that create an electronic record (s 34A(3) — 500 penalty units). No cash, no over-the-counter cheques without electronic record. The audit trail is itself a statutory obligation.
  1. A “penalty unit” takes its dollar value from the Penalties and Sentences Act 1992 (Qld), s 5 and the Penalties and Sentences Regulation 2015; the value is reset annually. The figures in the body of this page are the BIF Act maxima expressed in penalty units, not in dollars.
  2. The deeming rule in s 36(4) covers both authorising a withdrawal and knowingly contributing to one being made. Read with s 36(1), it is the principal route to personal liability for officers of a corporate trustee.
  3. Sections 38 and 39 are not reproduced here as the source files were not available; readers should consult the current reprint of the BIF Act for any obligations and penalties contained in those sections.

Valid subcontractor’s charge

Who can give a notice of charge

Chapter 4 reserves the subcontractor’s charge for a particular kind of person in a particular kind of relationship. Before you spend any time on form, content or timing, you need to be sure you are within the class the chapter protects — a “subcontractor” who has contracted to perform “work” under a contract with a “contractor”. If you are outside that class, the notice has nothing to bite on and any charge purportedly given under s 122 will not attach.

Section 104 BIF Act — Definitions (extract)

contractor, for a contract, means the party to the contract who is required to carry out work under the contract.

contract price, for a contract, means the amount the contractor is entitled to be paid under the contract or, if the amount can not be accurately calculated, the reasonable estimate of the amount the contractor is entitled to be paid under the contract.

land means any land within Queensland and includes land under water.

subcontractor’s charge see section 109(4).

work see section 105.

The contractor / subcontractor relationship

Section 104 defines “contractor” as the party to the contract who is required to carry out work under the contract. The chapter then operates downwards: a person who has contracted with that contractor (or with another subcontractor) to perform part of the work is the “subcontractor” entitled to give a notice of charge under s 122. The status is fixed by the contract, not by what happens on site. As the Court put it in Re Gradeline Contracting Pty Ltd [1998] 2 Qd R 251, “what characterises a subcontractor as defined is not what he does but that he contracts with a contractor for the performance of works”.1

Two points follow. First, you must identify the right counterparty. The chain matters: your charge attaches to money payable to your contractor by the person above your contractor (the employer or superior contractor), so you need a real, bilateral contract with the party immediately above you in the chain. A direct employee, a labour-hire worker placed on site by an agency, or someone supplying material to the head contractor on a sale-of-goods basis is not a subcontractor in the s 104 sense. Second, the inquiry is about what was contracted to be done, not what was actually done on the day. The court will read the agreement and ask whether what the claimant promised to do is “work” within s 105.2

The work has to be “work” under the chapter

Eligibility is not just about the label “subcontractor”; it is about the subject matter of the contract. Section 105 carries an inclusive definition of “work” that captures construction, alteration, repair, decoration, demolition, removal or relocation of a building or structure, plus the supply of materials used or brought on premises in connection with that work. Several recurring fact patterns sit awkwardly with that definition and routinely knock claimants out of the chapter:

  • Mere supply of goods. A contract that does no more than oblige a party to fabricate and deliver chattels, with no work to be done after delivery, is not a contract for work and will not support a charge — whether the head contract is for fabrication and delivery, or it is the subcontract that is so limited.3
  • Pure hire of plant or machinery. Hiring out compressors, plant or machinery, without the supplier doing work on the land, is excluded. Where the “hire” in truth involves the subcontractor providing plant and an operator who performs the work, the arrangement may be a contract for work despite the contractual label.4
  • Off-site work that is not integral to the construction. Work done off the land where the structure is being built is generally not work within the chapter; if a substantial part of what was contracted is not work, none of the contract may fall within the regime.5
  • Damages, interest and restitution. Damages for breach of contract, costs and interest on a judgment, and damages for conversion are not “work done” and cannot be the subject of a charge.6

The territorial limit — land in Queensland

The chapter only applies where the land on which the head contract work is being performed is in Queensland. That is the consequence of the s 104 definition of “land” (“any land within Queensland”) read with the operative provisions of the chapter. In Transfield Pty Ltd v Fondside Australia Pty Ltd [2000] QSC 480, Mullins J considered a notice of charge given by the receiver of a subcontractor for work on a gas pipeline running between Victoria and NSW. Her Honour held that the equivalent legislation applied only where the relevant land was within Queensland; the reference to “land” in a Queensland statute is prima facie a reference to land within the jurisdiction, and there was nothing in the Act to suggest a wider territorial reach. That construction is now reflected on the face of s 104. If your project is in another State, Chapter 4 simply does not give you a charge, no matter how clearly you fit the “subcontractor” description.

Strict construction of the entitlement

The courts have consistently treated the chapter as a remedial scheme conferring a special privilege on a restricted class, the price of that privilege being strict compliance with its terms.7 Eligibility is the first place that strictness bites: if you are not on the chapter’s side of the line — if you are a supplier rather than a subcontractor, or your “work” is really hire, or the project sits in another jurisdiction — the charge does not attach and a notice purporting to create one is, in the language of s 122(9), “of no effect”.

What this means on a project

  • Map the contractual chain before you serve. Identify who your contract is with, who that party’s contract is with, and where the money you want to charge is sitting. The notice goes to the person above your contractor — you need to be sure that person is “the person obliged to pay the money under the contract” for the purposes of s 122(1).
  • Read your subcontract and ask the Gradeline question: what did you contract to do? If the agreement reduces to delivering goods, hiring out plant, or providing services that are not integral to construction on Queensland land, you may not be a “subcontractor” for chapter 4 purposes — even if everyone on site calls you one.
  • Treat “hire” labels with care. A contract called a “hire agreement” that in substance requires you to operate the plant and perform the work can still be a contract for work; conversely, dressing up a bare equipment hire as a “subcontract” will not bring it within the chapter.
  • Strip out the parts of your claim that are damages, interest or pure cost recovery. Those amounts cannot be charged under s 122 and including them risks rendering the notice non-compliant.
  • Confirm the work is on land within Queensland. Cross-border infrastructure, offshore fabrication and interstate projects need a separate analysis — do not assume the chapter follows the work.
  • If eligibility is borderline, take advice before serving. A notice given by a person outside the protected class produces no charge and may expose you to the costs and damages consequences in s 130 if it has been used to freeze payment.
  1.   2. On the inclusive nature of the “work” definition, see Sun Engineering (Qld) Pty Ltd v Dynac Pty Ltd [2000] QSC 213 (Holmes J); Sempac Pty Ltd v Stockport (Q) Pty Ltd [2004] QDC 87 (Wall DCJ). On the “what was contracted to be done” inquiry, see Caldow Properties Ltd v H J G Low & Assoc Ltd [1971] NZLR 311 (Turner J at 315), cited with approval by de Jersey J in Re Leighton Contractors Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 377.
  3. Re Peter Fardoulys Pty Ltd [1983] 1 Qd R 345 at 348 (a contract for fabrication and delivery of chattels with no further work is not a contract for work); Re Bulk Materials (Coal Handling) Pty Ltd (Administrators Appointed) (unreported, QSC, Demack J, No 3 of 1997, 7 February 1997). The mere delivery of ordered goods is not work: Re Gradeline Contracting Pty Ltd [1998] QDC 099 (McMurdo DCJ).
  4.   5.   6. On liquidated debt claims that may still found a charge, compare Re Northbuild Pty Ltd (unreported, QSC, Shepherdson J, OS 6399 of 1998, 25 September 1998).
  7. Valid subcontractor’s charge

What money can be charged

A subcontractor’s charge does not bite on the world at large. It attaches only to a specific pool of money — the money owing up the contractual chain to the contractor who engaged you, plus any retention held under that contract. Identifying that pool, and describing it accurately in the notice of claim, is what makes the charge work.

Section 122 BIF Act — notice of claim (extract)

(1) To claim a subcontractor’s charge over money payable to the contractor under the contract, the subcontractor must give written notice (a notice of claim) to the person obliged to pay the money under the contract.

(6) The claim may relate only to—

(a) money payable to the subcontractor by the date the notice is given; and

(b) money to become payable to the subcontractor after the date the notice is given if the money is for work done by the subcontractor before that date.

(7) To remove any doubt, it is declared that a subcontractor’s charge on money payable under the contract includes a charge on a retention amount for the contract.

Money payable to the contractor under the contract

The fund the charge attaches to is the money the person above your contractor (the employer or a higher subcontractor) owes that contractor under their contract. You are reaching past your own contractual counterparty and grabbing what they are owed by the level above. The notice goes to that paying party, not to your own contractor: s 122(1).

Two things follow. First, your claim is capped by what is actually owed up the chain — if there is nothing payable to your contractor, there is nothing for the charge to attach to. Second, the moneys must be identifiable. The notice must allow the paying party to work out, on receipt, which contract’s funds are being charged and to what extent. Where a notice covers work spanning two separate head contracts and does not break the amount down between them, the money sought to be charged cannot be ascertained from the notice and the charge will not attach.1

Retention is included — and it changes the timing

Section 122(7) confirms that the charge over “money payable under the contract” includes a charge on the retention amount. You have two practical options. You can lodge a notice that captures money generally payable under the contract (which will sweep in retention as part of that fund), or you can lodge a notice expressly limited to the retention amount only. The choice matters because it changes the deadline.

A notice over money generally payable under the contract must be given within 3 months after practical completion: s 122(5). A notice limited to retention can be given at any time while work is being performed, and must be given within 3 months after the defects liability period expires: s 122(8). So if you are past the 3-month post-completion window and only retention is left, a notice expressed in terms of money generally payable will be too late — you need a retention-only notice.2

The Court of Appeal’s reading in Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Abigroup Contractors Pty Ltd [2005] 1 Qd R 610; [2005] QCA 61 confirms that “completion” for the 3-month rule means completion of the whole subcontract works, not completion of the particular item of work the claim is about. That is the appellate position you should plan around when calculating the cut-off.3

Describing the fund — don’t blur the categories

The charge will fail if the notice is ambiguous about which fund it is reaching for. The leading caution is Re FFE Group (Qld) Pty Ltd [1984] 1 Qd R 267, where McPherson J considered a notice that described the money as “money and/or retention money”:

such a formula led to uncertainty about which money was subject to the statutory charge and so liable to be retained and would therefore eliminate the distinction maintained between the two… It was accordingly a matter of substance and not merely a matter of form.

Compare that with Road Surfaces Group Pty Ltd v Brown [1987] 2 Qd R 792, where Williams J accepted a notice referring to “all moneys, including retention moneys”: a charge can be claimed on retention only, on other moneys only, or on both — the only consequence of the third option is that the tighter 3-month-after-completion deadline applies, not the longer retention deadline.4 The line between the two cases is whether the notice positively claims both funds or leaves the reader to guess.

Scope: what you can claim and when

Section 122(6) defines the temporal scope of the claim. You can claim money already payable to you when you give the notice, plus money that will become payable after the notice for work you did before the notice. You cannot reach forward to charge future work that has not yet been performed. If you keep working and earn more after lodging, that later work needs to be picked up by a fresh claim, not absorbed into the original one. And under s 122(4) the notice can be lodged before payment falls due or before the work is finished — you do not have to wait for an unpaid invoice.

The s 113 anti-avoidance rule

Section 113 closes off the obvious workaround. For the purposes of the charge, the “money payable to the contractor under the contract” is taken to include money the paying party has already paid out in reduction of the contract price to anyone other than you, unless that payment was made in good faith, not for the purpose of defeating the charge, and without contravening s 126. So an employer who hurries a payment to the contractor (or a third party) once it senses a charge coming cannot point to an empty account when the notice lands.

What this means on a project

  • Work out what is actually owed up the chain to your contractor before you draft the notice. The charge can only attach to that fund — if nothing is payable, there is nothing to charge.
  • Send the notice to the paying party (the employer or higher subcontractor), not just to your own contractor. The notice up the chain is the one that creates the charge under s 122(1).
  • Decide deliberately whether you are claiming over money generally payable, retention only, or both — and say so clearly. Avoid “and/or” formulations that blur the two.
  • Calendar both deadlines. 3 months after practical completion of the whole subcontract works for a general notice; 3 months after the defects liability period for a retention-only notice. If you miss the first window and the only money left is retention, lodge a retention-only notice.
  • If your work straddles two contracts between your contractor and the same paying party, split the amount between them in the notice. A single un-allocated figure across two contracts will not be ascertainable.
  • If you suspect the paying party is racing to pay your contractor out before your notice lands, s 113 is your friend — payments made to defeat the charge are still treated as money payable for charge purposes.
  1. CND Earthmoving Pty Ltd v Packer & Jonasson (unreported, Dist Ct Bne, 13 January 1995) — notice covering work under two separate contracts without allocation between them: money sought to be charged not ascertainable; charge did not attach. See also Walter Construction Group Ltd v J & L Schmider Investments Pty Ltd [2001] QSC 124 (Muir J) — notice struck out where the basis and make-up of the claim could not be detected.
  2. Re Bunnerin Pty Ltd (in liq) [1990] 1 Qd R 525 at 526–527 (Senior Master McLauchlan QC): retention is charged under a notice expressed simply as money payable under the contract, but only if that notice is given within 3 months after completion; for the longer retention-only deadline the notice must be limited in terms to retention. See also Re University of Queensland [2003] 2 Qd R 577; [2003] QSC 158 (Mullins J).
  3. Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Abigroup Contractors Pty Ltd [2004] QSC 198 (Chesterman J) at [35], upheld in Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Abigroup Contractors Pty Ltd [2005] 1 Qd R 610; [2005] QCA 61, [21]–[22] (Jerrard JA): “completion” for s 122(5) means completion of the whole of the subcontract works, not the particular item the claim is about.
  4. Road Surfaces Group Pty Ltd v Brown [1987] 2 Qd R 792 at 804 (Williams J); Re Galaxy Investments Pty Ltd (in liq) (unreported, QSC, White J, 17 August 1993) — a notice claiming a charge on “money and retention money that is now or will be payable” was held to depart from the form in a way going beyond mere inaccuracy.

Valid subcontractor’s charge

Content of the notice of claim

A subcontractor’s charge only attaches if the notice of claim itself is up to scratch. The notice has to be in the approved form, identify the contract and the parties, state a certified amount, and set out enough detail of the work for the recipient to know what is being charged. Get the contents wrong and s 122(9) is unforgiving: the notice is of no effect and the charge does not attach.

Section 122(2)–(3) and (9) BIF Act — what the notice must contain

(2) The notice of claim must be made in the approved form and—

(a) state the amount of the claim; and

(b) include details of the work done by the subcontractor, certified as prescribed by a qualified person; and

(c) include the other information prescribed by regulation.

(3) The amount of the claim must be certified by a qualified person, as prescribed by regulation.

…

(9) If the notice of claim is not given in compliance with this section, the notice is of no effect and the subcontractor’s charge does not attach.

The approved form and the certified amount

The first thing the notice has to do is be in the approved form and state the amount of the claim, with that amount certified by a “qualified person” under s 147(1) and the regulation. The form does the heavy lifting on identification: it requires you to name the contractor and the person obliged to pay (the recipient, usually the principal or the next contractor up the chain), to identify the contract under which the money is payable, and to state the money sought to be charged. The Court of Appeal has made clear that there is no specific statutory wording you have to use — the section is “permissive” on form — provided the substance is there.1

Particulars of the work and the money charged

Section 122(2)(b) requires “details of the work done by the subcontractor”. The test the courts apply is one of substance: the contents of the notice must be sufficient to indicate the moneys sought to be charged and the amount of the claim, both ascertainable with reasonable certainty from the face of the notice.2 A bare description of the works subject to the claim will not do. In McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd v Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd [2013] QSC 223 the notice described the moneys claimed as “Monies payable to claimant pursuant to subcontract for the earthworks, concrete and pond liner works on the project”. The Supreme Court held that was insufficient information to identify the work the subject of the claim, and the notice failed to comply with the section.

Two practical points fall out of that. First, where there is more than one contract between the contractor and the recipient, the notice must make clear which contract’s moneys are being charged and to what extent — otherwise the recipient cannot tell from the notice what to retain.3 Second, attaching invoices, payment claims or a statement of account that itemises the work and the amounts is the usual way of meeting s 122(2)(b). If you signal an annexure on the face of the notice and then fail to attach it, the document is incomplete and is not a notice at all until the missing material is supplied.4

Money or retention — pick a lane

The notice must be clear about whether it charges money payable under the contract, retention only, or both. The leading appellate guidance is Road Surfaces Group Pty Ltd v Brown [1987] 2 Qd R 792, where the notice referred to “all moneys, including retention moneys”. Williams J, reading s 10 of the SCA (now s 122 BIF Act) as a whole, held that a charge may be claimed on retention only, on other moneys only, or on both:

a charge may be claimed either on retention money, or secondly on moneys otherwise payable under the contract, or thirdly on retention moneys and moneys otherwise payable under the contract. The only material consequence flowing from the third categorisation is that such notice would have to be given within three months of the completion of the work, and not within the extended period permissible when the charge is sought against retention moneys only.

Hedged language — “money and/or retention money”, or “money and retention money that is now or will be payable” — has been struck down because it leaves the recipient unable to tell which money is subject to the charge.5 If your timing is anywhere near the three-month limit after practical completion, and you can only meet the longer retention-only window in s 122(8), restrict the notice in terms to retention money for the contract.

Identifying the contract and the parties

The recipient of the notice has to be the actual person obliged to pay the contractor under the contract. Naming the wrong legal entity — for example, an executive department instead of the State, or a parent instead of the contracting subsidiary — will usually be fatal, but a notice will still be valid where the inaccuracy can be cured because the moneys to be charged and the amount of the claim are ascertainable with reasonable certainty from the notice itself.6 Likewise, identifying the contract is straightforward where there is one subcontract; where there are several, spell out which one and tie each amount back to it.

What this means on a project

  • Use the approved form. Don’t reinvent it. There is no statutory requirement to use any particular wording, but the form prompts the information the section and the regulation require.
  • Get the amount certified by a qualified person under s 147(1) before service. An uncertified amount, or certification by the wrong person, takes you straight to s 122(9).
  • Particularise the work. Attach invoices, payment claims or a statement of account that itemises scope, periods and amounts. Avoid generic descriptions like “monies payable under the subcontract for the works”.
  • Decide before drafting whether the notice charges money payable under the contract, retention only, or both — and say so unambiguously. Avoid “and/or” formulations.
  • Name the recipient and the contractor as the legal entities they are on the contract. Cross-check ABN, ACN and contracting party against the executed agreement.
  • If you reference an annexure in the body of the notice, attach it. A signalled-but-missing annexure means there is no notice until the annexure is provided, and time runs from then.
  • If the first notice is defective, you can serve a fresh, compliant notice within time — provided no charge from an earlier notice has already been extinguished by operation of the Act.7
  1. Parker Bros Constructions Pty Ltd v Hancock [2001] QCA 397 (the section is “permissive” and does not require any particular form of words).
  2. Re Austco Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 1, 5 (Thomas J) (contents must be sufficient in substance to indicate the moneys sought to be charged); Paks Contractors Pty Ltd v Maruko Inc [1993] 1 Qd R 119, 122–124 (McKenzie J) (validity preserved despite inaccuracy or want of form where moneys and amount can be ascertained with reasonable certainty from the notice).
  3. CND Earthmoving Pty Ltd v Packer & Jonasson (unreported, Dist Ct Bne, 13 January 1995) (notice covering work under two separate contracts — recipient unable to ascertain the amount charged against each); cf Quality Concrete Pty Ltd v Honeycombes Townsville Pty Ltd [2005] QSC 192 (Cullinane J) (two interrelated subcontracts read together; notice valid because amount and moneys clear on its face); Walter Construction Group Ltd v J & L Schmider Investments Pty Ltd [2001] QSC 124 (Muir J) (notice struck out where basis and make-up of claim impossible to detect).
  4. Ed Ahern Plumbing (Gold Coast) Pty Ltd v JM Kelly (Project Builders) Pty Ltd [2008] 2 Qd R 123; [2007] QCA 452, [3]–[4], [42] (Holmes JA) (document signalling an annexure that was not attached was incomplete and not a notice; notice given only when missing annexure later supplied; time to commence proceedings ran from then).
  5. Re FFE Group (Qld) Pty Ltd [1984] 1 Qd R 267, 270–271 (McPherson J) (“money and/or retention money” produced uncertainty about which money was charged — matter of substance, not form); Re Galaxy Investments Pty Ltd (in liq) (unreported, QSC, White J, 17 August 1993) (“money and retention money that is now or will be payable” — departure from the form going beyond mere inaccuracy).
  6. Walsh & Donaghay v Queensland (unreported, Dist Ct Mackay, Wylie QCJ, 20 May 1987) (notice naming “The Manager, State Works Department” rather than the State of Queensland — saved on the facts because it reached the employer through its agents); Stumann v Spansteel Engineering Pty Ltd [1986] 2 Qd R 471; Paks Contractors Pty Ltd v Maruko Inc [1993] 1 Qd R 119, 124 (McKenzie J) (notice naming a wholly-owned subsidiary of the employer — inaccuracy not fatal).
  7. Re Peter Bushnell & Co Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 383 (a fresh, compliant set of notices may be served within time where the first set, due to irregularities, did not create a charge); cf Evans Deakin Industries Ltd v Commonwealth [1985] 2 Qd R 152, 153 (where a charge has been extinguished by operation of the Act, a further set of notices cannot resurrect it).

Valid subcontractor’s charge

Supporting certificate (s 124)

If a third party holds a security for the head contract — typically a financier with a fixed and floating charge, or a surety holding retention or a bank guarantee — your subcontractor’s charge will only bite on that security if the security holder is also given a copy of the notice of claim. Section 124 is the rule that drags the security holder into the loop, and the consequence of skipping it is narrow but real: the charge does not attach to the security, even though it can still attach to the money owed under the contract.

Section 124 BIF Act — copy of notice of claim to the holder of a security

124 Copy of notice of claim to the holder of a security

(1) This section applies if—

(a) a subcontractor gives a notice of claim to a person (the recipient) obliged to pay money to a contractor under a contract; and

(b) a person other than the recipient holds a security for the contract.

(2) The subcontractor must give a copy of the notice of claim to the person who holds the security.

(3) If a copy of the notice of claim is not given as required under subsection (2), the claimed subcontractor’s charge does not attach to the security.

(4) To remove any doubt, it is declared that subsection (3) does not affect a subcontractor’s charge to the extent it otherwise attaches under this chapter.

When the section is engaged

Section 124 only switches on where two facts coincide: you have given a s 122 notice of claim to the person obliged to pay your contractor (the “recipient”), and someone other than that recipient holds a security for the head contract. If there is no third-party security — for example, the principal holds its own retention internally and no separate party stands behind the contract — s 124 has no work to do, and your charge proceeds on the s 122 / s 123 service alone.

The trigger is the existence of a security “for the contract” held by a third party. Common examples in practice are a bank guarantee or insurance bond provided by the contractor and held by the principal’s financier or by a surety; an unconditional undertaking lodged with a separate trustee; or retention monies held by a third-party stakeholder rather than by the recipient itself. If you are unsure whether a third-party security exists, the head contract and any project-specific deed of guarantee will normally tell you — and if there is room for doubt, serve the copy anyway.

What “does not attach to the security” actually means

The drafting of subsections (3) and (4) is deliberately surgical. If you fail to give the copy to the security holder, the only thing you lose is the charge over that security. The charge can still attach to money payable to the contractor under the head contract in the ordinary way under chapter 4. Subsection (4) puts that beyond argument: failure to comply with s 124(2) does not unravel a charge that has otherwise validly attached.

That is a quite different outcome from a failure of service under s 122 or s 123. Under those sections, the notice itself is “of no effect and the subcontractor’s charge does not attach” at all. Under s 124, the charge survives — it just can’t reach the third-party security. In practical terms, you keep your priority over the money the principal still has in its hands, but you lose any claim against, say, the bank guarantee or the externally-held retention bond that you might otherwise have looked to if the principal became insolvent.

How to comply

The section requires that the security holder be given “a copy of the notice of claim” — that is, the same s 122 document that goes to the recipient. There is no separate prescribed form, no separate certification step, and no statutory time limit. As a matter of prudent practice, the copy should go out at the same time as the s 122 notice and the s 123 copy, by the same s 39 / s 109X service route used for the recipient. Section 124 inherits the silence of s 123 on timing, so the safer course is to treat the “commercial urgency” benchmark that applies to the s 123 copy as a working guide for the s 124 copy as well.

What this means on a project

  • Before issuing the s 122 notice, ask whether anyone other than the recipient holds a security for the head contract — a financier with a charge, a surety holding a bank guarantee, a trustee holding retention. If yes, s 124 is engaged.
  • Send the security holder a copy of the same notice of claim you gave the recipient. There is no separate form; the “copy” is what the section requires.
  • Use the same service route as for the s 122 notice — head office, registered office or principal place of business under s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld), or registered office / personal director-service under s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth). Avoid PO boxes and Dropbox-style links.
  • Send the s 124 copy at the same time as the s 122 notice and s 123 copy. The section sets no deadline, but treating the trio as a single mailing avoids argument later.
  • If you only realise after the event that a third-party security exists, serve the s 124 copy as soon as you find out. Your charge over the contract money is unaffected by the earlier omission — s 124(4) protects it — and you may still pick up the security if late service is accepted by the holder.
  • Keep the head contract, any deed of guarantee and any bond paperwork on file with your s 122 notice. If the principal later goes into administration, the security holder’s identity and address will be the first thing you need.

Valid subcontractor’s charge

Timing of the notice

A subcontractor’s charge only attaches to money that is still payable up the chain when the notice is given. Get the timing wrong and there is either nothing for the charge to bite on, or the notice is “of no effect” under s 122(9). The chapter sets two windows — one for a notice that covers progress money (and possibly retention as well), and a longer one for a notice that targets retention only — and it tells you what money the notice can reach once it is given.

Section 122 BIF Act — Notice of claim (timing extracts)

(4) The notice of claim may be given even if the work is not completed, or payment of the money relating to the charge is not yet due.

(5) However, if the work has been completed, the notice of claim must be given within 3 months after practical completion for the work.

(6) The claim may relate only to—

(a) money payable to the subcontractor by the date the notice is given; and
