(b) money to become payable to the subcontractor after the date the notice is given if the money is for work done by the subcontractor before that date.

(8) If the notice of claim relates only to a retention amount for the contract, the notice—

(a) may be given at any time while work under the contract is being performed; and

(b) must be given within 3 months after the expiration of the defects liability period for the contract.

(9) If the notice of claim is not given in compliance with this section, the notice is of no effect and the subcontractor’s charge does not attach.

The two windows

Section 122 contemplates two distinct timing regimes depending on what the notice is aimed at. A notice that claims money payable under the contract generally — progress payments, or progress payments together with retention — can be given before the work is finished and before payment is due (s 122(4)), but once the work has been completed the notice must be given within three months after practical completion for the work (s 122(5)). A notice that is expressed to claim against retention only travels on a different track: it may be given at any time while the contract work is being performed, and at the back end it must be given within three months after the expiration of the defects liability period (s 122(8)).

The choice between the two is a matter of substance, not labelling. Reading s 122 as a whole, a charge may be claimed either on retention money, or on moneys otherwise payable under the contract, or on both. The only material consequence of choosing the third category is that the notice has to be given within three months of completion of the work, and not within the extended period that is available where the charge is sought against retention only.1 A subcontractor who wants the longer window must restrict the notice in terms to retention money; a notice expressed simply in relation to “money payable under the contract” will charge retention as well, but it has to be given inside the three-month-from-completion window or it fails.2

What “completion” means for the three-month clock

The three months in s 122(5) runs from completion of the subcontract works, not from completion of any particular package of work that the claim happens to cover. That was decided in Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Abigroup Contractors Pty Ltd [2004] QSC 198, where Chesterman J held that “the work” in the equivalent provision had to mean the whole of the subcontract work in order for the related subsections to be reconciled. Reading the section that way meant the time limit did not start running until all of the work required by the subcontract had been done; the alternative would produce a separate time limit for each item of work as it was completed, which would defeat the protective purpose of the chapter and load an unworkable burden on subcontractors who were still on site. The Court of Appeal upheld that construction in Multiplex Constructions Pty Ltd v Abigroup Contractors Pty Ltd [2005] 1 Qd R 610; [2005] QCA 61, Jerrard JA endorsing the reasoning at [21]–[22].

The chapter also looks the other way. Section 122(4) makes clear that the notice can be given even though the work is not finished and even though payment is not yet due — you do not have to wait for an invoice to mature, or for practical completion, before you act. In practice, that means the safe course on a troubled job is to serve early rather than late: there is no “too soon” problem under s 122, only a “too late” one.3

What money the notice can reach

Timing is not just about when you serve. Section 122(6) limits what a validly-timed notice can attach to. The claim may relate only to money that is payable to the subcontractor by the date the notice is given, or money that becomes payable after that date if it is for work done by the subcontractor before the notice. You cannot use a single notice to capture future work you have not yet performed. If further work is done after the notice and you want to charge the money for it, a fresh notice is required — subject to the same s 122(5) and s 122(8) windows, and to the rule that successive notices can be given where the earlier ones were defective but not where a charge has been extinguished by the time-bar in the chapter.4

Withdrawal does not reset the clock

A subcontractor can withdraw a notice in whole or in part at any time, in the approved form, by giving notice of the withdrawal to the person to whom the original notice of claim was given, and by giving a copy to each person who received a copy of the original notice (s 125). Withdrawing a notice does not enlarge the time for giving a fresh one: any new notice still has to satisfy s 122(5) or s 122(8) as the case may be, measured from the original completion or defects liability period.

What this means on a project

  • Diarise practical completion of the subcontract works the day it occurs. The s 122(5) clock starts from completion of the whole of your subcontract, not from completion of a particular item or trade package — but it is still only three months.
  • If you want to claim progress money (with or without retention), serve before practical completion if you can, and in any event within three months after it. Section 122(4) lets you serve before payment is due; there is no advantage to waiting.
  • If only retention is left and the three-month-from-completion window has passed, you can still serve under s 122(8) — but the notice must be expressed to be limited to retention, and it has to be given before three months after the defects liability period expires.
  • Do not bundle future work into a single notice. The charge only reaches money for work done before the notice is given (s 122(6)(b)). Plan to serve a further notice if more work is performed after the first.
  • Track the defects liability period as carefully as you track practical completion. Many retention-only charges are lost because the DLP expired weeks earlier and nobody had it on the register.
  • If a notice has gone out with a problem — wrong addressee, missing certification, ambiguous description — consider whether there is still time to serve a corrected notice. A second, compliant notice within time is available where the first was defective; it is not available once a charge has been extinguished by operation of the chapter’s time-bars.
  1. Road Surfaces Group Pty Ltd v Brown [1987] 2 Qd R 792 at 804 (Williams J) (a charge may be claimed on retention money, on moneys otherwise payable, or on both; the only material consequence of the third category is the three-month-from-completion limit).
  2. Re Bunnerin Pty Ltd (in liq) [1990] 1 Qd R 525 at 526–527 (Senior Master McLauchlan QC) (to use the longer retention-only window the notice has to be expressed in terms of retention money only; a notice expressed as “money payable under the contract” will charge retention as well but must be given within three months of completion); see also Rees v Mount Isa City Council [1979] Qd R 553 at 556 (Matthews J) (during the course of the work there is no “retention money” for the purposes of the equivalent of s 122(5)).
  3. On the time of completion as a question of fact, see Re Austco Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 1 at 4 (Thomas J).
  4. Re Peter Bushnell & Co Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 383 (a second set of notices may be given within time where the first set, because of irregularities, did not create a charge); contrast Re Castley (unreported, QSC, Lucas SPJ, 18 September 1980) and Evans Deakin Industries Ltd v Commonwealth [1985] 2 Qd R 152 at 153 (where a charge has been extinguished by the chapter’s time-bar a further set of notices cannot be given to revive it).

Valid subcontractor’s charge

Service of the notice

A subcontractor’s charge under chapter 4 is a creature of statute, and it only comes into existence if two notices reach two different people the right way. Section 122 requires the notice of claim to go to the party above your contractor in the chain — the person obliged to pay the money — and s 123 requires a copy to go to your own contractor. Get either piece of service wrong and the consequence written into the Act is the same: the notice is “of no effect and the subcontractor’s charge does not attach”.

Sections 122 and 123 BIF Act — who is served, and what happens if you miss

122 Notice of claim

(1) To claim a subcontractor’s charge over money payable to the contractor under the contract, the subcontractor must give written notice (a notice of claim) to the person obliged to pay the money under the contract.

(9) If the notice of claim is not given in compliance with this section, the notice is of no effect and the subcontractor’s charge does not attach.

123 Copy of notice of claim to contractor

(1) This section applies if a subcontractor gives a notice of claim to a person obliged to pay money to a contractor under a contract.

(2) The subcontractor must—

(a) give the contractor a copy of the notice of claim; and

(b) advise the contractor of the name and address of the person given the notice of claim.

(3) If the subcontractor does not comply with subsection (2), the notice is of no effect and the subcontractor’s charge does not attach.

Two notices, two recipients

The chapter draws a careful distinction between the “notice of claim” itself, which is what creates the charge, and the copy that must go to the contractor under s 123. The notice of claim is served on “the person obliged to pay the money under the contract” — in practice the principal, employer or superior contractor sitting one step above your contractor in the chain. The copy goes to the contractor with whom you have your subcontract. Both must happen, and both must hit the right person.

Identifying the right payer matters because a notice given to the wrong entity will normally not save the charge. In Walsh & Donaghay v Queensland (Dist Ct Mackay, Wylie QCJ, 20 May 1987) a notice that named “The Manager, State Works Department” rather than the “State of Queensland” only survived because the notice in fact came into the possession of the employer through its agents; the inaccuracy was rescued by actual receipt, not by the form of the document. The lesson is to check the head contract before drafting: who is named as principal, what is their correct legal name, and what address have they given for service.1 A notice naming a wholly-owned subsidiary of the employer is not necessarily fatal where the subsidiary is in truth the contracting entity (Paks Contractors Pty Ltd v Maruko Inc [1993] 1 Qd R 119), but the safer course is to get the name right first time.

The s 123 copy — speed matters

Section 123 doesn’t set its own time limit, but the courts have read in a requirement of commercial urgency. In Re Austco Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 1, Thomas J described the obligation to give the contractor a notice of having made the claim as “something to be done with as little delay as possible” — a question of fact in each case, but one that “should commonly result in that notice being given to the contractor within a few days of the notice of claim of charge being given to the employer” (at p 4). The order of the two steps is not jurisdictional: the Court of Appeal in Ed Ahern Plumbing (Gold Coast) Pty Ltd v JM Kelly (Project Builders) Pty Ltd [2008] 2 Qd R 123; [2007] QCA 452 confirmed that a charge can attach where the copy is provided to the contractor before the notice has actually been transmitted to the employer, provided the subcontractor has at least taken the steps necessary to send it on (Holmes JA at [46]).2

Failure to serve the s 123 copy at all is a hard line. As Weld M put it in the unreported Re Austco decision, if the subcontractor fails to give notice of having made the claim to the contractor to whom the money is payable, “a charge does not attach”.3 Service on the contractor at somewhere other than its place of residence or registered office has been held to have the same effect (Re Peter Bushnell & Co Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 383).

How to actually serve — AIA s 39 and the corporate rules

Chapter 4 itself doesn’t prescribe a method of service, so the default rules in s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) do the work. For an individual, that means delivering the notice personally, or leaving it at, or sending it by post, fax or similar facility to, the address of the person’s last-known place of residence or business. For a body corporate, it means leaving it at, or sending it by post or similar facility to, the head office, a registered office or a principal office. Section 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) is also available for companies — leaving or posting to the registered office, or personal delivery to a director resident in Australia — and is not an exclusive code (Howship Holdings Pty Ltd v Leslie (No 2) (1996) 41 NSWLR 542).

Two cautions follow from the cases decided under the cognate s 102 service regime in chapter 3. First, service on a post office box is not, by itself, valid service under s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act or s 109X of the Corporations Act: CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209, [23] (Daubney J). Unless the contract authorises PO box service, do not use one for either the s 122 notice or the s 123 copy. Second, electronic service is not safely assumed. In Conveyor & General Engineering Pty Ltd v Basetec Services Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 30, McMurdo J held that emailing a link to documents on Dropbox was not effective service under either s 39 or s 109X — the document itself had not been “sent” or “left” at the relevant office. If you are going to email the s 122 notice or the s 123 copy, send the document itself to an address that is plainly the recipient’s business or registered email, and keep evidence of delivery.4

If you are relying on post under s 39A, hold the receipts. Service is taken to occur when the letter would be delivered in the ordinary course of post, but only if the contrary is not proved — and the onus of proving that it was properly addressed, prepaid and posted sits with the subcontractor. Bare evidence of attending a post office on a particular date will not always be enough to make out the regularity of postage.

What this means on a project

  • Map the chain before you draft. Identify the legal entity that is “the person obliged to pay the money” to your contractor — usually the principal or superior contractor — and find their correct corporate name and registered office. Get this from ASIC, not from the project directory.
  • Serve the s 122 notice on the payer at a recognised s 39 / s 109X address: head office, registered office, principal place of business, or to a director personally. Avoid PO boxes unless the head contract authorises them.
  • Serve the s 123 copy on your own contractor at its place of residence or registered office, and do it within days of the s 122 notice. “A few days” is the working benchmark from Re Austco; sitting on it because the section has no express deadline is not safe.
  • Don’t forget s 123(2)(b) — the copy must be accompanied by advice of the name and address of the person you served with the notice of claim. Putting the payer’s details in a covering letter to the contractor satisfies this in practice.
  • Use a service method you can prove. Personal delivery with a sign-off, courier with proof of delivery, registered post with tracking, or email to a clearly nominated business address all work. Keep the tracking numbers and delivery confirmations on file.
  • Avoid Dropbox links, OneDrive shares and similar “cloud” mechanisms for either notice. A link is not the document, and the chapter 4 service regime does not assume the recipient will open it.
  • If the head contract or your subcontract specifies a notice address or a method of service for the relevant party, follow it as well as the statutory route. Belt and braces is cheap; a charge that does not attach is not.
  1. Walsh & Donaghay v Queensland (unreported, Dist Ct Mackay, Wylie QCJ, No 194 of 1986, 20 May 1987) — an inaccurate description of the employer was rescued only because the notice in fact reached the employer through its agents; the case is authority for the proposition that failure to identify the actual employer will normally be fatal. See also Stumann v Spansteel Engineering Pty Ltd [1986] 2 Qd R 471; Paks Contractors Pty Ltd v Maruko Inc [1993] 1 Qd R 119 (McKenzie J at p 124 — naming the wholly-owned subsidiary of the employer is not, of itself, an invalidating inaccuracy).
  2. Re Austco Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 1 (Thomas J at p 4) — the s 123 copy is to be given with “all convenient speed” and “a sense of commercial urgency”, commonly within a few days of the s 122 notice; Ed Ahern Plumbing (Gold Coast) Pty Ltd v JM Kelly (Project Builders) Pty Ltd [2008] 2 Qd R 123; [2007] QCA 452 (Holmes JA at [46]) — a charge can still attach where the contractor is given the copy before the s 122 notice reaches the payer, provided the subcontractor has at least taken the steps necessary to transmit the s 122 notice. Parker Bros Constructions Pty Ltd v Hancock [2001] QCA 397 confirms that giving a copy of the notice of intention to claim a charge to the contractor is sufficient notice for s 123 purposes.
  3. Re Austco Pty Ltd (unreported, QSC, Weld M, OS 502 of 1984, 27 April 1984) at pp 2–3; Evans Deakin Industries Ltd v Commonwealth [1985] 2 Qd R 152 at p 153 — failure to give notice of having made the claim to the contractor means a charge does not attach; Re Peter Bushnell & Co Pty Ltd [1985] 2 Qd R 383 — service on the contractor otherwise than at its place of residence or registered place of business does not engage s 123.
  4. CMF Projects Pty Ltd v Masic Pty Ltd & Ors [2014] QSC 209, [16], [23] (Daubney J) — service on a post office box is not valid service under s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) or s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) absent contractual authorisation; Conveyor & General Engineering Pty Ltd v Basetec Services Pty Ltd & Anor [2014] QSC 30 (Philip McMurdo J at [29]–[32]) — emailing a link to a document hosted on Dropbox is not effective service under s 39 or s 109X because the document itself has not been sent or left at the recipient’s office; Howship Holdings Pty Ltd v Leslie (No 2) (1996) 41 NSWLR 542 — s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) is facultative and not an exclusive code for service on companies.

Valid subcontractor’s charge

Effect on the Chapter 3 process

A subcontractor’s charge under Chapter 4 and a progress-payment claim under Chapter 3 are two different ways to chase the same money — and the BIF Act does not let you run both at once. The moment you give a notice of claim of charge, s 62 reaches sideways into the Chapter 3 regime and shuts down the payment-claim, adjudication and enforcement machinery for the work caught by the notice.

Section 62 BIF Act — effect of giving notice of claim for subcontractors’ charges

(1) This section applies if a person gives a notice of claim under chapter 4 in relation to construction work or related goods and services the subject of a construction contract.

(2) Proceedings or other action may not be started or continued by the person under part 3 for all or part of the construction work or related goods and services.

(3) Without limiting subsection (2), if the person gave a respondent a payment claim for all or part of the construction work or related goods and services before or at the same time as giving the notice of claim—

(a) the respondent is not required to pay an amount to the person under section 77(2) in relation to the claim; and

(b) amounts may not be recovered by the person as a debt owing to the person in any court of competent jurisdiction in relation to the claim; and

(c) if the person made an adjudication application in relation to the claim and the application has not been decided by an adjudicator before the notice of claim is given, the person is taken to have withdrawn the application; and

(d) if the person made an adjudication application in relation to the claim and the application has been decided by an adjudicator before the notice of claim was given—

(i) the respondent to the application is not required to pay the adjudicated amount under section 90; and

(ii) the registrar must not give the person an adjudication certificate under section 91 relating to the adjudication; and

(iii) any adjudication certificate provided in relation to the adjudication can not be enforced by the person under section 93 as a judgment of a court; and

(e) the person may not suspend, or continue to suspend, carrying out all or part of the construction work or the supply of the related goods and services under section 98.

(5) This section does not stop the person serving, under this chapter, a payment claim in relation to all or part of the construction work or related goods and services and taking other action under this chapter in relation to that claim, if the notice of claim in so far as it relates to the construction work or related goods and services, or part, is withdrawn.

What s 62 actually freezes

Section 62 is triggered by a single act — giving a notice of claim under Chapter 4 — and its effect is matched to the “construction work or related goods and services” described in that notice. It is not all-or-nothing for your contract; it is scoped to whatever you have charged. Within that scope, s 62(2) blocks any further “proceedings or other action” under Part 3, which is the part of Chapter 3 that runs the payment-claim, payment-schedule, adjudication and enforcement process.

Section 62(3) then spells out the consequences for a payment claim already in flight when the notice is given. The respondent is released from its s 77(2) obligation to pay the claim by the due date; the claimant cannot recover the claim as a debt in court; a pending adjudication application is taken to have been withdrawn; and a decided but un-enforced adjudication is neutralised — the respondent doesn’t have to pay the adjudicated amount under s 90, the registrar must not issue an adjudication certificate under s 91, and any certificate already provided cannot be enforced under s 93 as a judgment. The s 98 right to suspend work for non-payment also drops away.

The freeze is not permanent. Under s 62(5), if you withdraw the notice of claim — or withdraw it to the extent it covers particular work — the Chapter 3 machinery is available again for that work. You can serve a fresh payment claim and run it through to adjudication and enforcement in the ordinary way.

Why a defective notice still bites

The trigger in s 62(1) is the giving of “a notice of claim under chapter 4”. That raises the obvious question: does a notice that is technically defective — wrong form, missing particulars, served on the wrong party — still flip the s 62 switch? The Court of Appeal addressed the equivalent provision under the predecessor BCIPA in Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2013] QCA 386, holding that it is implicit in the legislation that a notice of claim of charge must be a valid one before it engages the freezing provision.1 An invalid notice does not stop a Chapter 3 claim because, in legal terms, no notice of claim has been given at all.

The Court of Appeal also addressed what it takes to lift the freeze where a notice has been given. In the same decision, on the meaning of “withdrawn” in the equivalent of s 62(5), the Court said:

There is an even stronger argument that the reference to a notice of claim of charge being “withdrawn” in s 4(6) of the Act is a reference to the withdrawal of a notice of claim of charge in such a way that is effective in respect of all relevant parties regardless of whether it is in the approved form.

The practical takeaway is that withdrawal is judged by substance, not form. A clear written notification to the respondent and the higher party that the charge is withdrawn for the relevant work should be enough to re-open the Chapter 3 route, even if the original notice was not in the approved form.

What this means on a project

  • Decide which regime you are running on before you serve anything. A notice of claim of charge under Chapter 4 will freeze your Chapter 3 rights for the same scope of work the moment it is given — you can’t hedge by running both in parallel.
  • If you have already served a payment claim and you now want to charge, understand the cost of switching: any pending adjudication application is treated as withdrawn, the respondent’s s 77(2) due-date payment obligation falls away, and you lose the s 98 right to suspend.
  • If you have a live adjudication decision or certificate, giving a notice of charge for the same work disables enforcement under s 93. Don’t serve a notice of charge over work where you are about to file the adjudication certificate as a judgment unless you accept that consequence.
  • Match the scope of the notice to the scope of the dispute. Section 62 only freezes Chapter 3 for the work or services described in the notice, so if your charge covers only part of the contract, the rest of your Chapter 3 rights remain on foot.
  • Withdrawal under s 62(5) re-opens Chapter 3 for the withdrawn portion. If the charge isn’t getting traction up the contractual chain, withdrawing it — clearly and to all relevant parties — lets you serve a fresh payment claim and run a Chapter 3 process for the same work.
  • If you are the respondent and you have just received a notice of charge, the Chapter 3 clock for the work covered by the notice has stopped. Don’t pay, don’t adjudicate and don’t enforce against that scope while the notice is on foot — but check the validity of the notice itself, because an invalid notice does not engage s 62 at all.
  1. Heavy Plant Leasing Pty Ltd v McConnell Dowell Constructors (Aust) Pty Ltd & Ors [2013] QCA 386 (Queensland Court of Appeal) — on the predecessor s 4 of BCIPA, the Court held it is implicit in the words of the section that a notice of claim of charge must be valid before it engages the freezing provision, and that “withdrawal” under the equivalent of s 62(5) is judged by substance and is effective against all relevant parties whether or not the original notice was in the approved form.

Valid subcontractor’s charge

Withdrawal of a notice

A notice of claim under chapter 4 freezes money in the hands of the payer above your contractor — once the s 122 notice has been served, that party is statutorily required to retain the amount claimed pending resolution. Section 125 lets you turn that off again. It is the formal mechanism by which a subcontractor can dismantle its own charge, in whole or in part, before the matter goes any further.

Section 125 BIF Act — Withdrawing a notice of claim

125 Withdrawing a notice of claim

(1) A subcontractor may at any time withdraw, wholly or partly, a notice of claim by giving notice of the withdrawal, in the approved form, to the person to whom the subcontractor gave the notice of claim.

(2) If a subcontractor withdraws, wholly or partly, a notice of claim under subsection (1), the subcontractor must give a copy of the notice of withdrawal to each of the persons to whom the subcontractor gave a copy of the notice of claim.

What the section actually does

Three features of s 125 are worth pulling out. First, withdrawal is available “at any time”. There is no statutory deadline, no requirement that proceedings have been commenced or not commenced, and no need to show a change of circumstances. If you decide the charge should come off — because you’ve been paid, because you’ve agreed a settlement, because you’ve over-claimed, or because you simply want to walk away — you can withdraw it.

Second, withdrawal can be partial. You can reduce the quantum claimed (for example, where part of the claimed amount has been paid or compromised) without giving up the charge in respect of the balance. The Act treats a partial withdrawal as a valid form of notice in its own right, so the operative figure on the charge becomes whatever remains after the withdrawal takes effect.

Third, withdrawal must be done in the approved form. Section 125(1) doesn’t set out the content; it points to the form approved under the Act for that purpose. Using your own letter or email instead of the approved form risks the withdrawal being ineffective, with the consequence that the charge continues to bite money in the payer’s hands and the payer continues to be exposed under s 122.1

Service mirrors the original notice

Section 125 is built around symmetry with s 122 and s 123. The withdrawal goes to the same person you served the original notice of claim on — the party above your contractor in the chain who is obliged to pay the money — and copies must go to everyone who received a copy of the original notice. In a typical chain that means the payer gets the withdrawal, your contractor gets a copy, and any other party that was copied on the s 123 distribution gets a copy as well.

The same practical service rules that apply to the original notice apply to the withdrawal. Chapter 4 doesn’t prescribe a method, so s 39 of the Acts Interpretation Act 1954 (Qld) and, for companies, s 109X of the Corporations Act 2001 (Cth) do the work: head office, registered office, principal office, or personal delivery to a director. Get the legal entity right, use a method you can prove, and avoid PO boxes and bare cloud-link service. The point of the withdrawal is to release the money — if the payer can’t verify it, the payer will keep retaining.

Why subcontractors withdraw

The most common trigger is payment or compromise. Once the disputed amount has been paid, or a settlement deed has been signed, the subcontractor is contractually obliged to release the charge and a s 125 withdrawal is the mechanism. A second common trigger is recalibration: a subcontractor that has claimed too high a figure in the original s 122 notice will sometimes withdraw and re-issue, or use a partial withdrawal to bring the operative amount back into line with what is genuinely owed. A third is tactical retreat — for example, where it has become clear that the chapter 4 path is the wrong one and the subcontractor wants to fall back on its chapter 3 progress payment rights without the chapter 4 charge complicating the position.

Whatever the reason, the legal effect of a properly served s 125 withdrawal is to lift the statutory retention obligation on the payer to the extent of the withdrawal. The payer is then free to deal with the money in the ordinary course, subject to any other claims or notices it may have received.

What this means on a project

  • Use the approved form. Section 125(1) requires it. A letter, email or settlement deed recital is not a substitute — lodge the form so the payer has something it can act on without legal advice.
  • Mirror your original distribution exactly. Whoever received the s 122 notice gets the withdrawal; whoever received a s 123 copy gets a copy of the withdrawal. Don’t skip the contractor — s 125(2) makes the copy mandatory.
  • Decide whether the withdrawal is whole or partial before you sign. If you are settling part of the claim and pursuing the balance, the withdrawal needs to specify the amount being withdrawn so the operative figure on the remaining charge is clear.
  • Tie the withdrawal to the payment or settlement mechanism. In a deed of settlement, make execution and lodgement of the s 125 withdrawal a condition of payment, not a post-payment promise — otherwise you are relying on the subcontractor’s good faith to lift the charge.
  • Serve the withdrawal the way you served the original. Personal delivery, courier with proof of delivery, post to head office or registered office, or email to a clearly nominated business address. Keep the proof; the payer will want to see it before releasing funds.
  • If you are the payer, don’t release on a partial or informal withdrawal until you have the approved form and have confirmed the chain — your statutory retention obligation under s 122 only falls away to the extent the s 125 withdrawal is valid.
  1. The “approved form” is the form approved by the commissioner under s 199 of the BIF Act for the purpose of s 125. The current form is published on the QBCC website. The same approved-form requirement applies to the original notice of claim under s 122 and to the contractor’s response under s 124, so practitioners working in chapter 4 should keep the current versions of all three forms on hand.

Enforcing a subcontractor’s charge

Court proceedings to recover

A notice of claim of charge under Chapter 4 doesn’t pay you on its own. Once the contractor has responded under s 128, the next step is a court action: it is the court — not the principal, the contractor or the security holder — that ultimately decides whether the charge attaches and orders the money out. Sections 130 and 131 set up what the holder of the security must do in the meantime, and both routes funnel the dispute into the same place: “the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard”.

Sections 130 and 131 BIF Act — use of security pending court order

130 Use of security for benefit of subcontractor if contractor accepts liability for all claims

(1) This section applies if—

(a) a subcontractor gives a notice of claim to a person under section 122 because the person is obliged to pay money to a contractor under a contract; and

(b) the subcontractor gives a copy of the notice of claim to the contractor under section 123; and

(c) in the contractor’s response to the claim under section 128, the contractor accepts liability to pay the amount claimed; and

(d) the unsatisfied amount for the contract is more than the retained amount for the contract.

(2) The holder of a security for the contract must—

(a) retain the security until the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard makes an order under section 132 about enforcing the subcontractor’s charge over the security; or

(b) instead of retaining the security—

(i) if the security is held as an amount of money—pay the amount, up to the difference amount for the contract, to the subcontractor or subcontractors to whom the acceptance of liability relates; or

(ii) if the security is not held as an amount of money but may be converted into an amount of money—convert the security, wholly or partly, into an amount of money and pay the amount, up to the difference amount for the contract, to the subcontractor or subcontractors to whom the acceptance of liability relates.

131 Use of security for benefit of subcontractor if contractor does not accept liability for all claims

(2) The holder of a security for the contract must—

(a) retain the security until the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard makes an order under section 132 about enforcing the subcontractor’s charge over the security; or

(b) instead of retaining the security—

(i) if the security is held as an amount of money—pay the amount, up to the difference amount for the contract, into court; or

(ii) if the security is not held as an amount of money but may be converted into an amount of money—convert the security, wholly or partly, into an amount of money and pay the amount, up to the difference amount for the contract, into court.

(7) An amount paid into court under subsection(2)(b) may be paid out only under an order of the court.

Why you have to start a proceeding

The notice of claim mechanism creates a charge over money owed up the contractual chain and freezes that money in the principal’s hands. But the freeze is temporary. Sections 130 and 131 both contemplate the security being “retained” only until “the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard” makes an order under s 132. If you don’t start a proceeding, there is no court, no order under s 132, and ultimately nothing to keep the money sitting where the notice put it. The proceeding is the engine that turns the charge into actual recovery.

The proceeding is also the only way that disputed money paid into court under s 131 comes back out. Section 131(7) is express: an amount paid into court under s 131(2)(b) “may be paid out only under an order of the court”. No agreement between the contractor and the subcontractor, no instruction from the principal, and no withdrawal by the security holder can release that money. It moves only on a court order.

The two pathways into court

The s 128 response sorts your claim into one of two channels, and that determines the urgency and shape of the proceeding.

If the contractor accepts liability under s 128, s 130 governs. The security holder must either retain the security against a s 132 order, or pay it (or its converted value) directly to the subcontractor up to the “difference amount” for the contract. In an accepted-liability case the proceeding tends to be more administrative — you are confirming entitlement and getting the s 132 order so the security holder can release the retained amount cleanly. The personal-liability backstop in s 130(3) gives the security holder strong incentive to do the right thing without litigation, but a court order remains the safest route to a clean release.

If the contractor disputes the claim under s 128, s 131 governs. The security holder either retains the security or pays its converted value into court — not to the subcontractor. The dispute is now genuinely contested, and you need a proceeding to obtain a s 132 order that resolves whether the charge attaches and, if so, in what amount. This is the typical subcontractor’s charge proceeding.

What the proceeding has to achieve

The relief you are ultimately asking for is an order under s 132 enforcing the subcontractor’s charge over the security. Practically, that means the proceeding has to:

  • identify the underlying debt for which the charge is claimed (the work done, money owing, and the contractor against whom the charge is asserted);
  • establish that the notice of claim was validly given under s 122 and copied to the contractor under s 123;
  • establish the contractor’s s 128 response (acceptance or dispute), which fixes whether you are in s 130 or s 131 territory;
  • quantify the “unsatisfied amount” and the “retained amount” for the contract, because the s 130/131 machinery only engages where the unsatisfied amount exceeds the retained amount — the “difference amount” is the headroom available to the subcontractor; and
  • name the holder of the security (typically the principal or head contractor up the chain) so that any s 132 order is binding on the entity actually holding the money.

You will normally join the contractor (as the party against whom the underlying debt is owed) and the security holder (as the party who must hand over the money on a s 132 order). The court’s order under s 132 then unlocks the retained security or releases the funds paid into court under s 131(2)(b).

Don’t miss the time bar

A subcontractor’s charge has a tight statutory life. The proceeding to enforce the charge has to be commenced within the period the Act allows after the notice of claim is given — otherwise the charge lapses, the security holder can deal with the security free of the charge, and any money sitting in court can be paid out without the subcontractor seeing any of it. The time-limit element is dealt with on its own page; the practical point here is that starting the proceeding is not optional and not something to defer once you know the contractor has either accepted under s 128 or disputed.

What this means on a project

  • The notice of claim freezes the money; the court proceeding is what actually recovers it. Plan the proceeding from the moment you serve the notice, not after the s 128 response arrives.
  • Read the s 128 response carefully. Acceptance puts you in s 130 (security holder may pay you directly); dispute puts you in s 131 (money goes into court and only comes out on a court order).
  • Frame the proceeding around s 132 relief. That means joining the contractor and the security holder, pleading the notice and copy under s 122 and s 123, and quantifying both the unsatisfied amount and the retained amount so the difference amount is on the face of the pleading.
  • If the contractor disputes and money has been paid into court under s 131(2)(b), don’t expect any informal release. Section 131(7) means only a court order gets it out. Build that order into the relief sought.
  • Watch the time bar. Once the statutory window closes, the charge lapses and the s 130/131 machinery falls away — the security holder is then free to surrender the security back up the chain.
  1. Sections 130(1) and 131(1) BIF Act each require, as a precondition, that the notice of claim has been given under s 122, copied to the contractor under s 123, and that the contractor has responded under s 128 (accepting in s 130, disputing in s 131), with the unsatisfied amount exceeding the retained amount for the contract.
  2. The “difference amount”, “retained amount” and “unsatisfied amount” are defined in s 130(7) and s 131(8). Both definitions feed off amounts retained under s 126(2) and amounts paid into court under s 126(4), so the quantification on which any s 132 order rests is built on the Chapter 4 retention machinery upstream of the proceeding.
  3. Section 131(7) BIF Act — money paid into court under s 131(2)(b) “may be paid out only under an order of the court”.

Enforcing a subcontractor’s charge

Time limit to commence

A subcontractor’s charge does not look after itself. Once the notice of claim has been given under s 122, copied to the contractor under s 123, and the contractor has responded under s 128, the holder of the security is sitting on money that is frozen only until the court does something with it. Section 130(2)(a) makes that explicit: the security is to be retained “until the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard makes an order under section 132 about enforcing the subcontractor’s charge over the security”. If you don’t put a proceeding before that court, no s 132 order is ever made and the freeze comes off.

Section 130 BIF Act — retention pending a s 132 court order

130 Use of security for benefit of subcontractor if contractor accepts liability for all claims

(1) This section applies if—

(a) a subcontractor gives a notice of claim to a person under section 122 because the person is obliged to pay money to a contractor under a contract; and

(b) the subcontractor gives a copy of the notice of claim to the contractor under section 123; and

(c) in the contractor’s response to the claim under section 128, the contractor accepts liability to pay the amount claimed; and

(d) the unsatisfied amount for the contract is more than the retained amount for the contract.

(2) The holder of a security for the contract must—

(a) retain the security until the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard makes an order under section 132 about enforcing the subcontractor’s charge over the security; or

(b) instead of retaining the security—

(i) if the security is held as an amount of money—pay the amount, up to the difference amount for the contract, to the subcontractor or subcontractors to whom the acceptance of liability relates; or

(ii) if the security is not held as an amount of money but may be converted into an amount of money—convert the security, wholly or partly, into an amount of money and pay the amount, up to the difference amount for the contract, to the subcontractor or subcontractors to whom the acceptance of liability relates.

(5) Subsections (2) and (3) do not stop the holder of the security from exercising an entitlement to use the security for securing the performance of the contract, including by keeping control of the security until the security would be required to be surrendered, wholly or partly, if this section did not apply.

Why the clock matters

The whole architecture of Chapter 4 assumes that a court proceeding is on foot or imminent. The notice of claim under s 122 and the copy under s 123 are the front-end formalities; the contractor’s s 128 response sorts the claim into the s 130 (accepted) or s 131 (disputed) channel; and from that point the security holder is holding either the original security, or money standing in for it, against a future order of the court under s 132. If you let the time to commence pass without filing, the foundation for retention falls away and the security holder is free to deal with the security on the contract’s own terms — including, under s 130(5), surrendering it back up the chain when the contract would otherwise require.

What “commence” means in this context

Commencing in time means filing a proceeding in the court that will hear the subcontractor’s claim — the same court referred to in s 130(2)(a) as “the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard”. That proceeding must seek relief under s 132 enforcing the charge over the security. Filing a debt claim against the contractor on its own is not the same thing: a money judgment against the contractor for the underlying debt does not, by itself, engage the s 132 machinery that releases the retained security or the funds paid into court under s 131. The proceeding has to be framed so the court can make the s 132 order the security holder is waiting for.

What the proceeding has to capture

To stop time and keep the charge alive, the originating process should put the s 130/s 132 framework squarely before the court. That means:

  • identifying the contract under which the money up the chain is owed and the work that grounds the underlying debt;
  • pleading the notice of claim under s 122 and the copy to the contractor under s 123, with dates;
  • pleading the contractor’s s 128 response and whether liability was accepted (s 130) or disputed (s 131);
  • quantifying the “unsatisfied amount”, the “retained amount” and the resulting “difference amount” for the contract, since s 130 only engages where the unsatisfied amount exceeds the retained amount; and
  • seeking, as primary relief, an order under s 132 enforcing the charge over the security held by the security holder.

You will normally name both the contractor (as the party against whom the underlying debt is asserted) and the security holder (as the party who has to act on the s 132 order). Without the security holder before the court, an order under s 132 is of limited practical use — it has to bind the entity actually holding the money.

If the contractor accepted liability under s 128

Even where the contractor has accepted liability and the security holder may, under s 130(2)(b), pay the difference amount directly to the subcontractor, you should still treat the time bar as live. The holder may instead choose to retain under s 130(2)(a) pending the s 132 order, and the personal-liability backstop in s 130(3) does not extend the time within which the proceeding has to be on foot. If retention is the chosen route, the order out of the court is what releases the money cleanly.

What this means on a project

  • Diary the time-bar date the moment you serve the notice of claim under s 122. Don’t wait for the s 128 response to start the clock in your own system.
  • Plan the proceeding in parallel with the notice. Drafting an originating application that pleads s 122, s 123, s 128 and seeks s 132 relief takes time, and you don’t want to be doing it in the last week of the statutory window.
  • If the contractor accepts liability under s 128, still file. A s 132 order is the cleanest way to get the security released, and s 130(2)(a) contemplates the holder retaining until that order is made.
  • If the contractor disputes and money is going into court under s 131, the proceeding is unavoidable — that money only comes out on a court order. Build the s 132 application around it.
  • Name the security holder as a respondent. The s 132 order has to bind the party actually holding the security; otherwise you have a piece of paper but no mechanism to compel release.
  • If the deadline is genuinely at risk, file a protective originating process and refine the pleading later. A bare-bones filing inside time is recoverable; a perfect pleading filed out of time is not.
  1. Section 130(2)(a) BIF Act — the holder of a security must retain the security “until the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard makes an order under section 132 about enforcing the subcontractor’s charge over the security”. The same retention-pending-s 132 structure is reflected in s 131 for disputed claims.
  2. The “unsatisfied amount”, “retained amount” and “difference amount” are defined in s 130(7), and feed off amounts retained under s 126(2) and amounts paid into court under s 126(4). Quantifying these in the originating process matters because s 130 only applies where the unsatisfied amount exceeds the retained amount.
  3. Section 130(5) preserves the security holder’s contractual entitlements to use the security for performance of the contract once the Chapter 4 machinery is no longer engaged — which is what happens if the time to commence passes without a proceeding being on foot.

Enforcing a subcontractor’s charge

Necessary parties / joinder

A proceeding to enforce a subcontractor’s charge under Chapter 4 of the BIF Act is not a simple debt claim. It has to deliver an order under s 132 that binds the people actually holding, owing or contesting the money — otherwise the order can’t do its job of releasing the security or the funds paid into court. Getting the parties right at the start is therefore as important as getting the notice of claim right at the start of the charge process.

Section 131 BIF Act — use of security where contractor disputes the claim

131 Use of security for benefit of subcontractor if contractor does not accept liability for all claims

(1) This section applies if—

(a) a subcontractor gives a notice of claim to a person under section 122 because the person is obliged to pay money to a contractor under a contract; and

(b) the subcontractor gives a copy of the notice of claim to the contractor under section 123; and

(c) in the contractor’s response to the claim under section 128, the contractor disputes the claim (does not accept liability to pay the amount claimed); and

(d) the unsatisfied amount for the contract is more than the retained amount for the contract.

(2) The holder of a security for the contract must—

(a) retain the security until the court in which the subcontractor’s claim is heard makes an order under section 132 about enforcing the subcontractor’s charge over the security; or

(b) instead of retaining the security—

(i) if the security is held as an amount of money—pay the amount, up to the difference amount for the contract, into court; or

(ii) if the security is not held as an amount of money but may be converted into an amount of money—convert the security, wholly or partly, into an amount of money and pay the amount, up to the difference amount for the contract, into court.

(3) If the holder of the security does not comply with subsection (2), the holder is personally liable to pay the subcontractor the amount of the subcontractor’s claim to the extent that the security would have been capable under this chapter, if the holder had complied with the subsection, of satisfying the claim.

(7) An amount paid into court under subsection (2)(b) may be paid out only under an order of the court.

Who has to be in the proceeding

Section 131 sets up a triangle. The subcontractor has the charge and is the moving party. The contractor is the entity to whom the money up the chain is owed under the upstream contract, and is the party whose s 128 response either accepts or disputes the claim. The holder of the security — usually the principal or the next party up the contractual chain — is the entity sitting on the money or the bond and is the party who must comply with s 131(2). All three corners of that triangle have to be before the court if the s 132 order is to do anything useful.

The subcontractor’s position is straightforward: it sues as plaintiff or applicant on the underlying debt and asks for an order enforcing the charge over the security. The contractor is joined because the underlying debt is owed by the contractor — you cannot get a s 132 order without first establishing that there is, in fact, money owing for which the charge can attach. And the holder of the security has to be joined because s 131(2) imposes a personal obligation on the holder to retain the security or pay it into court, and s 132 is the mechanism by which the court tells the holder what to do with it.

Why the security holder has to be joined

The security holder is not optional. Section 131(2) makes the holder — not the contractor — the entity who must retain the security or convert and pay it into court. If the holder fails to comply, s 131(3) makes the holder personally liable to the subcontractor up to the amount the security would have satisfied. That personal liability is a powerful lever, but it also means the holder has its own distinct legal interest in the proceeding: the holder needs to know what to do with the security to discharge that liability under s 131(4).

Section 131(7) reinforces the point. Where the holder has paid the difference amount into court under s 131(2)(b), that money “may be paid out only under an order of the court”. No agreement between the contractor and the subcontractor will release it. So the proceeding has to be framed so that the s 132 order, when it lands, both binds the holder (so the holder can safely act on it under s 131(4)) and authorises the registry to pay out the funds in court. Joining the holder achieves both.

Why the contractor has to be joined

The contractor is the debtor on the underlying contract and the party whose s 128 response shapes the whole proceeding. Without the contractor in the proceeding, the court can’t finally determine the underlying debt, can’t test whether the s 128 response was correct, and can’t make findings that would bind the contractor in any later argument about the “unsatisfied amount” or about the contractor’s rights to the security under s 131(5).

Section 131(5) preserves the holder’s entitlement to use the security for securing the performance of the contract — that is, the contractor’s contractual obligations to the holder. The contractor has a real interest in how that entitlement plays out alongside the subcontractor’s charge, because every dollar that goes out under a s 132 order is a dollar no longer available to satisfy the contractor’s own contractual obligations to the holder. The contractor is therefore a necessary party on its own contractual interest, separate from being the underlying debtor.

Multiple subcontractors and competing claims

Where more than one subcontractor has given a notice of claim against the same contractor on the same upstream contract, the “unsatisfied amount” under s 131(8) is the total of all the relevant claims of charge, and the “retained amount” aggregates everything the holder is retaining or has paid into court for the contract. If the difference amount won’t cover everyone, the court has to allocate. That is only sensible if the competing claimants are before the court — either as co-plaintiffs, by joinder, or by consolidating the proceedings — so the s 132 order can rank and apportion the available money in a single hearing rather than producing inconsistent outcomes across multiple cases.

Effect of contractual provisions about the security

Section 131(6) overrides any provision in the contract, in another arrangement, or in the security itself that would either stop the holder from complying with s 131(2) or operate to the holder’s detriment if it does comply. That matters at the parties stage because it kills off any argument by the holder that, for example, a bond instrument or a deed up the chain entitles a third party to call on the security ahead of the subcontractor’s charge. You don’t need to join those third parties merely because the contract says they have rights over the security; the BIF Act has already neutralised those rights to the extent they conflict with s 131(2).

What this means on a project

  • Plead and join three parties: the contractor (debtor on the upstream contract and the party who responded under s 128), and the holder of the security (who must comply with s 131(2) and against whom the s 132 order will run). You are the third corner.
  • Don’t shortcut by suing only the contractor. A judgment against the contractor on the debt is not an order under s 132, and it doesn’t bind the holder or get money out of court under s 131(7).
  • If you suspect other subcontractors have lodged notices on the same upstream contract, raise consolidation or joinder early. The “unsatisfied amount” and “difference amount” in s 131(8) are calculated across all claims, so a one-claimant proceeding can produce a misleading result.
  • Identify the holder by name. The holder is the entity actually sitting on the bond, retention or money — not necessarily the entity named on the contract you have. Confirm before issuing.
  • Don’t be drawn into joining every party named in the security instrument or in upstream contracts. Section 131(6) flattens contractual rights that would frustrate compliance with s 131(2); you only need the parties whose presence is necessary for a working s 132 order.
  1. Section 131(2) BIF Act imposes the duty to retain or pay into court directly on the “holder of a security for the contract”; s 131(3) makes the holder personally liable to the subcontractor for non-compliance, and s 131(4) provides that a payment under s 131(2)(b) discharges the holder of further liability for the amount paid and the costs of any related proceeding.
  2. Section 131(7) BIF Act — an amount paid into court under s 131(2)(b) “may be paid out only under an order of the court”. Any s 132 order has to be framed and served so that the registry can act on it; that requires the holder to be a party.
  3. Section 131(5) BIF Act preserves the holder’s entitlement to use the security for securing the performance of the contract, which gives the contractor a distinct interest in the proceeding separate from its position as underlying debtor. Section 131(6) renders ineffective any contract, arrangement or security provision that purports to stop the holder complying with s 131(2) or to operate to the holder’s detriment if it does.
  4. The aggregating definitions of “unsatisfied amount” and “retained amount” in s 131(8) BIF Act mean that competing notices of claim on the same upstream contract are calculated together, which in practice requires competing claimants to be before the same court if the s 132 order is to apportion the difference amount sensibly.

Enforcing a subcontractor’s charge

The contractor’s response

Once a subcontractor serves a notice of claim of charge and gives the contractor a copy under s 123, the ball is in the contractor’s court. Section 128 forces the contractor to break cover — within ten business days — and say in the approved form whether the claim is accepted or disputed. That response is the hinge of the whole Chapter 4 process: it determines whether the security holder pays you out under s 130 or pays the disputed money into court under s 131, and it sets up the s 132 application that ultimately releases the security.

Section 128 BIF Act — Contractor given copy of notice of claim must respond

(1) This section applies if a subcontractor gives a contractor a copy of a notice of claim.

(2) The contractor must give both of the following persons a written response to the claim within 10 business days after the contractor is given the copy of the notice of claim, unless the contractor has a reasonable excuse—

(a) the person given the notice of claim;

(b) the subcontractor.

Maximum penalty—20 penalty units.

(3) The response to the claim must be made in the approved form and—

(a) accept liability to pay the amount claimed; or

(b) accept liability to pay an amount stated in the response, but otherwise dispute the claim; or

(c) dispute the claim.

Who has to respond, and to whom

The obligation falls on the contractor — the party with which the subcontractor has its contract — once it has been given a copy of the notice of claim under s 123. The response has to go to two people: the person who was given the notice of claim itself (typically the principal or party further up the chain holding money for the contractor), and the subcontractor making the claim. A response that is sent only to the subcontractor, or only to the principal, doesn’t comply.

The clock is ten business days from when the contractor is given the copy. There is no extension mechanism in the section, and missing the deadline carries a maximum penalty of 20 penalty units. The only escape valve is “reasonable excuse”, which is not defined and which a contractor relies on at its own risk — the safer course is always to respond inside the ten-day window, even if the response is provisional and disputes the claim.

Three options, and only three

Section 128(3) closes off any “wait and see” response. The contractor must do one of three things, in the approved form:

  • accept liability to pay the full amount claimed (s 128(3)(a)) — this puts the claim into s 130 territory, and the security holder must then either retain the security pending a s 132 order or pay the subcontractor up to the difference amount;
  • accept liability to pay a lesser amount stated in the response, and otherwise dispute the claim (s 128(3)(b)) — this is a partial acceptance, and the dispute over the balance feeds into s 131; or
  • dispute the claim outright (s 128(3)(c)) — this puts the whole claim into s 131, and the security holder must retain the security or pay the relevant amount into court rather than to the subcontractor.

A response that doesn’t fit one of these three boxes, or that is given outside the approved form, is not a s 128 response. Practically, the most common defects are responses that hedge (“under review”, “awaiting further information”), responses sent by email or letter rather than in the approved form, and responses sent only to the principal without copying the subcontractor.

Why the response matters downstream

The s 128 response is what sorts the claim into one of two statutory channels. Acceptance under s 128(3)(a) (or partial acceptance under s 128(3)(b) for the accepted portion) engages s 130: the holder of the security can pay the accepted amount directly to the subcontractor up to the difference amount, or retain the security against a s 132 order. Dispute under s 128(3)(c) (or the disputed portion under s 128(3)(b)) engages s 131: the holder must either retain the security or pay it into court, where it stays locked until the court orders otherwise.

So the response does two things that the contractor cannot undo later. It locks the contractor into a position on the underlying debt (acceptance is hard to walk back in the subsequent court proceeding), and it determines whether the disputed money sits with the principal/security holder or goes into court under s 131. Once funds are paid into court, only a court order will get them out — the contractor and subcontractor cannot release them by agreement.

If the contractor doesn’t respond

Silence is not neutral. The penalty in s 128(2) bites on a non-responding contractor regardless of the merits of the underlying claim. More importantly, a failure to respond doesn’t pause the Chapter 4 machinery: the subcontractor still has the charge over money in the principal’s hands, and the absence of a response will be evidence in any subsequent s 132 application that the claim should be enforced over the security.

What this means on a project

  • If you are the contractor and you receive a copy of a notice of claim, diary the ten business day deadline immediately. Use the approved form — not a letter, not an email — and send it to both the person given the notice and the subcontractor.
  • Pick one of the three boxes in s 128(3). If you genuinely accept part and dispute part, use the partial-acceptance limb in s 128(3)(b) and state the accepted figure precisely. Don’t leave the response open-ended.
  • If you accept liability under s 128(3)(a), understand the consequence: you have set up s 130, and the security holder may pay the subcontractor directly. Acceptance is also difficult to retract in the s 132 proceeding that follows.
  • If you dispute, dispute clearly under s 128(3)(c). That puts the matter into s 131, the money goes into court rather than to the subcontractor, and only a court order under s 132 will release it.
  • If you are the subcontractor, read the response carefully. It tells you whether you are heading into a s 130 (administrative) or s 131 (contested) application for s 132 relief, and it fixes the accepted amount you can build on in the proceeding. A non-compliant or absent response is itself a fact you will rely on in court.
  1. The trigger for s 128 is the contractor being given a copy of the notice of claim under s 123 — not service of the notice itself on the principal under s 122. The ten business day clock runs from receipt of the copy.
  2. The “approved form” for a s 128 response is the form approved by the commissioner under the Act; a response not made in that form does not satisfy s 128(3) even if it otherwise communicates acceptance or dispute.
  3. The response feeds directly into s 130 (acceptance) and s 131 (dispute), and ultimately into a s 132 application: see s 132(1)–(2), under which the court may make “the order it considers appropriate for enforcing the subcontractor’s charge over the security, including an order for realising the security”.

Enforcing a subcontractor’s charge

Set-off and counterclaim by the contractor

When a subcontractor lodges a notice of claim of charge, the contractor will often look for ways to chip the claim back — cross-claims for defective work, liquidated damages, back-charges, or a security the subcontractor has lodged that the contractor wants to apply against the very money the charge attaches to. Chapter 4 lets ordinary set-offs and counterclaims run, but it draws a hard line at one specific manoeuvre: a contractor cannot use a subcontractor’s own security to defeat the contractor’s performance obligations to a third party. Section 133 is the provision that polices that line.

Section 133 BIF Act — particular subcontractor securities of no effect

133 Particular subcontractor securities of no effect

To the extent that something is given by or for a subcontractor for securing, wholly or partly, the performance by a contractor of the contractor’s contract it is of no effect.

What s 133 actually voids

Section 133 is narrow and surgical. It catches anything — cash retention, bank guarantee, parent company guarantee, charge over assets, indemnity — that is “given by or for a subcontractor” and that secures, wholly or partly, the performance by the contractor of the contractor’s contract (i.e. the head contract or the next contract up the chain). To the extent it does that work, it is “of no effect”. The drafting is deliberately pitched at the function of the security, not its label: it doesn’t matter what the document is called or how the parties have characterised it, if it operates to underwrite the contractor’s performance to a higher party using the subcontractor’s money or credit, that operation is statutorily neutralised.

What s 133 does not do is wipe out every set-off or counterclaim the contractor might assert. A genuine cross-claim arising under the subcontract — defective work, delay damages, overpayments, unreturned plant — survives s 133 untouched. Those are debts owed by the subcontractor to the contractor in the ordinary course; the section says nothing about them. It is only the artificial device of leveraging a subcontractor-supplied security upstream that is targeted.

How this interacts with the charge

The mischief s 133 addresses is obvious once you map it onto the Chapter 4 machinery. A subcontractor serves a notice of claim under s 122; money up the chain is frozen under s 126; the security holder retains funds against a s 132 order. If the contractor could point to a security “given by or for” the subcontractor and apply it to discharge the contractor’s own obligations to the principal, the subcontractor’s charge would be stripped of its substance — the very money meant to back the charge would be siphoned off to perform the contractor’s contract one level up. Section 133 closes that exit.

The provision works alongside the prohibition in s 200 on contracting out of the Act. A clause in a subcontract that purports to let the contractor apply the subcontractor’s retention or bank guarantee against the contractor’s own performance obligations to the principal is, to that extent, a nullity under s 133, and is independently unenforceable as a contracting-out under s 200.1

Set-offs that do survive

Outside the s 133 prohibition, the contractor’s ordinary entitlements are unaffected. The court in a proceeding to enforce the charge will quantify the “unsatisfied amount” for the contract, and that figure is the net amount actually owing to the subcontractor after legitimate cross-claims have been taken into account. If the contractor has a real claim for defective work or liquidated damages, it can be pleaded in answer to the underlying debt, and it will reduce or extinguish the amount the charge can attach to. The charge attaches only to what is properly owing.

The point was put squarely by McMurdo J (as his Honour then was) in Karam Group Pty Ltd v HCA Queensland Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 95, dealing with the equivalent provisions of the predecessor Subcontractors’ Charges Act 1974 (Qld). His Honour treated the charge as attaching to the balance “justly owing” to the subcontractor, after taking into account proper cross-claims under the subcontract.2 Section 133 doesn’t stop that exercise; it only stops the contractor reaching across to a security the subcontractor itself has put up to make good the contractor’s performance further up the chain.

What this means on a project

  • If you are a subcontractor, your retention or bank guarantee cannot be used by the contractor to perform the head contract or any contract above it. Any subcontract clause to that effect is void to that extent under s 133.
  • Read any “set-off” or “application of security” clause in the subcontract carefully. A clause limited to set-off of cross-claims under the subcontract is fine; a clause that lets the contractor apply your security against its obligations to the principal is not.
  • If you are a contractor, expect to have to prove your cross-claims on their merits in the charge proceeding. You can’t shortcut that by pointing to a subcontractor-supplied security and saying the principal has called on it — s 133 strips that out.
  • When framing the charge proceeding, plead the underlying debt net of any genuine subcontract cross-claims. The court’s s 132 order will move on the “unsatisfied amount”, which is the real balance owing — not a notional figure ignoring legitimate set-offs.
  • If the principal has already drawn on a subcontractor-supplied security to cover the contractor’s default upstream, that drawing is “of no effect” against the subcontractor under s 133. Raise it in the proceeding; the money should be treated as still available to the charge.
  1. Section 200 BIF Act voids any provision of an agreement that purports to exclude, modify or restrict the operation of the Act, or that may reasonably be construed as an attempt to do so. A subcontract clause permitting the contractor to apply subcontractor-supplied security to the contractor’s own performance obligations engages both s 133 and s 200.
  2. Karam Group Pty Ltd v HCA Queensland Pty Ltd [2014] QSC 95 (McMurdo J) — charge attaches to the amount justly owing to the subcontractor under the subcontract, after legitimate cross-claims; decided under the Subcontractors’ Charges Act 1974 (Qld), the predecessor to Chapter 4 of the BIF Act.

Enforcing a subcontractor’s charge

Discharge of the charge

A subcontractor’s charge under Chapter 4 is a creature of statute, and so is the way it ends. Section 135 isn’t about the substantive rights you bring to court — it is the “one proceeding for everyone” rule. Once any subcontractor commences an action to enforce a charge, that action is treated as also brought on behalf of every other subcontractor whose charge over the same money is still alive. The practical consequence: you don’t need to start your own proceeding to protect your charge, but you do have to join in if you want to share in the money.

Section 135 BIF Act — enforcing subcontractor’s charge on behalf of other subcontractors

135 Enforcing subcontractor’s charge on behalf of other subcontractors

(1) An action to enforce a subcontractor’s charge under this chapter may be brought by or on behalf of any number of subcontractors claiming subcontractors’ charges.

(2) Every action brought by a subcontractor to enforce a subcontractor’s charge is deemed to be also brought on behalf of every other subcontractor—

(a) who has given a notice of claim under section 122; and

(b) whose charge has not been extinguished under section 136(3); and

(c) who becomes a party to the action in accordance with Uniform Civil Procedure Rules 1999 and subsection (3).

(3) Subject to the Uniform Civil Procedure Rules 1999, a subcontractor may become a party at any time before the date appointed for the hearing or any adjournment of the hearing.

One action, many claimants

Section 135(1) lets a single proceeding be brought by, or on behalf of, any number of subcontractors. That keeps the dispute about the same pool of retained money in one place and stops a principal or security holder from being chased through parallel proceedings over the same security. Where several subcontractors down the same chain have served notices of claim under s 122, the sensible course is one action with all of them as parties — the court can then sort out priorities and quantify each charge on the same evidence.

The deeming rule and why it matters

Subsection (2) is the critical provision for project managers. Once any subcontractor commences an action to enforce a charge, that action is “deemed to be also brought on behalf of every other subcontractor” who has given a s 122 notice and whose charge has not been extinguished under s 136(3). You do not need a fresh originating process of your own. The first proceeding off the rank carries the others.

That is a real protection — but it is also a trap. The deeming only operates while three conditions are simultaneously met: a valid notice of claim under s 122; a charge that has not been extinguished under s 136(3); and the subcontractor actually becoming a party to the existing action under the Uniform Civil Procedure Rules 1999 and s 135(3). Miss the third step and the deeming is empty: you may be “deemed” to be on the action, but if you never join it, you cannot take an order under s 132 in your own favour and you cannot share in any money paid out.

The cut-off for joining

Section 135(3) sets the joinder window. Subject to the UCPR, a subcontractor may become a party “at any time before the date appointed for the hearing or any adjournment of the hearing”. The window is generous — it runs right up to the door of the courtroom — but it is finite. Once the hearing starts (or, if the hearing is adjourned, once the adjourned hearing starts), the gate closes for any subcontractor who has not yet joined. The leading appellate treatment of the predecessor provision under the Subcontractors’ Charges Act 1974 is Ex parte Williams [1980] Qd R 67, which confirmed that the deeming and joinder mechanism is the route by which other charge-holders share in the proceeding — they do not get an automatic distribution merely by holding a charge. The same logic carries into s 135.

The link to extinguishment under s 136(3)

Section 135(2)(b) ties the deeming to s 136(3). If your charge has been extinguished — typically because the statutory life of the charge has run out without you starting or joining a proceeding — the deeming in s 135 cannot resurrect it. The discharge of the charge is dealt with in detail on its own page; the point here is that s 135 is not a workaround for a dead charge. It only carries live charges into the existing action.

What this means on a project

  • If you have served a notice of claim under s 122 and another subcontractor down the same chain has already started a proceeding, you don’t need to commence your own. But you do need to join that proceeding under s 135(3) before the hearing date if you want a s 132 order in your favour.
  • Treat the deeming in s 135(2) as a placeholder, not a payment. Until you become a party, you have no standing to take an order or to receive money out of court.
  • Diary the hearing date as soon as you know there is an existing action. The joinder window closes when the hearing (or adjourned hearing) starts — not when judgment is given.
  • Check your charge is still alive. If the s 136(3) extinguishment has bitten, s 135 cannot help you — you fall outside subsection (2)(b) and the deeming does not apply.
  • Where several subcontractors hold charges over the same retained money, coordinate. One properly pleaded action with all charge-holders as parties is faster and cheaper than parallel proceedings, and it gives the court the full picture for the s 132 order.
  1. Section 135(2)(a) BIF Act requires that the deemed-in subcontractor has given a notice of claim under s 122. Without a valid s 122 notice, there is no charge to be carried by the existing action, and the deeming has nothing to operate on.
  2. Section 135(2)(b) ties the deeming to s 136(3) extinguishment. The statutory life of a subcontractor’s charge and the conditions on which it ends are dealt with separately under s 136.
  3. Ex parte Williams [1980] Qd R 67 (Full Court) — predecessor provision under the Subcontractors’ Charges Act 1974; confirms that the deeming/joinder mechanism is the route by which other charge-holders take a share of the proceeding rather than receiving an automatic distribution.
  4. Joinder under s 135(3) is “subject to the Uniform Civil Procedure Rules 1999”, so the procedural route is the ordinary UCPR joinder rules, applied within the statutory cut-off of the hearing date or any adjourned hearing date.

Enforcing a subcontractor’s charge

Effect of insolvency of the contractor

If the contractor up the chain becomes insolvent, the subcontractor’s charge is often the only thing standing between you and the unsecured-creditor queue. The charge attaches to money owing from the principal to the contractor, not to the contractor’s general assets, so the money you have hit with a notice of claim under s 122 is not part of the contractor’s pool on liquidation. The practical question that then arises is: how far does the charge reach? Section 137 answers that question for the common case where, by the time the contractor falls over, all that is left in the principal’s hands is the retention.

Section 137 BIF Act — claims of charge relating to retention amount not limited

137 Claims of charge relating to retention amount not limited

To remove any doubt, it is declared that, for a subcontractor’s charge relating only to a retention amount—
